# The Line We Learned to Sing

# Chapter 1

By ten in the morning the condolence lilies had started to rot in their plastic sleeves. The ribbons still declared righteousness in black calligraphy, but the petals had gone the color of old dishwater. Ina stood beside the folding table in the crematorium lobby and pressed her thumb into the knot of one ribbon until the silk cut her skin, not hard enough to bleed, just enough to stay inside her hand and not her face.

The lobby had no windows. It smelled like wet wool and cheap instant coffee and that thin burned sweetness from industrial incense sticks, the ones that left no smoke and no mercy. Across from her, a wall-mounted television played election coverage with the volume down. Two men in navy ties argued in subtitles about flood aid to North Hwanghae, each one claiming the other had invented the water.

Aunt Hyejin arrived late with her reading glasses still around her neck and flour dust on her sleeve, church-kitchen proof she had come straight from kneading dough. She set down three paper cups, looked at the table, and took the ribbons off the worst lilies without asking.

"Don't let those sit where people can see," she said. "Your mother would've cursed us for paying full price for flowers that collapsed before lunch."

Hyejin always got busier when she was afraid. She tied the ruined stems together with one of her hair ties and tucked the bundle under the table like she was hiding contraband fruit.

Dohyun sat in the molded plastic chair nearest the exit. He kept one hand on his knee brace and one hand around an unlit cigarette. He had promised he wouldn't smoke inside the building. He honored the promise by rolling the cigarette back and forth between fingers stained a permanent yellow at the edges, the skin glossy where decades of nicotine had burnished it.

He had barely spoken since the death certificate came through. When condolences arrived, he nodded. When someone asked if he had eaten, he lied yes. Now he watched the television subtitles crawl by and did not blink when his wife's name passed under the funeral home logo.

Ina set down her mother's resident card, family registry papers, and the cremation authorization form. A young clerk with a tiny silver rabbit pin on his lapel leaned over the table. He had a blister on his thumb wrapped in a cartoon bandage and a voice that cracked whenever he said "ma'am." He was trying to perform dignity and looked about nineteen.

"For the memorial tablet inscription," he said, tapping the form, "do you want birthplace listed?"

Hyejin answered before Ina could open her mouth. "Yes. Sinuiju. Put it exactly."

Dohyun finally looked up. "No. Leave it off."

The clerk froze between them. His rabbit pin caught the fluorescent light.

Hyejin turned on Dohyun, not loud yet, but the kind of quiet that already had an edge. "She crossed that river at nine years old. You want to erase that on the one day someone writes her whole name on stone?"

"I'm trying to keep this simple," Dohyun said.

"Simple for whom?"

"For our daughter."

Ina could feel both of them pulling at her like two hands on the same door, each convinced force was care. On the television, the subtitles switched to a panel about border reunification visas. Outside, sirens moved along the river road.

"Put Sinuiju," Ina said. "Leave the old district off. Just city and year."

Dohyun's jaw twitched. "People talk."

"People always talk." Hyejin pushed the glasses up her nose with one arthritic knuckle. "Let them chew on something real for once."

The clerk nodded too quickly and wrote it down. His pen squeaked against the cheap carbon paper.

Ina's phone vibrated across the table. Unknown number. She ignored it. The vibration stopped. Started again. Same number.

"Take it," Hyejin said, already counting condolence envelopes into neat stacks by denomination. "Could be your editor."

No editor called three times before eleven. Ina stepped into the corridor between the funeral hall and the vending machines, where the floor tiles changed from gray to a fake wood pattern that made everyone look unsteady.

She answered on the fourth ring.

"Ms. Seo?" a woman said, breathy through a cheap headset. Keyboard clatter in the background. "This is Seoul St. Mary's billing office. I know this is a difficult time. I am calling regarding Account 7714, balance fifty-eight million won. We can enroll you in a hardship plan if you make a minimum payment today."

Ina watched a man wheel a trolley stacked with cardboard urn boxes past her. The boxes were printed with pink lotus flowers and gold foil lettering. Somebody had chosen branding colors for death.

"My mother was cremated this morning," Ina said.

The caller went silent for one beat, two. When she spoke again her voice had dropped the script and found her own age, maybe mid-twenties, maybe exhausted. "I'm sorry. I still have to read this notice. If no payment posts by Friday, collection authority shifts to legal."

"How much for Friday?"

"Two million."

Ina laughed once, not because anything was funny. She leaned her forehead against the cool vending-machine glass and watched packets of dried squid shimmer behind it. "I don't have two million by Friday."

"Then tell me what you do have." The woman clicked her pen three times, a nervous rhythm she probably hated in herself. "Even one hundred thousand buys time in the system."

Ina closed her eyes. Her thumb found the burn scar on her left wrist, rubbed it like a coin. "Call me Monday."

"Monday is too late for legal handoff."

"Then Monday is what I've got."

She ended the call before the woman could apologize again.

When she came back to the table, Dohyun was arguing with Hyejin about ash placement. He wanted the urn in the family plot south of the city, near his parents. Hyejin wanted to split the ashes and leave a portion at Imjingak where separated families tied ribbons and cried into cameras every year. Neither of them asked what Eun-sook had wanted. She had left no instructions, which felt exactly like her, one final refusal to make any single loyalty easy.

"We're not dividing her like evidence," Dohyun said.

"You divided her every time you told her to stop talking about the North at dinner," Hyejin snapped.

"I told her to stop because there were neighbors at dinner."

"You told her to stop because you were scared."

He stood too fast; his knee failed and caught. He grabbed the chair back before he fell. The unlit cigarette dropped and rolled under the table.

Ina moved between them, one hand on each shoulder, feeling bone under fabric. "Not here. Please."

Hyejin was still breathing hard through her nose, but she bent and retrieved the cigarette. Instead of handing it back, she snapped it in half and dropped it in the paper cup. "Sit down, Dohyun. Your leg's not making speeches today."

He sat. He looked at Ina, not at Hyejin. "Your mother kept files. Boxes. You know that. If you're clearing the apartment this afternoon, don't throw papers without checking dates."

It sounded practical and almost kind, but Ina heard the seam underneath. Files meant secrets. Secrets meant work he had tolerated in marriage and feared in widowhood.

"I'm not a child," she said.

"I didn't say you were."

"No. You just said it in your own language."

The clerk returned with the completed forms and a plastic tray for signatures. Ina signed her name seven times in three variations because each form demanded a slightly different legal relationship to the same dead woman. Daughter. Next of kin. Estate representative. She had never felt less representative of anything.

At noon they carried Eun-sook's portrait into the furnace hall. A monk in white robes chanted with the patience of someone paid by the hour. The chant vibrated through Ina's ribs like bus engine idle. When the steel door closed, she did not cry. She watched the temperature gauge climb and counted in Korean to one hundred, then to one hundred again. At two hundred twelve her phone buzzed with a new voicemail.

The screen read DOHYUN LANDLORD.

She let it ring out.

After the service, mourners peeled away in twos and threes, each one pressing condolences into Ina's hand: a bow, an envelope, a sentence about weather. Rain threatened all afternoon but never committed, the sky sitting low and white over the river like an unfinished thought.

In the parking lot, Hyejin opened her tote bag and brought out plastic containers of seaweed soup she had made at dawn. "Eat before we go up there," she said. "Grief makes people stupid with paperwork."

Dohyun refused at first. Then he took a spoon and held it too long before the first bite.

While they ate standing up beside the hearse, Ina played the landlord voicemail on speaker because secrets in this family always fermented. A man with the thick consonants of South Jeolla and the patience of somebody done pretending politeness said, "Mr. Han owes eleven million two hundred thousand won. End of month, I change the lock. I don't care if his wife died. Dead people don't pay rent."

Dohyun stared at the asphalt. "He has called three times this week."

"Why didn't you tell me?" Ina asked.

"Because your mother was still breathing this week."

There it was, the only confession he could afford in public.

Hyejin set down her soup cup. "We'll cover this month's interest. Between the condolence money and what I can move from church reserves."

"Church reserves are for the food pantry," Ina said.

"Then we call it advance reimbursement for widower meals and pray louder." Hyejin's mouth tightened in a shape that could have been a smile from far away. "Don't look at me like that. Jesus has seen worse bookkeeping."

Ina wanted to laugh and couldn't. Her shoulders ached from holding the portrait frame. The gold-painted corners had left grit on her palms.

They drove to the apartment in Mapo in two cars because nobody wanted the same silence. Eun-sook's building sat above a row of wholesale kitchen-supply shops. On clear days you could see a strip of river between two office towers, gray water cut by barges and commuter ferries. Today the glass was filmed with rain dust and the view was a rumor.

The hallway smelled of garlic and bleach. Someone on the fourth floor was frying mackerel with the window closed. Inside apartment 502, the air had already started to change from lived-in to emptied: less body heat, more paper and detergent and the metallic chill from the old refrigerator seal.

Hyejin tied on an apron she found hanging by the stove, Eun-sook's faded yellow one with a cartoon pepper stitched at the hem. She did not take it off all afternoon.

"Dishes first," she announced. "Then medicine disposal. Then closet inventory. No one opens old letters until we've eaten again."

She gave orders like prayer beads, one after another, because if she paused she might have to mention her sister in past tense.

Dohyun went to the balcony and sat on the low stool by the ashtray, though he still did not light anything. He kept folding and unfolding the death-notice pamphlet into smaller and smaller squares. Ina watched him turn an official document into a paper stone.

She started in the kitchen. Eun-sook had labeled every shelf with masking tape in tight handwriting: SOUP STOCK, NUTS, RICE, SPRING KIMCHI, BILLS. The bills shelf held two utility invoices and a packet of instant coffee. No hidden accounts, no farewell letters.

On top of the fridge sat a metal cookie tin full of batteries sorted by size. In the freezer she found anchovies, a bag of peeled chestnuts, and two film canisters of dried mugwort from some recipe no one had made in years. Everything looked normal and ordinary. Nothing explained the knot under her sternum since morning, the sense that her mother had not finished speaking.

By four, the rain finally started, hard enough to drum on the awning outside the kitchen window. Hyejin called from the living room for tape and scissors. Ina opened the lower pantry to look for packing supplies and found a twenty-kilo rice bag set inside a plastic storage tub to keep out bugs. She heaved the bag out by the handles. It was half full and heavier than it should have been.

Something inside shifted with a stiff rectangular sound.

She slit the top seam and plunged her hand down through cool grains until her knuckles hit cardboard. Her fingers closed around a hard spine wrapped in wax paper.

She pulled up a notebook the color of old sea glass. Damp had curled the cover corners but the elastic band was intact. A strip of masking tape on the front read in Eun-sook's hand: REHEARSAL LYRICS - 2003 REV.

"Ina?" Hyejin called. "Where's that tape?"

"One second."

Ina brushed rice from the edges and opened the notebook to the middle. The pages were dense with lyrics written in blue-black ink, some recognizable as patriotic anthems from school assemblies, others as folk songs from broadcasts her mother used to hum while scrubbing pans. But words had been crossed out and replaced. In one song, mountain peaks became transmission towers. In another, "our fatherland" had become "our signal." Dates crowded the margins in tiny ledger script: 4/19, 8/12, 10/3, each followed by times and three-digit strings.

These pages were transmission instructions disguised as song revisions.
Every crossed-out lyric carried operational weight.
It was code.

A memory arrived before she could block it: being twelve, wide awake at midnight, hearing Eun-sook in the kitchen singing one of those songs too softly, stopping before the chorus whenever the apartment phone rang.

"Ina! Tape, please!" Hyejin again, sharper this time.

Ina slid the notebook under her shirt against her stomach, cold paper against warm skin, and grabbed a roll of tape from the drawer. She walked into the living room with her face arranged for logistics.

Hyejin was wrapping framed photos in newspaper. She paused and peered at Ina. "You're pale. Did you eat anything besides sugar coffee?"

"I'm fine. Rice dust in my eyes." Ina handed over the tape.

Dohyun spoke from the balcony without turning. "Found anything in her files?"

The question landed with more weight than his tone.

Ina could feel the notebook edge pressing into her ribs. "Mostly utility bills. Church donation receipts."

"No notebooks?"

There it was. He was asking one thing: notebooks.

"If there are, I haven't seen them." She hated how easily the sentence came out.

Dohyun nodded once; the motion could have sealed a private fear or eased one, and she still couldn't tell which.

The rain thickened. Traffic noise from the river road softened to a steady wash. Ina carried two boxes to Eun-sook's bedroom and shut the door behind her. The room still smelled faintly of hair dye and camphor ointment.

She sat on the floor, pulled the notebook back out, and went page by page. Most entries paired altered lyrics with dates. Some had circles around certain words: BRIDGE, STATIC, LANTERN, RAINFALL. On the back cover, written vertically in pencil almost erased by friction, she found a number with hyphens in an old pager format and two characters beside it: RHEE.

Her first thought was fraud. Her second thought was that nobody ran pager networks now unless they wanted deniability. Her third thought, uninvited, was that deniability cost less than lawyers.

She copied the number onto her forearm with a ballpoint, then tucked the notebook into an empty duvet case and zipped it shut. Outside the bedroom door, Hyejin and Dohyun had moved from arguing to coordinated silence, which was somehow worse.

At six, Hyejin left to bring food from the church freezer and promised to return at dawn for the legal paperwork. Before she stepped out she adjusted Ina's collar, an old aunt reflex that survived any mood.

"Try not to read yourself into a fever tonight," she said. "Tomorrow we'll sort bank books and insurance and all the ugly stuff."

"You've already done plenty," Ina said.

"I've done dishes. That's not plenty." Hyejin patted her cheek once, rough thumb against damp skin. "Plenty starts when the creditors smell grief and line up." She glanced toward the balcony where Dohyun sat in shadow. "Keep him from signing anything stupid."

After she left, the apartment got larger and meaner in the way empty spaces do after sundown. The rain slowed to a hiss. The refrigerator motor clicked on and off like an impatient tongue.

Ina made instant ramyeon and did not eat it. She placed the notebook on the kitchen table beside her mother's old pager, a beige brick she had kept as a joke artifact. The battery was dead. The notebook number was not in its memory.

She opened her laptop, routed through a public VPN she used for subtitle leak forums, and searched the number. Nothing indexed under open records. She checked old telecom archive boards and found one retired technician thread mentioning a boutique paging relay still active for hospitals, funeral services, and "legacy clients requiring one-way alerts." The phrase \"legacy clients\" was a nice euphemism.

Fear arrived as administrative hesitation. She stared at the number for ten minutes and reorganized the desktop icons twice.

Then she took her umbrella, went downstairs to the convenience store kiosk at the corner, and paid 1,000 won cash to send a numeric page through the old relay terminal hidden under lottery forms.

The message read 41912?

She chose numbers from one of the margin dates and pretended that choice meant control.

The clerk at the kiosk, a college kid with a silver nose ring and a philosophy textbook propped under the cash drawer, watched her key in the message and said, "Haven't seen anyone use that in years. My uncle keeps one for mountain hikes. Says phones make him anxious."

"Good for your uncle," Ina said.

The kid shrugged. "He's still anxious."

Back upstairs, she locked the door, then unlocked it and checked the deadbolt again because anxiety liked rituals. Dohyun had fallen asleep in front of the television with the sound off, the election crawl painting blue and red bands across his face.

Ina sat on the bathroom floor with her back against the tub and waited for nothing to happen.

At 9:14 p.m., her phone buzzed from an unknown number.

She answered before the second ring.

A woman's voice, older, careful, with the flattening diction of broadcast training. "Who gave you that relay code?"

Ina kept her own voice neutral. "Depends who's asking."

A pause. Rainwater dripped somewhere inside the wall. Then the woman said, "When the river freezes, which edge breaks first?"

The phrase hit like a hand to the sternum. Eun-sook had used that exact line when Ina was little and the apartment phone rang after midnight. If Ina answered by accident, her mother would take the receiver gently, smile too bright, and ask that question into the line before saying anything else.

"The shadowed edge," Ina said before she could decide not to. "Because sunlight lies."

Silence again, shorter this time.

"Your mother taught you the old challenge set," the woman said. "This line is no longer secure."

"Then why did you answer it?"

"Because your page included April nineteenth, twelve past midnight. That date was not public."

Ina looked at the notebook open on her lap, at the date circled three times in blue ink. "Who are you?"

"Rhee Sun-ok. Former Office of Cultural Continuity, relocation desk." The title sounded like it had survived three coups and a filing cabinet fire. "I carried state credentials before your government and mine decided we'd call each other temporary."

"That's not proof."

"Correct. You'll get proof at a meeting."

"No. You can send proof now."

"Not on a compromised mobile line while your building's election CCTV watches every stairwell." Rhee's tone did not change, but something in it hardened. "If you're not ready, burn the book and sleep. If you are, come alone at ten-thirty. Seongdong, Geumhwa Printing, back entrance by the loading ramp. Bring only the notebook."

Ina pictured every true-crime cautionary segment she had ever subtitled. A woman in debt followed a stranger at night. A woman went missing by dawn.

"How do I know you won't hand me to intelligence?"

"You don't know." A small exhale, almost a laugh. "That's adulthood, Ms. Seo."

The call ended.

Ina sat still until the screen went black. In the living room Dohyun coughed in his sleep and groped for a cigarette that wasn't there.

She should have woken him, shown him the notebook, let family panic become collective panic. Instead she stood, went to her mother's closet, and chose dark clothes that didn't rustle. She tied her hair back and tucked the notebook inside a canvas document sleeve. From the kitchen drawer she took a cheap utility cutter and put it in her coat pocket, a ridiculous weapon against professional danger but better than prayer.

At the doorway she hesitated. Dohyun opened one eye.

"Where are you going?" he asked, voice rough.

"Convenience store. We need trash bags."

He nodded, already drifting. "Get the thick kind."

The elevator was slow and smelled like damp cardboard. On the street the rain had stopped, leaving the asphalt slick as lacquer. Motorbikes hissed past. Ina skipped the main avenue and took side roads, cutting through alleys behind welding shops where the air tasted of iron filings and old frying oil.

Mapo to Seongdong by taxi at night was thirty-five minutes if traffic behaved. She paid cash and asked to be dropped two blocks early.

Geumhwa Printing occupied the first floor of a brick warehouse with frosted windows and a sign whose bulbs had died one by one. The loading ramp sat in shadow. A single fluorescent tube buzzed above the back door.

Rhee was already there, holding an umbrella closed though the sky was clear. She wore men's loafers polished to a military shine and a camel coat mended at the cuff with thread two shades too dark. Her hair was cut blunt at the jaw, silver at the roots and dyed black at the ends, vanity and discipline still negotiating terms.

When she saw Ina, she did not wave. She checked her wristwatch, then said, "Two minutes late. City excuses are not useful in this work."

"If this is work, you haven't told me the job."

Rhee's mouth twitched. "Good. Keep that skepticism. It may keep you alive for a month."

She opened the back door with an old brass key and led Ina into a narrow corridor lined with stacks of poster tubes. The building smelled of ink and hot machine oil. Somewhere deeper in the warehouse, a printer head clicked in a steady mechanical prayer.

Rhee held out her hand. "Notebook."

Ina did not pass it over. "You first. Credentials."

Rhee reached inside her coat and produced a laminated card in a cracked leather sleeve. The photo showed her maybe thirty years younger, hair permed, expression severe. The emblem at the top was unfamiliar: a stylized peninsula split by a starburst line. Below, in faded print: PROVISIONAL CULTURAL SECRETARIAT. FIELD LIAISON. Her name in Hangul and older Romanization.

Forgery was possible. Good forgery was expensive.

"Anyone with a laminator can fake that," Ina said.

"Agreed." Rhee replaced the card and opened a metal locker set into the corridor wall. From inside she removed a flat envelope wrapped in butcher paper and tied with twine.

She offered it without letting go. "If you touch this, you accept confidentiality."

"You can't enforce that."

"Not tonight."

Ina took the envelope. Inside was a black-and-white photograph, edges scalloped, probably from the early nineties. Seven people stood in front of a chalkboard map of the peninsula. One of them was unmistakably Eun-sook, younger but still with that exact set of shoulders, half defensive, half amused. She wore a headset around her neck and held a folder labeled in thick marker: SONG REVISION UNIT C.

Beside her stood a man Ina recognized from history documentaries as a former vice minister who had publicly disappeared after democratization. On the back of the photo, in Eun-sook's handwriting, was a line Ina had seen on grocery lists and birthday cards all her life: Buy radish. Do not trust easy weather.

Her knees locked. The humiliation was smaller and meaner: she had to read the line three times because the letters kept blurring and snapping back into focus.

"Where did you get this?" she asked.

"From a cabinet your mother helped build," Rhee said. "From a history that has had excellent lawyers."

"Why contact me now? She's dead. Whatever game this is, you're late."

Rhee looked past Ina toward the warehouse floor, taking tempo from the printer rhythm. "Your mother's death did not trigger a slogan packet. It triggered a transfer file with three items: a cassette debrief on the April nineteenth operation, a ledger of payoffs tied to that operation, and two locker keys. If no successor clears screening this week, the file is pulped and an exposure memo goes upstairs."

The words were concrete enough to scrape.

Ina folded the photograph back into the envelope. "Say what you want from me in plain Korean."

"I need to know whether you're your mother's daughter in skill, not blood, and whether you can run narrative operations without panicking and confessing to the wrong people. Then I want one week of probationary labor while we assess risk."

Ina barked a laugh. "You called me to a warehouse to offer an internship in treason?"

"Language matters." Rhee adjusted the umbrella handle in her palm, a repetitive movement that suggested old tendon pain. "We don't ask for oaths. We ask for competence and discretion."

"And if I say no?"

"At zero-eight-hundred I'll log this meeting as civilian exposure. That log includes your pager query, street-camera captures from your taxi route, and this hallway's CCTV. Security Bureau can seize your laptop and freeze your accounts before lunch. Under security law, they question the nearest family registry entry first."

"Meaning my father."

"Meaning your father in an interview room while his landlord files a lock change."

The fluorescent tube buzzed louder, or her pulse did. Ina thought of the billing-office woman asking what she could pay by Friday. She thought of Dohyun's sock toe poking through cotton and his hand shaking over an unlit cigarette.

"You're threatening my family," she said.

"I'm describing the machinery already pointed at your family." Rhee pulled a folded sheet from her coat and opened it between them. "Hospital account 7714 moves to legal collection Friday. Han Dohyun's arrears filing is stamped for Monday. During your assessment week I can hold both files in place. If you refuse, I let the queue run."

Ina hated her instantly, which was probably useful.

"Terms," Ina said.

Rhee nodded once; they had finally reached the first competent sentence of the evening. "One week. You get access only to task materials. No pay during probation. You don't disclose this contact to anyone, including family. No personal devices in secure rooms. Follow route instructions exactly. First route breach ends access. Second breach escalates your file to security review."

"No payment?" Ina laughed again, sharper. "You heard the part where I've got debt, right?"

"I did." Rhee slid a second sheet from the same fold and tapped one line with her nail. Typed header, dated 2003, carrying Eun-sook's name. The line read: APRIL NINETEENTH WAS A SCRIPT, NOT WEATHER. "If money is your reason, walk out. If truth is your reason, probation buys you one supervised hour with your mother's full debrief tomorrow night."

The corridor seemed to narrow around Ina's lungs. Debt was a deadline. This was a blade. If she walked, the only voice left of her mother would be this photo and a joke about radish.

She kept the transcript sheet in her hand. "When do I start?"

Rhee let her shoulders drop half an inch. For the first time, something almost human crossed her face, not warmth, more like fatigue granting respect. "Tomorrow, twenty-three-thirty. Same district. You'll receive a one-line go or no-go by pager relay at twenty-two-hundred. Come alone to the alley by Geumhwa's service door. Bring the notebook and one pen."

"What if someone follows me?"

"Then you keep walking and we cancel."

"What if this gets my father hurt?"

"If you tell him tonight, he gets a door knock before breakfast. If you keep protocol, he sleeps."

They stood in the corridor with ink and oil in the air and no room left for cleaner illusions.

Ina slid the notebook from her sleeve and handed it over. Rhee checked the cover label, flipped to two random pages, then returned it immediately.

"Keep it," she said. "It is your key, not ours. Hide it better than a rice bin."

"You knew where it was?"

"Your mother was consistent."

Rhee stepped back and opened the door to the loading ramp. The meeting was over, and the dismissal felt like protocol.

Outside, the night had gone clear. Reflected neon shook in puddles. Ina walked three blocks before realizing she had been holding her breath through whole intersections and letting it out only when taxis passed.

On the ride home she kept the photo envelope under her thigh and watched election posters blur along retaining walls. Every smiling candidate promised stability in identical fonts.

It was past midnight when she let herself into apartment 502. The television was still on mute. Dohyun slept sideways on the sofa with one hand hanging over the edge, fingers opening and closing around empty air, still gripping an invisible rail in his sleep.

Ina stood in the kitchen under the humming fluorescent tube and considered the obvious hiding places: desk drawer, freezer, behind books. Every option felt wrong. She pulled a chair to the sink, climbed up, and removed the vent grate above the cabinet where Eun-sook had once hidden birthday cash. She wrapped the notebook and photo envelope in a plastic produce bag, slid the bundle into the duct void, and replaced the grate.

Metal scraped metal. The sound seemed loud enough to wake the dead.

Her phone buzzed with a new message from Hyejin.

`Did he take his blood pressure pill?`

Ina typed back: `Yes. We're fine. Just sorting bills.`

Another lie by omission, quick and clean.

Another message stacked under Hyejin's text, automated and polite in the way threats liked to be.

`Minimum payment overdue. Please contact Seoul St. Mary's Financial Services.`

She washed her hands even though they were not dirty, scrubbing rice starch and printer ink and warehouse dust from the lines of her fingers. At the sink window, the river lights blinked through mist. A ferry horn carried up between buildings, low and patient.

From the living room Dohyun spoke without opening his eyes. "You find anything worth keeping?"

Ina dried her hands on the towel with the cartoon pepper and looked at her father, at his knee brace unstrapped, at his sock with a hole at the toe, at a man who had spent thirty years choosing which truths a family could afford.

"Only recipes," she said.

He nodded and fell back into sleep.

Ina turned off the kitchen light and stood in the dark until her eyes adjusted to appliance LEDs and city spill. Tomorrow night at ten, the pager would buzz or it would not. Either way she had crossed something tonight, a line made of secrecy and debt and her mother's handwriting.

She went to bed in her clothes and set an alarm for 9:57 p.m., trying to pass precision off as control.


# Chapter 2

The alley behind the Seongdong print shop smelled like wet cardboard and frying oil. Rain had stopped twenty minutes earlier, but water still leaked from a cracked gutter and ticked against an empty paint can by the service door. Ina stood under the buzzing security light and read the same text from the hospital billing office for the third time.
At 10:00 p.m., the pager had buzzed once with four words and this address.

`Minimum payment overdue. Please contact Seoul St. Mary's Financial Services.`

She had not deleted the message because deleting it felt like pretending she had options.

Rhee opened the metal door before Ina could knock. In daylight the woman could have passed for any office administrator in Seoul, but at midnight she read like she belonged to a different utility service entirely: dark windbreaker zipped to the throat, old running shoes, hair pinned with two mismatched clips. She held a paper cup of vending-machine corn tea and a ring of keys heavy enough to bruise.

"You came alone," Rhee said.

"You asked me to." Ina tucked her phone into her pocket. "You keep checking like I'm a teenager sneaking out."

Rhee took a swallow of tea, grimaced, and shook sweetener from a packet into the cup. "I check because half our work is ruined by people who assume they're not being followed." Her gaze swept over Ina's shoulder into the alley, then she stepped aside. "Come in. The presses are off. You can hear yourself think down there."

Inside, the print floor was dark except for one strip light over stacked pallets of pamphlet paper. The air held that dry-metal smell that old copiers gave off when they overheated, plus stale coffee and toner dust that tasted faintly bitter at the back of Ina's tongue. Rhee moved between dormant machines without turning on more lights. Her keys tapped against her thigh in a steady rhythm that made Ina think of a metronome.

At the freight elevator, Rhee dug in her windbreaker pocket and brought out a tiny blue inhaler. She used it quickly, her face turned away, then slipped it back before Ina could pretend not to notice.

"Basement sticks in my chest this time of year," she said, pressing the elevator call button. "Mold and paper. If you're lucky, your body never gets a vote in your politics."

The elevator opened with a groan. They descended one level.

The safehouse was not a bunker. It was a cramped office carved out of storage space and lit too brightly, with blocked windows, humming servers in a steel rack, a long map of the peninsula pinned across one wall, and a conference table scarred by decades of coffee rings. Someone had tried to civilize the room with a potted fern. The fern had lost.

Director Kwon stood when Ina entered.

He was in a charcoal suit that fit like armor, silver hair combed straight back, no tie pin, no visible insignia. If Ina had passed him in a hotel corridor, she would have guessed retired judge, maybe a corporate chair who still terrified vice presidents. He gave a small nod, then sat only after she sat. His posture did not move even when he reached for the ceramic cup at his elbow.

He had steeped the same tea bag twice. Ina saw him squeeze it against the spoon and fold it carefully on the saucer as if he had been trained during rationing years to waste nothing that could be used again.

"Ms. Seo." His voice was low, formal, and annoyingly calm. "Thank you for honoring the meeting."

Ina kept her bag on her lap. The green lyric notebook pressed against her knees through the canvas. "I didn't come to honor anything. I came to find out if this is a scam with patriotic branding."

Rhee shut the door and stayed by it, arms crossed, eyes on the hallway camera feed mounted above the filing cabinet.

Kwon gave the smallest hint of a smile. "Skepticism is a useful organ. Keep it."

"Then start with names." Ina tracked from Kwon to the map and back. "Who are you, exactly? What is this group? How is any of this real?"

Kwon slid a cream-colored folder across the table. Her name was typed on the front in black serif font: `SEO INA - PROBATION REVIEW`.

Inside were copies of old letters, blurred photographs, and three pages of typed organizational history with footnotes and archive citations. On top sat one photo she had not seen in her mother's apartment: Eun-sook in a field jacket, cigarette between two fingers, standing beside Kwon thirty years younger in front of a broadcast van with patched antenna wiring. Her mother's handwriting ran along the white border.

`Signal held for 11m after sweep. K said hold anyway.`

Ina touched the corner of the print, then pulled her hand back.

"A photo can be faked," she said.

"Yes." Kwon nodded. "So can a constitution. So can election returns. Forgery is a universal language."

He opened a second folder, this one thinner. Hospital billing statements. Her father's lease arrears notice. A screenshot of her subtitle payroll account with the last three deposit dates. Every column was accurate down to the won.

Ina stared at him. "You went through my finances."

"We performed due diligence on a prospective contractor." Kwon folded his hands. "You may call it intrusion if that makes the ethics cleaner in your own mind."

"Do not tell me what makes my ethics cleaner."

Kwon did not flinch. "I do not. I observe your constraints so we can design around them."

She wanted to slam the folder shut and walk out. She also wanted to keep breathing in an apartment that still carried her mother's clothes in the closet and unpaid notices in the kitchen drawer.

"Say it plainly," Ina said. "No slogans. What do you do here?"

Kwon leaned back by exactly one inch.

"We preserve policy continuity for a state project that could not survive formal institutions," he said. "Military confrontation is catastrophic. Electoral rhetoric resets every cycle. Diplomatic channels narrow and widen according to the weather in Washington, Beijing, and domestic polling. Culture is the only instrument that crosses all those borders daily. Drama scripts cross by USB. Lyrics cross by memory. Fan edits cross by bluetooth in markets with no legal internet. You know this. Your mother knew this."

"Culture also gets people watched, arrested, disappeared." Ina heard her own voice sharpen. "You don't get to call that weather."

"I call it cost." He spoke the word as if he were reading a line item on a ledger. "The question is whether the cost purchases movement or only suffering."

"And who decides that in your office?"

"In this room, ultimately, I do."

Silence held for three breaths. Server fans whined. A train passed somewhere aboveground, the vibration traveling through concrete and chair legs.

Rhee pushed off the wall, crossed to the table, and placed a printed contract in front of Ina with a capped pen. Her nails were cut blunt and clean except for one thumb stained with blue copy ink. "Read all of it," she said. "Do not sign blind."

Ina let out one short laugh. "At least one decent person in the room."

Rhee gave her a hard look. "I'm not decent. I'm old enough to be accurate."

The contract was eleven pages, dense and hostile.

`Operational Secrecy and Narrative Services Agreement`.

Term: probationary appointment.

Compensation: KRW 7,000,000 monthly stipend paid via designated production affiliate.

Emergency support: authorized direct settlement support for immediate family medical arrears, contingent on compliance.

Key obligations:

- Permanent non-disclosure regarding council structure, personnel, assets, channels.
- Obedience to assigned narrative directives and revision deadlines.
- Participation in strategic message development under supervisor instruction.
- No unsanctioned contact with domestic or foreign security entities.

Breach clause:

- Referral under armistice-era security statutes.
- Immediate suspension of compensation and account review.

It was not employment. It was a leash with a salary attached.

Ina read each page slowly, her finger tracing paragraphs while Kwon waited without interruption. At the legal disclaimer on page eight she felt the burn scar on her left wrist catch against the paper edge. She lifted her hand, studied the shallow white line, and remembered her mother telling her, when she was nine, that kitchen burns taught timing better than lectures.

"This says if I disclose anything, I can be prosecuted." Ina tapped page nine. "This says you can direct my output and revoke support if I don't comply."

"Correct," Kwon said.

"And this language about 'strategic message development' can mean anything from rewrites to psychological operations."

"It can." He held her gaze. "We are not a church choir, Ms. Seo."

She dropped her gaze back to the contract. "You invited the wrong person if you want blind discipline."

"I invited the precise person," he said. "You can write for mass feeling without sounding like a pamphlet. You can hide intent in ordinary scenes. And you currently cannot afford moral vanity."

The words struck the exact place where she had been pretending the numbers could not reach.

Ina set the contract down. "You don't know me."

Kwon turned the hospital statement toward her and pointed to the outstanding number without touching the paper.

`KRW 58,000,000`.

Then he turned the lease arrears notice.

`KRW 11,200,000`.

"I know the arithmetic currently governing your decisions," he said. "That is enough."

Rhee turned toward the camera feed, jaw tight.

Ina's chest felt packed with dry paper. She heard herself over-explain, as if careful clauses might buy room to breathe.

"If I even consider this, and I'm not saying I do, then I need boundaries in writing. I do scripts and narrative architecture. I don't identify individuals. I don't draft content that calls for violence. I don't build fear campaigns."

Kwon listened without taking notes.

When she finished, he said, "Add an ethics addendum if you wish. It will guide your work. It will not govern events you do not control."

"Then what is the point of adding it?"

"To record that you were not asleep when you entered."

That answer pissed her off more than refusal would have.

"You make this sound noble," Ina said. "It sounds like blackmail with archival stationery."

Kwon's face did not harden. If anything, it softened by half a degree.

"Blackmail requires private shame," he said. "I am offering public purpose through private compromise. It is uglier than blackmail and often more durable."

Rhee snorted, almost a laugh, then covered it with a cough.

Ina read the contract again, this time as a map of punishments. Every page had one promise and two traps. Salary relief if obedient. Debt support if silent. Institutional protection implied but never guaranteed. She felt the familiar editor's impulse to mark up weak phrasing, tighten loopholes, insert precise verbs. It would have been easier if the text were just bad. It was competent, which made it dangerous.

"What is probation here?" she asked.

"Mutual testing period," Kwon said. "You assess our seriousness. We assess your discipline."

"Can I walk away?"

"You may decline further assignments after probation. Secrecy remains perpetual."

"So no." Ina lifted her chin. "Not really."

"Not fully," Kwon said.

He did not rush her. That was another tactic: let the room and the numbers do the pressure work.

Ina took the pen Rhee had set down. It was a cheap office ballpoint with a cracked clip. She signed her full name on page eleven, then initialed every margin where flagged tabs stuck out.

Her handwriting resembled her mother's when she was tired.

Rhee collected the copies, stamped each one with a small red seal, and handed Ina her contractor packet in a manila envelope.

Kwon folded his reused tea bag again, precise as surgery.

"Welcome," he said. "Your first assignment starts tonight."

Ina barked a short, ugly laugh. "Of course it does."

Kwon stood, smoothed his suit cuffs, and moved to the map wall. "MiraeWave Studio B has a pilot table read at two-thirty. A romance project with high projected share among women forty and older. Our target cohort. You will adjust one scene so it carries reunification grief without triggering broadcaster censors."

"You want propaganda inside a melodrama."

"I want memory inside entertainment."

"Same difference if people get hurt."

"No." Kwon turned back to her. "Difference is everything. Sequence matters. Form matters. Tone matters. You know this better than anyone in this room."

Ina hated that he was right about her skills.

Rhee grabbed her keys. "Car is out front. We should move before traffic dies and police decide to be curious." She paused by Ina's chair. "Eat something in the car. You look like static."

"I'm static," Ina muttered.

Rhee reached into her bag and dropped a convenience-store kimbap triangle into Ina's hand. "Then conduct it in the useful direction."


Studio B sat on the third floor of the MiraeWave complex, behind a lobby wall plastered with glossy posters where everyone seemed less tired than real people had any right to be. The security gate accepted Rhee's temporary badge without a beep. No one at reception asked Ina for identification. That scared her more than if they had.

The writer room smelled of stale marker ink and instant ramen broth. Script pages were spread across two pushed-together tables. Three assistants typed on laptops with the speed and panic of people paid to vanish errors before sunrise.

Head writer Cho Seungho did not stand when Ina entered. He sat sideways in his chair, ankle over knee, turning an unlit cigarette between his fingers like a coin trick. A nicotine patch showed above his collarbone where his T-shirt had stretched out. His gaze flicked to her, then to Rhee.

"You brought me another consultant," he said. "I already have two consultants and one censor pretending not to be a censor."

"She's not a consultant," Rhee said. "She's script support under Director Kwon."

Cho's mouth twitched. "That title means she's a consultant with handcuffs."

"Call it whatever keeps you working," Rhee replied.

Ina set her envelope on an empty chair and opened the pilot script. The cover page read `RIVER LIGHT, EPISODE 1`.

A young actress sat at the far end of the table, posture perfect even in exhaustion, dyed dark hair tied low at the nape. Park Ji-eun. Ina recognized her from bus-stop ads and subway screens, but in person Ji-eun seemed smaller, with a faint tremor in her left hand every time she reached for her water bottle. She wore a black hoodie over rehearsal clothes and had a strip of neon tape wrapped around her index finger where the pen rubbed.

Next to her sat an agency manager in a cream blazer, forty maybe, phone on speaker beside her notebook. The manager smiled like a tax auditor.

"We're only here for line rhythm, that's it," the manager said. "No political references, no historical slogans, no region-sensitive terms, no implied cross-border messaging. Legal's already cleared version six."

Cho waved the unlit cigarette at the ceiling. "And here comes version seven anyway."

Ina took the only open seat. She could feel Kwon's contract packet pressing against her leg through the chair mesh like a second spine.

"I'm not here to blow up your script," she said. "I'm here for one scene pass."

Cho gave a dry laugh. "That is what everyone says before they blow up my script."

They read from page forty-two, a kitchen argument between Ji-eun's character and her estranged older brother. In Cho's draft, the scene was competent and dead. He had them fight over a restaurant lease and then reconcile with an expensive bottle of soju placed for obvious sponsor visibility.

Ina let the full read run without interrupting. She marked three lines, then tapped the margin with her pen and met Ji-eun's eyes.

"Can we try a version where the argument is not about lease money," Ina said, "but about who kept setting an extra bowl at dinner after their youngest sibling went missing? No names, no geography, no institutions. Just family habit turning into grief."

The agency manager leaned forward. "The missing-sibling angle is trauma framing. That reads as advocacy in every watchdog memo, and we don't authorize it."

The contract packet dug into Ina's thigh through the chair mesh, a hard reminder that she had signed less than an hour ago and already spent her first favor. If this note got tagged as subversion, the blowback would not touch Kwon's office. It would land on the youngest body at the table.

"People have siblings," Ina said. "People lose them. That's not advocacy. That's Wednesday."

Cho scratched his patch absently through his shirt. "Network will ask what happened to the sibling."

Ina heard the safer answer assembling itself: debt, migration, any loss with paperwork and no politics. She killed that sentence before it reached her mouth.

"Let them ask," Ina said. "The scene doesn't answer for them. It lets the audience answer from their own history."

Rhee, standing by the whiteboard, said nothing. She watched Ji-eun.

Ji-eun's taped finger kept tapping at the edge of her script, tremor disguised as metronome. Ina felt her old subtitle instinct surge, the one that sanded dangerous edges before anyone powerful got nervous. She shut that instinct down and left the silence open.

Ji-eun cleared her throat and tapped the pen twice against her taped finger.

"Can I try one read?" she asked.

The manager started, "Ji-eun-ssi, we've already set improvisation parameters-"

"I heard you." Ji-eun did not raise her voice. "I'm asking for one read."

Cho shrugged. "One read won't kill us."

"You keep saying that like risk is free," the manager muttered.

They reset. Cho read the brother lines with more bite this time. Ji-eun listened, eyes on the page, then raised them when her cue came.

"You keep asking why I set three bowls," she said in character. Her voice stayed soft but landed clean at the back of the room. "I set three because my hand still remembers him. Your hand forgot faster."

Nobody moved for a second.

Ina felt the room recalibrate. The line was better than what she had suggested, less explanatory, sharper at the cut.

Cho lowered his script. "That passes censors if we don't add context," he said. "It reads domestic."

The manager was already typing into her phone. "I need legal to clear this before I'll confirm anything."

Ji-eun held Ina's gaze, not smiling.

"Is this the part where you tell me it's art, not politics?" she asked.

Ina could have lied smoothly. She had built a career on making hard material sound harmless enough for subtitles and broadcast notes. Tonight she did not have that muscle left.

"It is both," she said.

Ji-eun gave a small nod, as if she had expected exactly that answer.

"Then don't feed me lines you can't defend later," she said. "My contract debt doesn't disappear because a scene feels honest."

That was the first truly personal sentence anyone had spoken in the room.

The manager snapped, "We're not discussing contract debt in a script session."

Ji-eun kept her eyes on Ina. "I am."

Ina heard herself answer before caution could revise the sentence.

"Then put my name on the revision memo," she said to the manager. "If Standards flags this scene, charge the variance to me, not to her trainee account."

The manager stared at her, then gave one incredulous laugh. "You don't have authority to rewrite debt allocation."

"I just signed with Director Kwon," Ina said. "Use his authority, then. Put me in the chain."

Rhee's jaw tightened. She stepped closer to the table but did not interrupt.

"Do you understand what you're volunteering for?" the manager asked. "Her callable exposure is three hundred twenty million won before penalties."

"I know the number," Ina said. "Write the memo."

Cho broke the tension by slapping his script closed.

"Fine," he said. "We lock this version for table read and see if Standards screams tomorrow. If they scream, we trim the edge and keep the bone."

The manager lifted her phone and started dictating, words clipping harder as she went. "Version seven revision note: line variance requested by council script contractor Seo Ina. Liability transfer request pending legal review."

Rhee checked her watch and moved to the door. "No ten minutes. We leave now."

As assistants redistributed pages, Ina sat very still and listened to the room noise: laptop keys, plastic chairs dragging, someone tearing open another ramen lid. Normal production sounds. Ordinary labor. She had spent years in rooms like this, where meaning got tuned by fatigue and deadlines and whoever still had enough power at 3 a.m. to say no.

Now she knew another layer had been soldered under the table.

Ji-eun stood and stretched her shoulders like a dancer between takes. Up close, Ina saw two small crescent scars near Ji-eun's left thumb, old cuts from opening package tape with bare hands. Ji-eun followed Ina's gaze and flexed her fingers.

"Box cutters are always missing when you need one," she said. "So you improvise and bleed."

Ina almost smiled. "That sounds like this industry."

"That sounds like this country," Ji-eun said.

The manager called her name sharply. Ji-eun rolled her eyes so quickly only Ina caught it, then walked back to her chair with stage-perfect posture snapping into place before she sat.

Rhee touched Ina's elbow. "Car."

In the elevator down, neither of them spoke. Rhee unwrapped a butterscotch candy and offered one. Ina shook her head.

Outside, dawn had started to gray the sky above the river bridges. Delivery scooters cut through early traffic, headlights flashing over wet asphalt. Ina got into the back seat of a black sedan that smelled like vinyl and old pine air freshener.

Her phone buzzed.

An unknown number pushed a secure message app she did not remember installing.

`Welcome to Restoration Council probation.`

`Stipend transfer pending via Haneul Cultural Foundation affiliate.`

`Medical arrears partial payment initiated.`

`Liability request received: PARK JIEUN / RIVER LIGHT E1 variance.`

`Confirm assumption by 07:30. If unconfirmed, claim reverts to performer debt account.`

A second message arrived from Seoul St. Mary's billing office two minutes later.

`Payment posted. Updated balance available in portal.`

Ina signed into the billing portal on her phone. The updated page showed a smaller balance and a new line item: `Third-party guarantor registered.` A notice below it said verification outreach had been sent to the family contact ending in 1182, her father's number.

Before she could decide whether to call him first, Hyejin's name flashed on her screen.

`Hospital called before six. Your father thinks it's billing fraud. Call me before you come over.`

The secure app timer turned red at the top of the screen. Nine minutes remained on the liability request.

She opened the contract packet again in the car's thin morning light. Page eleven held her signature, slightly slanted because the conference table had been uneven. At the bottom, below the legal boilerplate, she had written one extra sentence before signing. Rhee had not stopped her.

`Contractor reserves refusal of directives intended to produce direct civilian harm.`

Kwon had initialed beside it in neat blue ink.

The addendum was weak. Any lawyer with patience and motive could interpret it into dust.

The timer dropped to eight minutes.

If she refused the transfer, the claim snapped back to Ji-eun's trainee debt before breakfast. If she accepted it, Haneul's legal team could tie performance penalties to her stipend stream and, by extension, to the same family finances Kwon had already mapped.

Ina pressed `CONFIRM ASSUMPTION`.

The app refreshed.

`Contractor liability activated.`

`Non-payment may trigger recovery actions against registered guarantor-linked assets.`

The sedan turned onto the bridge toward Mapo. Water below was flat pewter under dawn cloud. Ina watched her reflection in the window drift over the river and superimpose on cranes and apartment blocks and construction hoardings promising luxury towers to people who did not need to count coins before buying transit fare.

She pressed the burn scar on her left wrist with her thumb until the old skin whitened.

By seven-thirty she had made two entries on two different ledgers: one payment that kept her father out of immediate collections, and one transfer that kept Ji-eun from carrying today's punishment alone.

The mechanism was plain. If Ina kept delivering, her lines would move through actors, agencies, edits, and USB copies she could not track. If one of those lines tripped a sweep, the person pulled into a windowless room would not be Kwon, and it would not be the lawyers who wrote the clauses.

There was no clean version left, only distribution of damage.

That was the story she built before sunrise, and it did not forgive her.

By the time the car stopped outside her building, she had revised it three times and believed none of the revisions fully. She carried the contract upstairs anyway.
By late afternoon she had countersigned three intake addenda, bought eggs she could not afford without checking her balance twice, and headed to her father's apartment when Hyejin's second text replaced a question with an instruction.


# Chapter 3

By dinnertime, after a day of signatures she still wanted to spit out, Ina opened her father's apartment door. The first thing she noticed was the fan belt squeal from the old standing fan in the corner. It had a rhythm now, one short cry every three turns, like a bird trapped in plastic. The second thing was garlic and soybean paste catching in the back of her throat before the heat from the pot reached her face.

"You came in time to work," Hyejin called from the sink. She wore her apron over church slacks, blue flowers printed over a bleach stain shaped like Jeju Island. Her reading glasses hung on the chain at her chest and clicked against a ladle every time she leaned over the pot. "Don't stand there like a guest. Wash your hands. The zucchini won't cut itself."

Dohyun sat at the low table with his bad knee stretched straight, brace straps loose. He had spread an old paper road map over the table like a cloth and set chopsticks on top of Busan and Incheon, pinning the curled edges. Nicotine had yellowed the pads of his fingers so deeply they looked varnished. He did not look up right away.

"She brought those expensive eggs," he said, finally lifting his chin at the grocery bag in Ina's hand. "Means she did something she feels guilty about."

"I brought cheap eggs," Ina said. "You think guilt has a market rate now?"

Hyejin snorted. "Everything has a market rate now. Last week they sold strawberries that tasted like wet tissue for fifteen thousand won."

Ina set the bag on the counter and moved beside her aunt. The faucet stuttered before it gave a clean stream. She washed her hands and sliced zucchini into half-moons while Hyejin dropped tofu cubes into the bubbling jjigae with the neat precision of someone dealing cards. The window was cracked open, but the room still held yesterday's cigarette smoke in the wallpaper.

On the floor by the refrigerator, next to a bag of rice, lay three unopened envelopes with red stamps. A fourth envelope had already been slit open and taped back together. Someone had written DO NOT THROW AWAY across the front in thick marker. Hyejin saw Ina's eyes and placed a bowl over the stack as if she were covering leftovers.

"Eat first," she said without looking at her. "Money always tastes worse on an empty stomach."

They set out side dishes: kimchi too sour for polite company, stir-fried anchovies with too much chili, cucumber seasoned with sesame oil, omelet strips folded like yellow napkins. Hyejin lined up metal bowls by size without thinking, tapping each one once before she put it down. It was a habit Ina remembered from childhood lunches at church basement weddings, where her aunt could feed two hundred people out of panic and pride.

Dohyun folded the map in half and sat up enough to make room. "If you're using this table," he said, "don't spill soup on North Chungcheong."

Ina sat and looked at the faded lines. The map was old enough that some roads no longer existed, and some names had changed spelling. Near the top edge, a tiny crease had almost erased a river.

"You still keeping that thing?" she asked.

"It still tells me where things are."

"It tells you where things were."

"Close enough." He reached for the rice paddle. "That's my whole life now."

Hyejin thumped his shoulder with the back of her hand. "Don't start the tragedy talk before the rice is served. Your daughter came over. Let her pretend she has a normal family for twelve minutes."

Dohyun's mouth twitched at one corner. "Twelve? You're generous tonight."

"I found shrimp on sale. I'm soft."

They ate in standing heat from the pot, mouths burning on the first spoonful before their tongues adjusted. For a while the talk stayed in easy territory. Hyejin complained about a deacon who watered down sesame oil in the church kitchen and called it stewardship. Dohyun told a story about his former foreman falling asleep during a safety lecture and snoring so loud the training video paused because the microphone thought he was speaking. Ina laughed with soup in her mouth and had to cover her lips with the back of her hand while she coughed.

"Your mother would've fired him," Hyejin said.

"She tried to fire everyone," Dohyun said. "At home too."

"She fired your cigarettes once," Ina said. "Remember? She soaked a whole carton in dishwater and lined them up on the balcony like dead fish."

Hyejin slapped the table and barked out a laugh, head thrown back. "He cried over those cigarettes more than he cried at your wedding." She paused, then corrected herself with a dry tilt of her mouth. "At your cousin's wedding. Sorry. I was thinking of the one that never happened because men are unreliable and my memory is old."

Ina rolled her eyes. "Auntie, if you start arranging me at dinner again, I'll leave with the pot."

"Take the pot. I need less washing up."

Even Dohyun laughed then, a rough two-note sound like rusted hinges. He rubbed the side of his knee with his thumb while he chewed, and Ina saw the little relief that came when his face moved out of its careful set. She let the moment stay as long as it could, spoon in hand, steam wet on her upper lip.

The television in the next room bled muffled debate noise through the doorway: campaign promises, panelists snapping over one another, a map of the peninsula on constant rotation in red and blue. No one had muted it all the way, only lowered it to the level of weather.

"Turn that off," Hyejin said. "I can't hear people lie and digest at the same time."

Dohyun did not move. "Leave it. If you turn it off, they'll still lie."

"If it's on, they lie in my kitchen."

Ina got up and clicked the remote. With the TV dead, the fan belt owned the room; chopsticks rang against metal, and traffic hissed up from the street below. Somewhere outside a scooter backfired and shot away.

When she sat again, Dohyun was staring at the map under his bowl. He lifted the bowl and repositioned it so it no longer covered the border line.

"At the dock," he said, voice aimed at the table, "there was a guy from Hamhung. He came down with nothing but a coat and one shoe. This was before you were born." He pressed two fingers against a road line, tracing it from memory. "He kept saying his mother would follow by train because she promised. For ten years he stayed by the freight gate every Chuseok. He brought rice cakes in a plastic bag, like she could smell him through concrete."

Hyejin gave a small click of her tongue. "Dohyun."

"I'm eating," he said. "I'm allowed to talk while I eat."

Ina lowered her spoon. The broth had gone from scalding to warm and suddenly too salty.

"What happened to him?" she asked.

"Nothing happened. That's the point." Dohyun shrugged. "Year eleven he stopped coming to the gate. Year twelve he drank during shift and got his hand crushed under a pallet jack. Year thirteen he moved to Uijeongbu and slept in a laundry room behind his niece's kitchen. That's what the big promises paid him: one ruined hand, no overtime, and rent he could never catch."

Hyejin set down her chopsticks and wiped her fingers on a paper napkin, though they were already clean. "You always pick the ugliest version and call it realism."

"You always pick the church version and call it hope."

"Hope fed people in this house when realism could not."

He looked up at that, and Ina saw anger arriving not as volume but as stillness. The fan squealed in its loop. Outside, someone shouted up an address, then cursed when no one answered.

"Hope didn't pay the hospital," Dohyun said. "Hope didn't cover the cremation deposit."

"I know exactly what hope paid," Hyejin snapped. "I signed two of those forms because your handwriting looked like an earthquake."

Ina felt the pressure shift and tried to pull it back. "Can we not do accounting over soup?"

"You think the soup is separate from accounting?" Hyejin asked, softer now, looking at Ina instead of Dohyun. "Baby, this pot cost money. The gas under it cost money. Your father sitting in that chair cost money."

Dohyun exhaled through his nose and reached for the anchovies. "Your mother would've turned this into an argument about narrative by now," he said.

Ina kept her face neutral. "Narrative."

"That was her word when she wanted to win." Dohyun plucked a chili seed from his bottom lip. "Policy is for men who like hearing themselves explain maps, she'd say. Stories move people before policy even puts on shoes."

The sentence landed so cleanly it felt rehearsed, though she knew him better than that. He did not rehearse pain. He hoarded it until it leaked out sideways.

Hyejin made a sound that might have been agreement or warning. "She said that in every room where men thought she was decoration."

"She said it at me too," Dohyun said. "When I told her to stop taking those weekend train trips, she said if she stayed home and folded laundry while old men drew lines, then she'd be folding laundry for ghosts." He gave a short laugh with no joy in it. "I told her ghosts don't pay rent. She told me rent isn't history."

Ina watched a bead of broth slide down the outside of her spoon and collect at the handle. Her mother had used phrases like tools: not always elegant, always pointed. At the safehouse basement two nights ago, Kwon had spoken with almost the same confidence, except his hands had stayed dry and clean while he did it.

Dohyun chewed, swallowed, and looked directly at Ina for the first time in several minutes. "She believed it, though. The stories thing. Not as slogan. As work. She thought you could move a country by changing what people hummed doing dishes."

Ina heard herself answer before she had planned the tone. "Maybe she wasn't wrong."

Hyejin's eyes flicked up. Dohyun's did too.

"About dishes?" Hyejin asked lightly, testing.

"About songs," Ina said, then added quickly, "about how people remember things."

Dohyun leaned back and the chair joints complained. "Careful. That's how people get recruited."

The word hit the table harder than any chopstick. Ina forced herself to reach for rice so her hand had a job.

"Recruited by what, Appa?" she said.

"By anyone selling purpose to people with bills." He pushed his bowl away an inch. "Election guys. Church guys. Patriot guys. Startups. Doesn't matter. They tell you your private mess is noble duty, then they dock your pay, wreck your back, and tape an eviction notice to your door."

Hyejin gave him a hard look. "Don't talk like she's fourteen."

"I'm talking like she's thirty-five and smart enough to hear it."

Ina set the rice bowl down too hard and grains stuck to the lacquered table. "No one's recruiting me at this table."

Dohyun's expression softened around the edges, and that made it worse. "I didn't say at this table."

Silence gathered, but it was not empty. The television in the other room stayed dark while the fan kept its squeal and traffic scraped below. When Hyejin shifted, her glasses chain ticked once against her bowl.

Hyejin reached for the sour kimchi and put a strip on Ina's rice. "Eat," she said. "Your thinking face makes me nervous."

Ina almost laughed. Instead she ate. The kimchi hit sharp and cold against the starch.

After a minute Dohyun spoke again, quieter. "Your mother and I fought for twenty years about the line up north. Same fight in different kitchens. I wanted a life small enough to keep. She wanted a life big enough to mean something." He pressed his palm flat to the old map. "Both of us lost anyway."

"You didn't lose everything," Hyejin said. "You still have your daughter."

"For now," he said.

Ina put her spoon down. "I'm not going anywhere."

He met her gaze and gave a tired half-smile that did not agree with her. "Nobody leaves all at once."

The envelopes by the refrigerator seemed to tilt into her vision again even though she was not looking that way, with red stamps, late notices, and final reminders stacked in one accusing pile.

Hyejin stood and gathered bowls with brisk, irritated efficiency. "Fine. Enough philosophy. If either of you says border, policy, or destiny again, I'm putting both of you on dish duty and locking the soap." She pointed the ladle at Dohyun. "You, medicine after dinner." Then at Ina. "You, take those envelopes before your father uses them as coasters."

"I wasn't going to use them as coasters," Dohyun muttered.

"You used one as a fan last week."

"That wasn't an envelope. It was a flyer."

"It was a tax notice."

Ina laughed despite herself, short and unwilling, and the laugh hurt less than she expected.

She stayed to wash dishes while Hyejin wrapped leftovers for tomorrow's breakfast service. Her aunt labeled each plastic container with masking tape and blocky marker letters: TOFU, KIMCHI, DOHYUN PILLS DO NOT TOUCH. She did it like a person putting names on lifeboats.

In the small bathroom mirror, Ina caught her own face under the yellow bulb. Her eyes looked older than thirty-five tonight. A thread of zucchini skin clung to her sleeve. She rinsed it off, dried her hands, and came back to the kitchen where the envelopes waited.

The top one was from Seoul St. Mary's billing office. She knew the logo before she read the return address: blue cross bent into a ribbon shape. She slid a finger under the seal and unfolded the statement.

Minimum payment due within seven days: KRW 2,900,000.

Total outstanding balance: KRW 58,000,000.

Past due notices sent: 3.

"They're calling during daytime now," Hyejin said, tying a bag knot with a hard jerk. "Not just the evening shift. They called while I was gutting mackerel and asked if Mr. Han understood legal escalation. I told the woman he understood knives better and she hung up."

Ina scanned the smaller print and read each item in order: interest adjustment, late fees, processing charge. The rows and columns felt like polite bureaucratic violence.

"Did they call Appa directly?"

"Of course they called him." Hyejin lowered her voice. "He pretended he couldn't hear them and smoked half a pack on the landing."

From the other room Dohyun shouted, "I can hear you both. My hearing works when people are insulting me."

"Hear this," Hyejin shouted back. "You owe rent."

Dohyun did not answer.

The second envelope was from his landlord: arrears KRW 11,200,000. A typed paragraph threatened legal filing if no payment arrangement was made within ten business days. Someone had added a handwritten note in blue pen: Mr. Han, please call me before this goes to attorney. I know your situation.

The handwriting was small and careful, the kind used by someone trying not to look cruel.

"Landlord's daughter wrote that," Hyejin said. "She wears those huge black boots even in July. Nice girl. Keeps giving your father extra time because he fixed their hallway lights for free last winter."

A phone buzzed on the counter. Not Ina's. Hyejin checked the screen and exhaled through her teeth.

"Billing office again," she said. "Blocked number."

She offered the phone to Ina, then pulled it back before Ina could take it. "No. If you answer, they smell blood."

The vibration stopped, then started again ten seconds later.

This time Ina took it and swiped accept.

"Hello?"

A woman with a hoarse smoker's rasp launched into scripted courtesy so quickly the syllables tripped one another. "Good evening, this call concerns account services for Mr. Han Dohyun regarding unresolved medical balance. For privacy verification, can you confirm relation to account holder?"

Ina could hear office noise behind her: keyboards, cheap headset static, someone coughing without muting. She pictured a row of cubicles under fluorescent light, paper cups with instant coffee skin on top.

"I'm his daughter," Ina said.

"Thank you, Ms. Seo. I need to advise you this account is currently at escalation level three. We can suspend legal handoff if a minimum commitment is made tonight."

"How much?"

"Two million nine hundred thousand won by midnight secures suspension for fourteen days."

Ina looked at the statement in her hand as if a different number might appear.

"Tonight?"

"Yes, ma'am. We can process card, account transfer, or split authorization if you have family contributors available on the line." The woman paused, then dropped the script by half a degree. "If I don't log movement by shift close, it goes to the next team and they don't negotiate."

A pen tapped twice against a desk on the other end, maybe the caller's own habit, maybe the person in the next cubicle. Ina imagined her in a cardigan with pilled sleeves and gum hidden in her cheek to keep from shouting.

"What is your name?" Ina asked.

There was a tiny silence. "Agent ID is 7426."

"Your real name."

Another pause, longer this time. "Sunmi," the woman said, voice flattening as if she regretted it immediately. "Ms. Seo, I'm not allowed to do personal conversation on recorded line."

"I'm not trying to make friends. I'm trying to understand if any part of this is human."

Sunmi gave a brief, dry laugh that sounded like a scrape. "Human costs extra. I can offer you fourteen days. That's all I've got."

Ina closed her eyes. The kitchen smelled of dish soap and cooled broth. Hyejin watched her with her lips pressed thin.

"I can't pay tonight," Ina said.

"Pay partial by midnight. Even three hundred thousand changes my screen color. Right now you're red."

Her status was red.

Ina's eyes kept snagging on the same proof. Red-stamped envelopes by the fridge. Block-letter labels on leftover containers. A map corner trapped under the placemat where Dohyun had shoved it aside. Every object in the room had been stretched past its intended life and was still being asked for one more week.

"I'll transfer three hundred tonight," she said.

"Thank you," Sunmi said, pure script again. "I'm sending secure link now. Please complete before 23:59 to preserve temporary hold."

The line clicked dead.

Ten seconds later Ina's own phone buzzed in her pocket.

She expected the payment link. She got two messages.

The first was from an unlisted number labeled COMPLIANCE DESK:

If you resign before the first payout, Legal files breach at 09:00 Monday.
Security Crimes receives your intake file the same day.
Your accounts freeze first, and questions come later.

The second was from Council payroll, formal and bland:

Welcome packet processed. First stipend release scheduled Friday, pending compliance confirmation.

She stared at both until the screen dimmed.

Hyejin had not seen the texts, only Ina's face.

"What now?" her aunt asked.

Ina locked the phone. "Now I pay what I can and pretend that's strategy."

"That sounds like your mother." Hyejin tied another bag. "Except she would have called it revolution and made everyone else wash dishes."

Ina almost told her about the basement with blocked windows and hot server fans, about Kwon's dry hands on the contract pages, and about the clause that made non-disclosure a criminal edge instead of family privacy. She imagined saying it out loud and watched, in advance, what it would do: Dohyun standing up too fast on a bad knee, Hyejin reaching for the church phone tree, this apartment becoming a frightened committee.

She swallowed it.

"I picked up extra work," she said instead. "Script polish. Night schedule. It'll cover things for now."

From the living room, Dohyun spoke without turning around. "Television scripts?"

"Some of it still pays."

"People still pay for that?"

"People pay for whatever keeps them from hearing silence."

He grunted, which might have been skepticism or respect. "Charge double."

It was the closest he had come all evening to blessing.

By the time she left, the air in the hallway had cooled and smelled faintly of bleach from someone's late mop. In the elevator mirror she saw soup flecks on her shirt and wiped them with a tissue until the fabric darkened in uneven circles.

Outside, the night carried wet asphalt and fryer oil from the fried chicken place across the alley. A delivery rider idled at the curb scrolling his phone, helmet visor up, lips moving while he read. Ina walked past him to the bus stop and did the numbers again because numbers gave her hands a way to move even when she stood still.

The hospital minimum this week was 2.9 million.

The landlord threat window was ten business days.

If MiraeWave did not delay again, the subtitle residual due next month might be 2.1 million.

The cash in her account after the funeral, groceries, and her father's pills was not enough.

The council stipend on Friday would be 7 million.

The contract breach clause still meant immediate repayment of advance and liability exposure for operational disclosure.

She did not need a calculator. She needed money and silence and a stomach strong enough to hold both.

At home the apartment over the river felt too clean because she had spent the last week scrubbing grief into corners. The green lyric notebook lay where she had hidden it, inside an old rice bin under dish towels. She took it out and set it on the table beside her laptop.

The call log already showed three missed numbers from variants of the same billing office prefix. A voicemail icon glowed red; another appeared while she watched. She let both sit unopened.

She opened her banking app and transferred KRW 300,000 through the secure link Sunmi had sent. The confirmation screen showed an animated checkmark so cheerful it felt insulting.

Immediately, another call came in from a different number, and she answered without greeting.

"Ms. Seo?" a male voice asked. Younger than she expected, maybe late twenties, polite enough to be dangerous. "This is Min from Daewon Asset Recovery, legal-prep desk for Seoul St. Mary's follow-up unit. Did you receive our colleague's message?"

"I paid partial."

"I see that. Thank you." Keyboard clicks. "I also need to advise you of the next trigger. Because you paid from your account, you are now the payer of record. If the remaining minimum is not posted by Friday 18:00, our legal desk files a provisional seizure request on that payer account at 09:00 Monday."

Ina leaned against the table edge. "You freeze my account over my father's bill."

"The court decides the freeze. We file the request." His tone stayed polite. "And once filing starts, your bank can hold incoming funds while the petition is pending."

"How long is pending?"

"Usually five to ten business days." More keyboard clicks. "If you are expecting Friday payroll, transfer before 18:00 Friday. After that timestamp, my desk cannot pull it back."

The stipend. He was describing the stipend without knowing its name.

"Noted," she said.

"I also need a same-day payment intent on record with an exact amount."

"Two million six by Friday, before six."

"Thank you." He exhaled, and she heard office chatter swell behind him. "One more thing. If there is any chance you cannot hit that number, call before Friday noon. We can request a 24-hour legal hold once. After noon, we cannot."

"Don't miss my call either."

This time his laugh was short and tired. "I won't. Good night, Ms. Seo."
