When the call ended, the room became very small, with refrigerator hum, distant traffic, and river wind pushing faintly against the window frame. Her burn scar on her left wrist ached where the weather changed, a dull nerve tug she usually ignored.

She opened the council contract file on her laptop and read the breach section again. Section 11 named Council Legal as reporting authority. If she withdrew without clearance, the clocks started immediately: breach notice in twelve hours; demand for repayment of all disbursed compensation within seventy-two; intake recordings forwarded to National Security Investigation within one business day. Section 12 allowed them to seek temporary asset-preservation orders on suspicion alone, before any disclosure was proved.

She could quit in theory. She could send an email by morning and call it a bad fit. In practice, that email started clocks: hospital legal filing Friday night, seizure petition Monday morning, landlord attorney notice inside ten business days, Council breach packet by noon.

The unknown text sat at the top of her messages, plain and ugly.

If you resign before the first payout, Legal files breach at 09:00 Monday.
Security Crimes receives your intake file the same day.
Your accounts freeze first, and questions come later.

It could have been a bluff from someone low in the chain. It could have been policy. Either way, it gave her a date, an office, and a clock.

Ina pulled the green notebook toward her. The cover had softened at the corners from years in her mother's hands. Inside were lyric fragments, dates, grocery lists, a phone number crossed out three times, and one page where Eun-sook had copied a line from an old speech and then written in the margin, Too clean. Real life leaks.

Ina flipped to an empty page near the back. She sat with the pen uncapped, listening to the fan belt cry in memory, hearing her father say recruited like it was a weather report and a warning both.

She wrote one sentence in Korean first and copied it below in English because contract language had entered her head in English and she wanted no excuse to misunderstand herself.

No operation of mine will knowingly increase civilian harm.

She stared at it, added a date, and drew a box around the line as if a box could turn resolve into law.

After a moment she wrote two clarifications underneath, smaller, harsher:

No targeting civilians for pressure effects.

No concealment of civilian casualties if discovered.

The pen dug so hard on discovered that it almost tore the page.

She knew the rule had no enforcement mechanism. She had not told Kwon. She had not told anyone. Private ethics were easy to keep until invoices arrived and someone older called your compromise history.

Still, she wrote it.

She opened a new note on her phone. She copied the same three lines, buried the file under a grocery-list title, and made sure it would not glow like a confession if someone glanced at her screen.

She reopened the payroll message and tapped the secure link she had left untouched.

COMPLIANCE CONFIRMATION REQUIRED BEFORE FIRST STIPEND RELEASE.
By confirming active status, contractor accepts Clause 11 and Clause 12 remedies.

She entered the one-time code, typed her legal name, and pressed CONFIRM.

The receipt arrived before she could change her mind.

ACTIVE STATUS LOCKED - 22:41
FIRST STIPEND RELEASE - FRIDAY
WITHDRAWAL WITHOUT CLEARANCE TRIGGERS BREACH ENFORCEMENT

Outside, a siren moved along the river road and faded. Ina stood, took two steps toward the sink, and bent over hard when acid climbed her throat. Nothing came up but bitter spit and a thread of blood from where she had bitten the inside of her cheek. Her left wrist burned under old scar tissue, hot and sharp, and she gripped the steel edge until her fingers cramped.

In the dark kitchen window, her reflection floated over black water and construction cranes. She did not text her father. She sent Hyejin one line instead: I'll cover Friday. Then she opened MiraeWave's morning rundown and read until the sky went from black to dishwater gray.


# Chapter 4

At 8:11, the production board in MiraeWave's Building C already looked like a threat. First story conference in nineteen minutes. Second at ten with advertisers. Noon with Standards. Two with Director Kwon. Four with Haneul Entertainment and Ji-eun's manager. Final script lock by midnight if everyone lied about how long things took. She had read the same rundown in her kitchen at dawn, then walked into rain hard enough to pull fryer oil and wet cardboard into the curb while the Han pushed its sweet-rot breath upriver. Inside Building C, reheated coffee and monitor ozone clung to the hallway. Ina wiped rain from her glasses with the hem of her blouse, saw the smear she made, and wiped again. Her left wrist tugged where the old burn scar tightened in damp weather.

The writers' room looked like a school classroom designed by people who hated windows. The walls were paper-thin, and marker fumes hung in the heat from a fan that turned without moving any air. Three whiteboards divided into columns: ROMANCE ARC, SPONSOR TOUCHPOINTS, SENSITIVE CONTENT. Under SENSITIVE CONTENT, somebody had written in red, all caps: NO FLAGS. NO BORDER MAPS. NO SPEECHES.

Executive producer Nam So-young was already there, barefoot under the table, shoes lined up neatly beside her chair because her left heel blistered if she sat in pumps for more than an hour. She rolled a dried persimmon between her fingers like a prayer bead, then bit it in half without looking up from the rating sheet.

"You're late."

"I'm two minutes early," Ina said, hanging her wet tote on the back of her chair.

"That counts as late in post-election quarter."

Nam slid a stack of paper toward her: last night's comparative ratings, ad buy forecasts, and a memo from the network's legal team with three pages highlighted in yellow. Every page used the same words: risk of polarization, advertiser flight, reputational volatility.

Head writer Lee Sang-ho leaned back and cracked his knuckles one at a time in escalating rhythm. It was how he thought. It was also how he warned the room he was about to pick a fight.

"We have a love story," he said. "A clean one. Two leads from opposite classes, one rooftop bar, one old neighborhood market, one misunderstanding, two kisses before episode four. We know how to sell that. We don't need national healing hidden inside the ramen placement."

"Nobody said national healing," Ina said.

Nam snorted. "Your last treatment did."

"It said family fracture."

"It said inherited fracture in a divided land. That is national healing with a fake mustache."

PPL coordinator Woo Hye-rin clicked her mechanical pencil nonstop while she talked, six clicks for each sentence, as if loading bullets.

"Sponsors approved romance. They approved skincare close-ups, convenience-store dates, and a ride-share app integration in episode two. They did not approve ideological puzzles that get clipped into YouTube conspiracy channels before midnight."

She tapped the whiteboard with the pencil eraser. "Keep the line simple: they meet, they want each other, life gets in the way, they kiss anyway."

Ina sat. Her laptop fan started whirring before she opened a file. She had slept three hours and dreamed in subtitle timing bars: line in, line out, character count max forty-two. The green lyric notebook from her mother's apartment was in her tote under a legal pad. She had not intended to bring it. She had grabbed the wrong stack in the dark. Now she felt its hard corners through canvas every time she shifted her foot.

Lee tossed a scene card in front of her. "Episode one midpoint. Give me a cute misdirect at the river food stalls."

"I can give you that," Ina said. "But we need a second engine. If this is only flirtation, it dies after week two."

Nam folded her arms. "A second engine means conflict. Conflict means politics. Politics means angry uncle groups boycotting skincare serum. We're not doing that."

Ina looked at the whiteboard until the marker strokes blurred. She thought of the private rule she had written at two in the morning in a notes app she never synced: Do not let a story cover harm you would not name out loud. She had written it to sound legal, objective, as if grammar could keep it upright when money started leaning.

"Then we make the conflict domestic," she said. "Not policy. Not slogans. A sibling split. Female lead has spent twelve years looking for her younger brother after he's sent away by relatives during a debt collapse. She tells no one because shame. Male lead thinks she's avoidant. She's not. She's carrying a ghost who's still alive somewhere."

Lee stopped cracking his knuckles.

Woo clicked her pencil twice, then forgot to click again.

Nam chewed slowly, eyes narrowed. "Where was he sent away to?"

"To another city first. After that, no stable contact. The details stay ordinary: bad paperwork, predatory guardianship, a family that treats children like collateral. We don't say state, we don't say ideology, we don't say anything censors can underline in red."

Lee leaned forward. "That is melodrama."

"Romance is melodrama with better lighting," Ina said. "I'm giving you spine."

Nam put her persimmon down and reached for her shoes without putting them on. "How does this sell lipstick."

"The female lead has a private ritual," Ina said. "Every episode she hums a lullaby her brother hated when he was seven because he said it sounded old. Later we learn he still remembers it as an adult. The tune threads episodes without speeches. Viewers catch it even if they can't name why they're crying."

Woo grimaced. "Songs are rights issues."

"We commission four bars original," Ina said. "No lyric registration conflict."

Lee shook his head. "A humming motif can feel manipulative."

"So can slow-motion kissing in rain, and we still do it."

Nam laughed once, sharp. "She's right about that."

The room loosened by two degrees. It was not enough to relax, but it was enough to continue.

By ten-fifteen, the whiteboard held two stacks of cards instead of one. Romance beats on the left: convenience store, rooftop, mother-in-law fake-out, first date disaster. Sibling thread on the right: missing records office, lullaby recall, old photo with half a face burned off, anonymous message from a number saved as No Name 2.

At ten-thirty-six, the advertiser call started on speakerphone. Seven voices introduced themselves with perfect politeness and immediate demands.

"No unresolved social trauma in product scenes."

"Don't pair debt dialogue with brand logos."

"No crying in the dermal patch bathroom sequence."

"We need aspirational glow."

Nam managed the call with her producer smile voice, the one she used with men who held more budget than imagination. "Absolutely. We're keeping emotional stakes intimate and universal."

The word universal made Ina flinch. She wrote it on her legal pad and circled it until the paper nearly tore.

At noon, Standards sent preliminary notes before they had even read the full revision. That was how this phase worked: preemptive caution packaged as guidance. The PDF had comment bubbles every third paragraph.

Replace "lost country" with "lost family."
Delete "return" language in dialogue line 37.
Avoid "brother across the line"; line can imply unauthorized cross-regional rhetoric.
Lullaby motif acceptable if no historically coded vocabulary.

Ina read the notes twice. Her skin prickled in the airless room. The guidelines did not ban grief. They banned geography for grief.

At one-fifteen, Director Kwon arrived without warning, flanked by two council staffers who waited by the door and never sat. He wore a gray suit that looked pressed by someone else three minutes before he walked in. Silver hair combed straight back. Cuff links with no logo. The posture of a man who assumed chairs were optional.

Nam stood and bowed just enough to be polite but not enough to concede ownership of the room. "Director."

Kwon nodded. "Producer Nam."

He turned to Ina. "Writer Seo. I read your draft from last night."

She stood. "Which version did you read?"

"The one before legal edits."

Of course he had that version. Council servers mirrored everything she saved, including files she had deleted and replaced and deleted again.

Kwon held out a marked copy. "Your dialogue is technically clever. Your intention is timid."

Nam made a dry sound in her throat. "Timid works on television."

Kwon did not look at her. "Television has been used to train the emotional weather of populations for sixty years. Timid does not move weather."

Lee cleared his throat. "With respect, sir, weather that moves too fast kills ratings."

Kwon folded his hands behind his back. "Ratings without direction are market noise. We are not funding noise."

Nam crossed her ankles and slipped her shoes back on, one heel still bare where the blister tape had peeled. "Your stipend is meaningful, Director, but this show is still a broadcast product. If we trigger political review, the slot dies."

Kwon finally turned to her. "I am not asking for slogans. I am asking for signal."

He looked back to Ina. "Where is the signal."

Ina felt every eye in the room settle on her, including the two council staffers at the door who pretended they were furniture. She could hear the fan rattling. She could smell dried fruit sugar and solvent from fresh marker ink.

"In the sibling plot," she said. "In the lullaby. In what is missing from paperwork and still present in muscle memory. It's not overt. It's persistent."

Kwon took one page from the stack and tapped a highlighted line. "This sentence: 'You cannot keep dividing a house and call it home.' Standards will kill it."

"I already replaced it."

"With what line?"

"'You keep moving walls and then wonder why no one sleeps.'"

Kwon held her gaze for a long time. "Better. Keep that level."

Nam rubbed her forehead. "Can we lock content decisions to script and stop improvising ideological audits in my room."

Kwon's expression did not change. "You will have lock authority on line edit and ad choreography. Writer Seo will have final language authority on symbolic content."

Nam's jaw tightened. "That is not how this production hierarchy works."

"It is now."

The room went silent in the way rooms do when money changes owners without paperwork.

Kwon turned toward the door, then paused. "One more point. Actor Park will carry these layers on her face. Have you prepared her."

Nam answered first. "Agency handles their talent."

Kwon ignored her. "Writer Seo?"

"I meet her at four."

"Do not fail there. If she plays confusion instead of recognition, the entire architecture collapses."

When he left, one council staffer stayed by the door a half second longer, eyes moving across whiteboards, script piles, faces. He was younger than Ina, maybe thirty, with a stitched repair on the elbow of an expensive jacket. As he followed Kwon out, he offered a small apologetic bow to no one in particular. It was human detail where she had expected only surveillance. It did not make him less surveillance.

Nam sat down hard enough to rattle pens in a cup. "Congratulations. You just got promoted into a knife fight."

Lee whistled low. "Final language authority. They're going to blame you from both sides."

"They already were," Ina said.

Nam stabbed the table with a finger. "Then hear this clearly. I won't lose this timeslot because men in suits want to stage a seminar through my lead actress. Keep your symbols below detection. I mean below mine too. If I can see them, the audience can see them, and we all get dragged."

"That includes me," Ina said.

"It includes you first."

At three-forty, Ina printed the latest draft and took a taxi to Haneul Entertainment's rehearsal complex in Gangnam. Rain had stopped. Steam rose off asphalt. Motorbike couriers cut between buses and cursed at everyone. Her phone filled with unread messages: two from Finance reminding her council stipend deposit timing depended on approved deliverables, one from hospital billing about her mother's balance adjustment failure, one from unknown number: You're writing this too soft. She deleted that one without opening details and felt the phantom vibration anyway.

Haneul's lobby lights were bright enough to bleach fatigue into a brand look. Citrus cleaner and hair spray sat in the air under a wall of framed album covers that watched her with twenty-four versions of joy. At check-in, the receptionist asked her to surrender her phone camera sticker in a voice bright enough to sand wood.

Manager Choi Myeong-jin met her at the elevator, forty-something, navy suit, white sneakers with orthopedic inserts that made his gait slightly uneven. He carried two things at all times: a tablet full of schedules and a strip of peppermint antacids he snapped in half with his thumbnail between calls.

"We have fourteen minutes," he said before hello. "She has choreography at four-twenty, dialect coaching at four-forty, live commerce prep at five-ten, then vocal. No unscripted media interactions."

"I'm not media."

"You're a risk."

"I'm a writer."

"Same thing this quarter."

He swiped his tablet, checking boxes as they walked. "Also, we're not discussing contract terms with talent present."

"We have script lines that map to contract risk."

"We're not discussing contract terms with talent present," he repeated, slower, as if recording evidence by cadence.

They reached Studio B: mirrored walls, sprung floor, harsh overhead lights that flattened skin into polished fatigue. Ji-eun sat on the floor by a fan, legs folded under her, dark hair clipped up with a cheap plastic claw clip shaped like a cartoon fish. She had removed one false eyelash and forgotten to remove the other, giving her face a lopsided ferocity. Her left hand tremored lightly when she reached for her water bottle, then steadied when she wrapped two fingers around the neck and squeezed.

She saw Ina and smiled with her public mouth first. "Writer Seo. Thanks for saving me from dance cardio."

Choi checked his watch. "Twelve minutes now."

Ji-eun lifted her brows. "You're counting in front of me again."

"I'm counting for you."

She turned back to Ina, smile gone; her private voice came out lower and rougher at the edges. "Come in before he starts timing our breathing."

Inside the practice room, dance tracks from next door bled through the wall: bass thuds and instructor counts. One-two-three-four, again.

Ina spread the printed pages on the floor. Scene 14. Rooftop argument between leads. Ji-eun's character, Sun-a, had to admit she had spent years searching for a missing brother while pretending she did not care about family at all.

Ji-eun skimmed quickly. Script literacy was not a bullet point in her profile. It was in her fingers: she tracked emotional pivot points with her thumbnail, scratching tiny half-moons into margins at each turn.

"This line works," she said, tapping page three. "'I knew his shoe size longer than I knew my own blood type.'" She glanced up. "That is disgusting. I like it."

"It should feel disgusting," Ina said. "Care work and resentment living in the same sentence."

Ji-eun hummed the four-bar lullaby phrase written in notation above a scene break. She was off key at first, then corrected on the second pass without asking for pitch reference. She frowned.

"It sounds like a church song that got lost on the subway."

"My aunt would call that a compliment."

Ji-eun flipped to the end of the scene packet where a new monologue sat in yellow highlight. "And this. You moved my close-up to after the humming."

"I needed your face on recognition, not mystery."

Ji-eun traced the last line with her finger and did not read it aloud. Outside, Choi's voice cut through the door speaking to someone about endorsement penalties and sleep medication compliance windows.

Ji-eun looked up. "Say it plain. Am I being used as a political instrument."

There it was, direct enough to leave no polite exits.

Ina could have answered with strategy. She could have answered with legal wording, with industry's favorite solvent words like messaging, resonance, contextual relevance. Ji-eun waited, left hand still shaking now that she had stopped touching the page.

"Yes," Ina said. "Partly. Also as a ratings machine. Also as debt collateral. None of those cancel the others."

Ji-eun gave a short laugh with no humor in it. "Great. Multipurpose woman. Limited-time offer."

"I'm trying to keep the role survivable."

"Survivable for who."

"For you first."

Ji-eun leaned back on her palms. Her shoulder blades pressed into the mirror behind her and left two foggy half-moons in the condensation from her skin. "You know what they wrote into my rider last week? If the show enters political controversy index above threshold, I pay breach penalties for harm to brand partners. Me. Not the network. Not the men who invite retired ministers into writers' rooms."

Ina did know. She had seen the clause summary in a packet she was not supposed to have: callable debt, immediate liquidity seizure, training-cost acceleration. Numbers that ate years in one bite.

"How much is it?"

Ji-eun looked toward the door, lowered her voice further. "Three hundred twenty million won, plus campaign damages if sponsors bail. They call it recoupment. My mother calls it a second mortgage on a person."

Ina sat down across from her. The floor was still warm from dancers' bodies. "Your agency can negotiate if ratings hold."

"My agency sells my panic as professionalism. They negotiate lighting angles."

She lifted her left hand and watched it tremble. "This started last month, with that hand. They gave me magnesium. Then beta blockers. Then told makeup to hide it."

Ina took the pen from her own ear and placed it in Ji-eun's trembling hand without comment. Ji-eun squeezed until the tremor eased, anchored by pressure and stubbornness.

"I can push to keep the monologue coded," Ina said. "No overt terms. No line they can pin as intentional provocation."

Ji-eun's eyes sharpened. "And when everyone still reads it that way."

Ina thought of Nam's blistered heel, Kwon's cuff links, the council staffer's repaired elbow, the ad buyer voices on speaker telling grief where it could stand. Everybody in this machine had a way to pass cost down one level.

"I will stand in front of it with you," she said.

Ji-eun held the pen so hard her knuckles blanched. "That sounds like a promise."

"It's a promise."

Ina knew a promise did nothing unless it left a trail. She opened her notes app and typed in front of Ji-eun:

Get option clause in writing.
If they trigger debt, file it where they can't delete it.

Choi knocked once and opened the door without waiting. "Time is up."

Ji-eun did not move.

Choi's eyes flicked to the script pages. "We're keeping this to performance notes."

Ji-eun rose in one fluid motion, stage-trained spine clicking into place. Public brightness snapped on like a switch.

"Manager-nim, Writer Seo just gave me excellent performance notes. I'm very inspired to suffer beautifully on schedule."

Choi popped half an antacid into his mouth. "That tone won't help you."

"It helps me."

He turned to Ina. "Final approval chain: agency legal, network standards, producer sign-off. Not council. Please remember jurisdiction."

"I remember everyone's jurisdiction," Ina said.

Choi nodded once, not agreement, just acknowledgment of a shared trap. As Ji-eun walked to the floor marks for choreography, she slipped the pen Ina had given her into the elastic of her practice pants. It was a tiny theft to keep one thing chosen.

By six, Ina was back at MiraeWave in Edit Bay 3, where the air conditioner ran too cold and still could not beat the heat from stacked render machines. The walls were lined with black acoustic foam that absorbed voices and made every cough sound guilty. Assistant editor Park Sun-woo sat at keyboard station two, rubbing tiger balm into his wrists between shortcuts. He had a newborn at home, and milk-powder dust clung to his cuffs over old coffee stains.

"We got Standards round two," he said without turning. "They started with fourteen removals. Nam fought to nine."

He pushed a printed note sheet toward her. Red circles around lines, timestamps, shot IDs.

00:17:42 delete "a house split in half."
00:19:08 replace lullaby lyric word "returning."
00:20:31 remove map-shaped scar makeup close-up, too symbolic.
00:22:54 trim brother photo insert by 1.5 seconds.

Nam stormed in behind her, hair frizzing at the edges from humidity and stress.

"I bought you one survival gift," she said, dropping a takeout cup on Ina's desk. Iced Americano, no syrup. "Standards backed off deleting the whole rooftop monologue. But they want two line swaps and one shot trim."

"What is their rationale for the scar close-up," Ina asked.

"Too suggestive of territorial imagery."

"It's a burn mark on a prop arm."

"Everything's a map when they're scared."

The room's main monitor showed Ji-eun on rooftop set, city lights bokeh behind her. In raw take she hummed the lullaby before speaking, then swallowed hard, eyes bright but not wet. She looked controlled and human, dangerous exactly because she stayed controlled.

Nam tapped the screen. "Performance is strong. Maybe too strong. If this trends as political interpretation, advertisers panic. We need plausible deniability."

At seven-fifteen, Kwon called from a secure line patched through post supervisor desk. His voice came through compressed, slightly metallic, formal even through static.

"I have reviewed the near-lock cut."

Nam mouthed, speaker? Ina nodded and put him on room monitor audio. The nod cinched something under her ribs; once the room heard him, no one could pretend to mishear.

Kwon continued. "The emotional architecture holds. The ideological current thins by minute twenty."

The word thins landed like a tailor's blade, and he always chose tailoring verbs for cuts.

Nam folded her arms. "Thin's why we've still got a slot. No slot, no signal."

"The vector must survive first contact with passive viewers."

Ina leaned toward the mic. "It will. The lullaby cue lands at twelve, nineteen, and thirty-two minutes. The sibling artifact lands in all three acts. Viewers will map it even if they never say it out loud."

Kwon paused. "You removed the phrase about shared roof."

Ina pictured the tracked-change strike through that line, red and surgical.

"Standards cut it. We fought it -- no, we burned two hours fighting it -- and kept broadcast."

"You replaced it with kitchen language."

"With kitchen speech," Ina said. "How people actually talk when rent's due and someone's missing." Her own voice sounded flatter than she felt, which was usually the only way to keep from saying too much.

Silence again, then: "Proceed. I expect stronger layering in episode two."

The line clicked dead.

Nam exhaled through her teeth. "He just marked your homework."

Ina stared at the timestamp sheet. There were nine removals, nine tiny amputations, and maybe enough body left to walk.

"Run scene fourteen from top," she said to Sun-woo.

They watched take after take. In one, Ji-eun overplayed rage. In another, she went too soft and turned the scene into apology. Take six held the balance: contempt, shame, and a flicker of hope she looked embarrassed to reveal. Ina marked it.

At eight-forty, agency legal sent their own notes, written in precise bullets with no adjectives.

- Delete ad-libbed "I was sold with the furniture" line.
- Remove shot of contract folder in frame (logo visibility + legal implication).
- Confirm no explicit references to state institutions.

Nam slammed her palm on the desk. "Who let her ad-lib that line."

Ina did not answer. She had.

Sun-woo rotated in his chair. "If we cut it, we lose transition to close-up."

"Then rebuild transition from reaction shot," Nam said.

"It will look patched."

"We're patching."

By ten, everyone in Edit Bay 3 had stopped pretending this was about art. It was plumbing under pressure. Move this line so that this sponsor stays. Trim this frame so that this committee cannot flag intent. Keep enough wound visible that the whole episode does not collapse into pretty faces ordering delivery.

Ina sat with headphones clamped over both ears, scrubbing waveforms. She ducked the music under Ji-eun's breathing at 19:03 so the inhale became audible. She left one quarter-second of room tone after the lullaby hum before dialogue started, an empty pocket where memory could land. She cut every phrase Standards demanded and smuggled rhythm back in through pacing, prop placement, and where eyes moved before mouths did.

At ten-thirty-two, Nam stepped out to take a call and came back white around the mouth.

"Advertiser consortium wants a verbal assurance from me," she said. "Tonight. They're asking whether this episode contains subliminal political coding."

Sun-woo swiveled back to his monitors with impressive neutrality.

Ina kept her gaze on waveform peaks. "What'd you tell them."

"That it's a family romance drama about ordinary people under economic stress."

"That's true."

"It's also a dodge."

"A dodge keeps payroll from vanishing."

Nam crouched beside Ina's chair so they were eye level. Her voice dropped.

"If this blows, they cut you first because you're not on network payroll. They cut me second because I hired you. If we survive week one, I might -- might -- keep one of us standing in week two."

"Then keep you," Ina said.

Nam stared at her for a beat, then stood. "Don't make me pick out loud."

At eleven-fifteen, final QC run started. Audio check, subtitle sync check, logo blur check, legal phrase check. Sun-woo's fingers moved over keys like someone playing piano in a house with sleeping children.

Ina's phone buzzed. Ji-eun.

Can you talk.

Ina stepped into hallway, where the vending machine hummed louder than distant traffic.

"How did rehearsal go."

"They moved call time to five a.m. and added media training with a former anchor who says I need warmer vowels when discussing family pain."

"That sounds unbearable."

"It sounds billable."

A pause. Then, in a lower voice: "Manager Choi told me once this airs, my option year activates automatically. More episodes, more control, bigger penalty if I walk."

Ina leaned against cinderblock wall painted a yellow that made everyone look sick. "Did they give you paperwork."

"He didn't need to. He waved the tablet and read the number like weather."

"You can send me screenshots."

"He's not that stupid."

Another pause. Bass from dance studio downstairs thudded faintly through concrete.

Ji-eun said, "You meant it, right, about standing in front."

Ina closed her eyes and pressed her thumb into the cinderblock seam until grit bit her skin. The vending machine's drone and the rain-damp nylon at her cuffs made the hallway feel narrower.

"Yeah. I meant it."

"You hesitated."

"I was walking. I wasn't -- I wasn't dodging you."

Ji-eun gave a breath that might have been a laugh. "You're terrible at lying to actresses."

"Good."

"Stay terrible. If I hear polished comfort from you, I know we're fucked."

After the call, Ina stood another minute in the hallway, watching a moth beat itself against fluorescent light. It kept hitting the hot tube and dropping, then climbing the wall to try again.

Back in bay, QC flagged one issue: at 20:31, the trimmed scar close-up still held one frame where the shape looked unmistakably like the peninsula if you wanted it to.

Nam pointed at the frame counter. "Remove it."

Ina zoomed in. It was one frame, 1/24th of a second.

"If we remove, eye-line jumps."

"Then fix eye-line."

Sun-woo said quietly, "I can interpolate."

"Do it," Nam said.

Ina watched the frame vanish. The map was erased faster than a blink.

At 11:57 p.m., they exported delivery master, 4K broadcast package plus backup. File name: MIRAEWAVE_PILOT_EP01_FINAL_LOCK_v13. Everyone in the room knew v13 was not final. It was only the version that made midnight.

Nam signed digital approval with her stylus, then shoved the tablet at Ina. "Writer sign-off."

Ina signed.

The upload progress bar crawled to 100%. Nobody applauded, and nobody felt relief. Bodies just stood up too quickly and felt their knees complain.

It aired Thursday at nine.

Ina watched from the back of a nearly empty convenience store two blocks from her apartment because she could not stand being in a room full of colleagues during first transmission. The owner kept volume low by habit, so she listened close, shoulder against the refrigerated drinks case, cold seeping through her blouse.

By the first ad break, comments were splitting faster than captions could refresh.

Policy speech in lipstick.
Finally a romance where money hurts.
Border propaganda for housewives.
Coward script pretending history is private.

At 9:47, Nam called.

"Stop hiding in retail. Get back to Bay 3. Containment call in six minutes."

Ina left a basket with kimbap and cheap beer on the counter, ran through rain, and reached the studio with wet cuffs and lungs burning.

Nam had already patched four lines into the bay monitor: herself, network legal counsel Yoon Hae-jin, council policy liaison Oh Jun-seok, and Manager Choi from Haneul. Choi stood in a corridor under rehearsal lights, tablet in one hand, peppermint wrapper in the other.

Yoon went first. "Requirement one: we issue a neutrality clarification before midnight. Requirement two: all episode-two pages go through legal pre-clearance before producer circulation. Writer Seo, confirm."

Oh did not wait. "Council requirement is parallel and immediate. Policy liaison review precedes all producer circulation."

They were two demands aimed at the same choke point.

Choi lifted his tablet so the clause text filled his camera. "Agency notice. Option Trigger Clause 11.2 activates because pilot rating crossed contracted threshold at 21:44. Actor Park is now in mandatory option year. Any unsanctioned political statement or interpreted provocation triggers recoupment acceleration: three hundred twenty million won plus sponsor damages."

Ina leaned toward the mic. "Put Ji-eun on."

"Not necessary."

"Do it."

Choi turned the camera. Ji-eun sat on a tile floor outside a rehearsal bathroom, stage makeup half removed, one false lash still clinging at the corner. Her left hand shook so hard the water bottle cap rattled against the plastic neck. Off-screen, someone pounded the bathroom door and told her call time had moved to 5:00 a.m.

"I threw up twice," Ji-eun said, voice shredded. "He read clause eleven-two while my mom held my hair."

Choi stepped back into frame. "She is emotional. We still need script cooperation."

Ina hit record on her phone and held it next to the speaker. "This call is now documented. Send Clause 11.2 with guarantor language to me in writing. Ji-eun sees a doctor tonight, and call time moves."

Yoon's mouth tightened. "If you create an adversarial record, your account permissions will change during review."

"Then change them," Ina said. "I'm filing a performer-safety objection before midnight."

Sun-woo's station pinged behind her. A red banner snapped across her script portal:

OUTBOUND SHARING DISABLED: USER SEO_INA

Nam muttered a curse. Oh said, "Escalation is noted." Choi ended his feed without goodbye.

At 10:12, a message arrived from an unknown council number: Effective immediately, all subsequent drafts will be reviewed by policy liaison before producer circulation.

Two minutes later, a network email from legal: Due to elevated public sensitivity and pending internal review, writing submissions now require pre-clearance for socio-historical references prior to table read.

Two gates pointed in opposite directions, and both were attached to her keyboard.

Ina rode the elevator up to her apartment with rain in her shoes and her pulse still trying to outrun the call. She unlocked her door, dropped her bag on the counter, and opened the laptop before taking off her coat.

At 10:28, she filed the objection in the network compliance portal.

Subject: Performer Safety Objection - Park Ji-eun / Option Trigger Clause 11.2

She attached the call recording, quoted the debt number, listed the moved call time, and copied Nam, Yoon, agency legal, and her own off-network encrypted address so one server could not erase the paper trail alone.

She hit send.

Auto-reply hit in eighteen seconds: Case MW-COMP-2741 received.

Ina opened a new file called motif_map_ep2 and typed:

Lullaby appears in diegetic radio at minute 7.
Sibling thread intersects romance obligation scene.
No explicit geography.
No explicit doctrine.
Maximum emotional specificity.
Write escape hatch beats for Sun-a before sponsors ask for tears on cue.

She stopped typing and rubbed her left wrist where the scar pulled when she bent too far over keys. Her mother's handwriting flashed in her mind without invitation: narrow strokes, no flourishes, as if decorative loops were a waste when facts still needed room.

In the convenience-store glass she had watched her reflection split under passing headlights and scrolling comments. She tapped the cold case door once for each demand that kept following her home: Nam's deniability, Kwon's signal, agency-manageable tears, strangers asking for truth as long as it did not accuse them. Ji-eun's shaking left hand kept cutting through all of it.

Ina wanted, impossibly, for no one to be eaten alive by a story she wrote.

She deleted the last line in motif_map_ep2 and replaced it:

Protect actor breath in close-up.
Never write pain as ornament.
If forced to choose, choose the human face over the slogan.

At midnight thirty-one, Director Kwon called directly.

She answered on the second ring. "Director, it's Ina."

His voice was calm, almost warm. "Writer Seo. The episode has produced broad engagement."

"That is one way to say it."

"Public disagreement is not failure. Indifference is failure."

He paused. "Actor Park performed effectively. Her role must be stabilized. We cannot afford talent volatility."

The word stabilized sat between them, as if Ji-eun were scaffolding.

"What do you need from me."

"Episode two by sunrise. Increase coherence of the sibling line. Do not dilute under producer fear. Also do not trigger formal censure."
He paused long enough for her to hear paper move near the phone.
"Audit reach. Isolate high-performing payloads. Scale what travels. You understand the corridor."

"I understand the corridor."

"Good. Then remain in it."

The call ended.

Ina sat in kitchen light with laptop glow on her knuckles and the untouched beer sweating circles onto her mother's old table. She listened to rain and distant scooters and the building pipes knocking whenever someone upstairs flushed. She could feel scrutiny arriving from every direction: legal memos, policy liaison, ad partners, fan edits, manager tablets, men in suits who called coercion architecture and women in heels who called compromise survival. In the center sat a twenty-four-year-old actress with a shaking left hand and a new option year she did not choose.

Ina opened scene cards for episode two and started writing before the adrenaline dropped. If she waited for courage, she would miss deadline. If she missed deadline, someone else would write Ji-eun's mouth for her.

The first line she wrote for Sun-a was simple enough to pass any committee if read quickly:

I'm not good at saying what I miss.

Ina stared at it, cursed softly, and changed it.

I'm no good at saying what I miss.

Then she kept going.


# Chapter 5

Rainwater pooled where Studio C met the loading dock and bled into the hallway in thin tongues. Couriers had tracked enough mud across the threshold to make the linoleum shine. The analytics room behind the prop cage smelled of hot fans, cardboard glue, and the sweet chemical stink from wigs that had dried overnight on foam heads. Ina sat with her knees under a folding desk and watched campaign dashboards update in stuttering increments before nine.

The top line looked healthy: reach was up twenty-three percent in four weeks, rewatch loops were up in two border clusters, and fan-edit reposts were up enough to make the social team send fireworks emojis in the internal chat. Everything any executive sponsor wanted to see looked bright green.

She closed that tab and opened the raw export she had fought for since Monday. The sheet listed distribution IDs, shipment labels disguised as karaoke compilations and language drills, and device fingerprints from border-proximate handoffs. It was a broken spine of numbers that should have told a cleaner story than her body wanted to believe.

Kwon's instructions from the previous night were still pinned in her notes app: Audit reach. Isolate high-performing payloads. Scale what travels.

Nothing in that sentence asked what happened after arrival.

She had asked for Kang Minjae at 08:00 because no one else in the operation understood how those drives physically moved. At 08:47 he still was not there. At 08:51 her coffee had gone gray and cold. At 08:56 the door opened with a wet suction sound and he came in shaking rain from his hair like a dog that knew it was unwelcome.

He looked thinner than she remembered from the Seongdong meetings. The humid air had darkened his T-shirt where his backpack straps cut across his chest. A cheap pink elastic with two plastic strawberries sat around his right wrist, absurd against the calluses on his fingers; every few minutes he flicked it with his thumb as if checking whether it would break. She did not ask who had given it to him. She did not have to. No one in his line of work wore children's things for decoration.

"You wanted logs," he said, dropping into the chair across from her without taking off his damp jacket. "You got logs. Don't ask for route maps."

"I asked for source-level distribution and engagement," Ina said. "Not dashboard exports and not the cleaned deck your interns hand to sponsors."

"Those are source-level to you people."

"I can read your checksum columns." She turned the monitor so he could see what she had already done. "These are redacted route IDs and consolidated timestamps. Somebody rounded events into six-hour blocks. I need minute resolution."

Minjae stared at the screen, then at her, then reached for her coffee without asking and took a mouthful. He grimaced. "You drink this on purpose?"

"Give me the unrounded logs."

"You really know how to romance a contractor."

"I didn't invite you for romance."

He gave her a side look, almost a smile, gone before it formed. "No, I noticed."

He slid an external drive out of his jacket pocket. It was wrapped in anti-static plastic and labeled in black marker: TAX RECEIPTS 2024. He held it in his palm but did not put it down.

"Before this touches your machine," he said, "say it out loud. If this leaks, I was never here and you pulled it without authorization."

"You think I'm recording you?"

"I think everybody records everybody."

He nodded toward the smoke detector dome in the corner. The studio had converted old offices too fast; wires showed where acoustic foam peeled back, and someone had painted over the camera mount without removing it. Ina had never known if the thing was live. She had learned not to treat unknown hardware as decoration.

"Then we move," she said.

She unplugged her laptop, carried it and the stale coffee into the subtitle prep closet next door, and shut the door until the latch clicked. The closet held folding chairs, fake family-photo props, and three bins of period shoes for dramas set in the 1990s. Air barely moved. Rain hit the metal vent outside with the steady hiss of someone frying onions.

Minjae plugged in the drive and typed a password with two fingers, quick enough she could not track. A folder opened: invoices, device manifests, transit notes. Under that: a file called ROUTE_COST.

Ina looked at him.

"If I called it DETENTION_FEED," he said, "you'd tell me to stop editorializing."

"Who made this?"

"I did the merge. Source columns come from three brokers, one church relief channel, and a guy in Dandong who thinks spreadsheets are holy scripture."

"And you trust them?"

"I trust what repeats when nobody gets paid to repeat it."

He clicked open the sheet. There were names in Hangul she did not recognize, plus ages, district tags, pickup dates, reasons as recorded by family witnesses, and status updates when any had arrived. The reasons were short and ugly in the way translation never softened:

Watched southern serial in shared room after curfew.

Owned unauthorized storage device hidden in rice sack.

Repeated lyric phrase from actress scene.

Some rows had notes in a second color. One note read that a right hand had broken fingers. Another reported two front teeth removed in transit. A third said the detainee was released after a bribe and that her mother sold the freezer.

Ina felt the closet shrink by a foot.

"These are allegations," she said, and heard the defensive shape of the word as she used it. "Not adjudicated facts."

"Yeah," Minjae said. "Because we're definitely in a jurisdiction with a robust appeals process."

She let that pass. "What links this to our drops?"

"Column J." He pointed. "Route cluster and payload hash."

The hashes were partial, but long enough to match the campaign manifest when she ran a quick compare. She opened her own export, built a lookup, pasted. Green highlights propagated down the rows.

A cluster from June 12 lined up with a USB batch carrying the pilot episode she had rewritten in Chapter 4's final week, version v3_2_mixA. Another from June 21 mapped to an idol interview clip package with her subtitle timing marks embedded in frame transitions. A third matched the lullaby fragment she had argued to keep when producers cut overt language.

She kept testing for false positives. She shifted each series one day forward, then one day back. She mismatched route clusters on purpose, ran string-distance checks on witness descriptions, and cross-referenced payment receipts from route fees where she could.

The pattern held through every insult she threw at it.

"How many names are solid?" she asked.

"Solid solid?" Minjae leaned back until his chair hit a stack of shoe boxes and steadied himself with his heel. "Fifty-eight where I can tie route, timing, and content marker. Sixty-three if you're willing to accept two weak links and three family-only reports."

Fifty-eight.

She wrote the number on a paper prop script because her hands needed to make marks on something physical. Ink bled through the thin page and printed backward onto the fake dialogue about first love.

"Show me the weak links," she said.

He filtered rows. "This one might've been from a Chinese drama bundle in the same week. This one could be copied from a rumor thread. This one is probably true but I can't prove chain of custody unless you want me to burn a source that still has two kids alive."

He said it flat, as if discussing faulty hard drives. Under the table, his thumb kept catching the strawberry elastic and releasing it with a tiny snap.

Ina zoomed into one row until letters pixelated: female, nineteen, cooperative textile dormitory in Hyesan; reason listed as repeating a line from a southern episode at a work table; status marked transferred; note stating left ear torn by wire during questioning.

The line she had written was not political in any clean way. It was two siblings in a kitchen arguing about whether to save rice for winter or eat now, a domestic joke, and a rhythm she had stolen from an argument with her father when she was sixteen. On set it had looked harmless enough to survive censorship and still carry grief.

She touched the screen exactly where the torn-ear note sat and then pulled her hand back because the glass was warm and she hated that it was warm.

"You're sure about the line?" she asked.

"The witness quoted it with your subtitle typo."

"What typo?"

"You wrote 'steamed enough for ghosts' instead of 'steamed enough for guests.'"

She had done that at 3 a.m. on no sleep and fixed it in the main delivery, but one regional batch had gone out before correction. She remembered the argument with quality control, remembered saying nobody would notice one vowel in a border market nobody tracked in real time.

Someone had noticed.

Minjae watched her face and lowered his voice. "I didn't bring this to blow up your conscience for fun. I brought it because Kwon keeps asking for denser drops and higher emotional hooks. Higher hook means repeat viewing. Repeat viewing means neighbors notice. Neighbors notice and security gets quotas."

"Why didn't you report this through council channels?"

He laughed once, no humor in it. "I did. Last month. I got a lecture about macro pressure and historical patience and how data from authoritarian zones arrives noisy."

"From Kwon?"

"From everyone who needs stipend money to clear by Friday." He looked away toward the closet door. "Listen, I route hardware. I don't pretend I'm clean. But I need to know what we're actually doing before we pour fuel on it."

She scrolled farther. She saw older men, factory workers, one retired music teacher, and two schoolgirls listed under one household because they shared a borrowed laptop. She saw a teenage boy marked as released with visual impairment in one eye after custody. Those names had no reason to be inside her workday.

The closet had no chair for leaning. She sat on a box of plastic loafers and kept reading until the names stopped being data and became a corridor she could not walk back out of.

"There are route delays in these same weeks," she said, because she still needed process language to breathe. "Were delays caused by sweeps or weather?"

"Mostly sweeps. Monsoon gets blamed because it's convenient."

"I need route-level maps to model exposure probability."

"No."

"Minjae."

"No route maps on your device. If NIS raids this building, I'm not handing them my sister on a silver USB."

His voice had sharpened at the word sister and then gone careful again. He rubbed water from his eyebrow with the heel of his hand and left a smear of dust there.

Ina closed her laptop halfway and looked at him long enough to make him look back.

"Then give me what you can without burning her," she said.

"I already did."

"Not enough for intervention."

"Enough for honesty."

She hated that he was right and she hated more that rightness had become a smaller ambition than action.

"I'm taking this upstairs," she said.

"You take this to Kwon, he'll tell you history is made of acceptable losses."

"Let him say it with the names in front of him."

"He'll do better than that." Minjae ejected the drive and slipped it back into his pocket. "He'll thank you for rigor and ask how much money you need to optimize collateral." He stood, shoulders stiff from the low ceiling. "Don't hand him my source columns. Give him the summary table, keep the raw sheet with you, and if anybody asks who built it, tell them a ghost from Dandong."

"You're telling me to lie to the man who signs my payments."

"I'm telling you to decide which lie hurts fewer people."

The line landed and stayed.

He opened the closet door. Hot hallway air came in with printer toner and kimchi stew from the downstairs cafe. Before he stepped out, he looked back once, not at her face but at her left wrist where the old burn scar curved pale against damp skin.

"Eat something before you go talk to him," he said. "You argue better when you aren't shaking."

"I'm not shaking."

"You are now."

When he left, the room took a second to quiet, as if his presence had run on a separate current. Ina sat with the laptop closed and listened to rain hammer metal. She thought about calling Hyejin. She thought about sending Ji-eun a harmless text about tomorrow's table read just to hear a normal response. She did neither.

She reopened the file and built the summary herself.

She reduced rows to counts by district and week, added probability bands and confidence scores, and created a column for verified harm descriptions. She named it Operational Consequence because the council read blood faster when it arrived in office language. Then she opened a second sheet and named the columns what they were: person, route, payload, injury, household cost.

Route cluster C-17, payload hash v3_2_mixA: Ri Hana, nineteen, textile dormitory in Hyesan. She repeated Ina's subtitle typo at a work table; during questioning they threaded wire through the upper rim of her left ear and pulled until cartilage split. Her mother sold the freezer and borrowed against winter fuel to pay the release bribe.

Route cluster F-09, payload marker idol_interview_0621: Choe Minsu, seventeen, released with a crushed orbital socket in his right eye. His older sister left school and took double shifts at a laundry depot when his wages disappeared.

Route cluster D-04, payload marker lullaby_cut_B: Han Jisook, forty-one, detained three weeks and returned with two front teeth missing. Her husband pawned his welding mask and borrowed at market interest to cover transport payoffs.

She left mitigation blank because she still had not done a single thing that changed any of those rows.

By the time she reached Seongdong at dusk, the rain had thickened into a gray curtain that turned headlights into blurs. The print-shop safehouse sat above a shuttered signboard company, fourth floor, stairwell smelling of wet concrete and machine oil. She climbed with one hand on the rail because the power-saving bulbs left every landing dim.

The council room looked as it always did: blocked windows, stacked archival boxes, one long table pretending not to be a command center. The air held stale coffee and printer ink and the static heat of too many machines in too little space.

Kwon Taesik sat at the head of the table in a charcoal suit that had not wrinkled despite the weather. His silver hair was combed back with severe precision. Beside his notebook lay a navy pocket square folded into exact thirds; before meetings he habitually unfolded it, smoothed invisible creases with his thumb, and refolded it again, as if order could be manufactured by hand pressure alone. He did it now while reading the top page of her packet.

No one else had stayed. Either he had sent staff away for privacy or he had decided witnesses were unnecessary.

"You asked for a reach audit," Ina said, placing her laptop down but keeping the encrypted drive in her bag. "You have one."

Kwon glanced up. "Sit, Writer Seo. You look soaked."

"I'm fine."

"You are not fine. You are angry."

He said it mildly, almost with concern, and reached for the thermos near his elbow.

"Tea?"

"No."

She remained standing. He let the refusal pass and poured himself anyway, both hands on the cup because of a faint tremor at the wrist she had seen only twice before.

"Proceed," he said.

Ina opened the summary slide but did not project it. She wanted the paper in front of him where he could not minimize with a swipe.

"Cross-border distribution of our entertainment payloads correlates with named detentions in three northern districts," she said. "I can verify fifty-eight cases with route and content markers. I can verify timing windows within seventy-two hours for most clusters. The pattern is strongest on emotionally sticky content, especially repeatable dialogue lines."

Kwon scanned the first page, then the second. He did not interrupt. His eyes moved steadily, no visible reaction at the rows labeled injury and transfer.

"Your confidence intervals are conservative," he said at last.

"I rounded down where source fragility exists."

"Good practice."

"Director, this is not a quality-control note."

"Nothing in operations is ever only one thing." He set the pages down in a neat stack. "What is your recommendation?"

She had rehearsed three versions in the taxi. The polite one, the technical one, the one that said bodies without saying bodies. Under his gaze she abandoned all of them.

"Pause northbound entertainment drops for two weeks," she said. "Freeze high-hook content immediately. Run route-hygiene updates and strip traceable dialogue markers from active packages."

"No."

The answer came so quickly it felt prepared.

"You didn't ask why."

"I know why." Kwon folded his hands. "You believe pause reduces harm."

"I know it does."

"You know it may shift harm location. That is different."

She felt the heat rise in her face and was grateful for the rain-soaked hair that hid it.

"People are being detained for consuming the material we send," she said. "One woman lost part of her ear in questioning over a line from our pilot. A boy came out with one eye damaged. These are not abstract side effects."

Kwon inclined his head by a fraction. "I did not say they were abstract."

"Then act like they're real."

"They are real," he said. "So is the structure around them."

He stood and walked to the blocked window, where condensation had pooled on the metal frame. With his index finger he traced a short arc through the moisture and then wiped it on the pocket square, precise, controlled.

"When monsoon shuts the Hyesan-Musan road for two days," he said, still facing the wall, "rice doubles, guards skim every crossing, and families burn furniture for heat. Run our pause through that chain and it still lands on bodies."

"Don't turn them into a model."

"Then take tonight, not theory. If you pause now, route seven goes dark by Friday. Broker Han sells our handset roster to provincial security to clear his debt. Forty-three households on our remittance sheet miss transfers before month-end. You save several dozen this week and strand several hundred next quarter."

She hated his arithmetic because it always arrived dressed as inevitability.

"You're asking me to continue after seeing this," she said.

"I am asking you to graduate from symbolism." He turned back toward her. "You wanted measurable consequence. Here it is. Now you must choose whether you are a witness or an operator."

The room's server fan kicked to a higher pitch. Somewhere downstairs, the signboard press thudded once, twice, a mechanical heartbeat under the conversation.

Ina pulled the second packet from her bag, the one with case notes she had stripped down to initials and route marks. She spread pages across the table until names, ages, and injuries formed a paper map between them.

"Read line fourteen before you tell me about quarters," she said. "C-17, v3_2_mixA. Nineteen-year-old. Ear torn open with wire for repeating one subtitle line. Family sold their freezer to pay the bribe."

Kwon looked down. His jaw tightened once, then loosened.

"I have authorized emergency cash for families after fingernails were pulled and kneecaps broken," he said quietly. "I have not mistaken this for clean work."

"That is not a virtue."

"No. It is context."

He returned to his chair and opened a leather folder she had not seen when she entered. Inside were budget sheets, route projections, and a draft org chart.

"I anticipated this conversation," he said. "Not because I dismiss your findings. Because your findings were inevitable."

He slid a page toward her.

"Effective immediately, you will lead a risk-mitigation cell under Cultural Distribution."

She did not sit, but she looked.

Budget authorization: KRW 42,000,000 monthly discretionary.

Additional staffing: two data engineers, one field verifier.

Emergency line item: household support for detention-linked families via third-party channels.

Authority: revise payload density, retire compromised scripts, adjust release cadence by district.

Escalation requirement: pause requests require director sign-off.

It was everything she had asked for except the one thing she had actually asked: stop.

"You're expanding distribution," she said.

"I am expanding your control over harm."

"Under your throughput targets."

"Under reality." He tapped the emergency line item. "You wanted names attached to consequence. This gives you money to move for those names."

"Money doesn't regrow ears."

"No. But money pays surgeons, bribes transport guards, and keeps younger siblings fed when a wage earner vanishes." His tone remained calm, almost tender in its patience. "Moral purity is expensive, Ina. Usually other people pay for it."

He had shifted from policy to intimacy without raising his voice. She recognized the technique and still felt it work.

"If I refuse?" she asked.

"Then I assign this unit to someone with fewer objections." He folded his hands again. "And you return to script revisions, where your deniability can remain intact for exactly as long as events permit."

She heard the threat inside the courtesy. It was not direct punishment; it was replacement and the loss of the only leverage she had managed to pry open.

Rain beat harder against the wall, loud enough to blur the edges of silence. Ina sat down at last because standing felt theatrical and she did not have the energy for theater.

"What happens to current drops while this cell ramps?" she asked.

"They continue at planned cadence for one week," Kwon said. "After that, you may recommend district adjustments."

"One week keeps the same phrases in the same mouths. I need immediate kill-switch authority on any package with traceable phrasing."

"Conditional," he said. "Sign tonight, and you may retire flagged packages within your channels. Delay signature, and the switch remains with Operations."

"So mercy is a signing bonus now."

"Authority is not mercy. It is-"

"Don't package this for me." She heard her own voice turn rough. "I'm not doing blood math in cleaner fonts while kids come home missing teeth."

His eyes stayed on hers. "Then do the work that changes the math."

"Fine. I need raw route logs with minute resolution."

"No route maps."

"I didn't ask for maps. I asked for logs."

"You will receive pseudonymized raw logs. Courier identifiers remain sealed unless exposure risk requires release."

"I need permission to push off-book family support without preclearance when a detention is confirmed."

"Within the monthly cap, yes, with receipts to my office by dawn."

She knew a negotiated cage when she sat in one. Wider bars were still bars.

"And if intelligence pressure increases?" she asked. "If NIS starts sniffing around MiraeWave because our metadata footprint gets sloppy?"

"Then your compartmentalization plan activates." He paused. "If your improvisation draws attention, I freeze your unit and reassign authority."

The line hit a nerve she had kept numbed since chapter two. She thought of her mother's notebook in a rice bin, her own lies at dinner, and Ji-eun asking if a role was a trap, and all of it felt arranged in boxes labeled for later.

Kwon pushed a signature page and pen toward her.

"You are not signing an absolution," he said. "You are signing authority."

"That's the same document in this room."

"Often."

She read every line twice. It was an addendum to the existing secrecy contract with expanded operational scope, while personal liability clauses and external disclosure penalties remained unchanged. Her name sat in clean print at the top as if this were a promotion in any ordinary company.

When she reached the signature line, her hand hovered long enough for the pen tip to leave a dark dot on the page.

Ri Hana in Hyesan rose in her head: nineteen, repeating a joke Ina had written to make two fictional siblings sound like people who still teased each other through hunger.

Then she saw Minjae's thumb snap the strawberry elastic while he pretended the file was just another logistics problem.

With sudden ugly clarity, she saw what refusal bought her: a cleaner story about herself while someone else kept shipping anyway.

She signed.

Kwon did not smile. He capped the pen, folded the pocket square once more, and rose.

"You will have credential packets in the morning," he said. "Start with district triage and phrasing audits. Submit first mitigation report in forty-eight hours."

"I want the source tables preserved untouched."

"Preserve what you need."

"That's not what I asked."

"It is what I can grant."

She gathered her pages, including the names, and slid them into a waterproof folder. Kwon poured the last of his tea and drank it cold.

At the door, she turned back.

"When you call this throughput," she said, "what do you call the people who bleed to keep it moving?"

For the first time all evening, he looked tired.

"Necessary witnesses," he said.

She left before she said something that would have burned options she still needed.

The stairwell smelled like wet dust and old glue. On the second landing she set the folder on a radiator pipe, unlocked her phone, and opened the mitigation console that had appeared after her signature cleared.

Payload `v3_2_mixA` was still queued in tonight's northbound batch for clusters C-17 and D-04.

She selected EMERGENCY RETIRE.

A warning box filled the screen: `Propagation to active couriers begins on confirmation. Rollback unavailable after acknowledgment.`

Her thumb was slick with rain. She wiped it on her shirt and pressed confirm.

Thirty seconds later the phone rang.

"Writer Seo?" an on-call dispatcher said over engine noise. "I got your kill order. Two bikes are already at the Uijeongbu handoff. If I pull now, they eat the penalty and every broker asks who burned the run."

"Log it under my credential," Ina said. "Pull them anyway. Push family-support advance to both couriers out of my discretionary line."

Silence, then the dispatcher exhaled. "Understood. Route seven rerouted. Hash v3_2 is dead as of 19:42."

Outside, rain slapped the street hard enough to bounce. Delivery scooters hissed past, plastic ponchos snapping. Ina tasted blood where she had bitten the inside of her cheek without noticing.

She forwarded the reroute confirmation to the new mitigation inbox, sent Minjae a message asking for raw logs within the hour, and headed back toward Studio C before the crossing light turned.


# Chapter 6

By the next day, near noon, the studio floor smelled like wet dust and burnt coffee, the way it always did after one of the old wall units coughed itself through another summer morning. After a night of reroute calls and almost no sleep, Ina sat cross-legged on the vinyl chair with her laptop balanced on both knees, trimming a line that had already been argued six different ways. She had stopped hearing the dialogue as dialogue. It was only weight now, syllables dragged between legal risk and ratings risk and the quiet threat that a sentence could get somebody hurt three provinces away.

Yura from post-production leaned over the cubicle wall with a half-peeled boiled egg in one hand and her phone in the other. The frog pin on her lanyard had lost one plastic eye months ago, but she still rubbed it with her thumb when she was nervous.

"Did you hear that?" Yura asked.

Ina kept typing. "Hear what?"

"Lobby glass. Big hit. Might be loading gear, but it sounded like somebody kicked the door."

A bass thud rolled up through the floor before Ina could answer. That wasn't loading gear, and it wasn't a delivery cart either. It hit too hard, with too many feet driving it.

She set the laptop down and stood. Through the strip of uncovered window near the ceiling she could see two satellite vans parked crooked against the curb and three broadcast cameras swinging toward the entrance. The sky above the vans was that white monsoon glare that flattened shadows and made every chrome edge painful to look at.

Her phone buzzed with a message from an unknown number.

`Stay at your station. National Intelligence Service on site. Do not destroy records.`

Another buzz came before she finished reading.

`If you run, you will be detained immediately.`

Yura read Ina's face and dropped the egg back into the paper cup. "Shit. Is this tax?"

"No," Ina said, because lying would have sounded more suspicious than the truth she did not have time to explain.

The first officers came through the studio doors in navy windbreakers that still held rain dots on the shoulders. Behind them, two men in business shirts carried hard cases and barcode scanners. One of the officers read from a document without looking up.

"This warrant authorizes seizure of servers, endpoint devices, and physical scripts linked to unauthorized cross-border communications under national security provisions. Everyone stays where you are. Hands visible."

The floor went loud in broken ways: a dropped mug, someone cursing in the color room, the repetitive chirp of a phone alarm that nobody turned off. Ina watched one technician walk straight past the creative desks and kneel by the server rack near the sound booth with the confidence of someone working from a memorized floor plan. He popped the side panel, photographed each drive label, and started pulling trays into antistatic bags. His hands moved with careful boredom.

She heard herself say, "Those are licensed archive packs and subtitle assets. There's client data on those."

The technician did not look up. "Then your clients can contact counsel."

A woman with a clipped bob and a yellow legal pad approached Ina's desk. Her badge read `Captain Shin Mi-rae`. A silver ring had worn a pale groove into the skin on her left index finger, like she had taken it off in a hurry and not put it back.

"Seo Ina?" Captain Shin asked.

Ina nodded.

"Please unlock your machine and step away from the keyboard."

"I want counsel present before any interview."

"You can request counsel at intake. Unlock it now."

The request sounded polite enough that half the room might have mistaken it for optional. Ina placed her fingers on the keys. Ink stains still marked the side of her right hand from the marker she had used that morning to revise scene cards. She typed the passphrase, closed the lid halfway, and stepped back.

By then the hallway had filled with uniforms and two camera operators who had somehow crossed the building threshold despite the guards downstairs. Somebody from accounting started crying in the copier alcove. The sound came in short, angry bursts; she kept trying to clamp it down between breaths and kept failing.

Captain Shin glanced at the desk drawer and tapped it with the end of her pen. "Open it."

"It's office supplies."

"Open it."

Ina pulled the drawer out. Tape, charger cables, a packet of instant coffee, receipts, and beneath those, folded A4 pages with Kwon's signature block and her own name printed in both Korean and English. She had forgotten they were still there. She had meant to move them weeks ago and had told herself she would do it after the next deliverable, after the next meeting, after a night of full sleep.

Captain Shin slid on nitrile gloves before touching the pages. She unfolded the top sheet and held it at eye level.

"Confidential Service Agreement. Restoration Cultural Council. Monthly remuneration seven million won." Her eyes lifted to Ina. "This is yours?"

Ina's mouth tasted like dry paper. "That's old consulting language."

"Do not insult my intelligence in your first answer."

She handed the pages to the evidence clerk, who sealed them inside a transparent pouch and wrote the time in thick black strokes: `12:17`.

Around Ina, people pretended not to look while looking at nothing else. One writer she barely knew kept swiping his phone screen up and up, trying to outrun whatever headline was forming.

Ina reached for her own phone. Captain Shin put out a palm.

"Who are you contacting?"

"My lawyer."

"Name."

Ina gave a name she had never called.

Shin held out her hand. "Phone. You can request legal counsel through intake."

"I need to notify my father."

"Phone."

Ina passed it over. Shin turned it face down on the desk without checking the screen, which felt worse than if she had searched it on the spot.

Three desks over, a junior translator named Jisoo started saying, "I just do punctuation, I just do punctuation," like a prayer she had not believed in since childhood. An officer guided her into a chair with practiced gentleness and a hand hovering near her elbow but not touching.

When they moved Ina toward the elevator, cameras caught the hallway from both ends. She felt each click of shutter through the soles of her shoes, tiny kicks on tile. Someone shouted from the stairwell landing.

"Writer Seo, are you tied to a foreign influence network?"

"Are you under arrest?"

"Did you feed propaganda to entertainment channels?"

Captain Shin angled her body between Ina and the nearest lens. "No statements."

In the elevator mirror, Ina saw herself as the cameras had: damp hair curling at the neck, blouse wrinkled from the chair back, left wrist scar bright against the metal handrail. She was already marked. She wasn't convicted or charged, but she had been marked in public, where charges almost did not matter.

She watched floor numbers descend and thought of the green notebook wrapped in dish towels behind the loose panel under her kitchen sink. It wasn't in this office, and it wasn't in this raid. For now, that bought her a breath.

Outside, monsoon heat slapped her when the lobby doors opened. Diesel rattled in her sternum, tires hissed over wet asphalt, and fryer exhaust from the chicken shop pasted sweet grease onto her tongue. She was put in the back of a gray van with no visible logo. The bench seat had a tear that pinched the back of her thigh through thin fabric.

Captain Shin climbed in across from her and shut the door.

"Am I being arrested?" Ina asked.

"You are being transported for questioning under active warrant authority."

"That's not an answer."

"It is the answer you get right now."

The van pulled into traffic. Ina counted bridges without meaning to, an old commuting habit that had survived years of deadline panic. She watched scooters weave between lanes, watched a bus advertisement for a cosmetic surgery clinic slide past in pink script, watched the city continue like nothing had happened to her life in the last thirty minutes.

Her phone remained in Captain Shin's lap, facedown, silent. Whoever she might have called was already calling someone else. Kwon, probably, with that soft patient voice he used when declaring somebody expendable.

The intelligence building had no sign at street level, only mirrored windows and guards whose suits fit too well for private security. They took Ina through a side entrance where fluorescent glare bounced off waxed tile and the vents pushed coolant-cold air through her damp shirt. Her bag, laptop, and shoelaces went into plastic bins. Her bra underwire set off the scanner; the female guard wanded her chest and hips with bored efficiency while Ina stared at a poster about anonymous corruption hotlines.

At intake they rolled black ink across her fingertips one by one. Her right index finger left a darker print than the others because of the marker stain. The clerk noticed and gave a brief, tired smile.

"Writer," he said.

"Subtitle editor," Ina answered.

He stamped a form and did not smile again.

They put her in a holding room with no clock, two plastic chairs bolted to the floor, and a vent that breathed winter air into her damp blouse until her nipples ached. A paper cup sat upside down on the table. She flipped it over and found lipstick marks on the rim from whoever had been there before.

Minutes or an hour later, the door opened. A man in a government-issue navy suit entered carrying a file thick enough to make a point by itself. He introduced himself as Prosecutor Im Seung-ho from the national security division. His tie was perfectly centered, but one sleeve cuff had a coffee ring the shape of a coin. Behind him came Captain Shin with a recorder and another officer Ina had not seen before, broad-shouldered and balding with runner's calves visible above expensive loafers.

They sat opposite her. The recorder beeped.

Prosecutor Im folded his hands. "For the record, state your full name and current occupation."

"Seo Ina. Subtitle editor and script consultant."

"For which entities?"

"Multiple."

"List them."

"MiraeWave, freelance packages, sometimes direct producers."

He opened the top folder and slid a document across the table. Her signature stared back at her from the last page, sharp and stupid.

"Did you sign this agreement?"

"Yes."

"Say the entity name aloud."

She wanted to refuse on principle. Principle did not pay legal fees.

"Restoration Cultural Council," she said.

Im nodded, satisfied that she had finally stopped wasting his time.

"This agreement includes confidentiality obligations concerning cross-border media routing and historical narrative operations. It includes compensation beyond declared salary. It also references courier relationships. Do you deny any of that?"

"I deny espionage."

"That is not the same sentence."

The balding officer cracked his knuckles once, then placed both hands flat on the table, forcing stillness over them.

Im continued. "Under the National Security Act and related statutes, undeclared communication with actors facilitating prohibited Northbound transmission can be charged as espionage support, conspiracy, and material aid. Maximum exposure includes custodial sentence and financial seizure. Your bank accounts can be frozen today. Your father's lease guarantor can be audited before dinner."

He let the words sit. He did not raise his voice. He did not need to.

Ina pictured her father's cigarette tin in the kitchen drawer with five thousand-won notes folded inside for rent shortfalls. She pictured the hospital bill envelope still tucked under his microwave because neither of them could pretend it had been paid if she filed it away.

"You called this a questioning," she said. "You came in with punishment already drafted."

"We came in with leverage and options. Which one applies depends on you."

Captain Shin slid a tablet across the table and tapped play. Security footage from the studio showed Ina opening her drawer. Freeze frame: contract pages in her hand. Another clip showed her in a corridor with Kwon three weeks earlier, timestamped, no audio. A still image of a cash transfer ledger followed, with her name in the recipient field and monthly entries of `7,000,000`.

Im said, "Your financial pattern changed in week two of this contract. You began servicing large medical debt more consistently. You also paid down rent arrears linked to your father through intermediary transfers. That money did not come from subtitle residuals."

His tone made debt sound like character evidence.

Ina leaned back as far as the bolted chair allowed. Cold plastic pressed between her shoulder blades.

"I worked," she said. "I got paid. That's not illegal."

"The work may be illegal."

"Then charge me or stop pretending this is a negotiation."

A tiny flicker crossed Im's face, not anger exactly, more like approval that she had arrived where he wanted her.

"Fine," he said. "It is a negotiation."

He pushed a second folder toward her. This one was thinner, two pages clipped together with a yellow tab.

"Controlled Cooperation Memorandum," he said. "You provide prospective operational reporting on designated contacts, communications, and deliverables linked to this council structure. You do not leave the country. You do not delete records. You attend scheduled debriefings. In return, the prosecution recommendation remains suspended while compliance is active."

"Suspended is not immunity."

"Correct."

"So I confess into your file and hope you feel generous later."

"You cooperate and avoid immediate detention."
