The balding officer finally spoke, voice rough from either smoking or too many dawn runs. "If you are detained now, your father finds out from TV before you get a phone call."

There it was, the softest knife.

Ina rubbed the burn scar on her left wrist with her thumb until the skin flushed pink. She had gotten that scar at nineteen from a rice cooker lid and had hidden it under bracelets through her twenties. Today it looked like somebody else's timeline written onto her body.

"I want terms," she said.

Im's brows lifted. "You are not in a position to demand terms."

"Then lock me up and explain to your minister why this becomes a courtroom in election season. You don't want open filings naming media companies and intelligence handling in the same week." She leaned forward. "You want a quiet channel. So yes, I've got terms."

Captain Shin watched her with expressionless attention, pen ready.

Im made a small circling gesture with one finger. Continue.

"No compelled contact with my father. No approach to him through landlord or employer. If you need me, you call me." Ina held his gaze. "No fabricated tasking where you make me propose crimes to test loyalty. I report what is already happening around me. I don't perform entrapment theater for you."

The balding officer let out a short laugh. "You think this is a drama room."

"I think I've watched men like you script outcomes and call it inevitability."

Im cut in before the officer could answer. "Anything else?"

"Counsel at each formal statement. Written scope limited to council operations and direct channels. Nothing about my family, friends, or private relationships unless there's probable cause and a judge on record. And if you seize my personal devices, you isolate non-relevant client data."

Im tilted his head. "You are asking us to trust your boundary definitions while your own contracts hid half your life."

"I'm asking you not to burn the only witness you can still use."

Silence held for three breaths and the low hum of the vent. Somewhere down the corridor, a metal cart rattled and then stopped.

Im turned to Shin. "Addendum language. Limited familial non-contact, no proactive inducement without supervisory sign-off, counsel notice for recorded statements, relevance protocol for third-party data." He looked back at Ina. "Travel restriction remains. Device monitoring remains. Unscheduled summons remain."

"I expected that."

"You do not discuss this arrangement with unauthorized persons."

Ina almost laughed. "Who counts as authorized?"

"Us. Your lawyer."

"And when the council asks why I'm still outside a cell?"

Im closed the folder with precise fingers. "You are a writer. Be creative."

The anger hit hot and sudden, not clean. She saw herself throwing the recorder at his face and hearing the satisfying crack of plastic. Instead she pressed both palms against the table until the tendons in her hands stood out like cords.

"Before I sign," she said, "I want one call."

"To counsel," Im said.

"To Director Kwon. On speaker. Here."

Captain Shin looked at Im. Im considered, then nodded once.

Shin retrieved Ina's phone from an evidence pouch, unlocked it with Ina's passcode, and set it between them. Ina dialed Kwon's known number. It rang twice.

He answered with the smooth courtesy of a man who never rushed anyone unless he wanted them to feel rushed.

"Ina-ssi," he said. "Are you safe?"

Safe. She could have screamed.

"I'm at NIS intake," she said. "They seized the studio and contract pages. I need confirmation of legal support from the council."

A pause, short but unmistakable.

"I am very sorry," Kwon said. "You understand our institution has no formal legal presence. We are an advisory network. Any consulting agreements were individual and exploratory."

Im watched Ina without blinking.

"Exploratory?" Ina said. "You wired me seven million won a month for six weeks. You gave deliverables and deadlines."

"I offered patronage for cultural work. Nothing more. I cannot interfere in a national investigation." Kwon's voice warmed into the practiced tone he used to console younger colleagues after a bad review. "Cooperate where reasonable. Preserve your future. We will revisit collaboration once the weather changes."

The weather changes. He had used that phrase last week in the Seongdong print-shop, staring at Minjae's detention list like rainfall totals.

"You are leaving me out here," Ina said.

"I am preserving what remains possible." He lowered his voice. "Do not turn temporary pressure into permanent ruin."

The line clicked dead.

Nobody in the room moved for a second. Then Im held out a pen.

"Your legal support seems limited," he said.

Ina signed.

Her hand shook halfway through the surname, and she had to rewrite the final stroke. Captain Shin initialed each page, then slid a separate form under Ina's hand.

"Device compliance," Shin said. "We install monitoring software on your primary phone before release. If that phone goes dark for more than four hours without pre-clearance, it is a violation. You carry it powered on."

"I need it for work anyway," Ina said.

"Work has changed."

The balding officer stood and collected the folders. As he turned, Ina noticed neon green thread peeking from the heel of one sock where it had been darned by hand. Such a tiny domestic repair under the polish of a state uniform that it made her stomach twist.

They moved her to a different room to process the paperwork. The technician who installed the monitoring app wore cartoon dog stickers on his ID card and apologized before asking for her unlock code. The courtesy did nothing to clean the fingerprint grease from coercion.

He handed the phone back with a receipt.

"You'll get check-in messages from an encrypted channel," he said. "Do not ignore them."

"And if I do?"

"Then they come get you again."

A clerk returned her shoelaces in a clear bag and pointed to a bench. She sat to thread them through eyelets with fingers numbed by air conditioning. It took three tries to make a knot. The overhead lights buzzed loud enough to feel against her teeth.

When they finally released her, evening had already pushed the sky into dirty violet over the river. Rain had started again, thin needling rain that turned every streetlight into a white smear. Two camera crews waited outside the gate with her release timing down to the minute.

"Seo Ina!"

"Are you cooperating with investigators?"

"Did you take foreign money?"

"Are you tied to anti-state propaganda production?"

The first microphone hit her shoulder hard enough to spin her half a step. She did not speak. Captain Shin had vanished back through the security door before the first question landed, and nobody from the council was there to flank her, nobody from MiraeWave either. Just lenses, rain, and a freelancer with nicotine-yellow fingernails yelling for a quote like she was a vending machine that had finally opened.

Ina lowered her head and walked. The wet pavement sucked at her shoes. A scooter splashed gutter water over her calves at the curb, cold and greasy. She kept moving toward the subway entrance until the reporters peeled off to chase a ministerial sedan turning into the block.

Downstairs, fluorescent light flattened every face to the same tired color. Commuters pretended not to stare while staring anyway. Ina bought a transit card top-up from the machine and saw her own name trending on the news bar above the ticket gates.

`Writer Seo linked to covert influence probe.`

`Council source denies formal affiliation.`

The second line was the one that landed. Denies formal affiliation. A clean sentence designed to leave blood outside the grammar.

She boarded Line 6 and stood by the door because sitting felt like surrender. At Hapjeong a boy in a school blazer whispered her name to his friend and held up his phone screen with her intake photo already clipped and meme-captioned. Someone had added devil horns. Someone else had written `Script traitor` in neon font.

By the time she reached Mapo, her messages were a thicket of unknown numbers and silence from everyone who mattered. MiraeWave HR had sent one email: `Your building access is temporarily suspended pending internal review.` No call. No person. Just policy phrasing with rounded corners.

Her father called once and hung up before she answered. Then he sent a text in two lines:

`Saw TV.`
`Come by if you can.`

She typed `Later tonight` and did not send it. If she went to him now with cameras still sniffing, she would take heat to his door. If she did not go, he would drink and pretend he had not waited.

She exited onto a side street behind the convenience store where the owner kept broken umbrellas in a plastic bucket for anyone who asked. Brine from kimchi tubs slicked the alley concrete, and rain had turned abandoned cardboard into pulpy mats against the wall. Her shoulder bag strap had rubbed a raw line across her neck.

A voice came from the shadow beside the stairwell.

"You walk like somebody's counting your steps."

Minjae stepped into the light, hood up, rain darkening the seams of his jacket. He held two canned coffees and a folded black umbrella that looked too small for either of them. His left hand worried the paper label off one can in thin spirals, a habit she had seen before in server rooms when he was pretending not to be stressed.

Ina stopped three paces away. "How long have you been there?"

"Since before the first camera crew gave up." He handed her a can. "You looked worse on TV."

She snorted once despite herself. The can was cold enough to sting her palm.

"They put you on TV before processing even finished," Minjae said. "That means somebody wanted the image out fast."

"No shit."

"You tailed?"

"Probably."

He nodded toward the crosswalk where a dark sedan idled half a block away with wipers sweeping even though the rain had thinned. "Definitely. Two-man rotation, same car for forty minutes. Amateur by choice. They want you to see them."

Ina took a sip of coffee. Bitter, sweet, metallic from the can rim. "NIS offered me a choice between cooperation and prison theater."

"And?"

"And I'm standing in the rain drinking convenience-store sugar with you, so guess."

Minjae looked at her for a long second, jaw tight. "How deep?"

She shook her head. "Not here."

"You called Kwon?"

"In front of them. He called me exploratory patronage and wished me good weather."

Minjae swore softly, not at her, at the architecture of it.

"He'll feed your name to three factions by midnight," he said. "One to keep them calm, one to flush anyone still talking to you, one in case you survive and become useful again."

"You sound impressed."

"I sound like I've been billed by these people for years." He reached into his jacket and pulled out a cheap black burner wrapped in a pharmacy receipt. "Take this. Battery separate. Don't power it anywhere near your apartment or your father's place."

Ina stared at the phone. "You think I can still run routes after this?"

"I think you can still choose where the fire hits first." He tucked the burner into her coffee hand and closed her fingers around it. Calluses scraped her knuckles. "If they squeeze you hard, you need exits. I mapped one you can use and one you can't."

He unfolded the umbrella and used the metal tip to point at cracked pavement, diagramming routes on the concrete.

"Primary route: early freight bus from Seobu terminal to a warehouse stop outside Daejeon. Cash only, no ID scan if you board with produce workers." He tapped a second mark with the umbrella tip and shook his head. "Rejected route is KTX through Yongsan. Too many cameras and too clean a card trail. Your monitored phone burns you before the train clears Seoul."

Ina gripped the burner harder. "I'm not running tonight. If I disappear, they hit my father first. I stay visible until I can move him."

A delivery motorcycle roared through the alley and drowned them out for a moment. When it passed, rain ticked against the umbrella skin in a thin, regular pattern like fingers on a desk.

Ina slid the burner into the inside pocket of her bag, separate from her monitored phone. Double life in two rectangles of plastic.

"If I talk to you," she said, "you get pulled into this faster."

"Already in it." Minjae peeled the last strip of label from his can and let it stick to his thumb. "The question is whether you want a channel you can trust one percent or zero."

She almost said she did not trust him at all. What came out was different.

"I need one channel," she said.

"Then listen carefully." He lowered his voice. "Don't send me names by text. Don't repeat meeting addresses where your primary phone can hear it. If they make you wear audio, cough twice before you ask anything operational. I won't answer on first contact if you don't use the phrase we set."

"What phrase?"

"I'll send it from a clean line tomorrow."

"Tomorrow might be late."

"Then tonight you keep breathing and do nothing heroic."

He said it like an order, but his eyes cut briefly to the bruise forming on her shoulder where the microphone had hit and then away again, the closest he got to gentleness in public.

Ina leaned against the damp brick wall and closed her eyes for a second. Inside her head the day replayed in sharp clips: contract pouch sealed at 12:17, Kwon's voice saying `advisory network`, the pen dragging when her hand shook. She felt very awake and very far from her own body; she'd been cast to play herself and still had not learned the lines.

"They're going to ask for names," she said.

"Give them structure before you give them flesh," Minjae said. "Describe process, not people, until you can't. Buy time."

"Time for what?"

"For leverage to exist somewhere besides their file cabinet."

A window above them slid open and an elderly woman leaned out to shake a mop head into the alley. Gray water spattered near Minjae's shoes. She glanced at them, decided they were not worth a comment, and shut the window.

Ina laughed once, short and cracked. "That's Seoul's blessing, I guess."

"Could be worse. She could've recognized you and asked for a selfie."

"Give it ten minutes."

Minjae checked the street again. The sedan had moved closer, now parked beside a fire hydrant with hazards off. He flattened his empty can against the wall with his palm until the metal popped.

"I need to go first," he said. "If we split with me behind you, they'll tag me too soon."

"You think they haven't already?"

"I think they haven't decided my price yet." He pulled his hood tighter. "Ina. One more thing."

She waited.

"Whatever you signed in there, assume Kwon knows by morning. Maybe by now. He has ears in every office where coffee goes stale under patriotism posters." Minjae's mouth twitched without humor. "Don't confuse silence with safety."

She nodded.

"And don't go to your father tonight if cameras are still parked." He stepped backward toward the alley mouth. "Buy him groceries online so he stays in. Make it look routine."

Practical, unsentimental, exactly the kind of care he could afford to show.

"You're giving me domestic advice now?" she asked.

"I'm giving you survival advice with side dishes."

He turned and was gone before she could answer, swallowed by umbrellas on the main road.

Ina stood alone with the small black burner pressing against her ribs through the bag lining and the monitored phone vibrating every thirty seconds with notifications she did not open. Above the convenience store, the digital billboard switched from cosmetics to a scrolling news feed. Her name appeared again, then disappeared beneath weather alerts for heavy rain in northern provinces.

She took out her primary phone and saw the first encrypted check-in from the channel NIS had installed.

`Confirm location.`

Another message followed immediately.

`Tomorrow 08:00. Debrief Room 3B. Bring full contact list.`

There was no please. No threat either. Threat had already been delivered in person and entered into paperwork.

Ina typed back with thumbs still damp from rain.

`Received.`

She opened a grocery app and filled a cart for her father: instant rice, cuttlefish banchan he pretended not to like, nicotine gum he would mock and use anyway, low-sodium soup packets for the blood pressure he ignored. At checkout she hesitated over payment and chose the old card she had used before Kwon's money ever touched her account, because chronology might still matter to anyone auditing intent.

When the order confirmed, she started up the stairs to her apartment. Halfway to the landing she heard footsteps pause below and then continue after a deliberate count of three. Tail maintained.

Inside, she locked the door, turned off all lights, and stood in the dark kitchen listening to the refrigerator motor kick on and off. The loose panel under the sink waited where she had left it. She crouched, pulled it free, and touched the dish towels wrapped around the green notebook without unwrapping it. Cloth damp at the edge from trapped humidity, paper dry inside. Still there.

She replaced the panel and sat on the floor with her back against the cabinet. Rain drummed the window grille. Somewhere in the building a child practiced piano scales badly and stubbornly, repeating the same four notes until they held.

On the table above her, the monitored phone lit again.

`Reminder: disclose unauthorized contacts immediately.`

In her bag, powered off, the burner waited like a second pulse.

Ina closed her eyes and counted the space between raindrops hitting the metal sill, then the space between her own breaths, then the narrowing corridor of choices that still belonged to her. She had avoided the cell for one day. She had not protected anyone yet. She had not even protected herself, not really. But she had a channel, a lie she could keep alive for at least another morning, and enough fear left to stay precise.

Outside, tires hissed through standing water. Inside, the fridge motor clicked off and the apartment went very quiet, the kind of quiet that made every coming knock sound inevitable. Before midnight, Ina pocketed the burner, kept the monitored phone powered on, and stepped back into the rain.


# Chapter 7

Minjae's half-latched workshop door was visible from the alley mouth behind the radiator market, a strip of dark metal that meant hurry and also get out if anything looked wrong. Ina stayed under a dead pharmacy sign and watched the shuttered pawn window across the block until the reflected umbrellas stopped changing. Only then did she make a second lap and cut back in through rain hard enough to flatten cigarette butts into cracked concrete. Water spilled from broken gutters, hit rusted awnings, and came down again in colder drops that found the back of her neck and ran inside her shirt. The city smelled like wet asphalt, fryer oil, and hot wire insulation. Somewhere above her a compressor kicked on, coughed, and held a thin mechanical whine that made everything underneath it feel temporary.

She reached the workshop door and knocked once with her ring finger. He opened before the sound finished.

"You're late," he said. "Or you got tailed and took your time deciding whether I should die with you."

He wore a black sleeveless undershirt darkened with sweat across the chest. His left shoulder, where an old scar cut diagonally toward his collarbone, shone with flux smudges. He had a soldering iron in one hand and a paper cup of instant coffee in the other. It was nearly midnight and he was still standing like he'd just started his shift.

"If I got tailed, I wouldn't come to your door," Ina said.

"People always think that until they're scared enough." He shut the door, threw the dead bolt, and pointed at a plastic stool by the workbench. "Phone. Battery out."

She handed him the burner and popped the battery herself. He dropped both into separate foil pouches, then set them inside a dented stockpot under the sink. The workshop was one long room with no windows, stacked with routers, old set-top boxes, and cardboard cartons labeled as toaster ovens for customs. The single standing fan oscillated in a tired arc and pushed hot air from one end to the other. Every surface had the same thin grit of metal shavings and paper dust. When he moved, the calluses in his palms rasped against plastic housings.

He did not offer her coffee. He flipped a monitor toward her instead. A map of relay points glowed in green and red pixels, half of them crossed out.

"You wanted to talk about safer lanes," he said. "Talk."

Ina sat. The stool wobbled on one short leg, then found level on an old copy of a sports magazine shoved under it. She could feel the damp seam of her jeans against her thigh. "I need you to move one class of payload out of the entertainment stream. No mixed bundles. No names, no civic packets, no memorial videos piggybacking on drama files. We separate. We randomize dispatch windows. We stop teaching security teams where to look."

Minjae laughed once, dry and disbelieving. "That's your opening pitch? You walk in here a week after the raid and ask me to restructure a network on trust?"

"I'm not asking for trust first. I'm asking for mechanics first."

"Mechanics cost trust." He leaned both hands on the bench and looked at her like he could see the shape of every sentence she had rehearsed on the taxi ride. "Who're you reporting to now?"

The fan turned toward them and made her wet shirt cling to her shoulder blades. She had promised herself she would answer without spinning, without editorial notes, without the polished language she used when executives wanted conscience translated into legal risk. It still came out careful.

"NIS detained me for thirteen hours. They wanted live route keys, names, process notes, the whole production chain. I refused the live map and every real name. I gave them the April entertainment manifest instead: pickup timestamps, cropped plate stills from the Mapo loading bay camera, and two burner numbers we already retired."

"That wasn't the question."

"I know." She watched a drop of rain slide from her sleeve and darken the wood grain on the workbench. "I signed limited cooperation. Weekly check-in call. Monthly in-person debrief. If I miss twice, they can revoke immunity and reopen espionage charges."

He knocked his coffee cup against the bench hard enough to spill a dark ring across the map printout.

"So you're wired," he said.

"No."

"You are tracked."

"Probably."

"And you're in my shop."

"Yes."

He swore under his breath, then louder. "Fuck, Ina."

The soldering iron hissed where he set it down on a stand. Outside, a truck rolled through standing water and sent a slap of sound against the metal shutter.

"How much did you give them?" he asked.

"That April file and the old burner ending in seven-one," she said. "If they cross those timestamps with toll cams, they can pressure the two couriers who ran west-river pickups that month."

His jaw flexed once, hard.

"I gave them enough to keep my father out of asset seizure and keep me out of a cell. I did not give them current routes. I did not give them your name."

Minjae dragged a hand over his face and left a black streak on his cheekbone. "Do you hear what that sounds like to me? It sounds like you brought a camera crew into my apartment and promised they wouldn't film the bedroom."

"Then don't let me in your apartment," she said. "Keep this here. Keep it ugly and transactional. But we still need to reconfigure before another sweep reads our timing."

He gave her a look that was half anger and half reluctant respect for not pretending they were past it. "You want to split lanes. Fine. Which lanes do you think keep people alive?"

"Medical and household first. Language content second. Political content last and insulated."

"You say political like it's a file type. Over there everything's political if the wrong person sees it." He jabbed at the map. "This node outside Sinuiju used to pass cough syrup and pirated cartoons. Then one teenager added a song with a memorial subtitle track and three apartment blocks got searched."

Ina felt the old nausea from chapter five, that exact detonation of abstraction into bodies and doors kicked in before dawn. She put her palms flat on the bench to keep from rubbing at her left wrist scar. "That's why I need you. I can read patterns. You can change them."

"I can get people killed with a cleaner graph," he said.

"You're already getting people killed with the current one. So am I." She held his stare. "I'm done pretending intent matters more than outcomes."

He stopped mid-reach, muttered the cable colors he had been reciting under his breath, then yanked open a metal drawer hard enough to rattle every screw in it. He took out two packets of seaweed and a crushed carton of convenience-store kimbap, tossed one packet at her without looking.

"Eat," he said. "If we're going to burn our lives down, I don't want you fainting halfway through."

It was the nearest thing to care he had offered all night. Ina peeled the seaweed packet and tasted salt, sesame oil, and stale cardboard. Minjae opened his kimbap with his teeth and ate standing at the sink, shoulders squared toward the door the whole time.

"You know why I didn't answer your calls for three days?" he said finally.

"Because you thought I sold you out."

"Because I thought I might have to disappear before you did." He swallowed and looked at the wall instead of her. "My sister's route into Pyongyang is tied to two of these carriers. If one gets rolled up, she doesn't lose entertainment money. She loses medicine, access, and the broker's protection stamp."

He had never used the word sister with her before. Family came out of him in scraps, usually hidden inside sarcasm. Ina kept her voice level so he could keep going.

"What's her name?"

"Kang Suyon." He said it quickly, like if he slowed down he'd hear himself and stop. "Thirty-two. Works in a state textile line during the day, alters uniforms at night for cash. She keeps a blue tape measure tied around her wrist because she says she loses every other one. There's a kid in her apartment who wheezes through most winters. Inhalers don't come through official channels in her district unless you're related to the right colonel."

The fan turned again and blew the smell of solder toward them. Ina pictured a woman she had never met measuring sleeve lengths by dim light, counting breath sounds in a child she could not take to a clinic that admitted need.

"The broker knows she's mine," Minjae said. "He thinks that makes me reliable. It also means if I fuck up, punishment has an address."

Ina pressed her thumb into the corner of the seaweed packet until the foil edge bit skin. "I won't ask for your full map."

"Good, because you won't get it."

"I want one pilot lane. It stays quiet and small. No political payload, no memorial edits, nothing that'll get a kitchen turned inside out if a handset gets seized. We run it six weeks. If detentions jump, we burn it."

"Six weeks is spreadsheet weather. On my side, that's how you get me labeled a traitor by three different flags before lunch." He tossed the empty kimbap tray into a bin full of stripped cable jackets. "Two weeks gives me telemetry and maybe keeps my sister breathing. And nothing gets called safe. 'Safe' is how people stop checking."

She almost smiled despite herself. "Fine. Two weeks. Nobody labels anything safe."

Minjae came back to the bench and pulled a marker from behind his ear. On the glossy map he circled one inland transfer point and one northern handset relay, then crossed out two obvious alternatives so hard the marker squealed. His handwriting was cramped and impatient, every letter pitched forward, downstrokes gouged dark where his hand bore down.

"If this channel exists," he said, "it carries study apps, medical reference clips, and subtitled weather reports. It's boring on purpose. No martyr compilations. No coded anthems. No dead-parent propaganda and none of your clever edit games."

The dead-parent line landed where he meant it to. Ina accepted it without defense. "Agreed."

"And when your NIS handler asks why your contact map moved, what story do you feed him?"

"I say post-raid noise killed my visibility. That part's true."

"Half-true," he muttered.

"Half-true is the only currency either of us has right now."

He capped the marker and set it down with precise care. The rain hammered the shutter harder. In the pause after their bargaining, the room got smaller, defined by the fan's arc and the rectangle of light from the map display. Ina could hear his breathing shift from sharp to tired. Her own body registered the hour in annoying details: damp bra strap digging in, a cramp in her right foot from holding still, the cold edge of fear she had carried since the interview room now settling into her lower back like a weight belt.

"You should leave before three," Minjae said. "Cameras at the intersection cycle maintenance mode around then."

"I can stay till two forty."

"You always answer like a scheduler."

"It's either that or panic."

He looked at her for a long second. "I know."

A siren passed, distant and brief. He unplugged the map monitor and the room dropped into dim yellow from a single desk lamp. Without the screen light he looked less like a technician and more like a man who had not slept properly in months. There were grease shadows under his fingernails and a small burn blister at the base of his thumb. She wanted to ask when he last ate a meal that came in a bowl instead of a wrapper. She wanted to ask if anyone else knew about Suyon. She asked nothing.

"My place is three blocks," he said. "Workshop's hot and this fan sounds like a dying goat. If we're still talking, we talk there."

"Is that smarter?"

"No. It's quieter."

He locked the foil-pouched burner in a tool chest, turned off the lamp, and waited for her to decide whether this was tactical continuation or the beginning of a bad idea she had wanted since the first time he showed her detention metadata and watched her face instead of the screen.

Ina picked up her bag. "Three blocks," she said.

***

His building sat above a tire shop that closed at sundown but left one fluorescent tube buzzing all night in the stairwell. On the second-floor landing someone had taped a handwritten note to the wall asking tenants not to wash squid in the shared sink. The ink had run in the humidity until the word squid looked like a bruise.

Minjae's room was narrow enough that when he shut the door his elbow brushed the refrigerator and the coat rack at the same time. The fridge door was held closed with blue painter's tape. A cracked induction plate sat on a milk crate. Beside the mattress, a stack of old networking manuals propped up a fanless desktop tower with its side panel removed. He moved through the space without looking, clearing the fridge corner by a thumb's width and stepping over the power strip on muscle memory.

He set a clear glass bottle upright against the inside of the door. "If anyone opens it while we're asleep, this drops and shatters," he said.

"You sleep with alarms made of kitchen trash," Ina said.

"Kitchen trash has fewer firmware updates."

She laughed, surprised by the sound in her own throat. He handed her a faded T-shirt and pointed at the bathroom, which was mostly a showerhead above a floor drain and one narrow mirror fogged at the edges. When she stripped off her wet shirt, she saw the left-wrist burn scar pale against damp skin and thought of contract pages, court forms, signatures written to survive one week at a time.

When she came back out in his shirt and dry socks, he was standing by the windowless wall with two cups of barley tea and the expression of a man about to defuse a bomb he had wired himself.

"Before this goes where it looks like it's going," he said, "answer one thing straight. Are you here because you need the route, or because you want me?"

There was no useful answer that did not expose her. She sat on the edge of the mattress, cup warming both hands, and chose the one that was hardest to weaponize.

"Both," she said. "And I hate that it's both."

Minjae nodded once, the clipped motion he used when a checksum matched.

"Good," he said. "If you said one or the other, I'd call bullshit."

He sat across from her on the floor, back against the wall, long legs folded awkwardly. For a minute they drank tea and listened to rain tap the metal duct outside, irregular as typing. The city sounded farther away up here, reduced to scooter engines and a drunk couple arguing in the alley, their voices rising then dissolving into laughter.

Ina set her cup down. "I don't have clean hands in this."

"Nobody in this room does."

"No, listen." The careful clauses rose again and she pushed through them. "At the debrief they put photos on the table. My father outside his building. Your workshop from across the street. A frame from Ji-eun's rehearsal hall. The message wasn't subtle. Give us channels and we protect you. Refuse and we let the law do what the law can do."

"And what did you do?"

"I handed over one retired ledger: April pickup timestamps, two dead burner IDs, and cropped plate stills from the Mapo loading bay. Enough for them to pressure whoever drove those nights. Not enough to open your current lane."

Minjae's fingers dug into his shoulder scar until the skin around the knuckles blanched. "That works until they get hungry." His voice stayed low, not cruel, just exact. "You know that, right?"

"I do."

He rubbed the scar on his shoulder until the skin around it flushed pink. "Then don't ask me for forever. Ask me for the window we can survive."

"Two weeks," she said. "Then we reassess."

"Two weeks," he agreed.

She stood and reached for her damp jeans.

"Then I should go before your bottle alarm turns into evidence," she said.

Minjae stood too and put one hand on the doorknob. The glass bottle clicked softly against the wood. "If you stay, you carry my exposure into your next debrief. One slip and the broker pulls Suyon's protection stamp. After that she isn't a client. She's leverage."

Rain ticked down the pipe outside. Ina looked at the door and then back at him. "And if I leave?"

"I burn the pilot lane tonight and put everything back on old routes. People keep getting swept while we protect ourselves."

Her bag strap cut into her shoulder. She could leave with a clean procedural boundary and let that be the ethical pose she slept in. She set the jeans back on the mattress.

"I'm not clean either way," she said. "I stay. Two weeks, and I carry the risk with you."

Only then did she reach for his cup. Their fingers touched, and he caught her wrist with a grip that was firm but not trapping, his thumb tracing the raised edge of her old burn scar once, slow and deliberate.

"Ina," he said, quieter now, "if this is pity, don't."

"It's not pity."

"If it's fear, don't."

"It's fear," she said, and let him see it. "And it's you."

He exhaled like he had been holding his lungs for days. "Come here."

She moved from the mattress to the floor. He kissed her once, testing, then again with decision. His mouth tasted like barley tea and stale coffee. She could feel the roughness of solder nicks along his fingertips when he pushed her hair back from her face. The room was too warm and still too small, and the closeness made every sound larger: the fabric of his shirt sliding across her skin, the hum of the taped refrigerator motor, rainwater ticking in the downpipe.

"Tell me if you want me to stop," he said against her mouth.

"I won't go quiet if I do."

"Good."

She pulled his shirt over his head and saw the shoulder scar clearly for the first time, old tissue silvered and puckered where muscle had torn and healed wrong. She ran her fingers along it. He flinched once, then leaned into her hand. She kissed the scar and felt him shudder, not performative, not pretty, just involuntary and human. Her own body answered before thought caught up.

They undressed each other with the impatience of people who had been pretending not to want this through too many meetings, too many burner calls, too many sentences that ended before the dangerous word. He eased her back onto the mattress, then stopped, braced above her.

"Last check," he said, breath uneven. "You sure?"

"Yes. I'm sure."

He nodded and kissed her again, slower this time, giving her space to change her mind and reading her when she did not. When he moved inside her she grabbed his forearm hard enough to leave half-moon marks in skin already marked by work. The first thrust hurt, then the hurt opened and changed shape. She wrapped one leg around his hip and pulled him closer. The mattress springs complained with every movement, loud enough that he laughed once under his breath and turned his face into her neck to keep from making a sound that would carry through the wall.

"Thin walls," he murmured.

"I noticed."

He slipped his hand over her mouth when she got loud and she bit his palm lightly, warning, and he shifted so she could breathe and still keep quiet. The compromise made them both laugh, then made them both shakier. She felt sweat gather at his spine, felt the callus line across his thumb drag and catch against her thigh, felt the room tip from negotiation into something less orderly and more honest. Outside, somebody in the hallway coughed three times and turned on a faucet. Inside, Ina lost count of everything except the pressure of his body and the way he said her name like it was both apology and demand.

When he came, his forehead dropped to her shoulder and he stayed there, breathing hard against her skin. She kept one hand on the back of his neck until his weight shifted from urgent to heavy and trusted. Then he rolled onto his back and pulled her with him, her cheek against his chest, both of them listening to the rain slow from hammering to a steady hiss.

For a while neither spoke. Minjae sat up first, checked that the bottle alarm still leaned against the door, then wiped his face on the sheet like he could scrub off what he had just risked.

Ina stared at the water stain on the ceiling and bit the inside of her cheek to keep from asking the one question she could not survive hearing: if NIS dragged her back in before morning, would he shut the lane and save his sister by letting her drown alone.

Ina traced the edge of his collarbone and said, "I haven't slept through a night since the raid."

"I sleep in forty-minute pieces," he said. "Any longer and I dream in logs."

"Dream in logs?"

"Packet loss, retries, checksum errors. My subconscious is a cheap server rack."

She smiled into his skin. "That's bleak even for you."

"You haven't heard my top ten bleak list." He hesitated. "Suyon calls every Thursday at exactly six fourteen, if she can. Three rings means she's fine. One ring means money problem. No call means I don't eat for two days because my stomach decides we're done." He rubbed his eyes with the heel of his hand. "Tonight she did one ring."

Ina lifted herself onto an elbow. "How much?"

"Not huge. Bribe spike after a local inspection. Equivalent of maybe six hundred US through three hands. Big enough to hurt. Small enough to feel impossible when channels are unstable."

"I can cover some."

"No." His answer came fast, then softened. "No, not because pride. Because paper trails. If your accounts move toward my broker and NIS is bored that week, we're both dead and she still goes without inhalers."

"Right."

"You can help by not blowing up the lane we just negotiated."

She lay back down. The ceiling above them had a long water stain that looked like a river delta. She thought about her own numbers waiting at home: fifty-eight million won to St. Mary's billing office, eleven-point-two million in her father's rent arrears, and a stipend that could vanish the minute she stopped being useful to men in suits. Every month she translated grief for television and called it work. Every month she paid people to postpone punishment.

"When I was twenty-six," she said, "I thought debt was monsoon weather. You'd complain, buy better shoes, and wait it out."

Minjae made a tired sound that might have been a laugh. "And now?"

"Now it feels like architecture. It's load-bearing."

"Yeah," he said. "It's building code written in shame."

He turned onto his side and reached toward the stack of manuals by the wall. From between two books he pulled a flat black envelope the size of a passport. He held it for a second without handing it over.

"That's as far as I can go tonight," he said. "Past this, I'm spending blood."

Ina sat up. "What is it?"

"Limited route key. It's one lane only. It expires fourteen days from first use. You get entry-node windows, payload size caps, and sanitation rules. You don't get terminal contacts, broker names, or my fallback ladders." He looked at her until she met him squarely. "If this leaks, the broker flags Suyon's block, node-three couriers get dragged into questioning before sunrise, and I know exactly whose hand leaked it."

"If NIS catches this in my bag," Ina said, "they'll call it undeclared foreign-channel material and tear up the immunity paper."

"Right," he said. "So this costs both of us before breakfast."

He placed the envelope in her hand. It was warm from where he'd kept it against the books, and heavier than paper. Inside she felt the stiff edge of a microSD case and a folded card.

"What's on the card?" she asked.

"Authentication phrase and a dead-man condition. If the phrase changes without warning, the lane is burned and everybody aborts." He paused. "The phrase rotates every forty-eight hours. I send updates voice-only. I don't send text updates."

This was not trust. It was engineered vulnerability, constrained and measurable. It was also more than she had expected to get before dawn.

"Why now?" she asked.

"Because if I wait till morning I'll probably decide you're too risky and go back to old routes out of habit." He rubbed the back of his neck, embarrassed by his own honesty. "And because I just let you see where I hide things. That's either stupid or commitment. Maybe both."

Ina slid the envelope under the folded T-shirt on the milk crate, away from condensation dripping from the taped fridge door. She wanted to promise she would never use this against him, never weaponize what he had just given her with his body still warm beside her. She knew better than to promise forever in a room where both of them survived by revision.

"I'll run clean on this lane," she said. "No hidden payloads. No side deals. If pressure changes, I tell you before I move."

"And NIS?"

"Gets nothing about this key." She held his gaze. "That's the line."

He watched her face for the tiny delays that meant spin. Whatever he saw, he accepted for now.

"Then we're aligned for fourteen days," he said. "After that, alignment expires."

She nodded. "Fourteen days."

He reached for her again, less urgent this time, his hand settling at the curve of her waist with a familiarity that had not existed three hours ago. It frightened her more than the envelope did. Data leaks could be patched. Account freezes could be challenged. This was different: a man she could hurt and who could hurt her, both of them newly equipped with reasons not to.

At four forty-five, his cheap alarm buzzed from under the pillow with the tinny melody of a children's song. He groaned, slapped it silent, and sat up to start coffee in a dented steel pot. The room smelled immediately of burnt grounds and wet cotton. Ina dressed in yesterday's damp jeans, tucked the black envelope into the lining pocket of her bag, and watched him from the door as he poured coffee into two chipped mugs that did not match.

"You'll take the river road out," he said. "Less camera density before six."

"You memorized camera density?"

"I memorize what keeps people alive."

She took the mug he offered. His fingers brushed hers, then withdrew. Outside the rain had thinned to mist. Somewhere below, the tire-shop owner arrived early and started singing an old trot song while dragging metal racks onto the sidewalk, badly off-key and cheerfully committed.

Ina drank half the coffee, bitter enough to wake pain, and set the mug down.

"Minjae."

"Yeah?"

"If I get pulled in again, I won't let them surprise you. You'll hear it from me first."

"Do that," he said. "And Ina?"

"What?"

"Don't romanticize this. We're not healed. We're useful to each other and maybe something else on top. Keep both truths in frame."

She almost told him he sounded like a producer giving notes. Instead she nodded because he was right.

When she stepped into the stairwell, the fluorescent light flickered and steadied. She could feel the envelope's edge through the bag lining with every step down. On the landing, the squid note had peeled at one corner and curled like a tongue. She flattened it with her palm without thinking, then kept moving.

At street level she turned toward the river, hood up, pace even. Dawn pressed a pale strip of color behind the warehouse roofs. Her phone battery was still in the foil pouch three blocks away. Her body carried the ache of a night that had not solved anything and had still changed everything.

Fourteen days, she thought. Two weeks of borrowed alignment, two weeks to make a safer channel real before fear or law or love turned back into leverage.

By noon she'd be back under MiraeWave fluorescents, pretending this morning had left no mark she couldn't hide.

She walked faster.


# Chapter 8

The first export she pulled after lunch came back with holes where the useful fields should have been. Ina sat at her editing desk with her shoes off, one heel tucked under her thigh, the other foot on the metal rung of the chair because the floor held the damp. Rain had been falling since noon and had turned the loading dock behind MiraeWave into a shallow mirror full of cigarette butts, noodle lids, and the red blink of a delivery bike's taillight. The office AC had gone from cold to wet-cold. Her sleeves stuck to her forearms. Every time she moved her left wrist, the old burn scar dragged against the cuff.

The campaign dashboard had stopped giving her full exports three days ago. The fields she needed kept vanishing: location precision, detention confirmation tags, post-drop incident reports. Someone had narrowed her permissions and called it a "security tidy-up." Kwon's phrase, passed down through two assistants and an operations memo in polite font.

She opened three windows anyway: old script revision logs, message traffic from route coordinators, and the spreadsheet Minjae had once called the graveyard because that was where names went to be turned into percentages. She copied each usable line into a new file she titled `L-08-R`. Then she moved it to an encrypted drive and gave the visible folder an even dumber name: `PPL_Snack_Brand_Alt_Versions`.

At first she used the campaign's own categories because habit was faster than conscience.

`Exposure cluster.`

`Audience conversion.`

`Negative externality.`

By the fourth row she had changed the headers herself.

`Name.`

`Date.`

`Harm.`

`Who approved.`

The first name sat on the screen for a long minute before she could type anything after it.

`Ryu Jin-ho. 2027-06-29. Taken at Hoeryong checkpoint after USB lot M-SIBLING-04 surfaced in school dorm raid. Wife in Guro sold wedding ring to pay broker inquiry.`

Her fingers found a grim rhythm after that, hard enough on the keys to jolt her wrist at each period. She logged the proof chain in one blunt line, because if she got sloppy she would lose more than data.

`Source: route log 5C / Minjae cross-check / two-call confirmation from defectors' church relay.`

Then she kept going.

`Han Mi-ran. 2027-07-01. Brother detained in Chongjin media sweep. Family receives ration cuts after neighborhood denunciation meeting.`

`Pak Jun-seo. 2027-07-03. Courier missing after Dandong transfer disruption. Mother's dialysis appointment canceled; card declined.`

The cursor blinked after each period like it wanted permission to move on without her.

Her phone lit with an unknown number, then a text from Ji-eun with no greeting.

`You near your car? I can't come upstairs.`

Ina looked at the wall clock. 9:17 p.m. Ji-eun's rehearsal block ended at nine unless the agency manager kept her for corrective rounds, which happened whenever a camera had caught her frown or she had failed to look "grateful" in behind-the-scenes footage.

Ina typed back.

`Back dock in five. Black umbrella.`

She saved the file, copied it to a cheap USB, then printed two pages and folded them into the inner pocket of her laptop bag. Paper still felt primitive, but nobody remotely wiped paper.

The rain had thinned to mist by the time she got outside. Ji-eun stood under the awning beside a stack of prop crates, hood up, rehearsal bag clamped against her ribs. She had peeled her stage makeup off except for a streak of glitter caught near one eyebrow. Athletic tape wrapped her left ring finger to the knuckle. She kept flexing it, not because it hurt but because she checked it the way other people checked for their keys.

"You look like someone broke into your sleep," Ji-eun said.

"That was this morning. What happened?"

Ji-eun glanced toward the security camera and shifted so it caught only the side of her face. "Manager's with the boys' unit downstairs. We've got nine minutes, maybe ten if he gets stuck listening to himself." She unzipped the rehearsal bag and pulled out a plastic script binder. Sheet music on top, choreography notes beneath, then an envelope flattened between two pages of ad copy.

"Don't unfold this in a lobby," she said, and pressed the envelope into Ina's hand. "And don't send me a thank-you text. Just never mention it on anything plugged in."

Ina opened the flap anyway, shielding it from the wet with her body. Photocopies. Agency letterhead. Signature blocks. The kind of pages lawyers called routine and artists called handcuffs.

Clause 11.4: performer agrees to medically directed stabilization protocols before any appearance where reputational risk is elevated.

Clause 15.2: all reputational losses, including civic disturbance tied to performer imagery, may be charged against trainee recovery account.

Clause 18.9: unauthorized refusal of script, wardrobe, or public statement triggers immediate debt acceleration.

The number beside debt acceleration was blacked out on one page and visible on another.

KRW 320,000,000.

Ji-eun watched her read. "They keep the clean version in the onboarding packet. This is the rider they make you initial at two in the morning after your first brand shoot when you haven't eaten since lunch and they tell you everyone signs and don't be dramatic." She rubbed rain from her upper lip with the back of her hand. "I signed mine with a fake nail half off because they wouldn't let me go home till I did."

"How'd you get these?"

"A legal assistant who hates them and likes me on Tuesdays." A quick smile, mean and tired. "I bring her kimbap with too much danmuji. That's her thing. She says the yellow makes her feel like she still has choices."

There it was, the detail Ji-eun always offered when she was about to say something she could not afford to feel.

She tapped the page with her taped finger. "If they panic after the stadium event, they can medicate me, script me, then bill me for the panic they engineered. It isn't just bad management, Ina. It's architecture." She swallowed and looked at the rainwater collecting in the dented top of a road case. "I can survive being used. I can't survive being used and invoiced for it."

Ina slid the pages back into the envelope. "I won't waste this."

"Don't make that your noble voice." Ji-eun adjusted the hood and hitched her bag higher. "Make it your ugly voice. The one that actually does things."

Footsteps sounded from inside the dock door. Ji-eun stepped backward, face already shifting toward the bright, harmless expression she used for staff with clipboards.

"They're calling me Eun-Ji now," she said, rolling her eyes once before the mask settled. "New numerology consultant. New luck. If you ever hear me answer to it without flinching, drag me out by the hair."

Then she was gone, swallowed by fluorescent hallway light and the slap of staff shoes on linoleum.

Ina sat in her car with the engine off and read the clauses again until the paper edges softened from her fingers. She pictured the rider sitting beside her own secrecy contract in different filing cabinets, same logic. Obey, absorb, disappear.

Back upstairs, she built a second tab inside the hidden file and labeled it `Coercion architecture`. She typed the clauses as full sentences this time: agency rider authorizes sedation protocols, debt acceleration makes KRW 320 million callable the minute she refused a script, liability transfer language mirrors Council indemnity draft v3.

That last line made her stop. The wording was almost identical to the paragraph Kwon had added to her own contract after chapter four revisions: operational volatility shall not constitute grounds for compensation claims. She had signed because she needed the hospital debt to stop chewing through her father's sleep.

She logged source links, scan IDs, who had custody, where copies lived. She printed the first ten pages and numbered them by hand in the upper-right corner like her mother used to number interview transcripts: 8-1, 8-2, 8-3.

At 1:12 a.m. she closed the office blinds, unplugged the printer, and tucked the pages into an old accounting ledger Aunt Hyejin had given her years ago when she briefly tried freelancing and thought she might become the kind of adult who tracked invoices with dignity. The cover still read `SEO HYEJIN / CHURCH KITCHEN 2018` in blue marker and had two grease shadows that never came out.

Ina slid the ledger into her backpack between a spare blouse and a rain shell. She did not trust desk drawers. She trusted what she carried.

By morning, buses hissed through standing water and damp heat stuck to her skin before she even reached the curb. She took a taxi to Seongdong because the subway was stalled from flooding near Yaksu, and because she needed ten quiet minutes before seeing Kwon's face. She spent seven of those minutes drafting a restitution proposal on her phone and three deleting every sentence that sounded like a petition.

The print-shop safehouse had the same blocked windows and same fake calendar of mountain scenery as always, but someone had replaced the old fan by the server rack. The new one whined at a pitch that made her molars ache. Kwon stood at the head of the conference table in a charcoal suit, silver hair combed straight back, no tie pin today. A plain white pocket square, folded with mathematical contempt.

He nodded when she came in, nothing more. Around the table sat operations leads, legal staff, two young data analysts who still looked startled whenever Kwon used their names.

Ina waited for the slide deck to reach the budget page, then she spoke before she could talk herself out of it.

"I need five minutes for a separate item. Casualty-linked households."

A pause. Somebody stopped typing.

Kwon rested one hand on the table. "Define casualty-linked."

"People harmed directly after our distributions. Detentions, disappearances, utilities cut, cards shut off. We've got enough verified cases to justify a relief line. Small, controlled, routed through existing civic channels." She clicked her tablet. The monitor changed from engagement curves to a table of names and dates. Not all of them. Only five. Enough to stay specific, not enough to burn sources.

The room got quieter than silence, that office silence where you could hear power bricks warming under desks.

"This isn't sentiment," Ina said. "It's risk containment, yes, but no, that's your word for it. It's a wife selling her wedding ring and a mother losing her dialysis slot. If we don't build restitution now, these families become leverage for anyone who wants to force testimony later."

Kwon looked at the screen for a long moment. His expression did not change. Then he turned to the table and asked, almost conversationally, "Did anyone here authorize this list for meeting circulation?"

No one answered.

He looked back at Ina. "You have moved from script strategy into unsanctioned incident accounting. That is an internal violation."

"Then sanction it," she said. "Or tell me on record that we're keeping names only to optimize damage curves."

One of the younger analysts stared down so hard at his notebook that his pen tore the page.

Kwon stepped closer to the monitor. "You are mistaking an operation for a charity office." He tapped the first row of her table with his knuckle. "Do you know what happens in national transitions, Ms. Seo? People are displaced. Families lose income. Men are detained. Women are coerced. This is not novel. This is history at work." His voice stayed level, almost gentle, which made the words land harder. "Your duty is to reduce structural harm over decades. Not to perform moral theater over individual tragedies in quarter three."

Ina felt heat rise under her damp collar. "They're not theater. They have names, rent notices, and clinic deadlines."

"Everything has names." Kwon folded his hands. "A famine has names. A war has names. A policy has names if one is patient enough." A small smile, no warmth. "If we pause every time grief is legible, we guarantee permanent partition and infinite grief."

She heard herself breathing and hated that he could probably hear it too.

"I'm not asking for a pause," she said. "I'm asking for compensation."

Kwon glanced to legal. "Counsel, read the relevant clause from operational indemnity."

The lawyer beside him, Mr. Bae, adjusted his wire-rim glasses and read from a tablet without looking up. "Operational volatility and downstream civic consequence do not create compensable liability for Council entities, contractors, or affiliates." His voice had the dead texture of someone reading airport safety cards.

Kwon held out his palm to Ina as if inviting a dance. "There. On record. Are we complete?"

She did not move.

He let the silence stretch until it became a spectacle. Then he addressed the room, not her.

"For clarity: Ms. Seo's current stress appears to be impairing role boundaries. Effective today, all external incident data requests from narrative staff require my office approval."

He turned back to Ina. "You were hired because you can build language that persuades strangers. You were not hired to count bodies you cannot verify." Another small smile. "If you need pastoral work, your aunt runs a church kitchen. I recommend volunteering on weekends."

The analysts did not look up. Somebody coughed and pretended it was the fan.

It took effort not to throw the tablet. She closed the file instead and sat down. Kwon resumed the meeting as if nothing had happened, asking for stadium sentiment targets and opposition disinformation maps. Each metric came with neat verbs: amplify, suppress, redirect. By the time they broke, Ina's jaw hurt from holding still.

In the bathroom down the hall she locked herself in a stall and opened her notes app with wet hands.

`Humiliation tactic: public role correction + class reference (aunt/church) + access lock.`

`Decision: formal channel closed.`

She stared at that last line until it stopped looking like language and started looking like instruction.

Outside, rain returned in fat diagonal drops that bounced off the curb and soaked the hem of her trousers in thirty seconds. She took no umbrella this time. She walked to the bus stop, boarded without checking route number, rode four stops too far, got off by a hardware market, and stood under a plastic awning that rattled under runoff while she called Hyejin.

Aunt Hyejin answered on the third ring with the clang of pots behind her. "If this is about dinner, there is no dinner. We fed fifty-seven and a pastor who eats like two people."

"It's not dinner. Are you at church?"

"Kitchen, yes. Why do you sound like your teeth are cold?"

"Because they're cold. I need to see you."

A beat. "Come in the back. Front hall is full of men who think prayer exempts them from dish duty."

The kitchen was humid and loud, steam fogging the narrow windows. Hyejin wore the same faded apron with tiny pears on it and had three pens clipped to the neckline, red, black, and green, because she color-coded food costs even for donated cabbage. Her reading glasses hung on a beaded chain that clicked softly each time she turned her head. She was sealing leftover kimchi in reused yogurt tubs, each one labeled with date and sodium warning in thick marker.

She took one look at Ina's soaked blouse and set a dry towel on the prep table without comment.

"Talk," she said.

Ina dried her face and told her the short version first. Kwon refused restitution. Access restricted. Names piling up.

Hyejin listened while scraping rice crust from the bottom of an industrial pot with a wooden paddle worn smooth at the handle. "And now you're here because you've already decided to do something illegal and you want an older sinner to check your math."

Ina almost laughed. "That accurate?"

"You only call me by my full title when you're trying to be honest. So yes." Hyejin set the paddle down. "How much?"

Ina opened her notebook and turned it around.

`Family 1: Ryu household (Guro). Rent arrears KRW 1,900,000 + school meal debt KRW 340,000 + broker fee KRW 260,000.`

`Family 2: Pak household (Bupyeong). Dialysis deposit KRW 2,400,000 + utility arrears KRW 510,000 + route silence fee KRW 600,000.`

Total target: KRW 6,010,000 including transfer friction and cover expenses.

Hyejin sucked air through her teeth. "You're not skimming coffee money."

"I know."

"Source?"

"Campaign petty cash reserves under content localization." Ina met her aunt's eyes. "I can authorize reimbursements up to three million without second signature if I split line items. I file two fake vendor invoices, one for subtitling QA stipends, one for regional focus-group transport. Cash out in forty-eight hours."

"And me?"

"I need a temporary pass-through account that doesn't scream political operations. Your church meal account gets small transfers all the time. We label these as emergency grocery grants. You send onward through neighborhood bookkeepers we trust. No straight bank trail from me to the families."

Hyejin wiped her hands on the apron, leaving pale streaks of rice starch. "You're asking me to launder stolen money in the house of God."

"I'm asking you to move money where it should've gone before men in suits called suffering strategic weather."

Hyejin's mouth twitched. "That line is from your mother, not from you. You always steal her sharpest knives." She lifted the glasses from her chest and put them on. "Show me the names. Real names, not your operation labels."

Ina handed over the ledger pages. Hyejin read slowly, tracing each row with her index finger because her knuckles stiffened in damp weather. She paused at Ryu Jin-ho's entry and murmured the wife's name once under her breath like she was testing the weight of it.

When she finished, she pulled a biscuit tin from the shelf above the freezer. Inside were rubber bands, old receipts, and a church account checkbook wrapped in plastic.

"Two conditions," she said. "One: no children carry envelopes. Adults only. Two: every transfer gets a paper duplicate in my handwriting and yours. If they seize one set, another survives." She glanced up. "If they seize both, then we deserve prison for being sloppy."

Ina nodded before fear could interrupt.

"All right," Hyejin said. "Boil the kettle. My fingers work better when they're warm."

They sat at the stainless prep table with instant coffee and built the channel like women planning a funeral menu: exact, tired, no wasted adjectives.

Ina drafted invoice shells on her laptop.

`MiraeWave Language QA Microgrant Batch C-2.`

`Regional Audience Transport Reimbursement / Focus Cohort East.`

She used old vendor codes from closed projects and rounded totals to look boring. 2,980,000 on one form. 2,940,000 on the other. Under audit thresholds, under instinct.

Hyejin wrote parallel entries in the checkbook register with green pen, then recopied them in black on loose-leaf paper for redundancy.

`Emergency pantry subsidy - household RY-13.`

`Medical utility support - household PK-07.`

At 11:38 p.m. Ina logged into the campaign accounting portal from Hyejin's office computer, a beige desktop that wheezed when opening PDFs and still had a calendar background from 2019. She uploaded both invoices, attached forged receipt scans she had made from an old catering bill, and authorized disbursement with her token key.

A red warning bar snapped across the screen before the confirmation page loaded.

`Exception 44B: Vendor code inactive (188 days). Disbursement on hold pending compliance review.`

One payment cleared. One froze.

"Fuck," Ina said.

Hyejin leaned over her shoulder, glasses low on her nose. "How long does compliance take?"

"If nobody notices, Monday morning. If somebody notices, ten minutes."

"Pak's dialysis desk said six o'clock Friday is their hard cutoff. Miss it and they bump her chair to the standby list." Hyejin tapped the held invoice with one blunt fingernail. "We don't have Monday."

Ina deleted the frozen request, copied the amount into two live expense codes tied to Jamsil transport prep, and built new backup files while rain rattled the windows.
"Split it clean," Hyejin said.
Ina split the blocked amount into two rain-delay shuttle reimbursements, Unit D and Unit F, KRW 1,470,000 each.

She attached forged mileage sheets instead of vendor receipts, routed both reimbursements to the field debit float she controlled, and pushed authorize again.

Green check marks this time.

"Done," she said, and this time it sounded like a threat.

Hyejin did not say good or bad. She slid two blank envelopes across the table. "Write the real names on separate cards, no digital copy. Put one card in my hymnbook at home, one in your ledger. If you get grabbed, swallow yours before they ask polite questions."

"That's dramatic even for you."

"No. Dramatic is crying in a corridor. This is inventory." Hyejin capped the pen and flexed her fingers with a wince. "You have your mother's appetite for records and your father's talent for pretending you can carry consequences alone. Bad combination."

Rain hammered the kitchen roof so hard they had to raise their voices for a minute.

Ina copied the names by hand.

`Ryu Jin-ho / spouse: Choi Min-hee / Guro officetel B-402.`

`Pak Jun-seo / mother: Pak Seon-kyung / Bupyeong basement unit 1.`

Below each she wrote the transfer sequence, the intermediary, and the date stamp. She added a final column in her own ledger before closing it:

`Council response requested?` Yes.

`Council response provided?` No.

`Action taken by Ina Seo:` Fund diversion initiated.

By Friday afternoon the first transfer hit clean and the second did not. Hyejin called while Ina sat in a stairwell behind the studio smoking half a cigarette she did not enjoy.

"Ryu household got cash through Mrs. Lim's grocery line," Hyejin said. "Pak money bounced at the clinic desk. They changed policy after a fake-charity fraud case. No third-party transfer, no treatment slot."

Ina stood up so fast ash streaked down her fingers. "Where are you?"

"Bupyeong, Mirae Dialysis billing window. Come if you can move now."

Forty-nine minutes later Ina pushed through a sliding glass door that kept stuttering on its track. The lobby smelled like bleach and wet umbrellas. Pak Seon-kyung stood under a flickering panel light in a washed-out floral blouse, one arm wrapped around a plastic folder of receipts. A red stamp bled across the top page: `UNPAID / SESSION HOLD`. Behind it sat a yellow utility shutoff notice folded into quarters.

Hyejin lifted her phone. "I told her she should hear your voice, not just mine."

Pak Seon-kyung bowed too quickly and gripped the folder tighter. "Ms. Seo, I'm sorry to meet you like this. If this doesn't clear by six they cancel tomorrow's dialysis and move me to Tuesday. My ankles are already swelling."

Ina looked at the clock above the reception desk. 5:41.

"We're clearing it now," Ina said.

The clerk behind plexiglass refused the church transfer printout, then refused cash until Pak signed a payer authorization and showed her pink treatment card. Hyejin braced the form on her own back so Pak could write. Ina fed bills through the narrow tray one bundle at a time and listened to the counting machine click like teeth.

The receipt printed at 5:56 p.m. `Deposit received: KRW 2,400,000. Next session retained.`

At the utility kiosk next door, they paid the arrears with the rest and got a second thermal slip before the shutters came down.

Pak held both receipts against her chest with shaking fingers. "I kept telling my son I'd handle this before he came back. Now he's gone and I still couldn't do it." She swallowed hard. "Tell whoever this came from I won't waste the breath it bought."

Ina closed her eyes and pressed her thumb into the burn scar until pain made the moment crisp.

"Any flags?" she asked.

"Two," Hyejin said. "Clinic copied my ID because the payer name changed at the window. And your old held invoice didn't disappear. It rolled into an exception queue."

As if cued, Ina's phone buzzed with an internal finance alert.

`Document Preservation Notice: Token 7A-S (Seo) linked to duplicate support files across three disbursements. Mandatory review Monday 09:00.`

When they split outside the clinic, Ina opened the hidden ledger on her phone, entered confirmation codes, and attached a voice memo summary in case text got scrubbed.

She did not write I am sorry. She did not write this is wrong. She wrote what happened.

`2027-07-08.`

`Formal restitution denied in strategy meeting by K. Public humiliation used as enforcement.`

`2027-07-10.`

`Funds diverted from Campaign Localization Reserve. Initial PK invoice blocked by Exception 44B (inactive vendor); corrective action: reissued as two transport reimbursements (Unit D/F) totaling KRW 5,920,000 net after fees.`

`Relief delivered to two households linked to verified operational harm. PK transfer required in-person payment at Mirae Dialysis + utility kiosk before cutoff.`

`Consequence: finance system triggered document-preservation notice and Monday compliance review tied to my token.`

`Legal status: embezzlement risk accepted.`

She reread the entries once, then copied them by hand into the grease-stained ledger and slid the book into the false bottom of a plastic storage bin in her apartment, under winter blankets she would not need for months.

Outside her window the rain finally eased. Seoul kept moving: scooters, delivery alarms, the late train grinding over the river bridge. On the table, her phone lit with campaign messages about stadium choreography, sentiment targets for the Jamsil show, and a call-sheet update placing her in Broadcast Annex Three the next afternoon.

Ina muted the thread, opened a new page in the ledger, and wrote the next empty line herself before sleep could sand her down.

`Next: move witness collection up forty-eight hours and keep all future relief off campaign rails.`


# Chapter 9

The next afternoon, Rack C shuddered under Ina's palms as she slapped the checksum-compare key twice and watched the right progress bar outrun the left on two machines that were supposed to mirror each other exactly. Heat pooled in the concrete annex under Jamsil; hot dust, instant coffee, and the electrical sourness of cheap power strips sat in her throat while the headset bit into her ear.

The venue crew called the room Broadcast Annex Three. Nobody called it a room. It was a concrete slot behind the loading bay, no windows, one swinging door that never closed all the way, and nine people pretending they could breathe calmly because there was still an hour before Park Ji-eun walked onto the stage.

On the left monitor, the lyric package from Ina's team sat where it belonged: `chorus_patch_v6`, checksum green, timestamped 15:31, signed by the council key they had used since the pilot rewrite in spring. On the right monitor, a package with the same signature and a cleaner upload trail had arrived at 15:42 from a server she did not authorize.

The file name looked harmless, almost polite.

`chorus_patch_v6_final`

Minjae leaned over her shoulder, smelling like wet pavement and nicotine even though he had not smoked all day. He had rubbed his thumb so hard along the knuckle of his index finger that the skin had gone shiny. When he got anxious he counted cables under his breath, not numbers out loud, just color names in order like he was laying out paint: blue, black, yellow, blue.

"That second one piggybacked on house sync," he said. "Not from our relay. Not from agency either. Somebody inside venue ops opened a side tunnel, then pushed with our cert."

"Can we kill the tunnel?"

"Already dead. Doesn't matter. Payload's in the scheduler now."

Across the table, Lee Jisoo from venue tech kept scraping the edge of her molar with a thumbnail until it clicked. She had a tiny silver fish pin clipped to her lanyard, bent at the tail where somebody had stepped on it and she had bent it back. Every five minutes she straightened that pin with two fingers like she could flatten the evening with it.

"I flagged duplicate authorities at fourteen minutes to load," Jisoo said without looking up. "Control told me to ignore and maintain cue sheet. I've got that in text, if anybody suddenly wants records."

Ina could not tell whether that was courage or preemptive self-defense. Probably both.

The song itself had been built to do one thing. The opening verses held safe romance cliches for sponsors and parents. The bridge slipped in their coded narrative: sibling separation, hunger, border rivers that remembered names better than governments did. The chorus was meant to pull thousands of throats into one line about reunion as care, not conquest. They had tested every word for heat maps and trigger phrases, shaved off anything that could be read as artillery weather.

The duplicate package replaced three lines in the bridge and all of the final chorus.

Ina read it once. Then again. She did not need a third pass.

The new lines were a mobilization script dressed up as nostalgia. They called for marching columns, purification, traitor lists. The vocabulary was old war-radio language translated into fan-pop cadence. Whoever wrote it knew both registers and wanted blood.

"That's not drift," she said. "That's an intentional conversion."

Minjae nodded. "Rival faction. Has to be the hardliners Kwon keeps calling enthusiastic volunteers. They finally got their toy."

A runner from stage management burst through the bad door with laminated passes swinging against his chest. He could not have been older than twenty-one, and he wore a rain poncho around his waist like a skirt because he hated carrying bags. His voice shook while he tried to keep it bright.

"Fifteen-minute warning for final patch lock. Main bowl's at seventy percent seated. Agency asks if we're clear for lyric mirror to app."

Ina did not answer him. She spoke to Minjae, and to the two clocks that mattered: stage clock and street clock.

"If we hold lock and push manual from local?"

"We can shove ours at T-minus five," Minjae said. "But scheduler will still run both unless we get root on venue core. Root's in Directorate hands tonight, not ours."

"Meaning Kwon."

"Meaning somebody wearing his pass."

Ina took off the headset and wiped sweat from the back of her neck with the hem of her shirt. Her fingers came away with makeup and dust. She looked at Jisoo.

"Who has physical access to venue core?"

Jisoo turned her laptop so they could see the live badge log. "Three active entries. Me. House engineer. Director Kwon's office credential, temporary uplift at 14:08, no sign-out."

The badge log gave her the answer and tucked a trap inside it.

Kwon might not have ordered it. Kwon might have ordered it and would call it strategic inevitability. Kwon might have let a faction do it so he could later claim he had prevented worse. In every scenario, he stayed clean enough to testify.

Ina's phone lit with `Director Kwon` before she touched it.

She put the call on speaker. "We have conflicting chorus payloads in scheduler. One is militarized. We need a halt."

Kwon's voice came smooth and low, as if he were dictating condolences at a funeral where he knew everybody's secrets. "I am aware of the discrepancy."

"Then pull your credential from core and let us purge."

"No."

The single syllable sat in the room like a dropped tool.

"If we cancel now," Kwon continued, "forty thousand phones will capture a blackout, and every hostile office will call it intentional sabotage. That brings prosecutors on-site before midnight. You know this sequence."

Ina squeezed the bridge of her nose until she saw a white spark. "This isn't sequence management. This is incitement language."

"Language has always carried risk."

"Not like this."

"You are over-reading an unstable draft under deadline pressure. Proceed with your prepared delivery. I will handle the press bulletin, sponsor counsel, and police briefing after the set."

He said briefing the way accountants said reconciliation after money had already disappeared.

Minjae mouthed, Hang up, and dragged his hand across his throat.

Ina ignored him. "Ji-eun doesn't have these lines. Her in-ear prompts are tied to our version. If the screens run yours, she will break live."

"She is a professional performer. She has weathered worse than a telemetry conflict."

"She's twenty-four and your contract debt has her sleeping three hours a night. Don't tell me what she can weather."

A pause. Kwon did not raise his voice. He never needed volume.

"Ms. Seo," he said, full formal address now, "you accepted that public operations require imperfect control. If you choose unilateral cancellation, you will bear sole legal ownership of resulting disorder under the Armistice communications statute. You have read the statute."

Kwon had wrapped the choice and labeled it for her: cancel and carry the criminal file, run it and carry the bodies.

"I read everything I sign," Ina said.

"Then you understand. Execute the event."

The line clicked dead.

No one spoke for five seconds. The server fans filled the silence with a mosquito whine.

Jisoo was first. "I can produce a fault report right now. It gives us maybe ten minutes of hard stop before somebody above me reverses it."

Minjae shook his head. "Ten minutes doesn't solve scheduler contamination. It just gives them time to install guards at every door and freeze logs."

"So what solves it?" Ina asked.

He pointed to a cramped diagram on the wall where somebody had taped cable routes in fluorescent marker. "Two options. One: I tunnel into app mirror and poison their lyric push with stale cache so audience phones lag. Two: you get Ji-eun to follow your manual cue pack and ignore screens."

"Her in-ear feed can get overridden."

"Yeah."

"Stage manager will panic and force script."

"Yeah."

"We're not in control."

Minjae gave her a look that had no comfort in it, only respect for ugly facts. "We haven't been in control since month one."
