The runner was still in the doorway. He had gone pale, but he was listening hard, because listening was how kids in these buildings survived. Ina realized she was about to use him as if he were furniture. She did not.

"What's your name?" she asked.

"Seung-ho," he said.

"Seung-ho, close that door and send everyone not on technical authority out of this corridor. Nobody comes in without Jisoo's clearance. If anybody above you screams, tell them line sync is unstable and you're waiting for structural approval."

He swallowed. "If they ask whose order?"

"Mine. Spell it right when they write the report."

He almost smiled, then did not, then ran.

Minjae opened three terminal windows and started typing fast enough that the keys clacked like rain on tin. He worked with his left shoulder angled forward to protect the screen from anyone behind him, habit from years of doing illegal things in crowded rooms. Blue, black, yellow, he muttered.

Ina walked to the corner where Ji-eun's stage liaison stood with a makeup pouch and a clipboard. Yoon Sera wore a white blazer that had survived a monsoon commute because she kept a travel lint roller in her right pocket and treated every speck like personal insult. She rolled invisible dust off her sleeve now while pretending this was routine.

"You need to brief Ji-eun," Ina said. "There may be lyric drift on main screens. She sticks to cue cards and my hand signals, not visual feed."

Yoon's lips thinned. "She's rehearsed approved copy. We can't improvise tonight."

"If she follows screen feed and it's contaminated, she says words that can get people arrested by midnight."

"If she deviates, she breaches sponsor language and triggers a penalty schedule that could bankrupt her before dawn."

The balance sheet snapped shut around Ji-eun's throat like a choke collar.

Ina stepped closer. "Her trainee recovery account is already three hundred twenty million won. You're keeping her one invoice away from collapse. Don't talk to me about bankruptcy like it's theoretical weather."

Yoon looked toward the badge scanner, checking who might hear. "I didn't write that contract."

"You enforce it."

"And you," Yoon shot back, quietly vicious, "wrote the script that put a political fuse under her face." She snapped the lint roller shut. "I will relay your note. No promises."

She left before Ina could answer.

By sound, the stadium above them shifted from pre-show hum to expectation. The bass test hit through the concrete floor in slow thumps. Crowd chants rose and dissolved. Somebody in the corridor laughed too loudly and then shut up when nobody joined.

Minjae swore at his screen. "App mirror's dirty too. They're distributing from three shell nodes, probably bought by a fandom bot farm. If I nuke one, two reroute."

"Can you at least delay?"

"Thirty to forty seconds, maybe a minute if routing gods love me for once."

"Take the minute."

He nodded once and kept typing.

Ina stood in front of the rack again and watched the two progress bars reach one hundred percent together. The conflict check was complete, and it was too late to reset.

She hated how calm her own body felt at that exact second, the false calm that came when choice narrowed to one bad lane and another.

She keyed her headset channel to stage left. "Ji-eun, this is Ina. We have possible lyric instability on venue screens. Repeat: possible instability. Stay with rehearsal text. Follow my count, not monitor."

Ji-eun's voice came back bright and professional, the version she used for microphones and men with clipboards. "Copy, unnie. Staying on your count."

Then a second voice came in, private sub-channel only, quiet enough that Ina had to press the cup tighter.

"My left hand won't stop shaking," Ji-eun said. "If it gets bad, give me a cue to spin so nobody sees."

Ina closed her eyes for one breath. "I see it. We will hide it in choreography."

"I hate this dress," Ji-eun said. "It grabs when I inhale."

"Rip it if you need air. Sponsor can sue me."

A tiny laugh in Ina's ear, frayed but real. "That is the first useful legal strategy anyone has offered me today."

Stage manager counted down in all channels, voice already too fast.

"Ten minutes to open."

Outside, thunder rolled somewhere over the river.

---

From the tunnel mouth, the stadium looked like a bowl of wet light. Rain had started and stopped twice, leaving the plastic seats glossy and the air thick enough to chew. Ina stayed in the wing behind a stack of spare LED panels, headset on, cue cards in hand, watching Ji-eun stand at center mark while dancers took their opening poses.

Ji-eun wore black satin with silver stitching along the ribs, military enough to sell the sponsor concept and soft enough to keep fan cams romantic. Her dyed dark hair was pulled high, exposing the line of her neck and the clear tape that held her in-ear pack. Every few counts her jaw locked and she bit the inside of her cheek, then pinched the glove seam with two fingers until the satin creaked. A beat later the camera smile came back, patient as prayer.

The intro track hit. Forty thousand wrists lifted lightsticks in coordinated white.

Verse one ran clean. Ji-eun's public voice came warm, playful at the edges. She flirted with the camera on line breaks, took the ramp, hit marks. Ina counted bars under her breath and fed cues at the exact beat. For ninety seconds, the night behaved.

At bar thirty-two, the main screen behind Ji-eun flashed a lyric card that did not match rehearsal text.

The first replacement line was subtle enough most casual listeners would miss it. River became border. Home became front.

Ji-eun blinked once, almost invisible, and stayed on rehearsal. Good.

Minjae came over comms, clipped and fast. "Hijack active on north and east screens. App lag at twenty-eight seconds. I'm losing the west wall."

"Hold delay," Ina said. "Jisoo, can you cut screen lyric layer entirely?"

Jisoo answered through static. "Denied from core. Manual override rejected with director credential."

Director credential again. Of course.

The bridge began. This was where the coded narrative lived, where people who had buried siblings across borders heard themselves inside pop music for once. Ji-eun took one step forward, inhaled, and delivered Ina's line about a mother keeping two bowls on one table even after one child disappeared.

The crowd answered with a roar that started in the lower bowl and climbed upward in a wave. People cried in public and did not hide it. Ina saw a man in section C embrace a stranger hard enough to knock both of their lightsticks sideways. For a handful of beats, she thought they might still steer this.

Then the second hacked card hit all four main screens at once.

The text strobed in white over red: ADVANCE SO THE BLOOD IS NOT WASTED.

A beat later, audience phones lit with the same line through the event app push.

Ji-eun's in-ear crackled. Someone on production channel shouted, "Read monitor copy, read monitor copy now."

Ina cut across, voice sharp enough to hurt her own throat. "Ignore that. Stay with me. Stay with rehearsal."

Ji-eun looked to stage left. Ina gave the hand cue for spin and reset. Ji-eun spun, landed, and missed a half beat because her heel caught in wet confetti glued to the stage floor. She recovered, but the crack had opened.

In the bowl, singing fragmented. Half the crowd followed Ji-eun's sung line, half chanted the screen text because the screen looked official and authority traveled faster than context.

"What the fuck is this?" Minjae said on comms.

"They're forcing call-and-response," Ina said. "If crowd repeats those lines, cameras clip it and it becomes proof."

The third hacked line replaced the chorus entirely.

It named traitors, not by person but by category: negotiators, journalists, and anyone who spoke caution.

You could hear the flip happen. Catharsis had a sound, like one body exhaling. Aggression had a different sound, sharp edges, separate throats. The bowl changed key in less than a minute.

A section near the south goal started chanting the new lines in rhythm with the kick drum. Another section booed. Plastic bottles arced through humid air. One hit a dancer in the shoulder and burst, spraying sticky soda across black satin.

Ji-eun clipped a consonant, swallowed hard, and the mic slid in her grip for half a beat before she trapped it against her chest. Her smile vanished. She sang anyway, forcing rehearsal lyrics over a crowd now splitting into two songs.

In Ina's headset, voices piled up.

"Police liaison requests message clarification."

"Sponsors are asking whether this is approved political content."

"Agency requests performer return to monitor text immediately."

"No," Ina said to all of it. "No."

She switched channels. "Ji-eun, listen to me. New plan. Skip to coda after next eight. Repeat coda twice, then exit on my count."

"I can't hear the count," Ji-eun said, suddenly bare voice, no media polish left. "Somebody keeps stepping on your channel."

Behind Ina, a production assistant vomited into a trash can and apologized while doing it.

The crowd on the concourse side began to surge toward one gate where a rumor had started that the screens were hacked by northern agents. Rumors in Seoul moved like humidity: invisible, then everywhere. Ina saw it on her secondary monitor, a live social feed clipped from fan phones. One post said Ji-eun was signaling invasion. Another said she was warning against it. Neither post included full lyrics. Both had ten thousand shares.

Minjae shouted over keys. "I can black out app lyrics for fifteen seconds if I burn our mirror credentials. After that we're blind for tomorrow's transport."

He said tomorrow because he planned in boxes. He meant routes. He meant if he burned this door, their next door narrowed.

"Burn them," Ina said.

"Copy."

On stage, pyrotechnic jets fired for the final chorus because the cue system had not gotten the memo that politics had turned into a knife fight. White flame columns rose while the screens screamed text about cleansing and marching and unnamed enemies inside the city.

Ji-eun saw the words. She did not sing them. She stopped singing altogether for two beats, chest heaving, eyes searching the dark wing where Ina stood.

Then she spoke into the mic instead, voice cracking on the first syllable.

"This song was about people finding each other," she said.

A producer somewhere yelled, "Cut her mic."

Her mic stayed live.

"Not this," Ji-eun said. "Not this wording."

The confession was tiny and catastrophic. To one side of the bowl, it sounded like refusal. To the other, it sounded like betrayal.

Someone threw a lightstick at the stage. It spun end over end and bounced off the edge near her boot.

Security moved toward the pit as if motion alone could reverse what a sentence had done.

Ina felt the pressure wave before she saw it: concourse doors opening all at once, people pouring out faster than staff could direct them, the kind of movement that crushed ankles and children first.

A schoolboy in soaked uniform stumbled through the stage-left tunnel with one shoe missing and a scalp split open above his temple. An usher half-carried him by the backpack straps and screamed for a medic who did not answer. Ina dropped her cue cards, caught the boy by the shoulders, and pressed her palm and lanyard against the wound. Warm blood slicked over her fingers and down his eyebrow into his eye. He grabbed her wrist with both hands and gasped, "My sister's still at Gate E."

Ina shoved her emergency badge into the usher's hand. "Take him through camera corridor to the medic station. Don't wait for clearance."

Pepper spray fog rolled into the tunnel behind them, burning Ina's throat and eyes. A woman in a yellow rain hat crashed against the wall, coughing hard enough to retch, with chemical tears striping her face.

The stage manager grabbed Ina's elbow. "We are shutting down performance now."

"Do it."

"Agency says keep her visible for control messaging."

"Do it anyway."

He hesitated one fatal second and looked over her shoulder, waiting for authority with a bigger badge.

Kwon's voice entered her ear on the executive channel, quiet as ever. "Keep Ms. Park on camera until Gates E and F clear. If screens cut now, prosecutors will call this coordinated panic engineering."

"People are bleeding while you count camera angles," Ina said.

"If this fractures publicly, the legal response will be severe. You and Ms. Park are already vulnerable."

"You should have thought about that before loading war slogans into a pop set."

"Your voice is unsteady. Follow the emergency script."

Something in Ina snapped cleanly, not loud, not dramatic, just a line finally breaking under known weight.

"No," she said. "I'm done translating your violence into donor-friendly subtitles."

She yanked her headset free, tossed it onto the panel stack, and ran for stage stairs.

A security guard blocked her with one forearm. He was broad-shouldered, older, wearing cheap reading glasses on a cord around his neck because he hated admitting he needed them. "Authorized personnel only."

Ina shoved her pass in his face. "Then authorize me."

By the time she hit center stage, Ji-eun was crouched beside a fallen dancer whose ankle had folded wrong under a sprinting exit. The dancer's makeup had mixed with rain and blood into a pink smear across her cheek. Ji-eun held the mic in one hand and the dancer's wrist in the other, both shaking.

"Off," Ina said. "Now."

Ji-eun stared at her. Close up, her mascara had started to run into the corners of her eyes, and she looked younger than twenty-four for one brutal second.

"What did you put me inside?" she said.

She was not asking what had happened; she was asking what Ina had put her inside.

Ina opened her mouth to lie and decided she had no time left for that luxury.

"A messaging operation," she said. "Supposed to push reconciliation language. It got hijacked by hardliners."

Ji-eun laughed once, a short sound with no humor. "So everybody who called me a pretty mouthpiece was just early."

The house lights slammed to half. Music died mid-beat. Crowd noise surged louder without the track to absorb it.

"We move," Ina said. "Questions in the car."

"I'm not getting in any car with agency tonight."

"Not agency. Me."

Ji-eun looked at her for one heartbeat, then nodded.

They ran.

---

Backstage corridors had their own weather. The air behind the main stage was colder from overworked AC, but smelled like sweat trapped in fabric, hairspray, and panic. People moved in packs that kept breaking apart and reforming around whoever seemed to have answers.

Ina dragged Ji-eun toward Dressing Room B and found three people waiting outside: Yoon Sera with her lint roller clenched like a weapon, a contract lawyer named Bae Jin-woo in plastic shoe covers because he refused to scuff expensive loafers, and a medic with chipped green nail polish holding a silver case.

Yoon stepped in front of the door. "She needs controlled sedation before statement. We have a two-minute holding script and a live apology slot."

"No," Ina said.

Bae gave her a courtroom smile. "Ms. Seo, you are not principal management. Under exclusive performance rider clause seven, the agency may administer medically supervised calming treatment for broadcast stabilization."

Ji-eun recoiled at the phrase broadcast stabilization like it smelled rotten. "Say the drug name."

The medic answered because she was the only honest one in the line. "Midazolam injection, low dose. It will keep you from shaking on camera."

"It will keep me from speaking," Ji-eun said.

Yoon's jaw tightened. "It will keep you from self-destruction."

Ji-eun took one step back until her shoulder hit the cinderblock wall. "My whole twenties have been your damage-control plan."

Bae opened a folder with tabbed pages already highlighted. Of course he had prepared this before the show, before the so-called unexpected event. "If she refuses directive treatment and script compliance, we initiate breach acceleration. The recovery amount becomes immediately callable."

"How much?" Ji-eun asked, though she knew.

"Three hundred twenty million won plus penalties for sponsor loss."

The number landed in the hallway like a shovel.

Ina felt her own ledger in her bag, paper corners digging into her hip. Names, dates, harm columns. She had spent weeks pretending numbers could be restitution. Here was another number that was just a weapon.

"You're not sticking a needle in her for optics," Ina said.

Bae did not bother masking contempt now. "And you are not licensed to interfere. Move."

He reached for Ji-eun's elbow. Ji-eun slapped his hand away hard enough to make the folder fall and scatter annotated pages across the wet floor.

On Ina's dead headset hanging around her neck, a channel crackled to life by accident. Kwon's voice, faint through static.

"Contain Park Ji-eun immediately. Do not permit unscripted contact."

Yoon heard it too and went pale.

Ina lifted the headset mic toward her mouth and spoke so all of them could hear.

"Director, we have active crowd injuries and your first concern is sedation language."

"My concern," Kwon said, "is preventing irreversible civic damage. Return Ms. Park to agency care."

"No."

"If you remove her from licensed custody, you expose yourself to kidnapping charges, contract interference claims, and national-security obstruction. Consider your father's housing status before you choose spectacle."

The mention of her father was deliberate. He knew exactly which tendon to press.

Ina did not shout. "You already spent me, Director. You don't get to spend her too."

She tore the headset cord from her collar and let it fall.

For one second nobody moved. Then everything moved at once.

Yoon lunged for Ji-eun's wrist. Ji-eun twisted, slipped on confetti paste, and slammed her hip into a rolling costume rack. Metal screamed against concrete. The medic swore and grabbed the silver case before it burst open. Bae yelled for security.

Minjae appeared from the service stairwell like he had grown out of the wall, breathless, rainwater darkening his shirt from chest to hem.

"North loading ramp's clogged with police," he said. "South janitor lane is open for maybe two minutes. Fire door alarm will buy us noise."

"Do it," Ina said.

He slapped the red break-glass lever at the end of the corridor. Sirens erupted, high and constant, drowning individual orders. Strobe lights kicked on overhead, painting everybody's face alternating red-white-red.

"This way," Minjae barked.

Ji-eun hesitated, looking between Yoon and Ina.

Yoon's voice cracked for the first time. "If you walk out, they will destroy you."

Ji-eun dug her nails into her thigh until the muscle jumped, dragged one clean breath through her nose, and answered in her on-camera cadence with the sweetness stripped out. "They've already been doing that. They just called it training."

She went with Ina.

They sprinted through the props cage, ducked under hanging backdrops that smelled like mildew, and pushed through a door marked JANITORIAL, NO ACCESS. The alarm kept screaming. Behind them, footsteps multiplied.

The stairwell was concrete and wet. Ina's shoes slipped on the second flight and she caught herself with her scarred wrist against the wall, skin scraping rough paint. Ji-eun grabbed the back of her shirt to steady her and kept moving.

By the time they hit the service tunnel, Ji-eun was gasping from panic more than exertion. "I can't get enough air in this corset."

"Rip the side seam," Ina said.

"Sponsor will sue."

"Let them queue up."

Ji-eun hooked two fingers under the silver-stitched seam and tore downward. Fabric gave with a sound like paper splitting. She inhaled hard and coughed.

Outside the tunnel mouth, monsoon rain came sideways. Sirens from police and ambulance braided into one long mechanical howl. The loading yard lights threw everything into harsh yellow: puddles, crates, cable reels, two riot vans pulling into the wrong lane because no one had clear command.

Minjae pointed to a gray catering van idling with hazard lights on. "Driver's a cousin of a cousin. He thinks we're moving damaged wardrobe. He doesn't need details."

They almost made it.

At the van door, two private security men stepped out of shadow. One held a tablet with Ji-eun's contract photo open. The other had a plastic rain hood pulled tight around his face and kept wiping rain off his eyelashes in angry little jerks.

"Ms. Park returns to agency custody," tablet man said. "Now."

Before anyone else moved, he thumbed his radio and spoke in clipped dispatch Korean. "Haneul Response Two to Stadium Containment Desk: unauthorized extraction at South Yard, target vehicle gray catering van, plate 83-mo-2714. Initiate Ring Two perimeter and camera lock."

Minjae moved half a step in front of Ji-eun. "She's done performing tonight."

"This is not your jurisdiction."

"Nothing about tonight has jurisdiction," Minjae said.

Tablet man reached for his radio. Ina moved first, grabbed the radio strap, and yanked it hard enough to spin him toward her. He cursed. The second guard lunged for Ji-eun and caught only torn satin as she ducked aside.

Minjae drove his shoulder into the second guard's midsection and both of them hit the wet pavement, sliding through a shallow puddle that splashed black water up Ina's legs.

The driver of the catering van stared through the windshield, frozen, hands at ten and two like he was in a driving exam.

"Open the door!" Ina shouted.

He did.

She shoved Ji-eun inside first. Ji-eun scrambled across stacked plastic bins labeled CUTLERY and folded over herself, one fist jammed to her sternum while she counted under her breath.

Minjae slammed the first guard's radio under his heel until it cracked, then hauled himself up, rain streaming off his hair into his eyes.

"Go," he told Ina. "I'll peel off and meet you at point two."

"You're bleeding," Ina said.

He looked down at his forearm where a jagged cut had opened against something metal. "Yeah. So are half the city. Move."

He shoved the van door shut from outside and slapped the panel twice.

The van lurched forward.

Inside, the air smelled like kimchi brine and bleach. Metal trays rattled with every pothole. Ji-eun sat on the floor between crates, rainwater dripping off her chin, mascara gone, one false eyelash stuck to her cheek.

The driver's dispatch tablet chirped and flashed red on the dash: `RING TWO CONTAINMENT ACTIVE - VEHICLE 83-MO-2714 FLAGGED - HOLD FOR POLICE INSPECTION.`

Ina ripped the power cable out and shoved the tablet under a crate, but the damage was already done. Their plate was in the city net, and Minjae's point-two route had burned with it.

For thirty seconds they said nothing, both listening for pursuit at every turn.

Then Ji-eun laughed again, thin and close to crying. "Broadcast stabilization."

Ina slid down the opposite wall and sat on the ribbed rubber mat, knees up, heart punching at her ribs like it wanted separate escape.

"I'm sorry," she said.

"For which part?" Ji-eun asked. "The part where my face sold your message, or the part where your message got replaced by a call to beat each other in parking lots?"

"Both."

Ji-eun wiped her nose with the back of her hand. "Did you know before tonight that they could swap text like that?"

"I knew it was possible. I didn't know they had access at core level."

"That's not the same as no."

"No. It's not."

Rain hammered the van roof so hard conversation had to come in bursts. Ina pulled her phone from her pocket. Three hundred notifications climbed in real time.

`#JIEUN_TRAITOR` was already top trend.

`#SAVEJIEUN` was second.

A clipped video of Ji-eun saying "Not this wording" had fifteen million views and captions in six languages, each one claiming opposite proof.

Another clip showed men fighting outside Gate E with the song still playing in background, now detached from source, pure weaponized soundtrack.

Ji-eun watched Ina's face while she scrolled. "Say it."

"They're blaming you," Ina said.

"Of course."

"They're also blaming northern agents, and paid protesters, and stage pyros, and weather. Everyone gets a theory before midnight."

"Does your Director get blamed?"

Ina locked the screen. "Not unless somebody names him with documents."

Ji-eun pressed her forehead to her knees. "I gave you contract pages last month because you said we were building a safer lane."

"I believed that when I said it."

"Belief doesn't pay debt." Ji-eun looked up, eyes sharp despite exhaustion. "Do you know what they do when we're in breach review? They lock phones. They switch us to meal cards with exact calories. They make us recite apology language until our mouths go dry and crack. Then they tell us we should be grateful because at least we still have faces people recognize."

She held out her left hand between them. The tremor had not stopped. "This started when I was nineteen and they made me drink espresso and diet pills before camera test days. They said shaking reads as passion if you smile right."

Ina looked at that hand and thought of the ledger, columns too neat for what they held.

"I can't erase tonight," she said. "But I can keep you out of their room for the next twelve hours. Then we move you south."

"Busan?"

"Maybe. Depends which exits are watched."

"If I vanish, they call me unstable. If I stay, they sedate me and script confession."

"Yes."

Ji-eun gave a dry, exhausted nod. "Then unstable wins."

The van slowed at a flooded underpass. Through the rear window, Ina could see red-blue lights reflecting off rain and concrete in broken strips. She checked her phone again. One new message sat at the top from an unknown encrypted number.

`UNAUTHORIZED EXTRACTION NOTED. RETURN SUBJECT IMMEDIATELY.`

No signature needed.

Then another from Kwon, plain SMS this time, almost polite.

`You are converting a recoverable incident into structural failure.`

Ina typed back before she could rehearse herself.

`You converted a concert into a riot.`

She did not wait for response.

At the next intersection the driver looked back once, eyes wide in the mirror. He had a Tottenham keychain swinging from the ignition and a packet of roasted seaweed clipped above the radio, the kind of small domestic clutter that kept ordinary people sane while they unknowingly helped fugitives.

"Noona," he said to Ina, voice shaking, "where do I drop?"

She gave him Minjae's point-two address in Mapo first, then deleted it when she pictured Ring Two cameras reading their plate. She switched to a warehouse near Seongsu, then changed again to a church parking lot in eastern Songpa because both primary routes were now burned.

"Final location in two turns," she said. "Keep driving. If anyone asks, we're catering overflow."

He nodded too quickly. "I can do that."

Ji-eun leaned her head back against a crate and closed her eyes, but sleep did not come. Her mouth kept moving silently, running lines maybe, or apology scripts, or curses. Ina wanted to tell her it would slow down. It would not.

Blood from the schoolboy's scalp had dried in a dark crescent under Ina's thumbnail. Each time she flexed her hand, the skin on her palm pulled where his fingers had clamped down.

The city outside kept proving the consequence in real time.

At a stoplight they passed a bus shelter where two teenage boys in school uniforms argued over the song with fists half raised, phones in their other hands streaming opposite clips. Near a convenience store, an old man sat on the curb with a bandage around his scalp while a woman in an apron held a paper cup of water to his mouth. At an intersection near the river, police in rain capes shoved a crowd back from a parked camera van with one smashed lens.

None of this would stay in one night.

By morning there would be prosecutions, package edits, narrative committees. There would be interviews asking why a young woman had failed to control herself on stage. There would be experts explaining provocation as inevitable weather. There would be a legal brief with Ina's name in black text under words like interference and unlawful extraction.

She had known exposure as theory. In the van, with wet shoes and somebody else's catering boxes under her knees, exposure became logistics.

She needed cash routes, quiet beds, burner phones that could survive three hops, and one honest doctor who would not sedate a witness for television.

She needed Hyejin's church network before dawn.

She needed Minjae not to get arrested in the loading yard.

Her phone buzzed with a voice note from him, twenty-three seconds, recorded over wind and sirens.

"I'm clear for now," he said, breathing hard. "Shoulder's fine. Point two is burned. They pushed your plate on Ring Two dispatch. Take Olympic-daero east then cut. Don't hit major stations. They're scanning agency badges and event staff IDs. I'll meet you at fallback three with dry clothes and two burners. And Ina..."

He coughed, spat, continued.

"This wasn't an accident. They wanted visible rupture. Don't let them turn her into the only story."

The message ended.

Ina replayed the last sentence once and put the phone away.

Ji-eun opened her eyes. "Was that him?"

"Yeah."

"Is he okay?"

Ina thought of blood on his arm and rain on pavement and the way he had said only story.

"He's moving," she said. "That's enough for tonight."

The van turned off the main road into a narrow lane behind a closed hardware market. Neon signs buzzed in the rain. Shutter doors rattled in wind. Nobody looked up as they passed because Seoul had learned how to ignore strange vehicles after midnight.

Ina touched the ledger in her bag again, then touched the torn headset cord still in her pocket like a severed vein.

The operation had asked her to translate pain into strategy and strategy into ratings. Tonight the machine had dropped its polite mask in front of forty thousand witnesses, and then tried to sedate the witness with the best camera angle.

She looked at Ji-eun, who sat barefoot now because one heel had snapped in the run, massaging her own shaking hand with small furious circles.

"Listen to me," Ina said. "From this minute, nobody gets to medicate you for narrative cleanliness. Nobody gets to write your mouth without consent. If they want statements, they go through me first."

Ji-eun studied her for a long moment, as if checking for the familiar lie shape.

"That sounds expensive," she said.

"Everything already is."

Ji-eun gave a tired snort that might have been a laugh. "Fine. Then let's make the bill worth it."

The van rolled to a stop in darkness behind the church lot, wipers still working at full speed against rain that had finally started falling for real.

Ina reached for the handle and knew, with the same false-calm clarity she had felt at patch lock, that she had crossed from plausible deniability into open breach.

She opened the door anyway.

They had one stop for dry clothes and burners, then Seoul Station before dawn.


# Chapter 10

Seoul Station smelled like wet nylon and fried squid, the air still thick with tear gas residue from the stadium roads. Ina kept her cap brim low and her mask on, but it did not matter. Every phone around her was tilted up, every screen showing some version of the same freeze-frame: Ji-eun on the Jamsil stage with her mouth open and the hacked lyric line burning across the screen behind her like a threat.

Ji-eun stood half a step behind Ina, hood up, left hand shaking hard enough to make the zipper teeth chatter. She looked like any exhausted twenty-something after a bad night out until someone held a camera too long. Then the trained posture snapped into place by reflex, shoulders square, chin lowered at a practiced angle, even while her fingers kept stuttering.

The departures board flipped to BUSAN, 06:03 KTX. Ina had bought the tickets with an old corporate card Minjae had told her to burn afterward. She had cut it in half in the station bathroom and dropped each half into different bins, because paranoia had become muscle memory.

Her phone lit again.

DIRECTOR KWON: Report to Seongdong at 07:30. Do not move the asset without clearance.

A second message followed before she could clear the preview.

UNKNOWN NUMBER: Agency counsel requests immediate stabilization protocol. Sedation authorized. Prevent unscripted appearance.

Ina locked the screen without replying. The word asset sat in her head like grit under a contact lens. She had pulled Ji-eun out of a tunnel under Jamsil two hours earlier while a handler in a velvet blazer shouted for security and waved a syringe case like it proved professionalism.

"You're reading it again," Ji-eun said.

Ina turned. "Reading what."

"Your murder face. Left eyebrow, tiny twitch." Ji-eun made a little scratching motion over her own brow. "You only do it when somebody older and richer is giving you instructions."

The line was so dry it took Ina a second to catch it. Then, against her own will, she barked a laugh. Ji-eun heard it and gave her a crooked bow from the waist, as if she had just landed a joke on a variety show.

"There she is," Ji-eun said. "Writer Seo has feelings."

"Don't get used to it."

"I won't. You've been emotionally constipated since rehearsal week."

A man in line in front of them glanced back, annoyed, then looked again and did the tiny widening of his eyes people got when recognition and gossip touched in the same second. Ina shifted her body between him and Ji-eun. The gate opened. They moved.

The platform wind shoved diesel and rainwater up from the tracks. Above them, beyond the station roofline, a drone hummed, dipped, and steadied. News channel? Tabloid freelancer? It did not matter. Somebody had already guessed they would run south.

Inside the train, Ina chose the window seat and put Ji-eun inboard so she could not bolt on impulse when panic spiked. Ji-eun accepted the choreography without comment. She folded herself down and wrapped both hands around her phone, screen dark, thumb rubbing the corner where the front glass had cracked months ago.

The train lurched out of Seoul with the low metal groan Ina always felt in her ribs before she heard it fully. Apartment towers slid by, then warehouses, then the gray curve of river water. Her phone buzzed against her thigh every two minutes, a trapped insect.

She did not check it until they were past Daejeon.

There were fourteen new messages. Six from numbers linked to Haneul Entertainment legal staff. Three from Kwon. One from a council operations group chat with no names, only role tags.

OPS-2: If she is unstable, medicate now. We cannot absorb another live event.

OPS-2: Confirm sedation.

KWON TAESIK: You are not authorized to manage this alone. This is becoming disobedience.

Minjae had sent one line at 06:41.

MJ: Exit Busan through platform service stairs. Not main concourse. Camera swarm outside gate B.

Ina typed back with one thumb so Ji-eun could not see.

INA: Got it. You safe?

The read receipt never came.

When she looked up, Ji-eun was watching her without blinking.

"Everybody wants me sleepy," Ji-eun said.

"They want you quiet."

"Same product, different packaging."

Ina let that sit. "Do you want to sleep?"

Ji-eun shrugged. "I want my skin to stop buzzing. I want to stop hearing that crowd go from singing to screaming in one breath. I want to peel this face off and put on one where nobody has had meetings about my obedience since I was fourteen. But no, I don't want to sleep."

"Then we don't sedate you."

Ji-eun's mouth twitched. "You said that like a doctor signing consent."

"I said it like someone who's done being told what your body is for."

They rode in silence for a while. A toddler three rows up dropped a plastic truck; it clattered down the aisle and hit Ina's shoe. She picked it up and handed it back. The boy stared at Ji-eun's hood with solemn suspicion, then decided they were boring and climbed onto his father's lap.

At Dongdaegu, a train attendant pushed a cart through first class. She had a strip of cartoon bandage tape over one knuckle and a keychain shaped like a persimmon hanging from her belt. "Kimbap, fishcake, coffee, cup ramyeon," she sang in a voice that had gone hoarse from repeating itself since dawn.

Ji-eun looked at the tray as if it were museum art. "Do you have the cursed station sandwich," she asked, slipping into bright public politeness on instinct, "the one with too much mayonnaise and one apologetic lettuce leaf?"

The attendant snorted before she could stop herself. "Sold out at Osong. Mayonnaise won."

"Then two fishcakes and one failure coffee," Ji-eun said. "Please."

Ina added triangular kimbap and hot corn tea. When the attendant moved on, Ji-eun leaned over the fold-down table and whispered, "If we die, I want it on record that I died from train coffee, not conspiracy."

"You'd haunt the complaint hotline."

"I would. Very professionally."

They ate with wooden picks and burned fingertips. The fishcake broth was too salty and too good, the kind of station food that tasted like survival because it asked nothing of you. Ji-eun chewed fast at first, then slower as her breathing evened. Color came back into her face in small, grudging patches.

"Tell me something useless," Ina said.

Ji-eun frowned. "What."

"Something not about last night, not about contracts, not about strategy. Give me one useless fact."

Ji-eun thought, then said, "I hate the word ethereal. Every makeup artist who says it means she's about to bleach me until I look dead."

Ina nodded solemnly. "I hate the phrase content vertical. Every executive who says it means my weekend's gone."

Ji-eun pointed her fishcake stick at Ina. "You still alphabetize your spice jars?"

"That was one time."

"You posted a photo."

"I was depressed."

Ji-eun cracked up, sudden and loud, then clapped a hand over her mouth and laughed into her sleeve until her shoulders shook. People glanced over. No one looked twice. This was what fame looked like when it was stripped of lighting: two women with bad coffee, laughing too hard in a quiet carriage because panic had cracked and steam had to go somewhere.

When her laughter tapered, Ji-eun wiped her eyes with the heel of her hand and said, "Okay. Useless fact number two. My stage name almost was Eunice."

Ina nearly choked on corn tea. "No."

"Agency wanted global softness. They said Eunice tested well with aunties in Manila and teen girls in Toronto."

"What happened."

"I told them it sounded like a church soloist who reports tax fraud."

Ina folded forward over the tiny table and laughed so hard the lid popped off her cup. Hot tea spilled onto the napkin and soaked through to her jeans. For ten seconds, maybe fifteen, they were just two women wrecked by a stupid made-up name. They were not asset and handler, and they were not scapegoat and operative. They were exhausted bodies on a train, with Busan still ninety minutes away.

The calm held until Ji-eun's phone lit with a message preview from Manager Choi.

CHOI: Do not consume solid food before statement recording. Face swelling risk.

Ji-eun stared at the screen, then turned it toward Ina. "This is his first concern. My jawline."

She did not cry. She pressed her thumbnail into the cracked corner of her phone until the plastic clicked and clicked, then spoke in a scraped whisper that made Ina's shoulders lock harder than any shout.

"I owe them three hundred twenty million won," Ji-eun said. "Did you know how they write it in the monthly statement? Trainee Recovery Account. Sounds like rehab. Sounds like healing."

Ina set down her chopsticks. "Walk me through it."

Ji-eun gave a hard little laugh. "You want the whole humiliation menu?"

"Yes."

She leaned back and spoke to the window, watching her own dim reflection skate over fields and warehouses.

"First year trainee, they booked everything to debt. Vocal lessons, dance classes, dorm rent, stylist, skin clinic, transportation, media training, emergency dentist because one choreographer elbowed me and cracked this tooth." She tapped a canine. "Then they added penalties. Missed weigh-in: fifty thousand. Late to call time: one hundred. Hair color correction because a director said my tone read cheap: three hundred. I was sixteen and paying debt for bleach I never asked for."

The train hummed under them, steady as a ventilator.

"When I debuted, they said congratulations and gave me a bouquet and a debt statement with more zeros than my family had ever seen. We were from a two-room apartment over a stationery store. My mother still cut discount coupons by hand. I thought debut meant paycheck. It meant interest."

She dragged her sleeve over her lip. "Every comeback had recoupables. Music video sets, backup dancers, wardrobe losses, ad campaign penalties if engagement dipped, even fan-meet snacks. One manager charged us for bottled water at rehearsal and put it under image maintenance. I used to hide one bottle in my locker so I would not cost more than my own labor."

Ina kept still. She did not reach across the table yet. Let the line run.

"The rider I slipped you with the ledger? It's real. If I breach behavioral clauses or damage brand confidence, they can call the three-twenty immediately. Plus civil damages. They don't have to prove actual loss first. They only have to say the words national trust erosion in front of a judge who's older than my father."

Her left hand had started trembling again. She pinned it between her knees.

"When the crowd turned last night, I did the media smile. You know the one." Ji-eun bared her teeth in a perfect, dead arc. "And I thought: if I disappear now, maybe they only sue my ghost."

Ina reached over then and held Ji-eun's wrist, not tight, just enough pressure to anchor. Ji-eun let her.

"No disappearing," Ina said.

"Then confession. I go live, I tell them everything. The riders, the scripts, the way they kept beta blockers in a makeup bag like mints. I tell them exactly who handed me what line and who told me to cry on count three."

"If you go live right now, they will frame you as unstable and drug-seeking before you finish the second sentence."

"They already are."

"Yes. And I'm trying to keep you alive long enough to beat them somewhere that lasts more than one trending cycle."

Ji-eun pulled her wrist back, not violently, but with decision. "I'm not a campaign asset either, Ina. I'm not your long game."

The hit landed clean because it was true enough to hurt.

"I know," Ina said. "I'm saying I need one day. Not forever. One day to get you off their legal leash before you light yourself on fire in public."

Ji-eun looked down at the table, at the fishcake stick wrapped in napkin. "One day is how this started, remember? One day for rehearsal, one day for a rewrite, one day for a statement. Ten years later I'm still in one day."

Before Ina could answer, her own phone did not buzz; it rang at full volume because she had forgotten to mute calls during the sprint out of Seoul.

DIRECTOR KWON filled the screen.

Ji-eun watched her with an expression that was almost bored, almost daring. Ina let the call go to voicemail. Kwon called again immediately. She rejected it this time.

A message arrived within seconds, clipped and formal.

KWON TAESIK: If you continue to avoid command contact, I will interpret this as defection.

Ina typed without polishing.

INA: She's not being sedated. She's not recording today. I'll report when she's stable.

Three dots appeared, vanished, appeared again. Then:

KWON TAESIK: You are confusing sentiment with governance.

She did not answer. She turned her phone face-down and kept it there until Busan.

By the time they rolled into the station, rain had cleared and light was coming hard through breaks in cloud, bright enough to turn every wet surface into a mirror. The platform was already crowded with weekend travelers, office commuters, and three men with camera rigs pretending not to scan faces.

Ina and Ji-eun waited until most passengers had moved up the stairs. Then they went the other way, toward a service door marked STAFF ONLY and propped open with a crate of bottled water. A cleaner in yellow gloves smoked beside it, scrolling baseball highlights. She glanced up once, took in Ji-eun's hood and Ina's city shoes, and went back to her screen.

Minjae's directions were precise. Down one concrete flight, through a corridor that smelled of bleach and old fish, up again into a side lane behind a bakery where the shutters were still down. They walked fast, heads lowered, while scooters whined past carrying polystyrene boxes full of octopus and mackerel toward Jagalchi.

At the corner, a woman in rubber market boots lifted her hand twice from the driver's seat of a dented white van. She was in her fifties maybe, with a floral visor and a lipstick shade too bright for dawn. A rosary hung from her rearview mirror, wrapped around a tiny LED flashlight.

"You're late," she said as they climbed in. "I missed the first auction and my sister's going to accuse me of sabotage. Buckle."

"Ms. Bae?" Ina asked.

"People call me that when they owe me money. You can call me Sun-mi." She pulled into traffic with one hand and reached back with the other to pass Ji-eun a mint. "Chew this. You look like you're going to throw up, and this van's suspension is already a moral problem."

Ji-eun obeyed automatically. The mint clicked between her teeth.

Sun-mi kept talking as she drove, not to extract information but because silence made her itchy. She complained about tuna wholesalers, bragged about her daughter passing a civil service exam on the fourth try, and swore she could spot Seoul liars by their umbrella choices. Every sentence ended with the sound of someone who had long ago accepted chaos as normal working conditions.

When they reached the fish-market apartment, she killed the engine and pointed up at a narrow concrete building squeezed between a bait shop and a karaoke bar that would not open until dark.

"Fifth floor. Door code is your birthday if you're honest and your ex's birthday if you're not. I stocked rice, kimchi, and clean towels. No balcony photos. Drones love this block. If anyone knocks and says delivery, ignore. If anyone knocks and says church, maybe open. Depends which church."

She looked at Ji-eun directly then, eyes sharp behind smudged liner.

"I don't care who you are on television. In this building you're a woman who needs sleep and soup. Don't die on my floor. I just had the plumbing fixed."

Ji-eun blinked, then gave a short, real nod. "Yes, ma'am."

"Good girl. Bad hair color, though."

Ji-eun touched her hood and almost smiled.

Upstairs, the apartment was small and clean in the way of places kept for emergencies: no framed photos, no clutter, one extra phone charger in a basket with travel-size toothpaste and two packs of instant seaweed soup. Through the cracked window, harbor air pushed in smelling of salt, diesel, and blood from the fish stalls below. Gulls screamed like hinges that needed oil.

Ina locked the door, checked the second lock, then checked again. She made Ji-eun shower while she scanned local news feeds and clipped blackout curtains over the window with clothespins she found in a drawer.

By noon, the pressure campaign had escalated.

Agency counsel sent a PDF titled TEMPORARY WELLNESS CONSENT with signature lines already filled using Ji-eun's digital stamp.

Council ops sent a location pin for Seongdong and the message CONFIRM ETA.

Unknown numbers sent threats that pretended to be concern.

If she is not controlled she will embarrass everyone.

Take her to private clinic now. She needs chemical rest.

You will both be safer if she does not speak for 48 hours.

Ina deleted none of them. She forwarded everything to an encrypted folder and labeled it coercion_live.

Ji-eun came out of the shower in a faded T-shirt with a cartoon whale on it, borrowed from the emergency drawer. Her wet hair was darker than on stage, nearly black. Without makeup, she looked younger and older at once, the way people did after public humiliation when adrenaline had drained and shame was still in the bloodstream.

They ate reheated pollack soup at the floor table. Ji-eun held the metal bowl with both hands and blew on each spoonful as if this were all she needed from the world.

Then the intercom buzzed once and died. Both of them froze.

Ina put down her spoon, slid to the wall, and checked the peephole through the taped edge of the curtain. No one at their door. Voices from the stairwell. A boy laughing two floors down. A woman scolding him in a Busan accent thick enough to bend vowels.

Ji-eun exhaled slowly. "I hate that my body does this now."

"Mine too."

"No, yours does strategy. Mine does prey."

Ina sat back, anger lifting in her throat like bile. "They taught you that. They can unteach it, or we can."

Ji-eun gave her a look. "You're trying to write a hopeful ending while the building still shakes."

"I'm trying to keep you from skipping to the ending while we're still in the middle."

Ji-eun snorted despite herself. "Terrible line."

"Not my best work."

By late afternoon, the storm that had followed them south came in hard and loud. Rain hammered the metal railing outside and turned the alley below into moving silver. They sat on opposite ends of the futon, each with a phone and no appetite for what was on it.

At 17:12, Ji-eun stood and walked to the window.

"I'm going live at six," she said.

Ina set her phone down. "No."

The refusal came out sharper than she meant. Her pulse kicked under the burn scar at her left wrist, a private metronome she had trained herself to ignore and could not ignore now.

Ji-eun did not turn. "I wrote it already. Three minutes. Names, amounts, the rider clauses, the sedatives. I say I'm alive and I'm not apologizing. Then I log out."

"You think that ends it."

"I think it starts something honest."

Ina heard the wobble under the word honest and hated that she could map it like studio audio, where to cut, where the breath broke.

"It starts a feeding frenzy and gives them perfect footage to claim you're unstable."

"I'm unstable. Have you looked at me."

Ina stood. Rain hit the window in sheets, loud enough that they both had to raise their voices.

She was already seeing tomorrow's headlines and Ji-eun's face trapped under each one: unstable idol, dangerous witness, disposable liability.

"Being hurt is not instability. Being trapped and furious is not instability. They will still use those words if you hand them this video."

Ji-eun turned then, phone in her fist. "What do you want from me? Breathe quietly in a borrowed T-shirt while men in suits decide whether I'm useful again? That's your plan?"

For a beat Ina saw two versions of her layered together: the trained performer with her shoulders locked and the twenty-four-year-old whose left hand would not stop shaking.

"My plan is to buy you legal and physical room."

"With what. Hope. Encryption. Your aunt's church account."

The jab was cruel and accurate and Ji-eun knew it. She winced a half-second too late.

"I'm sorry," she said, then shook her head hard. "No, I'm not sorry enough to stay quiet. I can't carry this by smiling. I can't."

She unlocked her screen and opened the livestream app. Her thumb hovered over START.

Ina crossed the room in two steps and put her hand over Ji-eun's, not wrestling, just blocking the motion.

"If you press that, they sue you by midnight, freeze your family accounts by breakfast, and drag you to a clinic on a welfare order by tomorrow afternoon. They'll say you're delusional and exhausted and they're helping. You know they will."

Ji-eun's jaw worked. "Then I disappear."

"How."

"I walk out and don't come back."

"In this weather, with your face on every homepage. You won't make it six blocks."

"Then I find a bridge."

The sentence arrived without drama, flat as inventory, and that frightened Ina more than tears ever could. She loosened her grip, guided Ji-eun down to the floor, and let her own knees slam the coffee table hard enough to rattle the bowls.

"Look at me," Ina said.

Ji-eun stared at the phone.

"Look at me."

She did.

"I'm not going to drug you," Ina said. "I'm not going to tie your hands with legal language and call it care. I'm not handing you back to them sedated. I'd rather get arrested for kidnapping than do that."

Ji-eun swallowed. Her pupils were huge in the dim room.

"But I need you here tonight," Ina continued. "Alive. Angry. Mouthy. I need your brain intact tomorrow, not scorched by a panic broadcast they can spin. Give me tonight."

Ji-eun laughed once, a raw scraping sound. "You keep saying one night like it's cheap."

"It's expensive as hell. I'm paying for it with my own neck."

Ina peeled the phone from Ji-eun's fist, closed the livestream app herself, and held the power button until the screen went black. She slid the dead phone under the upturned soup pot on the counter and set the lid on top.

Outside, thunder rolled over the harbor. The window frame rattled. Ina stood, threw the deadbolt, hooked the security chain, and pulled the spare keyring off the nail by the door into her pocket.

She came back with a glass of water and pressed it into Ji-eun's hand. "Drink."

Ji-eun drank in three hard swallows, breathing through her nose between each one.

"Say it out loud," Ina said. "Short horizon. Tonight."

Ji-eun wiped her mouth with the back of her hand. "Tonight I stay in this apartment. No live, no bridge, no disappearing. I stay alive until morning."

Ina let out one breath she had been holding since Jamsil. "Okay."

Somewhere in the apartment below, a radio switched from talk show to trot music and the singer's high vibrato bled through the floor.

Ji-eun set the empty glass on the table and looked at Ina's backpack. "What are you offering me, exactly."

Ina went to her backpack, dug out the folder with the copied contract rider Ji-eun had passed her in chapter eight, and laid it on the table between them. The paper edges were already soft from handling.

"This," Ina said. "I get you out of this debt weapon. Full stop."

Ji-eun stared at her. "Do not do that."

"Do not do what."

"Do not promise heroic impossible bullshit to keep me from a ledge."

"I'm not promising heroics. I'm promising work."

"You can't cancel a contract because you feel bad for me."

"I know. So I won't do feelings. I'll do leverage."

She sat opposite Ji-eun and tapped the rider clauses one by one.

"Clause 4.2, mandatory sedation and behavioral management under reputation risk. That violates medical consent law if we prove coercion. Clause 6.1, immediate debt acceleration on moral harm. Overbroad and challengeable if we show unconscionable enforcement against a minor-signing trainee. Clause 7.3, arbitration venue stacked by their own sponsor firm. That's a conflict. It's ugly, but it's not untouchable."

Ji-eun's lips parted. "You already mapped this."

"I've been mapping it since you handed it to me. I just didn't have enough to move."

"And now."

"Now you're the witness and the harmed party and the public face they can't quietly disappear without making noise. That's risk, but it's also leverage."

Ji-eun looked down at the number printed in the debt schedule: KRW 320,000,000. She traced the commas with one nail.

"I can't pay that if they call it."

"I'm not asking you to pay it."

"Who then. You."

Ina held her gaze. "If it comes to cash before court, I will find it."

"From where."

"I don't know yet. I'll steal, borrow, and burn every favor I've got left. I won't let them use that number as a choke chain."

Ji-eun's eyes filled abruptly, and she got angry at her own tears.

"You're making this worse," she said. "If you fail, I've got to watch you drown too."

Ina gave a tired half-smile. "I'm already in the water."

For a long moment, neither spoke. Rain eased. The harbor noises returned: truck brakes, shouted prices, gulls fighting over offal.

Ji-eun pushed the contract toward Ina. "Say it cleanly, then. No metaphors."

Ina nodded. She put both palms flat on the table, like swearing in.

"I'll get you free of Haneul's contract penalties," she said. "I'll challenge the debt and move to block collection. If they call the three hundred twenty million, I'll take that fight and that cost with you. I won't hand you back. I won't let them sedate you. I won't let them silence you. That's my promise."

Ji-eun searched her face for the gap, the caveat, the producer note hidden in the margin. She did not find one.

"Okay," she whispered.

She set her phone down screen-first and pushed it across the table to Ina. It was not surrender; it was temporary custody, a trust shaped like a bruise.

At 18:03, Ina's phone lit again with a calendar alarm she had ignored all day.

COUNCIL SUMMONS: Emergency strategy review, Seongdong safehouse, 18:30 mandatory attendance.

She looked at it, then at Ji-eun, who was sitting cross-legged on the floor in borrowed whale pajamas, eyelids heavy from exhaustion and no chemicals, cheeks flushed from soup and crying and anger.

Ina swiped the summons away.

At 18:09, Kwon called. She let it ring out.

At 18:15, a voicemail arrived. Ina put it on speaker because secrecy would only rot things faster.

Kwon's voice came through thin and precise, each word clipped like paper.

"Writer Seo, you have failed to appear for a mandatory council review. Your unilateral relocation of Park Ji-eun constitutes operational interference. Return to Seoul and transfer her to approved supervision by 22:00. At 22:01, your stipend channel and legal indemnity will be suspended; at 09:00 tomorrow, agency counsel will file for emergency welfare custody and our liaison will provide your movement log. This is not escalation. It is sequence."

The line disconnected.

Ji-eun hugged her knees. "Support protections. He makes prison sound like customer service."

Ina snorted. "He thinks diction is governance."

Ji-eun leaned her head back against the wall. "Are we fucked."

"Yes," Ina said. "But we're fed, dry, and not sedated, so it's not the worst version of fucked."

A short laugh escaped Ji-eun. "You're a terrible comfort person."

"I'm all we have right now."

"Then stay."

Ina stayed.

Night came early under the weather. She made up the futon with clean sheets that smelled faintly of bleach and sea salt. Ji-eun fell asleep in uneven drops, jerking awake twice at hallway noises, then finally staying down with one hand under her cheek and the other curled around nothing.

Ina sat at the floor table with the contract rider, a notebook, and her phone on airplane mode except for one encrypted channel. She listed names: labor lawyer in Nampo who owed Hyejin a favor from a church bookkeeping mess, journalist in Osaka who loved contract abuse stories, former idol who had won a partial debt challenge and now sold vocal warm-up videos online to pay legal bills.

She wrote numbers in the margin. 320,000,000 principal. Estimated filing costs. Emergency housing. Family transfer buffer if Ji-eun's mother got targeted. Every figure looked obscene and necessary.

Around midnight, the rain stopped. Market trucks started up again below, engines coughing to life one by one. Ina flexed her left wrist where the burn scar tightened in damp weather and kept writing until the page edges grew soft under her hand.

She had missed the council summons. She had ignored direct orders. She had put a public figure under unauthorized protection in a city full of cameras. She had promised money she did not have and legal victories she had not earned.

For the first time since Jamsil, the panic in her chest shifted shape. It was not gone; it had been reassigned.

Across the room, Ji-eun slept without pills, mouth slightly open, hair drying in rough waves against the borrowed pillow. Every few minutes, her left hand twitched once, then stilled.

Ina watched that hand for a long time. Before she finally lay down on the blanket beside Ji-eun's bed, she opened a new page in the notebook and wrote at the top:

GET HER OUT, THEN PAY WHAT IT COSTS.


# Chapter 11

Ina lay awake on the blanket she had dragged to the floor beside Ji-eun's bed, counting gull cries through walls thin enough to leak everything. Salt came in from the harbor vents. Diesel pooled low in the hallway, damp as laundry left overnight. At four-thirty the birds started up, and by five their cries sounded like old hinges forced open.

Her notebook from the night before stayed open under her wrist, GET HER OUT, THEN PAY WHAT IT COSTS pressed hard enough to ghost onto the page beneath.

Ji-eun slept in short stuttering blocks, one hand curled under her cheek, the left hand twitching every few minutes like she was still hitting marks in front of a camera nobody had turned off yet. On the chair by the window, Ji-eun's stage shoes sat in a neat pair, cleaned with an old toothbrush and never worn outside. She kept them spotless like relics from a religion she had quit but did not trust herself to mock.

Ina's phone lit without buzzing. New meeting notice from Seongdong. Attendance mandatory. Eleven-thirty. Final readiness review: Mercury Night.

She read the line three times and felt the floor vinyl press its cold grid pattern into her bare forearm. She had skipped one council summons yesterday while moving Ji-eun between two taxis and a fish broker's storage loft. Kwon had not yelled on the call. He had thanked her for "protective initiative" in that calm voice he used when he was putting a knife in and wanted you to hold still.

Ji-eun rolled over and opened one eye. "You look like shit. Is it them?"

"It's a review meeting."

"That means you don't want me asking." Ji-eun pulled herself upright, hair flattened on one side, voice rough and almost middle-school plain. "Do I need to pack again?"

"Not yet."

"So yes, then."

Ina stood and stretched her back until it clicked twice. "Stay inside today. Mrs. Guk is coming at nine with lunch and the fake pharmacy receipts. If anyone knocks before she gets here, don't answer."

Ji-eun gave her the smile she used when she knew a director was lying to her and needed her to cooperate anyway. "Don't do the subtitle-editor voice on me. It makes me want to throw things."

Ina almost laughed. Almost. "I'll call you from the train."

She showered in water that never got fully hot. She dried her hair with a towel that smelled faintly of squid stock and bleach, then put on the only blouse she had that still looked like Seoul. In the mirror she looked older than thirty-five and younger than tired. A burn scar crossed her left wrist. Ink shadow stained the side of her finger where pen grooves never washed clean. She looked like somebody who had learned to stay composed in fluorescent light because fluorescent light never asked if she was okay.

Mrs. Guk arrived on time with an insulated pot and a canvas bag full of forged clinic forms. She was sixty if she was a day, with a permanent rubber thimble on her right index finger from mending trawler nets, and she kept hard candy in her bra strap so she could hand one to nervous children without digging through pockets. She did not ask Ina where she was going. She glanced once at Ina's blouse, once at her shoes, and said, "If it runs late, call before dark. The stairwell bulb on the third floor is dead again."

On the KTX north, Ina sat beside a man in a construction uniform who fell asleep before Ulsan and leaned into her shoulder every time the car swayed. She let him. The seatback in front of her had been repaired with gray tape, and the tape edge kept lifting then sticking back down each time her knee touched it. She opened the Mercury brief and read the latest packet timing notes.

Election-night model: high-credibility trigger window from 20:40 to 21:20.

Secondary amplification from pre-seeded fan channels and civic rumor accounts.

Deepfake insert sequence dependent on synchronized testimony leak.

They had finally stopped pretending it was an archival release campaign. They were building a panic event and giving it a historical justification afterward.

Ina typed a page of delay arguments and deleted half of them. She needed to lead with technical points, never moral ones. If she started with harm in that room, Kwon would hear sentiment and everyone else would hear weakness. She rebuilt the list around failure probability: lip-sync phase drift under compression; accent contour mismatch in northern vowels; metadata chain contamination from Jamsil remnants; route security overstretch after recent seizures. She wrote each point in full sentences. Then she wrote one line at the bottom and did not delete it.

If this launches now, people die in the confusion before anyone verifies anything.

By the time she reached Seoul the sky had gone that polished gray that made even expensive buildings look like they had not slept. A taxi took her to the Seongdong print-shop entrance used for old meetings. Two men she had never seen before were stationed by the loading bay with earpieces and patient faces. One of them held out a tray without looking at her.

"Phone. Watch. Access card."

"I need the card for internal doors."

"Not today."

She put everything on the tray and felt the little shame of being searched in front of a parked truck with flattened tires and rainwater pooled in the bed. The guard gave her a paper visitor strip with red ink at the edge. The strip read TEMPORARY.

Inside, the building smelled the same as always: hot server fans, stale coffee, offset ink. Somebody had changed the hallway bulbs to a cooler white, and the place now read as a failing clinic instead of a conspiracy.

The meeting room windows were still blocked with frosted vinyl. Kwon sat at the head of the table in a dark suit and pale tie, silver hair combed back, posture straight enough to make everyone else's spine look soft. To his right sat legal and comms. To his left sat technical operations. Song Mira from election analytics had three sharpened pencils lined up parallel to her notebook and a tiny brass inhaler on a chain around her neck; she took one measured breath from it every fifteen minutes as if she had timed her own lungs.

Kwon looked up when Ina entered. "Writer Seo. Thank you for coming on short notice."

She took the last open chair. "I was already on my way."

That was a lie, and it made nobody here safer.

Kwon nodded to Song. "Give us the volatility frame."

Song stood, projected the district map, and began in a voice that sounded perpetually one cup of tea away from steady. "On current models, undecided concentration is still highest in river-adjacent precincts and military-family clusters. Sentiment keeps swinging with live media cues between 20:30 and 21:30. If we miss that window, post-result consolidation cuts narrative elasticity by about forty percent."

She clicked to the next slide: turnout lines, rumor velocity curves, channel overlap. Every color trend ended at the same black bar labeled Mercury Night.

Kwon folded his hands. "We are not discussing whether to proceed. We are discussing precision."

Ina opened her notebook. "Then that's exactly why we need a delay."

She kept her tone level. "Current face model still breaks at broadcast compression. Mouth edges jitter on hard consonants. You can hide some of that with motion blur, but not enough for live scrutiny. Accent modeling still leans south on open vowels, and I'm the only person in this building who can hear that in real time. Route security is unstable after Jamsil and the raid cycle. If this packet drops while channels are raw, we get traceable leaks before we get uptake."

Bae cut in before Park could speak. "People don't run phonetics in a panic. They forward whatever matches the mood."

Ina turned to him. "They don't run phonetics, no. They smell a fake in two seconds and hand us to every counterintel desk in Seoul."

Park from legal tapped his pen. "Your recommendation?"

"Minimum three-week delay. We run falsification drills, then push a decoy rumor event first and measure correction speed."

Comms chief Bae snorted quietly. He wore fingerless cycling gloves indoors because his hands shook when they got cold, and he kept rubbing his thumb across the seam like he was trying to sand it smooth. "Three weeks and we miss the only night people are primed to believe a betrayal clip."

Ina did not look at him. "Three weeks and we don't torch the whole operation on a bad render."

Kwon watched her like he was waiting for a student to locate the answer he had already written on the board. "What you call obvious is often sufficient. Public cognition under electoral pressure does not require flawless artifacts. It requires emotional timing and institutional hesitation."

"Institutional hesitation means people make stupid decisions in the gap." Ina heard her own voice tighten. "At Jamsil, the rumor spike hit at 20:52. By 21:06 Songpa police had five North-born delivery drivers facedown in a parking lot, and one left with his jaw wired shut. That's fourteen minutes. That's why I'm asking for three weeks."

"No, this isn't abstract. At Jamsil--"

"Writer Seo." Kwon's voice stayed soft. "We have data on many things. Data does not govern history. Windows do."

She reached for the folder she had brought from Busan and slid a page across to Song: error captures, frame stills, timing drift. Song scanned it quickly, then looked at Kwon, not Ina.

"The artifacts are real," Song said. "Not catastrophic yet, but real."

"Of course they are real," Kwon replied. "Every instrument has failure signatures."

Ina leaned forward. "Then why are we acting like this is ready?"

Kwon tilted his head. "Because readiness is not perfection. It is alignment with consequence. Election night offers us ambiguity dense enough to survive first contact. After certification, every institution closes ranks around whichever story won. We either force reconsideration in the volatile window or we spend two years shouting at a locked door."

His language always did this: took a person and replaced them with a horizon line.

Ina tried another route. "Then let me run control-room oversight. If timing is fixed, at least give me final veto on release if the render glitches."

Silence moved around the table like a draft.

Kwon opened a slim aluminum case and turned it so they could all see what was inside: a matte-black hardware token the size of a lighter, etched with a tiny winged Mercury and a serial strip. The payload key. The room's center of gravity shifted one inch toward it.

He closed the case with a click. "Final authorization will be held by Technical and Security as of today."

Ina stared at him. "You're cutting narrative review out of a narrative operation."

"I am reducing cross-pressure in the last phase."

"That is a pretty phrase for blind launch."

Kwon's eyes did not move. "You are fatigued, Writer Seo. You have had two months of divided loyalties, one detainment sequence, and one high-profile extraction in Busan that was operationally useful but structurally undisciplined. I do not question your intelligence. I question your current fit for terminal decisions."

There it was: the bureaucratic language for removal, ironed flat.

Ina heard chairs shift, paper move, someone swallow. She could have thrown the folder. She could have stood and called him what he was. Instead she forced herself to count the fan rotations she could hear through the wall, one low whine every three seconds, until her mouth stopped shaking.

"You recruited me because I could hear when a line sounded wrong," she said. "Now you're telling me that hearing wrong is disloyal."

"I am telling you that urgency has replaced your discipline."

"Urgency is what you're selling right now."

For the first time he looked almost tired. "We are past the stage where delay equals prudence. Delay now equals surrender to the status quo."

Ina laughed once, short and ugly. "You keep saying status quo like it's weather. Fourteen minutes of bad signal put five men on asphalt and one in surgery."

Kwon folded the aluminum case into his portfolio. "I have said it many times. I simply do not recite it as apology."

Song inhaled from her brass capsule and kept her gaze on her notebook. Bae checked his watch. Park closed the legal file without reading the last page.

Kwon stood, signaling end of meeting. "Mercury Night launch is fixed for by-election night, twenty-one ten release queue, twenty-one seventeen full dissemination. Writer Seo will continue script and language assets under revised permissions. Access changes are effective immediately."

The line hit with the blunt finality of a stamp on wet paper. The launch was fixed. Her permissions were revised. The change was effective immediately.

Ina stood too fast and the chair legs scraped. "I want that in writing."

"You will receive protocol memo C-11 within the hour."

"And if I object formally?"

"You may object formally."

He offered the ghost of a courteous smile. "It will not alter schedule."

The meeting broke. People gathered cables and folders with the subdued speed of staff who had already decided what they would tell themselves about what happened here. Nobody made eye contact with Ina except Song, briefly, and Song's look was not pity. It was warning.

In the hall, a junior access coordinator named Hwang Yoori waited by the badge station. Ina remembered her from payroll signatures: twenty-something, blunt bangs, little silver stars painted on two fingernails and chipped down to points. She kept a roll of mint gum clipped to her lanyard and offered one to everyone who was about to be angry, as if menthol could preempt violence.

"Writer Seo," Yoori said softly, already apologizing with her posture. "I need your old credential."

Ina handed over the plastic card. Yoori fed it into a slot, watched the red light blink, then printed a new one with a yellow stripe.

"Editorial zones only," she said. "No server floor, no launch room, no comms bridge. Elevator access capped at level three unless escorted."

Ina took the card. It weighed nothing and still felt heavier.

"Who else got revised?"

Yoori glanced down the corridor before answering. "Only you."

Then, because she was young and could not stop herself from being a person inside this machine, she added, "My brother follows your old drama with my mom. She likes the grandmother character."

Ina swallowed and slipped the card into her wallet where the old one used to sit. "Tell your mom she had better taste than this building."

On the taxi ride back to Seoul Station, protocol memo C-11 arrived in her encrypted inbox. Three paragraphs of legal paste, two bullet points on compliance, one line confirming launch schedule fixed to by-election window. She read it again on the platform while a teenage couple argued about whose turn it was to buy fish cakes. Their fight was petty and alive and had nothing to do with national destiny. She wanted to step into it and stay there.

By the time she reached Busan it was night and raining hard enough to erase the top floors of the harbor cranes. The apartment stairwell smelled of mold and fried mackerel. Third-floor bulb dead, just as Mrs. Guk had said. Ina climbed by phone light and found Ji-eun sitting at the table in an oversized sweatshirt, scraping nail polish off her thumb with a butter knife.

"You're late," Ji-eun said. "I made ramyeon and forgot to eat it."

Ina set down her bag. "Did anyone come?"

"Only Mrs. Guk, and she yelled at me for chopping garlic wrong." Ji-eun studied Ina's face. "How bad?"

Ina could have lied cleanly. She could have bought one more night of quiet. Instead she pulled out the new yellow-striped credential and dropped it on the table.

Ji-eun let out a low whistle. "They benched you."

"Editorial confinement."

"You look like someone took your teeth."

"Close enough."

Ji-eun pushed the cold ramyeon bowl toward her. "Eat first. Fall apart after."

Ina took two bites because the noodles were there and her body demanded tribute before despair. Then she called Minjae from the burner he had given her in chapter-seven trust and chapter-nine panic, and told him to meet before dawn at the auction docks. He did not ask why. He only said, "Pier 4, back stairs. Wear shoes you can run in."

The harbor before sunrise was all metal noise and cheap light. Forklifts beeped in reverse. Men in rubber aprons dragged crates over wet concrete. Loudspeakers barked lot numbers while gulls tried to steal fish heads from the sorting bins. Ina waited under a corrugated awning where rain bounced off the edge in thick ropes.

Minjae came in from the service lane carrying a plastic tool case and a black umbrella turned inside out by the wind. He had not shaved. His hair was flattened by rain. One sleeve rode up enough to show the ridge of scar tissue across his shoulder, pale against wet skin. He set the broken umbrella in a bin and walked up without kissing her.

"You sound wrecked on the phone," he said. "What happened?"

She handed him the yellow card.

His mouth tightened. "Jesus."

"Launch fixed. By-election night. Twenty-one seventeen full push."

He stared at the card a second longer, then gave it back. "Kwon did this himself?"

"In front of everyone."

"And you asked for delay."

"Technical delay, controlled drills, anything."

He rubbed rainwater off his jaw with the heel of his hand. "He was never going to give it to you once he smelled dissent."

"I didn't ask what he smelled." Ina stepped closer so the auction noise could not eat her words. "I need a sabotage route."

Minjae looked at her as if she had switched languages mid-sentence. "Define sabotage."

"Checksum corruption at ingest. Spoofed clock skew. Kill a repeater node right after first release. I don't care which lever, I care that the clip doesn't propagate cleanly."

He gave a short, incredulous laugh. "That's not a lever, that's twelve crews getting interrogated and maybe disappeared."

"If it propagates cleanly, we get panic sweeps. You know that."

"I know exactly what panic sweeps look like." His voice stayed low but got sharper. "I also know what a network purge looks like when brokers think somebody burned the route on purpose."

He opened the tool case and took out a folded map laminated with clear tape. Route marks in red and blue crossed city names and coast lines like cracked veins.

"These two channels pay my sister's floor rent in Moran and the police envelope that keeps her landlord from reporting her phone." He tapped twice near the edge of the map. "This one moves antibiotics and memory cards together because customs ignores medicine crates half the time. If I jam Mercury from inside, broker audits hit all three routes. Not maybe. Always."

Ina stared at the map until the marker colors blurred. "Then we do it from outside."

"Outside still points back to me."

"Everything points back to you because they built it that way."

"Yes." He snapped the map shut. "That is exactly my point."

Rain hammered the awning. A forklift screeched turning too fast. Somewhere behind them two buyers started shouting over a missing crate and the words dissolved into dialect and curses.

Ina said, "If you do nothing, Mercury launches and your sister lives in the aftermath anyway."

"If I sabotage, she doesn't get to have an aftermath." He took a breath, then another, like he was counting bolts on a panel. "Ina, listen to me. I can harden one neighborhood channel. I can move warning texts through church lists before the clip drops. I can prep two extraction vans. I can't torch the core route. I won't."

"Won't or can't?"
