His face shut down for a beat. Then he laughed once, bitter. "Both. Happy?"

She hated the question as soon as it left her mouth. She hated his answer more because it was true.

"I'm out of decision rooms," she said. "I'm asking you because there's nobody left to ask."

"Then stop asking the machine for mercy and get out before it eats you."

"Don't give me survival slogans."

"I'm not giving slogans." He stepped in, voice rough now, no shield. "Run. Take Ji-eun and run south or west or anywhere without this signal radius. I can get you on a cargo ferry under crew manifests by tomorrow night. You disappear for two weeks, maybe three, you come back when the first crackdown cycle burns out."

She stared at him. "You're asking me to abandon everyone in range."

"I'm asking you to stay alive long enough to do something later."

"Later is what people say when they want today to happen to someone else."

"Later is what people say when they've seen bodies and know they can't stop the first hit." He dragged a hand through wet hair, suddenly exhausted. "I'm not Kwon, Ina. I don't have speeches for this. I've got route math and a sister with one winter coat and a landlord who counts every visitor at her door."

There it was, the private register he almost never let out in daylight: not cynical, just terrified and practical in the same breath.

Ina stepped back under the awning. Water dripped from the corrugated edge onto her sleeve and ran cold down to her wrist scar. She thought of Ji-eun's stage shoes lined up by the window, right shoe toe split where she kept pivoting on bad stage floors. Hyejin hunched over the church ledger with swollen knuckles, pressing numbers so hard the pencil snapped because computers made evidence easy to seize. Her father kept stacking rent notices under grocery flyers until the envelopes turned red and men came to the door before breakfast.

She could run.

The word sounded clean, and clean was suspicious.

"If I run," she said quietly, "Kwon gets uncontested launch. No language check, no internal friction, nothing. He already cut me out because I said no. Running would prove him right about me."

Minjae kicked the leg of an empty crate hard enough to tip it. "'Fuck Kwon's schedule. Fuck this launch.' He jabbed a finger at the yellow-striped card in her hand. 'This isn't about your pride, Ina. It's triage.'"
A buyer two stalls over glanced at them, then yanked his cart the other way, pretending he had not heard.

"It's not pride. It's leverage."

"You don't have leverage anymore."

"I've got memory. I've got documents. I've got people who still pick up my calls."

"For now." He looked toward the dock gate, then back at her. "If Mercury goes live and they need a culprit, they'll pin it on whoever's easiest to narrate. You're almost gift-wrapped."

"I know."

"Then why are you standing here asking me to break the only lines feeding my family?"

She met his eyes. "Because I'm out of clean options and I thought you might still choose ugly with me."

His jaw jumped. He looked away first.

When he spoke again, his tone was flatter, operational. "I can't sabotage. That answer doesn't change if you ask me ten different ways."

A fish cart rolled past them trailing brine and crushed ice. The worker pulling it had one earbud in and was singing off-key to himself, cheerful in a place built from exhaustion.

Minjae closed the tool case and clipped it shut. "I'll push warning prep through non-attributed lists. I'll move two backup phones to your aunt by noon. I'll send you district heat guesses every three hours on launch day. That's what I can do without signing my sister's death warrant."

Ina nodded once. Agreement felt like loss in her mouth.

He touched her elbow, then did not let go. "Come with me anyway. Just this once. I can take you to the old motel above Yeongdo, no records, no cameras in the stairwell. Sleep for eight hours. Then decide."

"I can't. Ji-eun's alone."

"Bring her."

"And then what? We hide in mold and cigarette curtains while the city tears itself open?"

He did not answer right away. Wind snapped a tarp loose on the next dock and men shouted to pin it down.

"And then maybe you're alive when the tearing stops," he said.

She wanted to lean into him. She wanted the simple body answer: warmth, breath, temporary oblivion. The two of them had used each other that way before and called it trust because they needed the word. But she could not make herself step forward.

"I can't run," she said.

"You can. You won't."

"Fine."

"Fine."

The word clanged between them, dropped metal on concrete.

He let go of her elbow. "I've got to move before daylight checks start."

"Minjae."

He waited.

"If it goes bad faster than expected, if they sweep in your sister's block first, call me before you call anyone else."

His mouth twisted into something close to a smile and nothing like one. "You still think you can fix timing with language."

"Sometimes I can."

"Sometimes." He lifted the broken umbrella from the bin, looked at it, threw it back. "Don't trust any message tagged with weather emojis on launch day. They're burning that code set."

That was his goodbye, practical and intimate at once.

He walked toward the service lane without turning around.

Ina stood under the awning until her socks were wet through. Then she went back up the hill to the apartment, bought coffee from a machine that tasted like scorched plastic, and climbed the dark stairwell by memory. Inside, Ji-eun was awake and practicing smile variants in the bathroom mirror, soft and bright and apologetic and defiant, one after another like scales.

"You saw him," Ji-eun said from the doorway.

"Yes."

"Did he fix it?"

Ina put the yellow-striped card on the table beside last night's cold ramyeon bowl. "No."

Ji-eun exhaled slowly. "Then what now?"

Ina opened her laptop, entered her council credentials, and watched three access requests fail in sequence. The launch room was denied. The comms bridge was denied. The server floor was denied. A fourth prompt arrived from legal requiring acknowledgment of revised permissions before she could download even transcript drafts.

What now was this: smaller windows, thinner tools, more expensive decisions.

"Now," she said, "we stay awake."

She signed the acknowledgment with a hand that had started to cramp, then forwarded protocol memo C-11 to two off-book addresses she trusted enough to endanger: the labor-rights attorney in Nampo and the Osaka investigations editor she'd flagged the night before.
The attorney replied in forty-three seconds with a court-duty stamp and a deadline: send full headers by 14:00 or lose emergency filing priority. The editor sent one line after that: "Once this lands, they'll know it was you."
A minute later, Hyejin sent a separate line: "Haneul Self Storage. Striped awning. Bring your mother's keyring."

Outside, the harbor loudspeaker switched to weather warnings for afternoon wind shear, voice metallic and calm. Inside, the apartment refrigerator clicked on and rattled with a loose-tooth chatter. Ji-eun sat across from her and peeled the label from a water bottle in one long careful strip, not looking up, listening to the keys as Ina typed.

Mercury Night had a date now. The room where it would be born had no chair for her.


# Chapter 12

Ina turned her mother's keyring once, twice, until the teeth printed half moons into her palm under the striped awning outside Haneul Self Storage. The keys were older than the building. Eun-sook had wrapped one in red thread, and the thread had gone dark with years of skin oil, like dried blood. Rain laminated Seoul around her: bus stop glass, crosswalk paint, bright fake smiles on campaign posters, everything shone too hard and then went dull when you touched it.

Aunt Hyejin came up the sidewalk in rubber kitchen clogs and a church cardigan carrying fryer grease and mothballs. The odor reached Ina before Hyejin did and dragged up a church-basement memory: flood buckets, wet hymnals, Hyejin assigning work before anyone finished saying hello. Her reading glasses hung on their chain and bounced against her sternum while she walked. She always moved as if she had one more tray to carry and no one else would carry it right.

"You're still here," Hyejin said. She looked at the keyring and then at Ina's face. "Good. I almost decided for both of us and went back home."

Ina heard the part Hyejin did not say: you almost ran. She had, three times, once at every crosswalk on the way here.

"You tried to," Ina said.

"I did." Hyejin tucked wet hair behind her ear. "Then I remembered where she hid the title to my old Matiz. Rice cooker. Inner basket. Your mother did not trust drawers."

She said it like a joke, but her mouth stayed flat.

Ina pushed the glass door. A little bell rang, and the front desk clerk glanced up from a handheld fan pointed at his own face. He wore a Tottenham cap with the brim stained white from salt, and he had three tiny bandages on the same finger, wrapped one above another, as if he kept cutting himself in exactly the same place. Next to his keyboard sat a plastic case full of model trains. One carriage had no wheels.

Ina hated rooms where strangers controlled access to family damage. She always over-corrected into politeness, as if manners could keep grief from spilling.

"Locker number?" he asked.

Ina gave him the number from the notebook margin: B-214.

He stopped fanning himself. "That one has had humidity alerts for months. We called the registered contact. No answer."

"Humidity alerts" sounded like a utility warning, not proof that her mother had been gone long enough for paper to rot in peace.

"Registered contact is dead," Ina said.

The clerk's eyes flicked to Hyejin, then back to the screen. "Right. Sorry. I need an ID and a signature for access after ninety days. Also, no open flames, no smoking, and if you move moldy boxes, don't leave them in the hall."

Hyejin snorted. "Who comes to a locker and starts a bonfire?"

The clerk gave a tired shrug. "A man in August. Insurance fraud."

He handed Ina a clip board and a pale green marker to sign with, no pen, because pens vanished here. She wrote her name and felt the marker squeak on laminated paper. The squeak sounded too much like the cassette rewinds she remembered from childhood, that scratchy reversing whine before Eun-sook played old radio dramas at midnight.

Damp cardboard and rust rode the elevator air. Ina breathed through her mouth and watched floor numbers blink down in red while Hyejin braced both hands on the rail.

"Say it once before we open," Hyejin said.

"Say what?"

"Say that if this is nothing, you will let it be nothing. No grand mission because she tied a ribbon on a key."

Ina laughed once, no humor in it. "I don't do grand missions."

"That's a lie and we both know it." Hyejin stared at the closing doors. "I'm asking for a sentence."

Ina swallowed. The elevator jolted and stopped. "If it's nothing, I'll let it be nothing."

"Good." Hyejin adjusted her glasses and started walking before the doors were fully open.

B-level was a concrete hallway with strip lights and a wet, fungal smell. Locker doors lined both sides like cheap apartment fronts, metal shutters with scratched numbers and little rust blooms around the locks. B-214 sat near the fire exit. The lock cylinder was newer than the surrounding metal. Someone had replaced it at some point, probably after Eun-sook forgot a payment and argued with management. Ina had seen her mother win arguments with silence and a single raised eyebrow. She had thought that was dignity. She had not thought it could also be rehearsal.

She fit the red-thread key, twisted, and felt it catch twice before the latch popped.

The door rolled up with a scream of metal.

The first smell was vinegar. The second was mouse piss. Inside were six boxes, one plastic tote, a folded TV stand, and two old rice sacks stuffed with clothes. Moisture had buckled the cardboard lids into shallow bowls. On top of the nearest box sat an unplugged dehumidifier, yellowed with age, with a strip of masking tape on it that read "DO NOT TRUST THIS" in Eun-sook's blocky handwriting.

"She was still writing jokes to herself," Ina said.

"That wasn't a joke. That machine never worked." Hyejin stepped in and immediately began triage. She tugged out a roll of garbage bags from her tote, spread one on the floor as a clean surface, and set out three clothespins to hold sleeves away from her wrists. She had done this a thousand times in church basements after flood season. "Left pile for paper. Right pile for cloth. If you find anything with rodent droppings, don't touch your face."

"You sound like the public health office."

"I listen when they come yell at us." She looked up. "You should have listened too."

They worked in wet silence for ten minutes. Ina lifted lids and found winter blankets, old drama binders, a dead portable heater, and a stack of unpaid utility notices banded together with a hair tie. The notices were from ten years earlier. Eun-sook had circled due dates in blue ink and written short notes in the margins: "call Thursday," "ask for installment," "no pride." The practical handwriting made Ina want to sit down.

Under the second box she found a rusted biscuit tin full of fountain pen nibs and two foreign coins drilled for necklace use. It was not what she needed.

Under the TV stand she found an envelope addressed to "Hyejin, if I become an idiot." It contained one page of recipes and a list of apology lines, all crossed out. Hyejin read it and laughed so hard she had to wipe her eyes on her sleeve.

"She was going to apologize for stealing my kimchi jars," Hyejin said. "She took them in 2014. Never apologized."

"Did you know about this locker?"

"I knew she had one. I didn't know what she left. She told me, if anything happens, burn the cassettes first." Hyejin folded the recipe sheet with too much care. "I told her to stop talking like a spy novel. She said she didn't have a better genre available."

Ina froze with a stack of moth-eaten coats in her hands. "Cassettes. Plural."

"That's the word she used." Hyejin did not meet her eyes. "I hoped she was being dramatic."

The third box had been sealed with old brown tape now gone gummy. Ina cut it open with her key and peeled back plastic. Inside sat a smaller gray lockbox, heavy enough that she had to brace it against her thigh to lift. The hasp had no lock. The lid stuck until she pressed both thumbs into the rust and rocked it loose.

Inside lay three things wrapped in grocery bags: a C60 cassette in a cracked clear case, two mini DV tapes, and a hardback ledger with water-warped corners. The cassette label read only "S-11 / 2009 / Keep order." The ledger's first page held a hand-drawn index:

1. S-11 audio confession, stitched from source clips.
2. Incident notes: Bukjin, 1998, witness cross-check.
3. Copy pathway instructions.

Ina's hands shook, not from fear exactly, more from the familiar click of narrative pieces finding each other. She hated that part of herself. The part that had survived by treating reality like material.

Hyejin saw the index and sat down on the plastic bag, slowly, as if her knees had become hinges someone else controlled.

"No," she said. "No, she didn't."

"Did not what?"

"She didn't leave this to you. She left this to the grave."

"She indexed it." Ina held up the page. "She wrote copy instructions."

"Your mother wrote many impossible things and expected women to clean up after them." Hyejin's voice got sharper when she was scared. She started stacking boxes that did not need stacking. "I'm not saying she was evil. I'm saying she was talented at asking other people to carry fire in paper bags."

Ina crouched in front of her aunt. "If this says what I think it says, we can't keep pretending she was only a victim in all this."

Hyejin's chin lifted. "You think I've been pretending?"

"I think you've been protecting all of us from details."

"Because details got people killed." Hyejin rubbed her arthritic fingers one by one as if counting beads. "You were ten when the border incident hit the news. You remember the headlines and your mother buying instant noodles in bulk because everyone said war was coming. You don't remember her waking up and vomiting every morning for a month. You don't remember her tearing up every tape in this house and then going out at two in the morning because someone called."

"Then tell me now."

"Now? In a mold closet with rat shit on your shoes?"

"Yes, now."

Hyejin looked at the cassette, then at Ina's face, and whatever argument she had lined up seemed to collapse under the weight of timing. "She told me one sentence. She said, if Ina ever asks why I drank before noon that summer, tell her I helped build a lie and the lie had names."

The strip light above them hummed and flickered. Ina put the cassette back in its case, not gently.

"We're not listening here," Hyejin said quickly. "Not in this building."

"I wasn't planning to."

"And if it's what she said, you don't take it to your phone first. Phones leak. People leak. You leak when you panic."

"Thank you," Ina said. "Very comforting."

"I'm not comfort staff." Hyejin stood with a grunt and grabbed the gray box. "I'm transport. Move."

They signed out forty-three minutes later with two bags of papers, the lockbox, and a damp blanket Hyejin insisted on salvaging because "wool is expensive." The Tottenham-cap clerk offered them a disposable mask packet on their way out. His hand shook when he passed it over.

"You okay?" Ina asked before she could stop herself.

He looked surprised, then embarrassed. "My daughter has an oboe exam. She practices at five in the morning. The neighbors filed a complaint. I didn't know an oboe could sound like a goose being strangled." A ghost of a smile crossed his face. "She says that means she's improving."

"Maybe she's improving," Ina said.

The rain had stopped by the time they reached Hyejin's church kitchen in Seodaemun. The kitchen sat behind the sanctuary, cinderblock walls painted cream, two industrial sinks, one flickering fluorescent tube, and a shelf of dented stockpots that all smelled faintly of garlic no matter how many times they were scrubbed. Hyejin turned on only the hood light above the stove. She never trusted full lighting when she was unsettled. Too much looked back.

"Tea or soju?" she asked.

"Both," Ina said.

"Good answer."

Hyejin set barley tea to boil in a kettle with a broken handle wrapped in electrician's tape. Then she opened a bottom cabinet and took out an old silver cassette deck used for choir practice. The play button had lost its black paint. She wiped the heads with a cotton swab and rubbing alcohol, routine first, crisis second.

"Where did you get this?" Ina asked.

"You think churches throw anything away?" Hyejin snorted. "This survived three pastors and one flood."

They set the ledger and notes beside the deck. Ina flipped pages while the tea steamed. The notes were in Eun-sook's handwriting, but different from grocery lists and bill reminders. The loops were tighter. The nouns were underlined as if they could run.

Bukjin market road, 1998-07-14, 05:37.

Council alias "S-11" approved operational dramatization.

Expected evacuated lane. Actual civilian transit present.

Casualty split by side not reported accurately in state brief.

K.T. insisted on maintaining narrative frame to preserve summit leverage.

Do not trust memory. Use timestamps.

Ina read that line three times.

Do not trust memory. Use timestamps.

Her mother had always told her memory was where dignity lived. Now here was the correction, in pen pressure hard enough to emboss the next page.

"Ready?" Hyejin asked.

Ina nodded.

The cassette went in with a plastic clack. Hyejin pressed play.

At first there was hiss and room tone, then the low grind of a chair being moved. Eun-sook's voice arrived warmer than Ina expected, textured by smoke and age, as if she were in the next room calling through a half-open door.

"If this tape is being played, I am either dead, detained, or finally honest. I suppose all three can happen together." A short pause. "Ina, this section is for you. Hyejin, if you are listening, I am sorry about the jars and a great many other things."

Hyejin muttered, "She starts with jars, unbelievable," but she did not press stop.

Eun-sook continued, the cadence measured, almost broadcast calm. "I am recording this in February 2009 and assembling earlier source clips from 1998. The file label S-11 is not poetry. It was my operational tag for one season. Director Kwon assigned it, because he likes systems that can be denied."

A click. Tape hiss thickened, then shifted to older audio quality. A man's voice, younger but unmistakable in its polished restraint.

"The provocation window closes at dawn," Kwon said. "If we miss this cycle, we lose six months of diplomatic leverage."

Another voice answered, female, maybe Eun-sook in her thirties, harder in the consonants. "There is still traffic on the market road. The evacuation call did not clear all buses."

"There are always civilians at the edge," Kwon said. "History does not wait for perfect weather."

The clip cut.

Ina felt her shoulders pull inward without permission. She could hear a fan in the original room, blades ticking at an uneven rhythm. She imagined two people deciding what counted as weather.

Eun-sook's 2009 voice returned. "That exchange took place at 04:51 before Bukjin. We called it a controlled border theater operation. That phrase should disgust you. It disgusts me now. At the time it felt like professional language, and professional language makes cowards comfortable."

She cleared her throat, paper rustling close to the mic.

"Operational goal: stage a limited clash that could be framed as northern aggression, harden domestic support, and force delayed summit terms favorable to the Restoration bloc. Mechanism: contracted shell team fired tracer rounds from pre-positioned ridge points, then triggered two old mortar stocks for visual confirmation. We expected empty roads because curfew was announced. Curfew implementation failed. A produce truck, one private bus, and at least four pedestrians were still on the Bukjin market road when secondary fire landed. The truck rolled into the drainage ditch and pinned the driver under the steering column; onions and blood ran into the same gutter. The bus windows blew inward, and passengers used apron strings and ticket rolls as tourniquets while they waited for transport that came late."

Hyejin had stopped pretending to drink tea. Her hands were wrapped around the cup only for heat.

Eun-sook read names, each with age and fragment details. Not all. Some marked unknown.

"Kang Yeon-hee, thirty-two, carrying onion sacks, recovered beside the truck ditch."

"Unknown male, approximately fourteen, school satchel with blue zipper, right femoral bleed not controlled in field."

"Pak Sun-im, fifty-nine, left leg traumatic amputation, died during transport after checkpoint delay."

The list went on long enough for Ina to stop tracking count and start tracking breath. Then came one logistics note in the same measured voice: Bukjin clinic had three stretchers for nineteen wounded, so nurses wrote blood types on forearms with laundry marker and laid people on vegetable crates. Ina hated herself for noticing that Eun-sook's delivery stayed smooth while she read the dead. Not smooth like indifference. Smooth like someone who had practiced not breaking where microphones could hear.

Then another source clip. A metal door shut. Voices closer, no room echo.

Kwon: "Civilian numbers are unverified. Do not circulate estimates."

Eun-sook: "Field contact says twelve likely dead on the north side and at least two here."

Kwon: "Do not put numbers in writing. The strategic frame is that deterrence prevented a larger war."

Eun-sook: "And if they ask about the bus?"

Kwon: "Then we call the bus an unconfirmed rumor fed by panic."

A scrape of chair legs. The clip ended.

Ina put her fingertips on the ledger page so hard the damp paper wrinkled. Her burn scar dragged against the edge and stung, an old injury waking up because new pain needed a place to land.

"Fuck," she said quietly.

Hyejin let out one breath that sounded like a laugh and then was not. "She played this for herself, over and over."

Eun-sook came back, older and thinner in tone. "I stayed in role. I edited state radio package lines to remove mention of domestic munitions and redirected visual evidence to archive bins marked maintenance. I told myself if we survived this season, I would leave. I did not leave. I accepted the argument that one ugly lie could preserve a larger possibility. I repeated that argument until it wore a groove in me."

Ina closed her eyes. She remembered her mother at the sink, sleeves rolled, wrist bones thin as chopsticks, telling her to rinse rice three times because clouded water made bad texture. She remembered Eun-sook saying there were no clean stories, only accountable ones. Back then Ina heard wisdom. Tonight she heard confession hidden inside instruction.

The tape clicked forward.

"Director Kwon did not invent cruelty," Eun-sook said. "He organized it. That is different and worse. I helped because I believed narrative pressure could prevent endless border bloodletting. I still believe stories move policy. I no longer believe secrecy keeps stories ethical."

Paper rustled again.

"This packet contains incident notes, chain markers, and two backup pathway options. If you release any part, redact current route identifiers, witness family names, and medical volunteers. Do not sacrifice living civilians to punish dead men."

Another pause. Ina waited for a plea, an absolution request, anything maternal and soft. Instead Eun-sook gave process details, clipped and exact.

"Phase order recommendation. First, verify source integrity with independent timestamp checks and voice comparison. Second, release contextual evidence before the most inflammatory clips. Third, distribute through mirrored civic channels outside single-jurisdiction takedown reach."

She inhaled, and for one second her voice bent toward something private.

"Ina, if this reaches you, I suspect I taught you to confuse endurance with virtue. Do not inherit my silence because you think it is loyalty. Loyalty to what? To men who count bodies by policy outcome? To my vanity?"

The tape went quiet except for hiss. Then, faintly, Eun-sook again, almost a whisper leaning too close to the mic.

"I did not tell your father because he would have looked at me like a witness, not a wife. I could not survive that while I was still useful to people who frightened me. That is not noble. It is what happened."

The cassette reached side end and stopped with a mechanical clunk.

Neither woman moved for several seconds. The hood fan rattled over the stove. Somewhere in the sanctuary a motion light clicked on and off.

Ina stood too fast, knocked her knee against the prep table, and made it two steps before she bent over the sink and retched. Nothing came up except bitter tea. Her throat burned anyway.

Hyejin did not touch her. She set a folded dish towel by Ina's wrist and waited.

"She left us a manual," Ina said when she could speak.

"She left us a bomb with instructions," Hyejin corrected.

"Same thing, depending who opens it."

"No. A manual assumes consent." Hyejin turned the cassette case in her hand like she wanted to snap it and knew she could not. "A bomb takes whoever is nearby."

She got up slowly, knees cracking, and crossed to the sink. She washed a tea cup that did not need washing. "I told her in 2009 to destroy those clips. She said, if I destroy them, only the men survive. I called her dramatic then too."

"You knew this much?"

"Not this much." Hyejin shut off the tap and turned. "I knew Bukjin was not an accident. I didn't know she had source audio with his voice. I didn't know she kept a list with names. She kept that from me because she knew I would force her to pick family over strategy."

"Did she ever pick family?"

Hyejin winced, not from the question, from how quickly Ina asked it.

"She tried," Hyejin said. "Trying and succeeding are not twins."

Ina went back to the ledger. The page edges were soft from water damage. If she rubbed too hard, entire lines would come off on her skin. She thought of every meeting where Kwon used the word necessity with his hands folded like a patient teacher. She thought of the day he gave her a stipend contract while debt collectors called during her mother's funeral week. She thought of how often she had translated harm into cleaner words so she could keep working.

She heard herself ask, "How many casualties did we move past because they were old enough to be history and far enough to be statistics?"

"More than either of us can stomach tonight," Hyejin said. "So don't make a sacred speech. Make a plan."

Ina almost laughed. Even now, Hyejin's answer came as logistics.

"Fine," Ina said. "Plan."

She grabbed a legal pad from the shelf labeled DONATIONS and uncapped a marker; solvent stung the back of her throat and yanked her to old classrooms where she learned to outline lies cleanly. She drew three columns: EVIDENCE, RISK, PROTECTION.

"We start with preservation," she said. "Not release. Preservation."

"Say what that means in grown-up Korean, not your writer jargon."

"Two offline copies tonight. One stays with you, one with me. No cloud, no phone, no USB we already use for route work."

"No." Hyejin's voice cut flat through the room. She pulled her cardigan on with jerky, angry motions and reached for the lockbox. "Nothing stays with me after dawn. Not in this church. I open this kitchen at six. Choir kids and pension ladies come through here with tote bags and gossip. If warrants land, they won't just take me."

"We need distributed custody."

"Then pay for it." Hyejin grabbed her keys and took two steps toward the side door. "Pay real money for anonymous storage and legal cover. Don't hide this under my freezer fish and call it strategy."

"The only liquid money I can move tonight is Appa's rent buffer."

Hyejin stopped at the door but did not turn. "Then choose your first loss out loud."

Ina stared at the legal pad. She wrote in block letters: FIRST LOSS - APPA RENT BUFFER. Her hand shook anyway.

"Fine," she said. "I burn the buffer before sunrise. Two anonymous lockers and a legal retainer before anything goes public."

Only then did Hyejin come back to the table.

"Then authentication," Ina said. "We need voice verification outside our circle. If this gets out and they call it fake, we're dead before day one."

Hyejin nodded once. "Who can do that without selling us?"

Ina thought of three names and crossed out two immediately. One drank and talked too much. One owed Kwon a favor from twenty years ago.

"Professor Kim at Sogang retired from broadcast forensics," Ina said. "He still hates me because I killed one of his pet subtitles in 2018, but he's worse with fake audio."

"Can he keep his mouth shut?"

"If I pitch it as an anonymous civic evidentiary review, he might keep his mouth shut. If he doesn't, we're done."

"Maybe is not a lock."

"Nothing's a lock." Ina wrote AUTH CHECK - EXTERNAL, then added another line beneath it: NO ROUTE CREW UNTIL AUTH PASSES.

"Including Minjae?" Hyejin asked.

Ina hated how quickly she answered. "Including Minjae. If I freeze him out for a day, I'll lose trust. Better that than his sister catching blowback before we lock down names."

"Good," Hyejin said, though her mouth said it cost her.

"Third," Ina said, "phased release. Families first."

Hyejin pointed at her with the dish towel. "Define phased, not dramatic."

Ina wrote PHASE 1 and stopped. Blue zipper. Ticket-roll tourniquet. Vegetable crates under bleeding people. She put the marker down, pressed her knuckles against her mouth, and waited until the room stopped tilting.

"Write the living first," Hyejin said softly. "Dead people can wait ten more minutes. Their children can't."

Ina nodded and started again.

Phase 1: Context packet plus family notice window.
- Chain-of-custody notes.
- Financial links showing Restoration shell payments around Bukjin window.
- Quiet contact to reachable next-of-kin before publication, with transit cash and legal hotline numbers.
- Eun-sook ledger excerpts with names of dead partly masked until family status confirmed.
- No operational route details. No current handlers.

"Phase one says a staged incident happened and gives families a head start before the internet tears at them," Ina said. "Pattern and motive first. We don't feed them to spectacle."

"And Kwon's voice?"

"Phase two."

Phase 2: Audio release, edited for civilian protection.
- Kwon command clips and post-incident suppression language.
- Eun-sook confession segments including her own complicity.
- Spectral analysis report attached.
- Metadata screenshots and checksum chain.

"Why not lead with the voice?" Hyejin asked.

"Because raw rage makes people sloppy. If the first drop is only an old man saying ugly things, his camp calls it deepfake and half the public decides before facts load. I need groundwork first."

Hyejin gave a grudging nod. "You sound like your mother when she was sober and impossible."

Ina kept going.

Phase 3: Dead-man switch archive if suppression escalates.
- Full packet encrypted and mirrored to fan legal teams, diaspora journalists, and two foreign rights groups.
- Auto-release trigger on detention or disappearance.
- Separate civilian redaction index to prevent doxxing by chaos accounts.

She circled CIVILIAN REDACTIONS and wrote beneath it: no child names, no exact home blocks, no current route maps, no church volunteer lists, no clinic rosters.

"We don't become what we're exposing," she said. "No revenge leaks. No collateral theater."

Hyejin sat across from her and rubbed the chain of her glasses between thumb and forefinger. "You said that like a vow."

"Maybe that's it."

"Vows are easy in kitchens." Hyejin leaned forward. "You need to hear this from someone who watched your mother and loved her and wanted to strangle her twice a week. If you do this, you lose money first, then safety, then sleep, then any clean story about yourself. There will be no chapter where people clap because accountability won."

"I know."

"No, you know it like a subtitle line. I'm telling you in grocery terms. Your card will decline. Your father's landlord will call at midnight. Old women at church will stop sitting with me because their sons work in ministries and they're afraid."

Ina met her eyes. "I know that too."

Hyejin's shoulders dropped a fraction. "All right. Then we do it right."

They worked until after midnight. Ina digitized the cassette through the deck's line-out into a laptop with network adapters physically removed. She named files with date and minute marks, no adjectives. Hyejin transcribed ledger names on onion-skin paper in neat columns, pausing every few lines to flex her fingers and shake out pain. She cursed only once, when a water-damaged page tore across a surname.

"Damn it," she said. "He doesn't get erased because mold won."

Ina fetched archival tape from a drawer and helped mend the page. She touched the page, breathed, aligned fibers, and pressed. She had spent years fixing mistranslations by moving one clause at a time until a sentence stopped lying. This felt similar and infinitely heavier.

At 12:47, she used Hyejin's old office desktop to push two timed payments: cash pickup authorization for two anonymous lockers and a retainer transfer to a civil-rights attorney who never wrote full names in email subject lines. The account tagged APPA RENT BUFFER dropped to a number that would not survive another landlord visit.

At one-thirty, her phone buzzed with a text from Dohyun.

You awake? Landlord came again. I told him next week.

Ina stared at the message until the words blurred. Next week meant he had no next week.

She typed: I'm awake. Don't answer unknown calls tonight. I'll handle it.

He replied: Don't write in command voice with me.

Then, after thirty seconds: Sorry. Knee hurts. Couldn't sleep.

Ina put the phone face down. Hyejin watched without asking.

"He doesn't know anything about this tape," Ina said.

"Keep it that way for now," Hyejin said. "One household panic at a time."

By two, the first digital copy was complete. Ina checked waveform integrity against the live tape, then wrote hash values by hand on the legal pad so she would not trust one machine's memory. She labeled the external drive CHURCH INVENTORY 2019 and wrapped it in a grocery bag printed with cartoon pears.

"Pretty disguise," Hyejin said.

"Nobody steals pears."

"You would be shocked."

At two-forty, rain started again, harder, drumming the kitchen vent. Ina sat on an upturned milk crate and read Eun-sook's final note page while audio exported.

If you are deciding whether to use this, ask who pays for your silence. The answer is usually not the men who request it.

There was no apology line and no motherly blessing. There was only cost accounting.

Ina felt grief shift shape. It did not disappear and it did not forgive, but it shifted. She had spent months trying to prove her mother's work served something like reunification, something worth the damage. The tape did not erase that longing. It stripped the romance off it. A border could be wrong and still be weaponized by people who pretended to heal it.

At three-ten, she opened a new document and titled it ARCHIVE LEAK DRAFT - CONTROLLED PHASE.

She wrote:

Objective: establish factual accountability for Bukjin staging and downstream narrative manipulation while minimizing contemporary civilian exposure.

Constraints:
1. Protect currently vulnerable civilians, route workers, clergy volunteers, and medical contacts.
2. Preserve evidentiary chain to withstand deepfake/disinformation counterattack.
3. Sequence disclosures to prevent single-clip sensational collapse.
4. Assume account freezes and device wipes within 24 hours of phase one.

Operational steps:
1. Prepare redaction schema with unique IDs so names can be privately verified by future commission without public disclosure.
2. Build mirrored package for three release windows, each with independent checksum.
3. Pre-brief two legal advocates and one journalist under embargo, contingent on verification packet.
4. Trigger phase one through distributed civic channels during peak traffic, then rotate access keys every six hours.
5. If targeted detention begins, auto-trigger phase three package.

She stopped typing and looked at Hyejin, who was asleep in a plastic chair with her glasses still on and her hands folded over her apron. In sleep, Hyejin looked younger and more stubborn at the same time.

Ina got up, draped a blanket over her aunt's knees, and stood by the sink listening to rain and compressor hum and the tiny click-click of the cassette deck cooling down.

She spoke to the dark window, low enough not to wake Hyejin.

"I'm not doing this because you asked me to," she said, meaning Eun-sook, meaning the tape. "I'm doing it because you didn't stop when you should have, and neither did he, and I already know what silence costs in this house."

The window gave her back a blurred version of her face and the hood light behind it, nothing mystical, just reflection and bad glass.

At dawn, they took a taxi to a twenty-four-hour storage block near Noryangjin and finished the split there, not at church.

Ina paid the locker clerk in cash, signed the receipt under a church meal-inventory alias, and felt the hit of the choice all over again when he counted the bills twice. Locker 17C got the original cassette sealed in a rice bag inside the biscuit tin. Locker 22A got a hashed digital duplicate and chain notes in a sealed envelope for the attorney.

Ina kept digital copy one, the repaired ledger, and the legal pad plan. She left by the side alley to avoid the morning volunteers, who arrived in practical shoes and gentle gossip and did not need this night in their mouths.

Outside, Seoul smelled of wet ginkgo leaves and diesel. Delivery scooters cut through puddles. A bakery down the block opened its shutters and dumped yesterday's bread into a bin with a hollow thud.

Ina walked three blocks before she texted Minjae.

Need to see you. Not by phone. Tonight if possible.

She stared at the sent check mark and then locked the screen.

For months she had told herself she could steer this machine from inside if she worked harder, translated cleaner, pushed back at the right moments, kept debts paid, kept people fed, kept promises in small practical portions. The cassette did not make her pure. It made her done.

By the time she reached the bus stop, the first line of her release statement had settled in her head, hard and plain.

This archive is not a weapon for one faction against another. It is evidence of how both used civilians as materials.

She rewrote it mentally twice, then once more, until every word could survive being quoted by enemies.

The bus came full. She stood in the aisle, one hand on the rail, legal pad pressed under her coat while the carriage lurched through standing water. At the second stop she wrote the line in block letters, tore the sheet clean, and folded it into her coat pocket. She let the city carry her toward whatever came next.


# Chapter 13

Ina came down the paint-chipped stairs to Minjae's workshop a little after eight, rain misting her coat and turning the metal handrail slick under her palm. The first line of the release statement she had drafted on the bus was still folded in her pocket, edges gone soft with sweat. The basement always smelled like hot dust and solder flux, plus the peach hair spray from the nail salon upstairs that leaked through the vents in sweet chemical breaths. Minjae had every lamp on. Light hit the chipped concrete floor in white rectangles, and in each rectangle he had laid out cables by length, coiled like obedient snakes.

He did that when he was scared: sorted hardware like order could be welded into the world.

He looked up from a stripped router and took in her face in one sweep. "You didn't answer three calls."

"I was on Line Two all day. Kwon put me in a room with three policy people and a retired general who thought subtitles were a branch of artillery." She kicked off her wet shoes and held up the bakery bag. "I brought chestnut bread. It already tastes stale."

"Everything tastes stale this week."

He took the bag anyway, thumbed the paper open, tore one bun in half, and passed her the bigger piece before he ate. She watched his hand out of habit, watched how he turned his wrist to protect the old scar tissue across his shoulder even in a simple motion. In the first weeks she had mistaken that guarded movement for aloofness. Then she had seen him asleep once, not long, only twelve minutes between calls, and she had seen him cradle that shoulder against his chest like he was sheltering an animal.

She sat on the floor mat beside his low worktable. The table was crowded with burner phones, two portable SSDs, a coil of shrink tubing, and the cheap blue lighter he always stole from convenience-store counters and then never used. He lit nothing. He just clicked lighters open and closed when he needed to think.

"I listened to the cassette again," she said. "All of it. Slow speed, then normal, then with phase cleanup. There isn't any ambiguity left." 

He exhaled through his teeth. "You've been saying that for four days."

"Because it's true for four days." She wiped a drop of rainwater off her phone and put it face down. "He staged civilian deaths at the border and packaged it as patriotic defense footage. My mother knew enough to hide the chain, not enough to stop him, or she did stop him and still kept the fucking tape in a storage locker for me to find after she died. I can't keep waiting for a cleaner version of this story."

He chewed, swallowed, and looked at the routers instead of her. "So this is the speech before you ask me for something expensive."

"It's not a speech."

"Then say it plain."

She slid her messenger bag over, unzipped it, and took out three folded sheets of thermal paper from a convenience-store printer kiosk. The ink had already started to gray at the edges. She flattened the pages on the table and held each corner down with hardware: a screwdriver, a phone battery, a hex key, and one of his heavy brass nuts.

The first page was a map of release paths. The second was a timing grid. The third was a list of names in initials only, matched to payment amounts and account endpoints.

Minjae stopped chewing.

"Leak architecture," she said. "Not content yet. Infrastructure. If we do this wrong, the files die in ten minutes and we both disappear before sunrise."

He gave her a look that mixed irritation with reluctant interest. The look was one of the first things that had made her trust him: he never pretended not to care when he cared.

"Talk me through it," he said.

Ina traced the first route with her fingertip, nail catching at the cheap paper fibers. "Phase one is decoy pressure. We push redacted transcript fragments and timing anomalies to fan boards and church legal clinics in the same six-hour window. That gives public eyes but no operational signature."

He nodded once.

"Phase two is liquidity and witness protection. We move money out before freezes hit. Not huge sums. Enough to keep people in apartments and out of panic decisions. We split across auntie accounts, church kitchen books, and two fish-market vendors in Busan who already move cash in hand." She tapped the third sheet. "I need your route rails for that split."

"And phase three?"

"Full archive release with custody trail. Cassette digitization notes, the shadow ledger, contract riders, transfer logs, all of it. Mirror to civic servers, fandom mirrors, and one labor legal node that doesn't scare when keywords trip."

He stared at the timing grid, then at her. "You're planning this before Mercury goes live."

"Yes."

"You think Kwon won't smell it?"

"He'll smell something. I need him guessing wrong for thirty-six hours." She paused. "Forty-eight if we're lucky."

He let out a small laugh with no humor in it. "Luck. Right."

The pipes rattled overhead. The salon owner dropped something heavy on the tile, and both of them flinched before they could stop themselves. Then they both laughed, too loud, because fear loved an audience.

Minjae scratched the stubble on his jaw, eyes still on the page. "My sister's transfer is due Friday. If her handler misses one month, they cut her access and she disappears behind someone else's debt. You know that."

"I know." She swallowed. "I'm not touching that line."

"You can't promise that with your fingers crossed behind your back, Ina. If this blows open, every line gets touched."

"Then help me keep it tight enough that it doesn't blow open before we're ready."

He said nothing.

She heard the old refrigerator in the corner click on and hiss. She heard rain hit the basement window and run down the outside glass like fingers tapping to be let in. She also heard, more clearly than either, the question she had not wanted to ask aloud because she needed him to answer it without being cornered.

"Are you with me?"

He leaned back on his palms. "On what?"

"On the leak. On me. On not giving him one more season to package harm as strategy." She hated how pleading the sentence sounded. She hated that she did not retract it.

He closed the lighter in his hand. Click.

"I'm with you on ending him," he said. "I'm not with you on pretending this is clean."

She almost corrected his full form, then caught herself. He only went formal when he was trying to keep from yelling.

"I don't need clean," she said quietly. "I need possible."

Minjae stood and crossed to the sink in three steps. He rinsed his hands like he'd touched engine grease though he had only handled paper. Water rattled through old pipes. He stayed with his back to her long enough that she felt each second like an individual bruise, then he shut the tap, wiped his hands on a towel that had once been white, and turned.

"I can give you two route windows," he said. "One tomorrow night through Incheon cargo and one forty hours later through the fish crates from Busan. I can mask packet headers through a shell traffic dump, but if they run deep trace after freeze order, we're cooked."

Relief moved through her so fast it made her dizzy. She leaned forward, grabbed his wrist, and pressed her forehead against the heel of his hand. "Thank you."

"Don't thank me yet."

"Too late."

He tugged her up and kissed her hard enough that her lower lip caught his tooth. She tasted blood and chestnut sugar. His hand slid under her sweater and found her scar before anything else, thumb resting on the raised skin at her left wrist like he was checking she was still there. She had never told him the whole story of the burn, only the easy version about a kettle and an inattentive morning. He had never pretended to believe it.

The workshop floor was warmed by old heating pipes that ran beneath the concrete in uneven lines, and they learned those lines with their knees and hips as they moved, one patch warm, one patch cold. He pulled her shirt over her head and cursed when her earring snagged in the fabric. She laughed into his shoulder, then bit down because the walls were thin and the salon owner's teenage son already hated both of them for parking in his spot. Minjae's breath was rough by the time she got his belt undone.

"Tell me if it hurts," he said.

"Don't stop."

He got the condom on with shaking fingers, pushed her onto the mat, and entered her in one careful thrust that turned hard when her back hit a cold seam in the concrete. She gasped into his neck. A phone vibrated on the table behind them. He ignored it and caught her wrist when she reached for balance, pinning it above her head hard enough to make the old burn scar throb. She took the pressure for hunger and met it, knees slick against warm pipe lines, his shoulder scar under her palm, his breath breaking into short curses against her mouth. He fucked her in uneven bursts until her thighs shook, until the mat dragged her shoulder blades raw, until he came with his forehead pressed to hers and a bitten-off groan that sounded almost like her name.

When he pulled out, the condom sagged warm in his hand. Four red crescent marks were already rising along her wrist where his fingers had been. She looked at them and told herself they came from wanting, not from him counting down a clock she could not see.

Afterward he lay flat on the mat, one forearm across his eyes, chest still jumping from effort. She straddled his hips, tracing the old shoulder scar with a fingertip damp from sweat. The scar had a ridge in the center like cheap rope under skin. He kept still while she traced it, though his jaw tightened.

"We'll make it through this," she said.

"That's a dangerous sentence from you."

"I know."

She got off him, found her shirt, and sat with her back against the table leg while he rolled the condom in tissue and tied it closed. He was always careful with trash that held DNA. Not paranoid, he called it. Experienced.

He handed her a bottle of barley tea gone warm. "Give me the route key requests tonight. Not over the app. Write them by hand."

"Done."

"And don't sleep at your apartment."

"I've got to pick up the printed ledger pages first."

"Then pick them up and go somewhere else."

She nodded, because agreeing cost less than arguing. Then she folded the thermal pages again, this time along new lines so the previous creases did not telegraph to a camera. He watched her fold and smiled in that quick, sideways way he used when he approved of a trick.

She took that smile as confirmation.

At the door she kissed him once, no urgency left, and he touched the back of her neck as if he could press a blessing through skin. "Call me when you're upstairs," he said.

"You'll get sick of hearing my voice by midnight."

"Not likely."

She climbed the stairs with the damp paper tucked inside her bra and the taste of him still in her mouth. On the landing, she looked back. He was already bent over the table, phone in one hand, thumb hovering above the screen while the other hand reached for the blue lighter and clicked it open, closed, open.

Outside, the rain had stopped. Ina stood under the salon awning and called him from the sidewalk like he asked. He picked up on the first ring.

"You good?" he said.

"I'm good."

"Get home and move fast."

"I will."

She ended the call smiling despite herself, turned toward the station, and did not see him step into the stairwell shadow to make another call before her train doors even closed.

At 1:37 a.m., while she slept in a borrowed room above a closed jjimjilbang in Hapjeong, the first freeze order hit her accounts.

She woke at 5:12 to the sound of her phone rebooting. The phone was not ringing; it was rebooting, with a startup chime too cheerful for the hour and a language-selection screen she had not seen since she bought the device. The options flashed in sequence: Korean, English, Japanese, Chinese. The device was back to factory state.

For half a second she thought she had grabbed the wrong phone from her bag. Then she saw the burn-marked sticker she used to hide a cracked corner of the screen and felt her stomach drop so hard she almost gagged.

"No," she said to no one. She tapped Korean. The setup wizard slid forward as if mocking urgency. She skipped everything she could skip and opened the banking app.

Her banking app was gone.

She installed from scratch with shaking fingers, logged in, and got a red banner: ACCESS RESTRICTED PENDING NATIONAL SECURITY REVIEW. CUSTOMER SUPPORT REF: 8902-A.

She tried her second bank and got the same banner with a different reference code. She tried the MiraeWave residual account she had kept for months as emergency oxygen, and it was suspended. She tried the church cooperative account where she parked small relief transfers in rotating amounts under invoice notes for rice, onions, and cleaning solvent, and it was frozen.

She checked her wallet: one transportation card, three 1,000-won bills, two coins, one receipt from last week for instant noodles and batteries. She dug in her bag, found an envelope she had forgotten with 74,000 won in mixed notes, and laughed out loud because she could hear exactly how long seventy-four thousand lasted in Seoul when landlords smelled fear.

By 6:10 she was in the line outside an ATM on a corner where the bakery opened before dawn. Two delivery men in padded vests stood behind her stomping their feet against the cold. The machine ate her card on the first try and spat out a polite message about institutional hold procedures. The man behind her muttered, "Always before election month," and she wanted to turn and ask him how many times this had happened to him, what flavor of panic he kept in his mouth while he waited, but the ATM beeped for the next customer and she stepped aside like a well-trained citizen.

She bought one Americano from the bakery with cash because she needed the cup heat in her hand more than she needed caffeine. The lid leaked down her fingers. Her thumbprint blurred on the paper sleeve.

At 6:42 she reached the Seongdong print-shop safehouse. The street shutters were still down; graffiti tags from a youth crew ran along the metal in acid green paint. She used the side key and went in through the alley door.

Inside, the air smelled wrong. Not stale coffee and ink like usual. Ozone and hot plastic.

Three tower units on the back desk were powered on but blank-screened. Their fans screamed at full speed, and every status LED pulsed red in the same rhythm like a synchronized heartbeat. A wipe routine, not a crash. Somebody had pushed a remote command and left no pretense.

She yanked a network cable and sat at terminal three. The login account field reset as soon as she typed. She opened local storage directly. Empty directories. Directory names still there, everything inside gone. She pulled the external backup drive from her bag, plugged it in, and watched the system report fresh format date: 03:03, this morning.

"You smug old bastard," she said, voice low and almost admiring despite herself. Kwon always believed in theater.

She paced the room once, then forced herself to stop because pacing did not restore data. She opened the metal cabinet where she had kept paper copies in a folder labeled TAX INVOICES 2024. The folder was gone. In its place sat a new folder, same label, filled with blank copier paper, edges still warm. Someone had taken the time to replace weight with weight.

She put both hands on the cabinet shelf until the vibration from the server fans traveled up her arms and into her shoulders. She could feel each screw head under the paint through her fingertips. She fixed on that texture until she could think again.

Not all copies were here.

Three weeks ago, after Kwon humiliated her in the strategy room for raising civilian cost, she had printed narrowed extracts of the shadow ledger in six-point type, wrapped them in wax paper, and shoved the packet into the split spine of one blue choir binder while volunteers set tables for Wednesday service. It had not been a system or a code, just one panicked hide she had not told anyone about.

If that binder was untouched, she still had fragments.

Her phone buzzed with an unknown number before she reached the door.

She answered on instinct. "Hello."

"Ms. Seo." Kwon's voice was unmistakable even through mobile compression, dry and measured, every syllable pressed flat as if he had ironed it before speaking. "I am told you attempted a series of irregular transfers before dawn."

She leaned against the doorframe and stared at the alley wall where rainwater had darkened old posters into illegible gray skin. "I tried to pay medical bills. That's irregular now?"

"In your current status, yes."

"What status is that?"

"Protective review. Your devices have been standardized and your liquidity temporarily constrained so no one mistakes panic for principle."

She shut her eyes. "You froze church food money."

"You conflated civic charity with covert disbursement. I corrected a category error."

"You mean you punished me for moving first."

A pause. She pictured him in his office with the antique clock behind him and the tea cup he never finished.

"I am giving you a chance to pause," he said. "You have not yet committed an irreversible act."

"You already did."

"You are tired. Sleep, then report to Line Two at ten."

"I'm not coming in."

"You are mistaken about your options, Ms. Seo."

He ended the call without goodbye.

Her hand had clenched around the paper coffee cup hard enough to crush it. Lukewarm coffee leaked down her wrist and into her sleeve. She let the cup drop in the alley and ran for the main road to catch a taxi because buses were too slow and she had exactly one place left to salvage what mattered.

Aunt Hyejin's church kitchen was already loud when Ina arrived. Metal bowls hit stainless counters. Someone had started chopping cabbage for kimchi and the room smelled of garlic, fish sauce, and wet newspaper. Hyejin stood at the center table in her apron and rubber gloves, reading glasses hanging from the chain at her neck, counting out egg trays with the concentration of a bookkeeper balancing a battlefield ledger.

She looked up once. "You look like a person who got bad news before breakfast." Then she squinted. "Worse. You look like a person who expected bad news and got it anyway."

Ina pulled off her shoes at the door and stepped onto the warm vinyl floor. "I need the old blue choir binder. Split spine, taped corner."

Hyejin did not ask why. She nodded toward the pantry. "Top shelf, left side. And wash your hands first. You touch those books with city dirt, I'll haunt you myself while alive."

Ina washed, dried, and hauled down one binder that smelled of old paper and camphor. She set it on a side table under the fluorescent light, pried back the loosened cloth at the spine with a butter knife, and felt wax paper crackle under her fingertip. Inside were folded strips of printed ledger entries: dates, transfer IDs, shell company abbreviations, and initials tied to payout amounts.

It was not enough for a full release. It was enough to prove a pattern.

Hyejin came over with a bowl of peeled radish, sat across from her, and started slicing with a short knife that had lost its handle finish years ago. "Cards not working?"

Ina kept unfolding the strips slowly so the damp ink would not flake. "Everything froze. Accounts, devices, access keys."

"Your father's landlord called at dawn," Hyejin said. "He used his polite voice, which means he's preparing his rude voice for next week."

Ina shut her eyes for one breath. "How short are we?"

"For this month? Two million and change if we want to keep your father where he is and keep Saint Mary's from adding another late penalty to your mother's account." Hyejin flicked a radish slice into the bowl. "You can survive broke. You can't survive broke and surprised. Decide which one you're willing to be."

Ina looked at her aunt's arthritic fingers inside the gloves, how the knuckles bulged when she gripped the knife but the cuts stayed exact anyway, each radish piece the same thickness.

"I've got maybe seventy-four thousand cash," Ina said. "And a transit card."

Hyejin snorted. "Then you have seventy-four thousand more than last winter. We have had worse numbers." She pushed the bowl toward Ina. "Eat one. You're pale."

Ina took a slice. Salt and chili burned the cracked skin at the corner of her mouth where Minjae's tooth had caught her. She had forgotten the cut until then.

Hyejin noticed. Her eyes narrowed, then softened, then went sharp again. "Did he do this?"

Ina did not answer quickly enough.

Hyejin sighed. "I'm not asking if you love him. I'm asking if he's the hole in your boat."

Ina folded the ledger strips into a legal envelope and slid it inside her coat lining. "I'm going to find out tonight."

By afternoon she had borrowed a laptop from a freelance captioner she barely knew, paid in promises because cash was dead, and rebuilt enough of her comm stack to check one surviving secure log. The metadata gave her a pattern, not a verdict. She had sent Minjae the first hand-written route request at 12:58 a.m. He had read at 1:03. At 1:11 his old line made a six-minute encrypted voice call to an unlabeled number whose exchange matched Kwon's emergency tree and, unhelpfully, two Incheon freight-dispatch lines Minjae used for reroutes.

Freeze order timestamp: 1:37.

One explanation was betrayal. Another was simpler and uglier: Kwon had already flagged her transfer attempts and fired a batch freeze, while Minjae called dispatch to move his sister's money before the sweep hit.

Ina tested that second theory the only way she could. She dialed the 1:11 number from a throwaway SIM. A recorded female voice answered, "Night dispatch. Enter route code." Then the line dumped her to static and died.

She got no name and no proof.

She texted Minjae one line: `Need to see you. No phones. Under Seongsu rail bridge, 22:00.`

He replied in thirteen seconds: `I'll be there.`

She spent the next six hours moving like someone carrying water in open hands, careful and furious. She copied the ledger strips by hand twice in block print, one set for herself, one set for a dead drop she had not yet chosen. She wrapped both sets in wax paper from the church kitchen so rain would not eat the ink.

Outside Hongdae Station, a film student in a varsity jacket turned her old camera over in his hands and clicked the shutter three times.

"Body only?" he asked.

"Body and two batteries."

He squinted at the screen bleed. "Shutter count is brutal. Three-fifty."

"It was worth seven last month."

"Then keep it," he said, already starting to hand it back.

"No." She held out her palm. "Three-fifty."

He counted out three hundred and fifty thousand won in damp bills that smelled like cigarette smoke. The amount covered less than half the camera's resale value and all of her pride for the afternoon. She watched him walk away filming sidewalk pigeons with the strap still warm from her neck.

At Saint Mary's billing office, the cashier window glass had a spider crack in one corner taped over with clear packing tape. The clerk recognized Ina's name before she looked up.

"Minimum today to stop penalty accrual?" Ina asked.

"Three hundred thousand," the clerk said. "If you pay less, a late fee posts at midnight."

"I have two-seventy in cash."

The clerk slid a pink form under the slot. "Then you'll owe two-seventy plus penalty tomorrow. Three hundred prevents that."

Ina peeled thirty thousand from the last emergency envelope in her coat and pushed the full three hundred through. The clerk stamped the receipt and clipped it to a balance sheet that still showed fifty-seven million seven hundred in red.

At 9:42 she stood under the Seongsu rail bridge with her hood up and counted each train by sound before she saw it. The bridge trembled every nine minutes. Water dripped through expansion joints and tapped the rusted guardrail in irregular rhythms. A fried-chicken truck had parked two blocks away, and the air held that hot oil smell that could make hunger feel like insult.

Minjae arrived on foot from the river side, hands in jacket pockets, cap low. He stopped three steps away and studied her the same way he did a suspicious drive: from edges inward.

"You look wrecked," he said.

"You look rested."

"I slept an hour."

"Luxury."

A train thundered overhead and made them both wait. When the noise passed, she spoke before fear could soften her.

"Did you call Kwon after I left last night?"

His jaw moved once. "Why would I do that?"

"Don't feed me the polite lie. We don't have time for version two."

"Ina-"

"No. Don't toss my name at me like it'll calm this down. At 1:11 your line hit a number tied to his emergency tree or Incheon night dispatch for six minutes. At 1:37 my accounts got frozen, my phone got scrubbed, and my paper backups got replaced with blank weight. Did you call him?"

He looked past her toward the dark river for so long she heard every drip from the bridge. Then he met her eyes.

"Yeah," he said.

The word did not crack anything audible. No glass shattered and nobody shouted. She heard only the small click of a structural bolt failing where she could not see it.

She laughed once, short and ugly. "You could've told me before I spent the day selling gear and begging cashier windows for mercy."

"If I'd told you then, you'd have blown every lane we still had."

"Don't say blown like this is weather. You sold the story to the man who stages bodies and calls it strategy."

"I called to buy time."

"For who--for me, or your sister?"

"For my sister. You think I called to sell you out? I called because if your drop hit before Friday, her handler would've flagged her apartment and-"

"And what, I was supposed to clap?"

His mouth twitched, then he pushed through. "For the Friday transfer window. For the cousins moving drives through Incheon with my debt on their backs. For route runners who vanish when a sweep starts early. And yeah, for me, because I'm done pretending this board has clean squares."

She stepped closer until she could smell the machine oil in his jacket. "You chose him."

"I chose forty-eight hours where she stays alive."

"By giving him me."

"By giving him a warning that kept him from doing what he does when he's surprised."

"You mean disappearing people?" she snapped. "How generous. He took my money and my tools and called it protective review. You want me to clap for technique?"

His face flinched at that, not from guilt but from rage he was trying to leash.

"You think I don't know what he is?" he said. "You think I haven't watched men lose fingers and names because someone in a clean office wanted a cleaner headline curve? I called because your plan had no survivable lane for my family. You asked me to torch routes that feed them and trust your redactions would hold. Redactions don't stop knock-on sweeps."

"And Kwon does?"

"No. But he negotiates when he's got leverage. Dead heroes don't negotiate at all."

She shoved her hands into her coat pockets because if she did not she might hit him, and she did not trust what would happen after that first impact.

"What did you give him?"

"Architecture only. I didn't give content or names."

"You gave him enough to preempt. That's the same result."

"That's not the same result."

"Tell that to my frozen accounts. Tell it to Saint Mary's billing office. Tell it to my father's landlord when he tapes an eviction notice over his mailbox."

He swore under his breath, then louder. "Fuck. I moved cash this afternoon. I brought it for you."

He pulled an envelope from inside his jacket and held it out. Thick. Maybe two million, maybe a little more.

She stared at it and did not take it.

"What's this? Hush money?"

"Emergency money. Route fees from last run."

"How many people did you underpay to make this stack look generous?"

"Don't--don't do that." His hand shook now. "Don't turn this into a TV ethics segment because you're hurt."

"I'm hurt."

"I know."

"No," she said. "You know the strategic version. You don't know the body-count version."

A truck backfired on the road above and echoed under the bridge like a small explosion. Neither of them ducked.

She took a breath and forced her voice flat. "Say it exactly. Why did you call him?"

He dropped his gaze to the wet concrete between them. "Because when you left, I pictured my sister waiting in that apartment in Pyongyang with the broken boiler and the blue gloves she wears indoors because her fingers crack in winter. I pictured what happens when her handler misses one transfer. I pictured your leak going loud before I could move her line. I called Kwon and told him you were building release rails. I told him if he froze you instead of grabbing you, I'd keep my routes quiet for one week and he could avoid public mess before Mercury." He looked up. "He agreed."

"He agreed to freeze me because you asked nicely."

"I asked with leverage."

"Your leverage was me."

"My leverage was the routes he needs."

"Routes you gave him back for free the second I trusted you."

He dragged both hands through his hair and laughed once, bitter. "Trusted me? Ina, you gave me architecture after three weeks of hiding half your intelligence deal and all your fallback plans. You don't trust people. You allocate them."

The sentence landed because it was not entirely wrong.

She felt suddenly, vividly, the weight of the envelope strips sewn into her coat lining, the scratch of paper edges against her ribs. She had built redundancies out of everyone she loved and called it prudence.

"Maybe I do allocate," she said. "But I didn't hand your life to Kwon for forty-eight hours of comfort."

"Comfort?" He barked a laugh. "You think I'm comfortable? I haven't sat through a full meal in months. I sleep with one shoe on because calls come at any hour. I count men at every handoff and recalculate who is missing before I get home. Don't call this comfort."

"Then call it what it is."

"Triaging an unwinnable board."

"That sounds exactly like him."

At that, he looked down, dragged his thumbnail along the envelope seam until the paper tore, then sealed his mouth for one controlled breath. When he spoke again, his voice was low and tired enough to break her if she let it.

"I learned from him because he paid everyone in my world long before you did. I hated that and I still learned it. You learned from him too. We both did. The difference is you still think your version's clean."

"I never said clean."

"You said possible." He wiped rain from his mouth with the back of his hand. "Possible for who, Ina?"

She opened her mouth and closed it again. The bridge dripped. A train roared overhead. Somewhere beyond the pillars, a couple argued in slurred voices and then laughed at themselves.

When the noise thinned, she said, "I needed you beside me on this. Not in front of me with a warning flare."

"I was beside you until beside you meant my sister gets sacrificed first."

"So now what?"

"Now we stop pretending we're one team." He looked at the envelope still in his hand and shoved it back into his jacket. "I can still keep your name off some route chatter. I can still buy you small time."

"I don't want your small time."

"You'll want it when they come."

"They're already here."

He took one step toward her. She stepped back.

That, more than any sentence, ended it.

His face folded inward for a second, then reset into the operational mask she had met in the first month of their lives together, before desire, before confession, before the bad habit of sleeping with hope.

"If you need to send anything physical now," he said, voice almost neutral, "don't use Seongdong lanes. They're burned. Use market cold-chain trucks only and rotate pickup points every thirty minutes. Don't trust repeat drivers."

The advice was good. She hated him for giving it.

"You sound like a subcontractor handing off a project," she said.

"Maybe that's what we were."

"No." She shook her head. "A subcontractor doesn't memorize where I keep burn cream or how many sugars I take in hospital coffee. A subcontractor doesn't ask me to call when I get upstairs." Her voice broke on the last word and she let it. "Don't reduce this so you can live with yourself."

He flinched and looked away.

For a moment she thought he might apologize, properly, without strategy. He did not.

Instead he said, "I am sorry." Formal, clipped, built for legal record, not repair.

She laughed with no humor left. "There it is."

She reached into her wallet and pulled out the small metal route key he had given her in summer, the one etched with a scratched MJ on one side and a cartoon squid on the other because he said all serious tools deserved one stupid mark. She held it between thumb and forefinger, then set it on the wet guardrail between them.

"Keep your infrastructure," she said.

He did not pick it up.

She turned and started walking toward the main road. After twelve steps she heard him call her name once. She kept walking.

At the bus stop she checked the borrowed phone. Two new messages waited.

The first was from Line Two scheduling: `MANDATORY APPEARANCE 08:00. NONCOMPLIANCE WILL BE DOCUMENTED.`

The second was from her aunt: `Landlord taped a paper on your father's door. Come by before midnight if you can.`

Ina looked up at the apartment towers on the hill, their windows lit in uneven grids. Somewhere in those rectangles, families were folding laundry, fighting over remote controls, paying bills they could barely afford, or pretending not to hear the neighbor downstairs crying. She felt the wax-wrapped ledger strips against her ribs and imagined every number on them turning into a hand reaching for her at once.

When her bus arrived, she did not board. She walked instead, because the fare would be cheaper tomorrow if she rationed the card and because movement was the only thing that made the anger stay in useful shape.

By the time she reached Mapo, her socks were wet through and the sky had gone the color of old metal. A dark van without logos idled across from her building entrance. Its side door unlocked with a clean metallic snap before she reached the curb. The driver did not look at her.

He did not need to.


# Chapter 14

Ina lost the first night because the room did not have a night. The metallic snap of the van door in Mapo was the last clean timestamp she trusted.

The ceiling light in the Hongje flat stayed on long enough for her pupils to ache, then snapped off for what felt like twenty minutes, then came back while she was still halfway down into sleep, mouth open, neck bent against the wall. The wall paint carried a sweet chemical smell, fresh enough that it stuck on her tongue. Somewhere behind the drywall, a pipe ticked every few seconds, then stopped for long stretches that were worse than the ticking. She tried to count those gaps and map a clock from them. The gaps kept changing.

A man with a delivery cap brought food in white paper bowls and never answered questions. He set down rice gone stiff at the edges and soup slicked with red pepper oil and then stood by the door while she ate, treating the spoon like classified material. He had a cheap silver wedding ring that had flattened into an oval, and he turned it around his finger whenever she looked at him directly. When she asked what time it was, he said, "Eat while it's hot," in the same bored tone each round, even when the food was cold.

She had started this detention with seven locations in her head. She had repeated them in order on the ride here, cheek against the van window, running them to subtitle timing windows she could not miss: Mapo mirror, Ilsan node, two offshore proxies, a cloud escrow account under a fake veterinary clinic, one encrypted drive with Hyejin's choir roster, one cash-only dead-drop she had not touched in months. By the time the second light blackout ended, she had rearranged them into fake order and true order and decoy order. By the fourth blackout, she could not hold all three orders at once. She began to recite subway stations instead, then childhood multiplication tables, then the names of people she had paid from the diverted fund.

Nobody hit her. That was the point.

They woke her by sound and temperature and surprise. At one interval, they shoved the thermostat low enough that the vinyl chair turned slick with condensation and her jeans stayed wet at the back. At another, they played a recording of children's choir scales in the corner speaker, the same four bars over and over with one soprano voice always half a beat late. On the third cycle of that, someone banged a metal tray against the hallway wall and shouted numbers she could not parse because sleep had pooled behind her eyes like glue.

When she asked to call a lawyer, a woman in a gray cardigan said, "There is no charge yet. This is internal containment," and placed a bottle of ion drink on the table like hospitality could settle law.

The woman introduced herself on the second pass. "Choi Yoonsun. Compliance review." She was maybe forty, with cracked cuticles painted in chipped pearly polish and an orange pharmacy sticker still on her wrist where someone had drawn blood that morning. She set down a folder, aligned it to the table edge with two taps, then took a peppermint spray from her cardigan pocket and misted her mouth before each question. The room smelled like candy cane and disinfectant.

"Your account access is frozen," Choi said. "Your father's landlord filed an arrears notice. Eleven-point-two million won. Did you know?"

Ina laughed once, too dry to sound like laughter. "Yeah. I know his number by heart. He calls Sundays before church so guilt gets a head start."

Choi did not smile. "The landlord is willing to wait if the right people ask him."

"Then ask him."

"That depends on you."

The folder held printouts from bank transfers Ina had made through Hyejin's church account, each line highlighted with the same unforgiving yellow. Choi asked about routing keys, shell names, and why she had exported server logs to an unauthorized mirror in Ilsan. Ina answered slowly and true where truth did not matter.
