She gave old passwords from dead projects. She gave a cloud key with one character wrong. She described a script that did not exist. She watched Choi's left foot under the table, heel lifting and dropping at a steady tempo whenever Ina stalled, like a metronome she could not switch off.

On what might have been day two, they moved her from chair to floor mat and back without warning. On what might have been day three, they let her sleep ninety minutes, then woke her with the overhead light and a man's voice reading old election speeches through the door. Ina could hear rain hitting the metal awning outside one short cycle, then dry traffic hiss the next. She built a false calendar from those sounds and tore it down every time it failed.

When Choi came back, her cardigan had a different brooch pinned near the shoulder, a tiny violin with one string missing. "I've got a daughter who does winter recital auditions this week," she said while checking her tablet battery. "If I leave this office late, I miss it. I don't say that for sympathy. I say that because everyone is paying for your silence."

"Do you want me to clap for your work-life balance?"

Choi's voice stayed level. "I want one address. Not your whole architecture. One active backup with chain-of-custody notes."

"Then you should've recruited an idiot."

Choi tapped the tablet and turned the screen toward her. It showed a photo of Dohyun outside his building, knit cap pulled low, cigarette hanging from his mouth, one hand braced on the rail because his right knee had locked again. Timestamp in the corner: fifteen minutes earlier.

The cigarette had burned almost to the filter. Ina knew what that meant. Her father only smoked that low when he was rehearsing a conversation he was scared to have.

"You have eyes on my father," Ina said.

"We have concern for all linked parties."

"Don't call this concern."

Choi breathed peppermint across the table. "Your father has unstable day labor and no legal buffer. He signed two transfer receipts for funds with no documented origin. If another agency sees those before this is resolved, he can be charged."

Ina leaned back, and the vinyl made a wet peeling sound against her shirt. "Charged with what? Being broke near paperwork?"

"With financial concealment tied to national security investigation."

The phrase had the bureaucratic calm of something practiced in rooms colder than this one. Ina heard the word tied and pictured rope. She pictured her father at twenty, shirtless on a dock in an old family photo, arms thick and dumb with labor, then pictured him now in his landlord's hallway trying not to show that stairs hurt.

"He can't read half the forms he signs," she said.

"Then he will depend on your precision."

They took the photo away. They brought in soup. They turned the light off for one hour or six. She slept in slices and dreamed in subtitles: white letters over black screen, no sound, then sudden sound with no letters. In one dream she was fixing timing on a confession tape while her mother stood behind her counting in Japanese, and when Ina turned to ask for the next number, it was Kwon's voice finishing the count.

She woke to Kwon sitting across from her, hands folded, suit jacket still holding its sharp shoulder line even in this cheap room.

He had changed to a charcoal tie, one she remembered from donor dinners when he wanted to signal austerity rather than victory. A tiny burn mark sat near the knot, no bigger than a grain of rice. Somebody had once brushed him with a cigarette and he had kept the tie anyway. He could keep damage if it supported the story.

"You look unwell," he said.

"You look expensive," Ina said.

"I came because this has gone on too long."

"Then open the door."

"I can open many doors. I need one key first."

He spoke with the same measured cadence he used on stage, as if this room had an audience hidden behind one-way paint. He did not raise his voice. He did not call her names. He laid his phone on the table between them, screen down, then nudged it once with two fingers.

"The election environment is unstable," he said. "Rumor compounds in minutes. If your archive releases in fragments without context, you will not get accountability. You will get panic and retaliation."

"You already planned panic."

"You mistake sequence for intention."

"I don't."

He watched her for a full breath before answering. The longer her silence stretched, the narrower his language got, until everything became verdict words. "History punishes hesitation," he said. "It punishes sentimental leakage."

"It also punishes old men who confuse a country with their ego. Yeah, no, men like you."

His mouth moved by a millimeter, not quite a smile, not quite contempt. "Your mother used harder language than you do. She still understood operational boundaries."

Ina tasted metal where she had bitten her tongue in sleep. "Don't bring her in here."

"Then do not force me to bring in your father."

Kwon tapped the back of the phone. A second later, it vibrated and lit up with an incoming call interface. He pressed speaker and held it near the table, not handing it to her.

Dohyun answered on the fourth ring with his cautious public voice. "Hello."

"Mr. Han," Kwon said. "I apologize for the hour. We are checking records."

There was a pause, then the scratchy exhale Ina knew from years of her father trying to sound sober when he was angry. "What records? I already gave those papers to the young woman. The one with the church coat."

"Yes. We only need to confirm whether your daughter asked you to hold external drives."

Silence. Ina could hear traffic and a television somewhere in her father's apartment, baseball commentary too loud because his hearing dipped in one ear when he was tired.

"My daughter edits subtitles," Dohyun said. "She does not have drives. She has deadlines."

Kwon's tone stayed cordial. "You are protecting her. That is admirable. It can also be interpreted as obstruction."

"Obstruction? I fix boilers and toilets. I don't obstruct anything."

"Please remain available today. An officer may visit."

Kwon ended the call before Dohyun could answer.

Ina had not moved during the call because she knew any movement would become data for them. She sat with her hands flat, palms damp against fake wood grain, while every muscle in her back screamed to stand up and grab the phone and throw it through the wall. She watched Kwon's thumbnail, neatly buffed, press the lock button.

"That call was unnecessary. No, it was the point," she said.

"It was a courtesy preview," he said. "The next conversation may not be with me."

"You're threatening a sixty-six-year-old man over rent debt."

"I am describing legal pathways that already exist."

"You built those pathways."

"I inherited them. I maintained them. I did not invent this state's appetite for scapegoats."

He leaned forward a little, enough that she smelled his aftershave under the room's bleach and peppermint. Cedar and smoke, expensive and careful.

"You can still shape outcome," he said. "One server. One location. We close the loop quietly, and your father keeps his lease."

"And then what? I hand it over and you bury everything deeper, and I stand there and--what--thank you?"

"Then we survive the month. Survival permits future argument. Martyrdom does not."

There it was again: he drove every argument back into the same narrow lane, survive the month and surrender the rest. She could hear his logic and also hear the choir recording from earlier trapped under it, the same four bars looping until meaning dissolved. She had lived inside edited narrative long enough to know when someone was forcing a cut before impact.

She held his gaze and gave him a decoy. "Mapo storage, sublevel two, rack C."

He did not blink. "That mirror burned last week."

"Then your people are behind."

He slid a single printed sheet across the table. It was a photo of the Mapo rack, timestamped two days earlier, empty rails and cut cables hanging like tendons.

"You are tired," he said. "I know that because I am tired too. Let us avoid theatrical resilience."

"I'm not performing. I'm trying not to black out, and I'm not doing a great job."

"Everyone performs. The question is for whom."

The room stayed silent long enough for the pipe in the wall to tick three times and stop. Ina could feel sleep deprivation changing texture, turning thought into static bursts and sudden holes. She tried to summon the true order list. The list came with wrong colors, wrong file names. She saw the Ilsan node and then saw Hyejin's church basement and then saw Minjae's hands over a keyboard, tape wrapped around two fingers where cables had sliced him last summer. She remembered him saying run while there was still time. She remembered him calling Kwon instead.

Kwon checked his watch and stood.

"I will give you ten minutes," he said. "After that, Compliance will proceed without my preference." He paused with one hand on the chair back. "When this state wants a culprit, it always starts with the poor and the elderly. You know this better than most. Do not test whether I can slow that machine for your father."

When he left, Choi came in immediately with her tablet and peppermint spray. She did not sit.

"He offered you a narrow exit," she said. "Give me one address and I can close this intake file before dispatch starts stacking."

"You mean I hand you a server so you can stamp a seizure sheet and call that mercy."

"I mean pick one seizure report instead of five arrest notices."

"Do you tell your daughter that before recitals? Play with one hand and leave the other in your pocket--sorry, sign the waiver, smile, pretend that's a choice?"

For the first time, Choi's expression cracked. It was not rage. It was exhaustion. "My daughter vomits before every audition," she said quietly. "She still plays."

Ina closed her eyes for two seconds and saw her father's cigarette down to filter. When she opened them, the fluorescent light had a halo around it, bright edge tearing into two copies. She knew she was close to losing language entirely. If that happened, they would get everything anyway, not because of violence but because her brain would turn to noise.

She chose one thing to lose.

"Ilsan," she said. Her voice came out rough, as if she had swallowed paper. "Baekyang Dental Building, basement utility room, unit B-12. No--B-12, gray tower case behind the water softener. The drive label says maintenance logs. The passphrase starts with Gyeongbok and ends with eighty-two."

Choi did not move for half a beat, then typed fast with both thumbs. "Spell Gyeongbok."

Ina spelled it.

"Eighty-two as numerals?"

"Yes."

Choi stepped into the hall and made a call. Ina heard only one side.

"Dispatch now. B-12, utility. Confirm casing color... no, don't kill power until copy is complete... yes, chain bag and photo every port."

The hall intercom snapped on less than ten minutes later, volume too high for the room.

"Team Three at Baekyang. Tower secured, live. Recovering one tea tin with eight USB sticks, one envelope bundle tagged ledger strips, and one folder marked choir roster with handwritten payout columns. Starting seizure log now."

Ina stopped hearing the rest at ledger strips. Those were names she had promised would never touch an official desk. Her hands started shaking in tiny, useless tremors that made her thumbnails tap the laminate. She pressed her palms flat until one nail cut skin and a hot bead of blood slicked her lifeline.

Choi came back in with her tablet still lit. On the screen, a gloved hand held a clear evidence pouch. Through the plastic, Ina could read her own block letters: Q4 RELIEF / MAPO / CASH.

"Thank you," Choi said, procedural and almost gentle.

"Don't thank me."

"You made a rational choice."

Ina looked up. "No. I made the choice you engineered."

Choi held that gaze, then looked away first.

They did not release her immediately. That would have felt like a bargain honored, and this machine did not honor anything cleanly. They kept her through one full shift change. At 03:12, stamped on the slate held under her chin for another wrist photo, the delivery-cap man handed her paperwork to a woman in a white padded vest who never used her name. At 05:40 someone shoved in a blanket that smelled like wet dog and detergent. At 06:03 an officer photographed her face again and wrote post-cooperation on a form he forgot to turn away from her. She slept bent over the table with her cheek on folded arms and woke to find drool dried on the sleeve of her own sweater, a dark patch she scrubbed with spit and thumbnail like a child hiding evidence.

When the door finally opened for real, the delivery-cap man handed back her phone in a plastic evidence pouch and her tote bag with its zipper split.

"You are free to go," he said.

"What time is it?"

He turned his wedding ring once and shrugged. "Morning."

Outside, the alley air hit like a slap. Winter had sharpened overnight. Her breath rose white and immediate in front of her, and the back of her throat tasted of metal and peppermint and cheap soup. A garbage truck idled at the corner, hydraulic arm whining as it lifted blue bins with frozen kimchi crust at the rim. She stood still too long, and a woman walking a tiny white dog glanced at her shoes, then at her face, then looked away fast in the practiced Seoul style of seeing without acknowledging.

Ina checked her phone. Thirty-two missed calls from unknown numbers. Six from Hyejin. Three from Dohyun. One message from Minjae at 02:11 with no text, only a location pin that had expired.

Her battery read fourteen percent. A new tracking app she had never installed sat on the second screen, icon plain gray, permissions already granted. She laughed once, then almost threw up in the gutter and swallowed it back down because there was nothing in her stomach except broth salt.

A black sedan idled half a block down, engine running, driver pretending to scroll his feed. It was a tail, and they made no effort to hide it. Fine. Let them watch.

She took bus 701 because the subway tunnel hum made her dizzy. She sat by the rear door and counted the red LED stops like rosary beads while the heater blasted dry air that smelled of old wool coats. At the Seodaemun overpass, she nodded off for a minute and woke with panic tearing through her because she had dreamed she was still in the room and the bus light was only another interrogation trick.

Dohyun opened his apartment door on the chain first, then unlatched it when he saw her. He wore long underwear under his work pants and had one sock half-pulled because his knee brace strap had twisted. A bucket of screws and washers sat by the shoe rack where he was sorting salvage by thread size, something he did when fear made him restless.

"Where the hell were you?" he said, voice breaking in the middle and then hardening to cover it. "I called all night. Some bastard asked about drives."

"I know." She stepped in, toeing off shoes that suddenly felt too tight. "I'm sorry."

"Don't say sorry like a politician." He limped back to the kitchen and flicked ash into a tuna can on the windowsill. "Tell me if I need to run."

The room smelled like stale smoke, floor cleaner, and the soybean paste stew he had burned then salvaged with water. The eviction warning envelope lay on the table unopened, red stamp bleeding through thin paper. On top of it sat his reading glasses wrapped in tissue, one arm mended with electrical tape.

Ina wanted to tell him everything. She wanted to put the whole architecture on the table between the ashtray and the stew pot and let truth do whatever truth was going to do. Instead she heard Kwon's sentence again: this state starts with the poor and the elderly.

"You don't run," she said. "You stay visible. Go to work if they call. Do normal things."

"Normal for who?" He gave a short, bitter laugh. "I already got normal debt, normal bad knee, normal landlord kicking the door. Do I get a prize now?"

She almost smiled because that was his humor when he was scared, sour and stubborn and too proud to beg. "I can cover part of the arrears soon. Not all at once."

"With what money?"

She did not answer fast enough.

Dohyun sat at the table and rubbed his thumb over the tape on his glasses arm. "You're in trouble bigger than debt collectors," he said. "I'm not blind."

"I know."

"Did your mother know? Back then?"

Ina looked at the stew pot lid rattling with low boil and thought of cassette hiss, her mother's careful handwriting, Kwon's tie with that tiny burn by the knot. "Yeah," she said. "She knew enough to leave me a map and no safe way to read it."

He nodded like that confirmed something he had long suspected and never wanted proved. Then he stood, grabbed two bowls, and served stew with hands that shook only when he reached for the hot pot.

He set her bowl down and pointed a spoon at her phone. "Your aunt called six times. She said to check the church bag she dropped by. It's in the hallway closet. I didn't open it because she said there was holy paper inside and she would haunt me if I touched it with oily hands."

That sounded like Hyejin exactly, threatening postmortem haunting while alive and very loud.

Ina ate three spoonfuls too fast and burned her tongue. Salt and chili snapped her fully awake for the first time since detention. She stood, knees unsteady, and went to the closet.

The church tote hung from a hook under Dohyun's old dock jacket. It was the navy canvas bag Hyejin used for everything from choir folders to illicit cash envelopes, one strap mended with floral fabric from a dress she had worn in the nineties and refused to throw away. Inside were two tangerines, a packet of instant coffee, a pair of wool gloves too small for Ina, and a hymnbook wrapped in a dry-cleaning sleeve.

It was not a church publication. Their church had switched editions years ago.

This was the older green hymnal with faded gilt letters, the one Hyejin insisted on keeping because "the new translation sounds like it was written by a committee that hates breath." Hyejin used to pencil grocery totals in the back margin and erase them with her thumb when she forgot a calculator. Ina flipped it open and saw no penciled totals. Instead she found three tiny dots in blue ink beside hymn numbers 43, 118, and 270.

It was a code format, not random marking.

She sat on the hallway floor with the book on her knees and felt the chapter title in her own head: not salvation, not escape, only pathway.

Hyejin had once taught her this system when Ina was sixteen and hiding letters from a boyfriend her father disapproved of. First number gave district locker bank. Second gave row and column by verse count. Third gave key phrase from opening line. They had turned it into a family game and then, years later, into logistics.

Her pulse jumped not in a generic surge but in a practical cascade of tasks: confirm the locker site was still open, secure transport without digital ticket trail, recover physical media before Council sweeps reached church routes.

She turned to the inside cover. Hyejin had written one sentence in blunt slanted script.

Use the old station, not the fancy one. Cameras are lazy where paint peels.

Ina flipped to hymn 270 for the opening line and felt cold run down her back. The top half of the page had been torn out cleanly, maybe years ago, maybe yesterday, leaving only the last three words and a ragged edge.

She knew the station lockers tied to this cipher. Two bad phrase entries and the keypad hard-locked for security, with a guard pinged to clear it in person. Guess wrong there with a tail behind her and she would burn the last route in one minute.

Ina laughed into her sleeve anyway, half sob and half relief. She could hear Hyejin's voice clear as kettle whistle, practical even in coded warning. A surviving path existed. It came with a missing line and no margin for error.

Dohyun called from the kitchen, "Did your holy paper explode?"

"No," Ina said, standing with the hymnal pressed against her chest hard enough to dent the dry-cleaning plastic. "It's just ugly theology."

He grunted approval. "Bring your bowl. It's getting cold."

She returned to the table and ate while the black sedan stayed parked outside in the alley, visible through the kitchen window if she leaned left. The driver had changed; now it was a younger man with acne scars and fingerless gloves, tapping his steering wheel in time with a song she could not hear. He looked nineteen and bored and probably underpaid, and he was still part of the cage.

Ina set her spoon down and opened her notes app, then closed it again. She would keep no digital notes. She tore a strip from the eviction envelope, wrote 43-118-270 on the blank side with Dohyun's carpenter pencil, folded it twice, and slid it into the inside seam of her coat pocket.

She made a small list in her head instead. Buy a burner with cash. Reach Hyejin in person for the missing hymn line. Borrow Hyejin's church van keys. Change route twice before touching any locker. Do not call Minjae yet. Keep Dohyun out of transit and paperwork for seventy-two hours.

The list steadied her more than sleep could. She had given up one mirror. The rest now depended on one torn page and whether she could lose the sedan long enough to ask her aunt a single question.

When she stood to wash dishes, her hands still shook, but now the motion had purpose. Water hit steel sink in a fast bright pattern, and the noise covered the alley, the sedan, the state's patient appetite. She scrubbed the soup bowls and listened to the pipe in her father's wall tick and stop, tick and stop, and this time she did not try to turn it into a clock. She let it be what it was: proof that pressure moved through hidden lines and still left room for leaks.


# Chapter 15

Cardboard rasped against church kitchen glass as Ina sealed each window against the alley light.

She had burned two route changes to lose the black sedan and arrived with just enough steadiness left for work her hands could finish.

The cardboard smelled like onion and old freezer burn. Hyejin had pulled it from behind the dry-goods shelf, where she kept broken-down produce boxes tied with twine. She stood beside Ina in her apron, reading glasses knocking softly against the buttons as she moved, and boiled water she forgot to pour three times.

"Drink," Hyejin said at last, shoving a mug into Ina's hand. "You look like you got ironed flat."

Ina drank because her aunt was watching. The tea was too hot and too sweet and tasted faintly of dish soap because the church never replaced its cracked mugs. A stripe of fluorescent light buzzed overhead. The strip had been buzzing for months and Hyejin kept saying she would ask Mr. Paik to change it, and she never did, because there was always another emergency first.

On the stainless prep table, Ina had arranged three burners, one old laptop with a missing F key, and a legal pad she had torn into columns: `contacts`, `status`, `last signal`, `location confidence`. Her handwriting started neat and turned jagged by the second page.

She had not slept properly since the windowless flat. She still felt that room in her wrists: the pressure mark of plastic cuffs, the heat of server fans, the rhythm of a steel chair leg scraping every time someone entered to ask the same question with different threat language. She had given them one backup address. She could still hear herself giving it, voice steady like she was reading subtitles in a sound booth.

The last surviving path sat in her coat pocket now, folded into the back pages of Hyejin's hymnbook code, soft at the corners from being touched too often. She checked for it every ten minutes like an old woman checking for house keys.

By 20:11, Minjae still had not answered.

Two minutes later Ji-eun sent a voice note from Busan with harbor horns under her breathing. "There are people at the terminal checking IDs. Are they ours? Are they theirs? Just tell me if I run."

Ina typed: `Don't move. Indoors. No livestream. Keep curtains closed.`

The message sat with a spinning clock icon and then failed to send.

At 20:17, every screen in the kitchen glitched at once.

The church's old TV, tuned low to KBN news, froze on a weather graphic. The laptop's browser window went gray. One burner, the one Minjae had modified with a bent antenna and electrical tape, flashed a system alert in red: `Emergency Civil Defense Broadcast`.

Then the same face appeared on all three devices.

Lieutenant General Park Seong-hoon, vice chair of the Joint Chiefs, appeared in full dress uniform under the blue-and-gold emblem backdrop from the Defense Ministry briefing room. Ina had subtitled him before. He spoke in clipped press language, with bureaucratic care that irritated viewers and reassured them at the same time.

This version of him breathed too loudly and blinked too little.

"At 20:05," the deepfake Park said, "North Korean forward artillery units crossed demarcation markers near Yeoncheon under weather concealment. Civilian citizens in districts north of the river should evacuate to designated shelters immediately. Reservists will report through the National Defense app within one hour. This is not an exercise."

The lower-third graphics looked authentic at first glance. The date stamp used wrong kerning. The subtitle line break on "designated shelters" was wrong. The emergency code sequence had a typo. Tiny machine mistakes hid inside official confidence.

Ina felt the bottom of her mug tilt against her palm and realized her hand was shaking hard enough to spill.

"It's live everywhere," she said.

Hyejin leaned over her shoulder and squinted. "He looks like he got skin from somebody younger."

Ina almost laughed. She did not. "It's Mercury. They synchronized the feeds."

As if to confirm it, phones began screaming up and down the alley, one after another, then all at once, emergency tones stacked in ugly harmonics. A bus braked outside with a metallic scream that made the kitchen windows rattle. Somewhere nearby, a child started wailing and did not stop.

Ina switched to the reserve network panel. Channel after channel had identical clip injection points within seven seconds. Public broadcaster, two cable news stations, transit signage API, the military reserve app, and at least one major portal homepage. Whoever launched had rehearsal logs. They had timing maps down to frame intervals.

She opened her contacts sheet and started calling in order.

The two route couriers in Eunpyeong rang out to carrier hold music and then dropped.

The volunteer nurse on the Bukhansan shelter route sent her straight to a full voicemail box.

Only one person answered: a high school teacher she used to pass package drops through, Mr. Yoon, whose hobby was building radios from surplus parts and whose first question in any crisis was always about battery life.

"Mr. Yoon, stay inside," Ina said. "Don't move students unless there's direct local instruction from district office."

"I was welding a wire when this came through," he said, voice crackling. "The app says artillery risk. The TV says chemical warning now. Which lie are we buying?"

"Neither. Wait for district confirmation." She looked at the panel again. New injected text was rolling under the fake general: `Secondary contamination risk near water plants.` "Keep your masks ready anyway. And fill containers while you still have pressure."

"You sound like your mother when she did election coverage," he said. "That's not a compliment."

The line died.

Outside, engines revved and doors slammed. The alley was too narrow for two cars abreast, but people were trying anyway, headlights shoving against each other while neighbors shouted over hoods. A delivery rider clipped a side mirror and went down, scooter skidding sparks.

Ina called Minjae again.

A stranger grabbed the call over sirens, shouted that she had the wrong number, and cut the line.

She called an NIS number she hated having memorized.

Agent Choi picked up on the fourth ring. He never said hello. He spoke with a permanent sinus blockage and a collector's obsession with expensive fountain pens; in interrogation he had clicked a burgundy Sailor cap on and off until Ina wanted to bite him.

"You're seeing it," he said.

"It's your network," Ina snapped. "Shut it down."

"If it were ours, it would be cleaner."

"People are evacuating based on a fake mobilization order."

"People were primed to evacuate before the clip. Election week did that work for free." Papers rustled on his end. "Telecom is preparing a throttle in ten minutes."

Ina stared at the signal map. "A throttle will make this worse."

"Everything is worse." He lowered his voice. "Don't go public tonight. You're already in several draft packets."

"What packets?"

"Unverified foreign-collusion narratives. You don't want to know the details until we can—"

The call cut out mid-sentence.

There were no signal bars, and there was no fallback route.

She pushed back from the table and grabbed her coat. "I'm going out."

Hyejin caught her sleeve. Arthritic fingers, stronger than they looked, dug into wool. "You're not running into traffic because a screen told everybody to panic."

"People are going to get crushed in subway entrances."

"Then we open downstairs and we keep them where we can see them."

Hyejin was already moving. Fear made her practical. She shoved stockpots off the side stove, dumped two bags of rice into boiling water, and yelled up the stairwell for Deacon Kim. Her voice carried like a market vendor's despite her age, rolling over the noise outside in command sentences.

Within twenty minutes, the church basement had thirty-two people in it.

A grandmother with a perm flattening under sweat kept counting rosary beads though she said she had quit church years ago. A middle-school boy in soccer shorts sat on a folded table hugging a rabbit backpack and trying to hide his crying from his younger brother. A taxi driver with a cracked wedding ring and one shoe still wet from stepping into an open gutter handed out convenience-store kimbap like communion wafers, saying he could not eat when his stomach turned and somebody should.

Ina moved between them with a marker and an old attendance sheet, taking names, medication needs, missing relatives.

When the basement reached forty-one people, someone banged on the side door hard enough to shake dust from the ceiling pipes.

Constable Bae came in backward, hauling a woman by both forearms. Bae was younger than Ina had expected, with a cartoon bee patch sewn over a tear in his left boot and pepper spray crust drying on his collar. He kept glancing at his phone screen between dispatch updates; a wallpaper photo of a preschool kid in dinosaur pajamas flashed each time it lit.

"She fell on the station steps," he said, breathless. "Her ankle bent wrong. We got no ambulance."

The woman, fish blood on her pants from a market crate that had split over her, spat at him. "I did not fall. You people shoved everybody into one staircase."

"Yes," Bae said, not arguing. "And now we need a chair."

Hyejin produced one. The woman sat and cried without sound while a volunteer wrapped her ankle with a torn dish towel and tape.

Above ground, the sirens changed. Ina recognized the interval pattern from riot control deployment drills. She hated that she recognized it.

She climbed to the sanctuary level to get a signal through the stained-glass window gap and looked down toward the arterial road. Traffic had locked into a metal knot. Two buses stood jackknifed across lanes, and between them a man in a puffer vest pounded on bus doors and screamed for someone named Hana. A scooter burned near the curb with the tight, oily flame of plastic parts.

Her burner vibrated once.

`MJ: WHERE`

She typed with numb thumbs: `Mapo church basement. You?`

The sent checkmark never arrived; the message sat gray with `pending` until the burner timed out.

A second message came from an unknown number: `Civic notice: suspected foreign-linked media operatives active. Report unfamiliar neighbors. Cooperation protects families.`

The message included the Seoul Metropolitan logo, slightly pixelated.

Hyejin came up carrying a stack of blankets that smelled like cedar storage and old laundry soap. "Basement is full. We can use choir room next."

"You should lock the side entrance," Ina said.

"If we lock it, somebody freezes out there."

"If we leave it open, somebody follows the panic packets and drags people out by rumor."

Hyejin held Ina's gaze for a long second, then nodded once. "Half lock. Chain on, latch off."

That was how Hyejin made most decisions in crisis: split the risk and keep moving.

At 22:04, the first verified casualty report reached Ina through a district volunteer line that still held signal.

A seventy-four-year-old dialysis patient died in a taxi queue outside Seodaemun Clinic because the clinic shut its doors after the fake chemical-warning ticker and rerouted staff to municipal shelters.

Her burner buzzed again at 22:19: a warehouse worker in Guro had his chest crushed when crowd pressure snapped a temporary shelter gate inward.

Before she capped the marker, another call came at 22:31: a twelve-year-old asthmatic in Mangwon went into respiratory arrest after his inhaler ran out and his mother could not cross three blocked intersections to reach a pharmacy.

Each death was preventable. Each one.

Ina wrote each line on the legal pad with the same black marker she had used for ration counts. Her wrist cramped by the third name. She kept writing.

By 23:00, telecom throttling had started. Messages took minutes or never arrived. Calls connected and dissolved into robotic echoes. The city shrank to whatever block your voice could cross.

Around midnight, the deepfake clip mutated.

Edited versions appeared with new subtitles, accusing "internal sympathizers" of aiding border infiltration. One cut inserted a still image of a bus explosion from ten years earlier, color-corrected to look current. Another overlaid a fake geotag near the Han River and told civilians to avoid bridges, which drove even more cars into smaller streets and emergency lanes.

Ina watched one version loop on a shelter volunteer's phone while two elderly men argued over whether to walk home before curfew and ended up shoving each other hard enough that one hit a pew and split his eyebrow.

The room smelled like wet wool, instant ramyeon broth, and fear sweat.

By 01:14, people had stopped asking if the clip was real. They asked only which rumor would kill them slower.

The basement lights flickered twice, steadied, then flickered again. Somebody started praying out loud. Somebody else told her to shut up. Hyejin banged a soup ladle against a stockpot and assigned chores in escalating volume until everyone had something to do with their hands.

Ina went back upstairs and tried Minjae again.

His phone did not ring this time; the relay returned a three-note error and dropped her back into silence.

At 02:07, the door chain rattled in three sharp jerks, a pattern Ina recognized from their old route meetings.

She ran.

Minjae stood in the entry hall with rain in his hair and pepper-spray tears dried white on his cheeks. His jacket was unzipped, shirt half out, and there was blood on his cuff that was not his own. The scar on his shoulder, the one Ina had traced with her thumb months ago, showed through a ripped seam.

He did not hug her. He did not ask permission.

"Where were you?" he said.

"Here. Trying to keep people from stampeding to nowhere." Ina looked past him into the alley. "What happened?"

"Sweep happened." His laugh broke on the second syllable. "You remember sweeps, right? Door kicks, zip ties, floor face down, everybody's a traitor until paperwork clears."

Hyejin appeared with a towel and froze when she saw his face. "Minjae-ya, come sit."

"No." He wiped his nose with the back of his hand. "My sister's gone."

The words landed clean and hard, no room for mishearing.

Ina stepped closer. "Tell me from the beginning."

"Beginning is they hit Mullae at midnight. Third and fourth blocks first, then the row houses by the machine shops. They had addresses printed. They had apartment numbers." He shoved a crumpled paper at her. It was a photocopied intake sheet with blurred headers and a handwritten district seal. "They called it a protective relocation sweep."

The paper smelled like rainwater and copier toner. On the back was a list of resident IDs, some crossed out in red.

"She texted me at 00:42," Minjae said. "She sent a photo of our stove because she forgot if she turned it off. You know how she gets. She worries about stupid domestic shit in the middle of hell." His voice cracked and then sharpened. "At 01:03 she called. I heard boots in the hallway and one of her kids screaming because somebody stepped on a drawing board. Then dead air."

His sister's kids came back to Ina in details instead of faces: the cartoon-peach sleep mask one of them wore when alley neon leaked through the curtains, the zip pouch full of bus transfer cards sorted by color because the world felt less random that way.

"Where are the kids?" Ina asked.

"Neighbor grabbed them. They're at his cousin's floor. They keep asking why their mother left without shoes." Minjae swallowed hard enough she saw his throat work. "She didn't leave without shoes. They took her while she was boiling anchovy broth. Pot burned to black."

Hyejin set the towel on a chair and took over without asking. "Eat first, then we move."

"I can't eat."

"Then chew and spit. You need salt."

He stared at her, then took the rice ball she pushed into his hand and bit once like it was punishment.

Ina spread the photocopy on the prep table under the buzzing light. Some names had check marks. Some had arrows to handwritten codes: `S-4`, `screening`, `transfer pending`. The header said `Joint Stability Taskforce`.

"This isn't standard police format," Ina said.

"I know what police paperwork looks like."

"These codes are intelligence intake tags."

Minjae's eyes snapped up. "How do you know that?"

The leverage shifted, swift and ugly.

She met his stare. She could lie. She did not. "They used similar tags in Hongje. During detention."

"So they used your server and your lessons both."

The sentence hit like a slap because it was close to true.

Ina forced herself to keep her voice level. "I gave up one backup location under threat. I didn't give them family addresses."

"Your one location connected route logs. Route logs have family addresses when people are stupid and tired and trying to deliver medicine before curfew."

He slammed his palm on the table. The burner phones jumped.

Hyejin stepped between them, not dramatic, just present. "You can fight after we find her. Right now you need a place that still has lists."

Minjae looked away first. "District gym in Yeongdeungpo had intake tables. They moved people out before dawn. Nobody says where."

"Then we go there and make somebody say it," Ina said.

By 03:05, they were in Hyejin's van, the one with a dented rear door and a rosary hanging from the mirror that hit the windshield on potholes with a soft tick-tick sound.

Every tap of the rosary against glass made Ina count without meaning to. Four taps, inhale. Four taps, exhale. If she let panic choose her timing, she would start making the same category errors that had already filled half her pad.

Roadblocks turned the city into a maze of impatient headlights and uniforms. At one intersection, riot police redirected traffic away from a bridge while a loudspeaker repeated, "Remain calm and follow official instructions," in the same synthetic female voice that did subway etiquette announcements. Nobody sounded calm. People shouted district names through cracked windows like passwords.

Near Mullae, they passed Minjae's block. The machine-shop shutters were down. Every third door had red tape cross-sealed with municipal stickers. A burned pot sat on one windowsill, black ring up the side, exactly as he had said.

The red tape and handwritten unit numbers flashed her back to Hongje for one bad second: gloves, clipboards, names read out like inventory. She dug her thumbnail into her palm until the memory snapped and the windshield came back into focus.

They reached Yeongdeungpo District Auxiliary Gym at 03:42.

Inside, fluorescent lights burned at full brightness over folding tables and plastic chairs. The air smelled like bleach and sleep deprivation. Volunteers in orange vests sorted paper stacks with frantic precision.

Ina scanned exits, camera domes, and badge colors before she reached Table C. If this desk froze, she needed a second point of entry ready in under a minute.

At Table C, a municipal clerk with glitter nail polish chipped to the quick and a hand brace on her right wrist took Minjae's resident numbers without looking up.

"Family name?" she asked.

"Kang," he said. "Household from Mullae three-block row houses. Adult female, dependent minors already separated."

Her fingers paused over the keyboard.

"I need given name," she said.

Minjae gave it. Ina heard it and held it privately.

The clerk typed, frowned, typed again. "No release log under that combination."

"Try screening backlog," Ina said.

"I did." The clerk rubbed her brace with her left thumb. "There were two transfers from that block around 02:10. Destination code blank."

"Blank how?"

"Blank like above my pay grade." She lowered her voice. "If code is blank it means agency handoff, not hospital."

Minjae leaned in. "Which agency?"

"Sir, don't do this here. There are cameras."

"My sister got pulled in a fake-war sweep and you have a blank box where her life should be."

The clerk finally looked up. She had mascara smudged under both eyes and the exhausted focus of someone who had been useful too long. "I've got a brother on reserve call tonight. I'm not your enemy. I'm telling you what I can tell you: this list won't save her. Get a lawyer with security clearance or someone inside."

Minjae laughed once, raw. "Inside is how we got here."

He turned away before security volunteers could notice his volume.

Outside the gym, dawn had not arrived yet, but the sky had gone from black to bruised violet. A coffee cart at the curb was doing business like a war economy. Ina bought three cans of hot sweet coffee from the vendor, a woman in a padded vest who kept clicking a metal tally counter each time she sold one, as if counting gave shape to chaos.

Minjae took the can and did not drink.

"You said assessment," he said after a long silence. "Assess this. She's gone."

"Missing isn't dead."

"Don't give me subtitle optimism." He crushed the can in his fist, metal cracking. "You had one job after Hongje. Keep your head down till leak day. Instead you went full triage saint in Mapo while they emptied my block."

"I was keeping people alive."

"You were keeping strangers alive because they were in front of you." He stepped closer, voice low and shaking. "My sister wasn't in front of you. She was in a spreadsheet you already surrendered."

That was another near-truth. Worse than accusation, because it fit.

Ina looked at the gym entrance where families queued with paper cups and stiff shoulders. "If I apologize now, does it bring her back?"

"No."

"Then use me." She forced each word to stay steady. "You want inside access? I still have one dead route. I still have names of people who traded intake lists. We work or we bury people separately."

He stared at her long enough that she counted four siren passes on the main road.

"I don't trust you," he said.

"I don't trust me either tonight."

He gave a short, wrecked laugh and wiped his face with both hands. "At least that sounds honest."

He reached into his jacket and pulled out a USB drive wrapped in chewing-gum foil. "I copied this from a taskforce terminal while they were yelling at each other about bridge closures. Partial pickup logs, maybe one hour window. If you burn this, I bury you."

"Fair," Ina said, and took it.

On the drive, back in the van with laptops balanced on their knees, they found forty-seven intake entries from three districts and eight destination codes. Two codes mapped to known municipal shelters. Three mapped to temporary police holding centers. Three were alphanumeric strings with no public directory match.

Minjae's sister was under one of the unknown codes.

By 06:20, morning broadcasts had shifted tone.

The emergency clip stopped replaying. Official stations now ran calm male anchors in navy ties, promising stability and condemning "malicious synthetic media." A red banner crawled under every channel: `Foreign Influence Investigation Underway`.

Then Ina's face appeared.

The first image was a screenshot of her leaving MiraeWave during the chapter-six raid, eyes narrowed against camera flash.

The second image was an old passport photo, cropped too tight.

The third image was a graphic linking her name to shell companies and fabricated remittance routes, with a headline that used the phrase `foreign narrative asset` in quotation marks designed to look like evidence.

The packet propagated faster than correction channels could breathe. By 06:34, neighborhood chat groups were sharing her image with warnings. By 06:41, one major portal had a liveblog citing anonymous intelligence sources. By 06:52, a conservative radio host called her "the woman who wrote panic into our bloodstream for foreign pay."

Ina stared at her own face on the van screen until features became geometry: eyebrow line, scar at wrist in one grainy still, mouth half open like she was guilty and surprised to be caught.

"They seeded this before midnight," she said. "Choi warned me about draft packets."

"So warn back," Minjae said. "Go live."

"If I go live now, I confirm I still have active channels. I burn the only leak path we have left."

"People already think you're a spy."

"People can think whatever they need to survive breakfast. If I answer in real time, they trace the reply to Hyejin's network, then to you, then to her kids." She swallowed the next sentence and said it anyway. "And to your sister, if she's still in a transport queue and not off-grid yet."

He slammed his hand against the dashboard once, then gripped the steering wheel and breathed through his teeth.

"Kwon will go on air formal as a funeral and call for unity," he said. "He'll pretend restraint while he feeds them your bones."

He was right.

At 07:10, Director Kwon appeared at a podium with the national flag and a civic-seal backdrop. His silver hair was perfect. His suit was untouched by overnight panic.

"Our nation has endured orchestrated psychological aggression," he said in measured cadence. "We will respond within law and with composure. Individuals who collaborated with foreign information organs will be identified through due process. Citizens should refrain from rumor and trust institutions."

He did not say Ina's name. He did not need to.

Hyejin called while Kwon was still speaking.

"Come back," she said. Her voice was rough from hours of shouting over frightened people. "Basement families are leaving and fighting in the alley over taxi priority. We got one old man with chest pain and no ambulance for forty minutes. Also somebody pasted your photo on our gate."

"We're coming," Ina said.

They drove in silence.

At a red light near Hapjeong that never turned green because officers were manually waving lanes, Minjae spoke without looking at her.

"Her kids keep asking if their mother forgot them."

Ina watched rain bead on the windshield and get smeared by overworked wipers. "What did you tell them?"

"I told them she's stubborn and she bites people who touch her stove." His mouth twitched once. "That made the little one stop crying for a minute."

Back at the church, somebody had spray-painted `TRAITOR` across the side wall in black enamel that dripped like motor oil.

Inside, the basement had thinned to twenty people. Those who stayed looked wrung out, less panicked, more ashamed. Crisis had moved from adrenaline to accounting.

Ina sat at the prep table and opened a new page on the legal pad.

`DEAD`

`MISSING`

`DETAINED`

`DISPLACED`

She built the list from calls, from scribbled notes passed by volunteers, from Minjae's copied logs, from one nurse's photo of a triage board, from rumor she could half-verify and rumor she refused to write down yet.

By 08:05, confirmed dead stood at eleven.

At 09:12, she crossed out the count and rewrote it as nineteen.

A fish-market vendor arrived at 09:26 in rubber boots slick with thaw water, dragging a boy whose school blazer was buttoned wrong and streaked with dried broth. She slapped a damp resident card and a convenience-store receipt onto Ina's table.

"Seodaemun stair crush," the woman said. "Dispatch told me to check online. I don't have internet. I have this and his lunch thermos. Tell me if my husband is alive."

The boy hugged a stainless thermos hard enough to leave finger dents in the metal. Anchovy broth dripped from the lid and ran over his knuckles.

Ina checked Bae's folded list against the triage photo and found the same name twice, once misspelled, both entries ending at the same time stamp.

"Ms. Lee," Ina said, and hated the clean shape of her own voice, "he was pulled from the station stairs at 08:41. They tried CPR. He didn't make it."

The woman folded at the waist like somebody had kicked out a hinge. The thermos hit tile with a steel crack and rolled under the sink, leaving a hot fish-salt trail.

Ina drew a new column on the ledger: `FAMILY NOTIFIED`.

She looked at Hyejin. "No one gets sent back to portal rumors. We confirm names here, then route them straight to body-ID desks before records get laundered."

Hyejin was already tearing church bulletin paper into referral slips. "Write fast."

By noon, every number came with two versions: official and observed. The official count was always lower.

Hyejin set down a plate of cold jeon nobody ate and pointed at the `MISSING` column. "Put ages. Families hear age first."

Ina added ages where she had them.

Constable Bae returned around midday with his bee-patched boot and no voice left. He handed Ina a folded note from station triage.

"Your false-evacuation crush at Seodaemun is now four dead," he rasped. "I should not give you this."

"Why are you giving it?"

He looked down at his phone wallpaper kid and then back at her. "Because my wife is six months pregnant and spent the night under a desk at daycare because an app told her artillery was coming. I would like one true document in this city."

Ina nodded once and copied the names.

At 13:20, Agent Choi finally reconnected on an encrypted voice relay that stuttered every few seconds.

"You're trending under hostile labels," he said. "Don't issue statement."

"I didn't plan to."

"Good. We can maybe contain prosecutorial timeline if you remain nonreactive."

"Contain for whom?"

A pause. Paper shuffling again. He always handled paper when lying.

"For everyone still breathing," he said.

"Minjae's sister is missing under agency handoff code. Can you trace?"

"I can't on this line. Send code through dead protocol."

"Dead protocol was compromised in Hongje."

"Then pray your church cipher still works." His voice flattened. "And Ina? If you post denial now, the packet team wins twice. They want you desperate and traceable."

The line cut.

Ina sat with the silent phone against her ear until Hyejin took it from her hand and set it down gently.

"You can't out-shout a loudspeaker with one throat," Hyejin said.

"I know."

"Do it later when it hurts them, not when it soothes you."

That was Hyejin's ethics in one sentence: utility first, absolution optional.

Minjae spent the afternoon on the floor against the pantry door, knees up, laptop open, trying route code variations with bloodshot concentration. Every twenty minutes he called his sister's kids from a different number so location tracing would have to work harder. He spoke softly on those calls, voice almost unrecognizable.

"Did you eat?" he asked the younger kid once.

A small voice crackled back through speaker. Ina heard only two words clearly: "peach mask."

"Yes, wear it," Minjae said. "Wear two if you want."

By evening, rain turned to sleet.

People stopped arriving at the church. People started knocking to ask if the rumor was true that foreign agents had poisoned shelter water. Hyejin sent each one away with a cup from the tap and made them drink in front of her.

Ji-eun's message from the night before finally delivered at 18:03, followed by a new one.

`I saw your face on TV in a noodle shop. Lady next to me said she knew you looked expensive. I laughed and then I threw up in the sink. Tell me if I should cut my hair and disappear again.`

Ina typed: `Stay put. Eat. Charge everything. No public posts.`

Three dots appeared. Disappeared. Returned.

`You always sound calm when everything is burning.`

Ina did not answer that line.

The official casualty bulletin published at 19:40: seven dead, thirty-two injured, ninety-one relocated for security screening.

Ina's sheet, now taped across two prep tables, held twenty-six confirmed dead, at least fifty-nine injured, one hundred forty-three screening detentions, and thirty-four unresolved missing entries.

Twelve minutes later, Sunhee from the next block came back through the basement door carrying a clear belongings bag from Seodaemun overflow intake: cracked glasses, one house key, and a wallet with water-warped photos still inside. Her brother had been listed as `relocated for screening` on the noon bulletin.

"Then why did the morgue clerk make me sign for this?" she asked, voice scraped hollow.

Ina took the bag. The plastic was warm from Sunhee's grip and smelled like antiseptic and cigarette smoke. She matched the ID number to Minjae's copied logs and found the noon status line still unchanged.

She circled the entry so hard the pen tore paper, then flipped to a clean sheet and rewrote the board in a new order: `NAME`, `STATUS`, `SOURCE`, `LAST VERIFIED HUMAN CONTACT`.

"No more total-first updates," she said to Minjae. "We go name by name until their neat bulletin runs out of places to hide."

He nodded and pulled his laptop closer. "Then we stay up."

Minjae's sister remained unresolved.

When the fluorescent strip finally gave out and the kitchen dropped to amber emergency lighting, Ina lit two candles from the church supply drawer and set them in metal bowls so wax would not drip on records.

She looked at the page where her own name now sat under a new heading she had not meant to write:

`FRAMED NARRATIVE IMPACT`

`- doxxed to neighborhood chats`

`- tagged as foreign asset on three major portals`

`- church gate vandalized`

`- witness reluctance increasing after image circulation`

`- can't rebut without exposing active evidence path`

The last line she underlined twice.

Minjae came to stand across from her, hands braced on the table. He looked older than he had that morning, as if panic had burned through a year's worth of pretense.

"I still blame you," he said.

"I know."

"I still need you."

"I know that too."

He nodded, once, like signing an ugly contract.

"Then we work tonight," he said. "No speeches."

"No speeches."

Hyejin turned off the stairwell light to save power and sat down with them, glasses chain clicking softly in the dim.

They worked with paper because paper could not be throttled, could not be geolocated unless someone physically stole it, could not be overwritten by an elegant lie in a server room.

By 23:00, they had cross-indexed seventy-two names against three source lists.

By midnight, they had a probable match between one unknown transfer code and an old civil-defense warehouse used for undocumented labor screenings years earlier.

It was a thin lead. It was not nothing.

Ina tucked the hymnbook pages deeper into her coat and wrote the date at the top of a clean sheet.

Mercury Night had started as a synchronization problem, code and timing and trusted channels. It ended, in her hands, as handwriting and wet coats and a list that kept growing while official numbers stayed neat.

Outside, somebody in the alley cursed at a frozen scooter engine. A train crossed the river in the distance, a low iron sound through sleet.

Ina did not go online to defend herself.

She kept counting the dead, the missing, and the living who might still be pulled back if she did not spend the last evidence path on tonight's fire.

At 04:37, one clean ring broke through from Seoul St. Mary's emergency desk. Ina copied the number in the margin, finished the line she was on, and reached for her coat.


# Chapter 16

Forty minutes after Seoul St. Mary's emergency desk broke through the sleet with one clean ring, Ina found parking two blocks from the emergency wing, where the snow had melted into black slush that held tire tracks like fossil prints. She stepped over a busted bucket of road salt and kept the discharge packet under her coat because the paper had gone soft from her hand sweat. The packet had her father's name, his resident number, the phrase mild ischemic stroke, and a billing estimate that sat in her mouth like a coin she could not spit out. He had been admitted less than a day, and the hospital had already printed a menu of therapies that looked like restaurant options for people who had backup money.

She pushed through the sliding doors into hot recycled air that smelled of bleach, burnt coffee, and boiled seaweed from the staff cafeteria. The lobby television was muted, but she did not need audio to recognize her own face on the scrolling panel beside the weather map. The lower-third called her a foreign-influence script operative. Someone had picked a photo from six years ago, before the scar on her left wrist whitened, before her eyes learned how to stay open all night without ever feeling rested.

The billing clerk at window three wore a cardigan pilled at both elbows and lined up paper clips by color while she spoke. She tapped the page with a pink acrylic nail and said this was only the immediate estimate, not the total, then lowered her voice when she saw the red hold tag on Ina's insurance account.

"Your automatic payment failed three times," the clerk said. "Do you have another card?"

Ina gave her the same card. The machine rejected it. She gave her a second card she had not used in months. The machine rejected that one too.

"System freeze?"

The clerk nodded without looking up. "You might need to resolve it with your bank. We can continue treatment, but discharge planning requires a guarantor signature." She slid the form forward and added, almost kindly, "If family can rotate home care, that can reduce cost."

Family could rotate shame and unpaid labor. Family could rotate who took calls from creditors. Family could not rotate money that was not there.

Ina signed where the clerk pointed. She had signed so many forms in the last ten months that her own signature looked counterfeit to her now, each version more cramped than the last. She tucked the carbon copy into her coat and took the elevator to neurology, where the hallway lights were too bright and the air was cold enough that every cough sounded sharpened.

Dohyun sat upright in bed in a gown patterned with tiny blue squares. He hated lying down if anyone could see him, even now, with the left side of his mouth still dragging a little and his knee brace looped around the visitor chair because hospital socks would not fit over it. His fingers, permanently yellowed from decades of cigarettes, kept folding and unfolding the foil lid from his medication cup into perfect little rectangles, then smoothing them flat again with the side of his thumbnail.

He looked older than sixty-six when he was still and younger when he swore. Today he was still.

"You ate?" he asked.

It was the first thing he asked every time he got scared: never how bad it was, never whether they were safe. You ate.

"I had kimbap from the convenience store," she said.

"You always lie when you say that."

"Then don't ask if you don't want to hear it."

A ghost of annoyance crossed his face, which was a relief. Irritation meant he still recognized the shape of their old fights.

He looked at the muted television mounted near the ceiling. Her image had rotated off. A celebrity divorce had replaced her.

"They said you did this," he said. "All that panic last night. The dead at the station. They said you wrote it like a movie."

She pulled the plastic chair closer and sat so her knees almost touched the mattress rail. "I didn't trigger Mercury Night."

"You were in it."

She could have denied that too, but his right hand shook when he reached for water, and she was tired of lies that only bought three minutes.

"Yes," she said. "I was in it. Not in that part."

Dohyun drank and missed his mouth a little. Water ran down his chin into the collar of the gown. He wiped it with the back of his hand and got angry at the hand for being clumsy.

"The doctor says rehab," he said. "Three times a week if we can pay. Maybe less if we can't. He says if I skip, my left leg might get worse." He stared at the blanket while he spoke, as if reading figures off hospital cotton. "Landlord came yesterday. He posted the notice in red. Whole building saw it. I took it down before your aunt got there."

He paused to breathe, then added in a flatter tone, "I'm not doing prison food at this age."

That was the lever: not ideology or history, just rice arriving cold in an aluminum tray.

Ina let the sentence sit between them until the monitor counted ten heartbeats. "No one has charged you."

"Not yet. They froze your accounts. They can freeze me too. They can call me a collaborator and then what? You think judges care if my knee locks in winter?" He turned toward her, one eyebrow climbing with old dockworker sarcasm. "I can't even fill out hospital forms without your aunt checking boxes."

He only used full forms when he was trying to sound in control. The effort made the room feel smaller.

"Appa, listen to me."

"No, you listen." His voice stayed low, but the foil in his hand tore. "Whatever files you still have, burn them. Throw the drives in the river. Say nothing, take the small shame, and keep breathing. People survive that way."

"And if you dump everything now," he went on, "the ones still trying to talk across the line will get called traitors too. You could kill the last path before it even opens."

She felt the old heat in her chest, the one that used to send her into speeches she regretted. She did not trust speeches anymore. She put both hands on the bed rail and pressed until the metal bit her palms.

"People already died," she said. "They died because we let men like Kwon call it weather."

"Don't say his name like he's the only one."

She blinked. "What?"

He stared at his own hands, then laughed once without humor. "You think this started with him and your mother and your fancy council office? You think that was the first room where everyone told themselves it was temporary?"

He shifted in bed, wincing when his weak leg dragged against the sheet. "In ninety-three I was on night maintenance at Incheon dry cargo, Pier Seven. We got sealed crates marked educational media for church relief up north. We were told to log them as hymnals and medicine because customs would move faster. We did it. I did it. I stamped every form."

Ina's fingers loosened on the rail.

He kept going, words gathering speed now that they had started. "Your mother knew the courier list before I did. She said the material could cool tempers, help families hear each other, keep soldiers from doing stupid things. She always had the story first, facts later. I believed her because belief paid overtime and because your school fees were due." He coughed into his fist. "One winter run came back light. Four crates missing. Two boys from our crew did not come home. We were told there had been weather trouble on the road. We signed amended manifests and moved on."

Outside the door, a cart wheel squeaked in a steady rhythm, then stopped.

"You knew," Ina said. Her voice came out thinner than she expected. "You knew people were getting taken."

"I knew something was wrong and I told myself wrong was still better than war. That was the line. Everyone had one." He looked up at her finally. His eyes were wet, irritated more than tender. "Your aunt moved cash through church meal budgets when payments could not appear in the open. Your mother wrote scripts with phrases that told some families when to leave and told others to stay put and wait. I signed repair logs so hidden transmitters looked like broken pumps. We were all useful. We were all cowards on schedule."

Ina heard blood in her ears, a rushing sound like subway brakes screaming into a station tunnel. The room's white paint seemed to flare and flatten at the same time. For months she had held the story like a triangle of guilt with Kwon at one point, her mother at another, and herself at the third. Dohyun had always stood outside it, gruff and broke and mostly absent. Now he stepped inside and changed the geometry.

"Why are you telling me this now?" she asked.

"Because I had half my face go numb while watching your name crawl under dead people on TV." He swallowed hard. "Because if they come for us, I can't run and I can't lie fast anymore. Because I'm tired of hearing this family talk like we got dragged into history by accident."

His right hand reached for her wrist and closed around the burn scar. It was not gentle, but it was deliberate.

"Truth doesn't feed old men," he said. "Truth doesn't pay landlords. You want to expose everything, fine. Just don't pretend it's clean. It'll crush what's left of us."

She wanted to say she understood. She wanted to say she would think. What came out was uglier and more honest.

"What's left of us is already built on amended manifests and fake church ledgers." She pulled her wrist free and stood. "If I stay quiet now, I'm not protecting family. I'm extending your shift."

He flinched, then laughed again, softer this time. "You got that from your mother. She cut with nouns."

He leaned back against the pillows and closed his eyes. The fight had used more oxygen than his lungs wanted to give.

"I'm not asking for forgiveness," he said. "I'm asking for one selfish thing. If you do it, redact the people who didn't choose this. Neighbors. Kids. The woman at the fish stall who carried envelopes because we told her it was donations." He opened one eye and looked at her. "Don't make small people pay for our big lies."

That landed harder than his warning about prison.

"I won't," she said.

He nodded, exhausted. "Then do it fast before your courage gets practical." He shifted his shoulder against the pillow, eyes already closing. "And eat something real. You look like static."

She stayed until his breathing slowed into the shallow rhythm she remembered from nights he came home after dock overtime and slept in jeans on top of the blanket. The monitor beeped with machine confidence, the sort of certainty bodies never matched.

In the stairwell she called Hyejin. Her aunt answered on the second ring, breathless, with kitchen noise behind her.

"Don't tell me he pulled out his IV again," Hyejin said.

"He kept it in. I need the hymnbook."

Silence. Then the sound of a metal ladle set down hard.

"Which one?"

"Blue cover, 1984 printing, split spine around page one-forty. The one you said was gone."

"I said hidden, not gone." Hyejin sighed through her nose. "Meet me at church basement in forty minutes. Bring gloves. The archive room heater died and it feels like God forgot the room on purpose."

The church basement smelled of sesame oil, damp cardboard, and old paper mold that no amount of bleach could kill. Hyejin had changed out of her kitchen apron into a padded vest, but she still wore pink dishwashing gloves under fingerless wool mittens because the soap cracks on her knuckles had reopened in the cold. She kept worrying each split with her thumb while she talked, like she could sand pain down by friction.

The hymnbook looked ordinary unless you knew her habits. She never underlined verses. She marked recipes, debts, and warnings in hymn margins, always in mechanical pencil, always with numbers that looked like page references. Ina laid the book flat on a stainless prep table beside a crate of onions and photographed every marked page.

"I thought they took this in fourteen," Ina said.

"They took the decoy." Hyejin snorted and adjusted her glasses chain where it had twisted. "They raided my kitchen office first. Of course they did. Men with badges always think older women hide state secrets inside kimchi jars because we're quaint. So I gave them a hymnbook with fake offsets and a nice tea towel. They left feeling clever."

Ina found page 143. Beside verse three was a penciled string: 6-11-4 / 2-19-7 / 14-2-5.

"Hash segments," Ina murmured.

"No, first gate." Hyejin tapped the margin with a gloved finger. "You map hymn number to the old church phone tree, then reverse by district code. Your mother designed it after a pastor in Uijeongbu snitched in '98. She trusted liturgy more than servers."

Ina opened her laptop, tethered through a prepaid hotspot, and built the key from the sequence. Her fingers moved fast, then stopped, then started again when memory caught up. The login prompt on the dead-drop node accepted the first token and rejected the second.

"Fuck." She rechecked the order. "Did you ever rotate this after they held me in Hongje?"

"I rotated two nodes, not this one. Wait." Hyejin pulled the book closer, squinting. "You read verse numbers as Arabic. That slash mark means batch break, not division. Use Hangul index order for the second line. Your mother hated standardization when she thought people were watching."

Ina corrected the parse and tried again.

The node opened.

A plain text directory appeared on the screen with six folders: cassette_audio, ledger_fragments, contracts, route_logs, medical_lists, media_payloads. Timestamps showed activity from three nights ago. Nothing had been corrupted. Nothing had been deleted.

She had expected relief to feel warm. It felt like a drop in an elevator shaft.

"Integrity check," she said.

She ran hash verification across the files she could compare locally. Green lines stacked down the terminal window. Match. Match. Match. A single mismatch flashed in route_logs/segment_c. She opened it and saw that coordinates had been manually replaced by neighborhood names. Hyejin had done that herself.

"You redacted this already," Ina said.

"I'm old, not reckless. Current routes stay alive or people don't eat." Hyejin lifted the thermos she had brought and poured barley tea into paper cups from the funeral stock closet. "Sit. Your shoulders are up by your ears."

Ina sat.

Hyejin wrapped both hands around her cup, even with the gloves on. "Your father called me from the ambulance. He sounded like he was chewing gravel. He said if he died before you got there, I had to tell you where the real book was."

Ina stared at the tea surface until it stopped shaking. "He told me about Pier Seven. About you moving money through church accounts."

"Then he finally quit pretending he only touched clean work." Hyejin's mouth tightened, but her voice stayed practical. "Good. Secrets age like fish. They don't improve."

"Did you believe any of it would help unification?"

"Sometimes." Hyejin gave a small shrug. "Mostly I believed if I refused, someone worse would do it and skim more. That's not morality. That's bookkeeping with excuses." She took a sip and winced because the tea was too hot. "You're not going to get a pure choice. You're choosing which damage you can stand looking at every morning."

Ina looked back at the laptop and opened the redaction worksheet she had built after she decoded her mother's cassette, then abandoned when detention shredded the schedule. She added new rules in a blunt list: remove minors; remove present safe-house addresses; remove route names still active; keep dates, command chains, payment ledgers, and signed approvals; keep her own contract and NIS cooperation terms; keep her mother's cassette full audio, but bleep one survivor surname at request.

"I need mirrors before I hit publish," Ina said. "If they get one legal order in the first ten minutes, this dies."

"Already arranged." Hyejin reached into her vest pocket and produced a folded receipt from a bakery in Suwon. The back had six handles and two onion-routing links written in lipstick, dark red. "Fan subtitle kids owe me for hiding one of their cousins during the station crackdown. Civic lawyers in Toronto owe me because I fixed their Korean tax paperwork for free. They all said yes."

Ina laughed once, a dry sound she barely recognized. "I keep calling this distribution strategy so I don't call it panic. You hide whole channels in bakery receipts and church lunch ledgers."

"People answer a food call faster than a manifesto," Hyejin said. "And nobody raids the deacon who tracks onions. Drink your tea and send the files."

By midnight they moved to Ina's apartment because the church internet throttled after eleven and the basement heater had died again. The apartment smelled like instant ramen broth and damp plaster. She had not cleaned since release from detention. A cracked cereal bowl sat in the sink with a spoon fused to dried sauce. A stack of legal envelopes leaned against the wall beside her mother's green lyric notebook.

She set up three machines: her wiped work laptop running from a live USB, an old desktop tower Minjae had salvaged for her months ago, and Hyejin's church office computer that still had Bible software stickers on the case. She taped paper over the webcam on all three, then checked hashes one more time and began packaging.

First bundle: Evidence Core. The cassette in full, with transcript and timecoded annotations linking voice references to old shipping manifests. Shadow ledger fragments, scanned at six hundred dpi so handwriting pressure and corrections were visible. Contract scans showing stipend schedules tied to silence clauses.

Second bundle: Harm Index. Detention metadata crosswalk, casualty tallies from Mercury Night hospitals, payment routes to shell firms, and internal messages where civilian injury was described as acceptable narrative weather. She kept the ugliest line in, the one where a strategist had written, If panic spikes, we can convert it into turnout. She left it in because euphemism was how this survived so long.

Third bundle: Redacted Operations Appendix. Current addresses removed. Minor names replaced by stable pseudonyms and age bands. Active border logistics stripped to district-level references. In place of full coordinates she inserted a note: Deliberately withheld to prevent retaliatory targeting of non-command participants.

She wrote a statement file and stared at the blinking cursor for twelve minutes before the language stopped sounding like a grant proposal and started sounding like a person.

I participated in this operation. I accepted payment, protection, and instruction. I also collected evidence because the operation harmed civilians while denying harm in public. This archive shows command intent, financial incentives, and manipulated media infrastructure over decades. I redacted non-command identities where publication would create immediate physical risk. I did not redact decision makers, payment authorizers, or myself.

She signed it with her legal name and resident district.

"No alias?" Hyejin asked.

"Alias was the problem," Ina said.

Hyejin nodded once and went to the kitchenette to make ramyeon like it was a hospital protocol. She always added half a slice of processed cheese and called it nutrition. While the water boiled, she turned on the apartment radio low. A late-night call-in host was talking about school closures, then switched to rumors about emergency decrees.

Ina messaged the mirror contacts in sequence.

`seed_1_fansub_union`: package incoming in 120 seconds.

`civic_records_TO`: hold publication until hash confirmed.

`hanriver_archive`: if legal notice arrives, repost immutable link and timestamp.

Responses came back almost immediately.

`fansub_union`: received. seeding from 14 nodes.

`civic_records_TO`: confirmed. legal review waived under urgent public interest exception.

`hanriver_archive`: upload when ready. we already got two phishing lures in your name.

She inhaled, held, exhaled. Then she pushed `publish_all.sh`.

The first upload bar crept, stalled at sixty-two percent, then resumed when the backup path kicked in through a university mirror in Daejeon. Timestamps rolled across all three screens. At 00:17 a takedown notice hit the primary node under emergency national security authority, citing unauthorized disclosure and cross-border destabilization risk. At 00:19 a second notice demanded host shutdown and user identity preservation. The legal language should have felt dry, but it landed in her ribs like someone testing the door.

"There it is," Hyejin said from the table, blowing on noodles. "Big men discovered copy and paste."

Ina rerouted the torrent manifest to six additional trackers and posted hash values to fan boards that normally argued about dance line distribution and subtitle timing. The first repost came from a teenager account with a profile picture of a cartoon rabbit. The caption said, If they're deleting, we keep it alive. Her throat tightened at that, gratitude and dread arriving as one taste.

At 00:24 the primary civic server went dark for forty seconds under load, then came back with mirrored links hosted in Taiwan, Canada, and Germany. Seven minutes later another takedown order appeared, addressed directly to her employment email and copied to MiraeWave legal plus two agencies she had never heard of. She realized she had been grinding her teeth hard enough to make her jaw ache.

Her phone vibrated with a bank alert, skittering across the table like an insect.

Account status changed: temporary suspension pending compliance inquiry.

Then MiraeWave:

We are terminating all contractual relationships effective immediately, including residual payment streams.

Then a text from an unknown number she recognized anyway from interrogation room days.

You have escalated exposure under Article 14 and 22. Counsel recommended.

She had two options, both irreversible. Option A: shut down the home node, wipe local logs, and call counsel before dawn, hoping to preserve a legal lane and a few hours of physical anonymity. Option B: keep the node hot and bind the evidence to her own body in public, which would make suppression harder and make her easier to find.

"If you pick B, you don't get to take it back," Hyejin said.

Ina opened the front camera. "I'm done taking it back."

She chose Option B. In a twenty-second verification clip she showed her face, her burn scar, and the first checksum line, then pushed it to every mirror thread.

The cost landed inside two minutes. Her name started trending again, but this time the tag split: traitor and witness. Both sides used her legal name, apartment district, and old school photos. A doxxing thread added her building entrance, guessed floor number, and the time the convenience store downstairs restocked beer.
