The intercom buzzed once, then again, a flat electric rasp that made the apartment shrink. Hyejin killed every light and pulled the living-room curtain tight. Ina disabled the smart-door feed, shoved a metal shoe rack against the deadbolt, and peeled the handwritten name strip off the mailbox key tray.

By 00:45 the package had splintered beyond any single jurisdiction. Fan translations of her statement appeared in Japanese, English, and Spanish before she had finished eating half the ramyeon. Someone extracted the casualty chart and overlaid it with subway maps. Someone else clipped Kwon's internal inevitability memos and posted them beside old television speeches about national dignity.

The takedown rhythm accelerated. New notices arrived every few minutes, each one sterner than the last, each one legally precise in a different way, as if precision could reverse replication.

By 01:03 the council cyber team hit the Hanriver mirror with a credential-stuffing attack. Two minutes later the fansub union switched to read-only cache and spawned twelve volunteer mirrors. By 01:11 the civic records site posted a notarized hash ledger and invited external verification, pulling in law students, bored engineers, and retired librarians who liked impossible tasks.

Ina watched the node count climb: 14, 37, 82, 141, 233. The numbers steadied her breathing better than any prayer had in years.

She called neurology on speaker without taking her eyes off the tracker map. She gave Dohyun's patient number, set a passphrase for visitor verification, and asked the charge nurse to flag his room for extra hall checks before morning rounds. The nurse said she would notify security and block room confirmation to unknown callers.

Minjae finally replied at 01:26 to the one message she had sent hours earlier.

I saw it. My sister's alias is still covered. Thank you.

She read it three times. She did not answer. There was no sentence in the language that would not either reopen the wound or close it too neatly.

Hyejin collected empty cups and wiped the table with methodical circles, as if calm could be manufactured by surface friction.

"When this gets bigger," Hyejin said, "they'll try to turn you into either saint or devil. Don't help them."

"I don't have the budget for sainthood."

"Good." Hyejin tied the trash bag and set it by the door. "Saints are useless in court."

At 01:40 Ina recorded a two-minute chain-of-custody video because text could be forged and she had learned how fast fabricated certainty moved when it had the right lighting. She kept the camera tight on her face and hands, showed the burn scar and the old ink stains on her index finger, held up each source object in order: her mother's cassette, the shadow ledger fragments with torn spiral edges, the contract page with her own signature, the hymnbook open to page 143 with the pencil notation still visible. She read the checksum string aloud in Korean and English, then uploaded the video to the same mirrors.

A minute later, deepfake accusations began anyway.

By 02:10 she had lost every income stream that once made caution feel rational. Council stipend was already gone. Residual subtitle payments were terminated. The freelance captioning platform suspended her profile for policy review. One publisher canceled a translation contract she had signed in better months. Her legal shelter followed right behind the money: a summons notification hit her encrypted mail with a preliminary inquiry date and a warning about flight risk assumptions.

The room got very quiet after that, except for fan noise from the desktop tower and the far-off siren loops from the avenue.

Ina opened the hospital app and checked her father's chart. He was asleep. His blood pressure was stable. The next rehab evaluation was scheduled for 08:30.

She remembered him saying, Truth does not feed old men.

He was right. Truth fed nobody by itself. It did not cover rent, did not refill prescriptions, did not reverse clot damage in a brain that had already learned too much compromise. Truth was not food. It was exposure. It was the removal of insulation after years of sealed rooms.

Still, as she watched the mirror map blossom across time zones, she felt a different kind of accounting settle in her body. Not relief. Not absolution. More like when she used to subtitle live broadcasts and finally locked timing after hours of drift: the line matched the mouth at last, and the sync did not redeem the content, but at least it stopped lying about when the words were said.

By 02:27 Hyejin stood in the doorway with her coat on and the hymnbook tucked under her arm.

"I'm taking this back to church," she said. "If they search your place by morning, they don't get my pencil notes as bonus material." She looked at Ina's bare feet on the cold floor. "Wear socks. Also call the landlord yourself before sunrise. If they hear your voice on TV first, they'll charge extra out of spite."

Ina almost laughed. "So what, landlords have a breaking-news surcharge now?"

"It's called a penalty and they've always charged it," Hyejin said. Her expression softened for half a second. "Call first, before morning gossip does it for you. Bring cash if you can."

After Hyejin left, Ina checked the hallway through the peephole, waited through an elevator chime and two sets of passing footsteps, then locked the deadbolt again and sat back down between the machines. She did not cry. She did not pray. She monitored packet loss and reposted mirrors when they slowed.

By 03:02 the first civic assembly member had shared the archive hash publicly.

An overseas newspaper requested comment at 03:11, and at 03:19 a prosecutor's office spokesperson announced a task force.

By 03:34 the package crossed one million downloads across combined mirrors, counting only nodes that still reported honestly.

The sky outside shifted from black to industrial gray. A garbage truck clattered in the alley. Somewhere upstairs, a child started practicing piano before school, hesitating through the same four bars until muscle memory took over.

Ina shut her eyes for four breaths and saw the hospital room again: her father's hand crushing foil into squares, his voice saying we were all cowards on schedule. She opened her eyes and posted one final message to every mirror thread.

If you republish, keep redactions intact. Do not post unredacted names of minors or active couriers. Accountability is not a hunting game.

Some users mocked it. Many ignored it. Enough complied.

When dawn fully reached her windows, the takedown notices were still arriving and the files were still multiplying faster than any office could stamp them out. She had traded her last practical shield for a record no single institution could own again.

Her phone buzzed with a morning reminder she had set months ago and forgotten to delete: Rent transfer day.

She dismissed it, stood, put on socks, and packed a bag for the hospital.

There were forms to sign, rehab schedules to negotiate, creditors to stall, lawyers to call, an evidence case to carry into Civic Center security by 08:30, and a father who would wake up and ask if she had eaten. The archive was out. The household still existed. Both facts were true, and neither canceled the other.


# Chapter 17

Bullhorn feedback ricocheted off Seoul Civic Center until the plaza sounded like three wars layered on top of each other, every speaker at full volume. It was only eight-thirty. Family groups from the missing-person network had brought laminated photos and candles in glass cups they were not allowed to light. Council loyalists had come in matching navy windbreakers with stitched peninsula outlines over the left breast. A third crowd moved between them with no matching clothes and the same hard posture, men and women with bullhorns and perfect slogans who did not seem to know one another's names.

Ina stood in line at the security gate with her evidence case and her coat open because the scanner kept flagging the metal zipper. The morning wind cut through the shirt she had ironed at two in the morning. Her burn scar on her left wrist felt dry and papery, the skin tightening when she flexed her hand around the case handle. She had not slept enough for fear to feel dramatic. Fear had become bookkeeping: folder, timestamp, witness list, what she could prove, what would still drown her.

A woman in front of her dropped a box of nicotine patches from her tote, swore, and crouched to gather them while security guards shouted for people to keep moving. She wore a black clerical collar and one sneaker with a split sole taped shut with silver duct tape. She looked up at Ina, shrugged, and said, "I promised my congregation I'd quit by Lent. Today would've been a bad day to start anyway." Ina passed her three patch envelopes and the woman took them with a little embarrassed laugh.

Inside, the hearing room was kept cold enough that breath hung pale over the first two rows. The panel sat on a raised platform that had once hosted zoning appeals and parking disputes; someone had draped a national flag behind it to make collapse feel official. Chief Commissioner Choi Hyun-woo sat in the center with two lozenges lined next to his water glass like spare teeth. His tie had tiny embroidered pine trees, the sort of tie bought by a spouse who still hoped formal occasions might improve a man's mood.

To Choi's left sat Commissioner Yoon So-jin, who had steel-gray hair and a fountain pen she kept uncapping and recapping whenever testimony wandered. To his right sat Commissioner Ryu Min-cheol, broad-faced, former union lawyer, knuckle scar visible when he turned pages. Every chair behind them was full. Journalists stood along the wall with recording rigs balanced on one shoulder. The overhead lights buzzed faintly, a trapped-insect sound that kept climbing in Ina's ears.

The prosecution table had two stacks of binders, one white, one red. Senior prosecutor Im Seong-jun sat between them in a suit too expensive for his fraying cuffs. He rubbed his thumbnail against the edge of a callus on his index finger each time a camera clicked. On his legal pad he had drawn three perfect squares before writing a single note.

Kwon Taesik sat three seats away from Im with his counsel and a folded umbrella across his knees. He had chosen a charcoal suit with no pocket square, no ring, no visible watch. He wore the expression of a man attending an academy lecture he expected to find simplistic. When his lawyer leaned over to whisper, Kwon held up one finger without looking at him, asking for silence and receiving it.

Ina's chair had a cracked vinyl seat. The seam pinched through her skirt. She set the evidence case on the table, unlatched both metal clips, and lined up her documents in the order she had memorized in the taxi: funeral inventory photo from her mother's apartment, phone extraction record, checksum report, cassette digitization chain, ledger fragment scans, copy of Ji-eun's contract rider, seizure report from the Hongje detention flat, mirror-release logs from the night the archive went public.

When the clerk called her name, she stood, swore in, and gave her full legal identification. Her voice came out one register too high on the first sentence. She swallowed and tried again.

Im began with politeness sharpened into a blade.

"For the record, Ms. Seo: you were employed by the organization you now call the Restoration Council."

Employed. He wanted the verb to sound voluntary, maybe even grateful. She kept her jaw loose and chose payroll over apology.

"I was on their payroll, yes."

"You signed a secrecy agreement with criminal penalties, then entered controlled cooperation with the National Intelligence Service. Two institutions, two loyalties."

"Two coercive contracts," Ina said. "Different logos."

Im's pen stopped. "This panel did not ask for slogans."

"I'm not giving slogans. I'm giving sequence."

Choi lifted one hand. "Ms. Seo, answer the prosecutor directly."

"I accepted both agreements," she said, eyes still on Im. "One before detention, one after."

"Then why should this panel treat your archive as anything more than an edited survival narrative?"

The room made a low sound, not quite a groan. Ina saw Choi lift one lozenge and set it down again.

The phrase edited survival narrative stung because it was almost true. If she defended motive, he could drown her in confession theater; if she answered with mechanics, he had to touch the evidence.

She kept her hands flat on the table. "Because survival narratives don't come with hash values, source-device IDs, and independent custody witnesses who hate each other."

Im gave a small smile for the cameras. "Fine. Let us test that claim. Chain of custody, first transfer, no speeches."

She did. She described finding the green lyric notebook in her mother's rice bin, pages wrapped in wax paper that still smelled faintly of sesame and mildew. She described photographing each page that night with a phone she had not yet registered to her own name, then saving raw files to two offline drives. She described the first drive copied in a Seongdong print shop back room with Ji-eun present and the second drive sealed in an envelope by Aunt Hyejin's church office stamp, then hidden inside the hymnbook circulation box as insurance.

Im interrupted before she reached the second paragraph of notes. "No forensic officer observed that first duplication."

"No. At that point I was trying not to get disappeared by the people now asking why I did not file cleanly."

"Strike the editorial. Did any forensic officer observe it?"

"None. Not one."

He nodded as if she had conceded something larger. "Proceed."

She moved through the cassette, the old hiss on the tape, the studio engineer's extraction log, waveform fingerprint comparison against archived broadcast clips of her mother. She described the shadow ledger: printed entries, handwritten corrections, names of civilians harmed after specific drops, family contacts, hospital dates, money transfers routed through church meal accounts when official relief never came.

Im leaned in. "This ledger includes entries you wrote yourself."

"Yes."

"Entries based on your interpretation."

"Some are mine. Most are corroborated by receipts, witness calls, and distribution logs."

"Logs that you accessed while cooperating with NIS."

"Logs that existed before I ever saw an interrogation room."

"But you curated what was released."

Legal language queued up in her throat, precise and evasive. She pictured school photos taped to funeral tables and cut the sentence down to what mattered.

"I redacted minors and active route identifiers so people wouldn't be killed for helping me."

"You redacted strategically."

Ina felt the old reflex kick in, the one that made her over-qualify until language softened into apology. "I redacted with a legal review matrix built from-"

She stopped herself. She could hear how it sounded, how quickly she could drown the room in process.

"I cut names of children," she said. "I kept names of adults who gave orders. That's the strategy."

Commissioner Ryu finally spoke. His voice had Busan gravel in it. "Ms. Seo, answer this for me. Why did you wait until after Mercury Night?"

The air seemed to narrow. Ina could see one of the journalists already framing his headline in the tilt of his phone.

"Because they had me in a windowless flat with lights on for forty-one hours and my father's landlord's eviction photos on a tablet," she said. "Because I gave them one backup server under that pressure. Because the only path I had left was the hymnbook dead-drop, and that took time to confirm. Because if I had posted half a package and it failed, they would've erased the rest and called me unstable."

Im lifted a document. "You are describing coercion by the Council. Yet according to NIS memorandum, you accepted immunity-linked cooperation during the MiraeWave raid case. Was that coercion too, or was that opportunism?"

The NIS liaison at the second table, Director Lee Mi-jung, drew a hard bracket around one paragraph, capped her pen, and angled her microphone stem a few centimeters away from Im. She wore a hearing aid that looped clear around her right ear, nearly invisible until she tilted her head.

Ina looked at Lee, then back at Im. "At NIS they put my contract on the table, then my father's medical debt file, then espionage language from the armistice statute. They said choose cooperation terms today or detention without charge while they froze everything. If that's your definition of free choice, keep it."

"Did you benefit financially from either side?"

"I was paid by the Council."

"Monthly stipend."

"Yes."

"And you diverted campaign funds."

Murmurs rose. Choi banged the gavel once, thin wood, sharp crack.

Ina answered before her lawyer could object. "I diverted money to families on the detention list when restitution requests were denied."

Im's eyes flicked up, surprised she had stepped into it. "So you admit embezzlement."

"I admit theft. Call it what you want."

"And you expect this panel to trust your moral framing."

She heard her own pulse in the buzzing lights. She thought of coins counted on Aunt Hyejin's kitchen table, of her father's hospital invoice folded and unfolded until the paper tore at the crease.

"I don't need your trust," she said. "I need the records read in order." She tapped the binder spine with one finger. "If these are fake, prove that with forensic analysis. Don't ask me to be pure first."

Commissioner Yoon asked for the chain exhibits. She read the timestamps silently for almost a minute, uncapping and recapping the pen with quick precise clicks. "Director Lee," she said without looking up, "did your service alter any source material in these exhibits?"

Lee adjusted her hearing aid and spoke into the microphone. Her voice was quiet and made the room lean in. "Our analysts validated file integrity against device extractions available at seizure points. There is no evidence of post-seizure fabrication in the materials submitted here."

Im turned. "No evidence is not proof of authenticity."

Lee did not look at him. "No. It is not. It is, however, the answer to the question I was asked."

Kwon's counsel requested cross-examination and was denied by Choi, who reminded everyone this was an emergency civic inquiry, not trial procedure. Kwon raised his hand anyway.

"Commissioner, with your permission."

Choi hesitated, then nodded.

Kwon stood, buttoned his jacket, and spoke as if he were delivering a lecture to graduate students who had disappointed him by arriving underprepared.

"Ms. Seo is articulate," he said. "Articulation often wins the first hour of a national panic. It does not settle institutional truth. This republic survived war years, currency collapse, and mass dislocation by insisting on process before purge. Exhibit C-12 moved through a church office without sealed forensic intake. Ledger L-3 contains handwritten edits by the principal witness. Mirror release logs were uploaded from devices already in intelligence contact. These are chain fractures, not a mandate to liquidate an institution in one afternoon."

His voice stayed level and almost clinical, but people in the gallery straightened as if the room had dropped another degree.

She asked for permission to respond. Choi gave it.

"Director Kwon just called survival contamination," she said. "Your team held me in Hongje for forty-one hours, seized half that ledger, and left seizure receipts with your signatures. The records we kept before detention still checksum against what was posted. Ji-eun's rider clause was your switch for feed manipulation. Families are in this room because your chain hurt bodies, not because my chain hurt your pride."

Kwon clasped his hands behind his back. "You mistake burden for innocence."

"No," Ina said. "I stopped mistaking your burden for necessity."

By the time lunch recess was called, phones across the room were vibrating nonstop. Reporters pushed into the hall to file updates. Outside, the noise from the plaza had changed pitch. It had lost chant rhythm and become the jagged roar people made when they were about to start throwing things.

In the corridor, Ji-eun waited with her witness badge clipped crooked to her coat. She had dyed her hair back toward black, but blonde still showed at the roots, old bleach refusing to die. Her left hand trembled against her thigh when she was still, so she kept moving, folding and unfolding a gum wrapper into tiny squares.

"How bad was it?" she asked.

Ina gave her the condensed version. "He got the theft admission. He wanted confession theater. He didn't get that."

Ji-eun gave a short laugh. "I can do confession theater. That's literally what my agency trained me for."

"Don't." Ina touched the folder in Ji-eun's hand, the copied rider pages. "Don't perform for them."

Ji-eun looked down at the folder, then at Ina. Her public smile clicked into place for exactly one second and vanished. "I know. I'm trying not to."

Then the doors at the far end of the corridor banged open and a marshal shouted for all witnesses to stay inside. Through the glass wall, Ina saw a camera tripod go down, then a spray of plastic shields moving forward in a wave.

She did not stay inside.

The plaza air smelled of cold rain, road salt, and pepper spray blooming sharp above it all. Barricades had buckled near the south stairs where loyalists in navy windbreakers had pushed into the families' section. Somebody had set off red smoke; it smeared the air into an open wound and made everyone look feverish.

A man with a megaphone stood on a planter and shouted that traitors were selling military secrets to foreign handlers. He wore polished loafers that kept slipping on wet stone and cursed each time he caught his balance. A woman from the missing-person network swung a candle cup at him, missed, and shattered it against the planter edge. People screamed more from adrenaline than injury, then pushed harder.

Police lines tried to split the mass into lanes and failed. Shields punched forward. Protesters shoved back with signs and elbows. Someone threw a battery pack. It hit a helmet and bounced into a puddle.

Ina kept her head down and moved toward the side gate where witness vans had parked. She lost sight of Ji-eun within ten steps. Her phone buzzed with three messages at once: stay inside, where are you, panel reconvenes in fifteen.

Near the bus stop she saw a girl in school uniform pinned against a railing, one shoe off, white sock black with mud. The sign in her hand had her brother's photo taped crooked, the corners peeling in the rain. She could not have been older than sixteen. She had a cartoon bandage printed with oranges around her thumb and was crying with furious concentration, trying to tie her hair back while a man in a navy windbreaker grabbed at her sign and shouted in her face.

"Go home," he yelled. "Children don't belong in state matters."

"My brother was seventeen when they took him," the girl yelled back. "Shut up."

The man raised his arm again, not a punch yet, but the shape of one.

Minjae came from the edge of the crowd fast and low, moving on pre-measured angles. He wore a soaked black hoodie under a borrowed police-issue rain shell with no insignia, and there was fresh tape on two fingers of his right hand where old skin had split. He shoved the man sideways with his forearm, pulled the girl behind him, and took the first baton strike across his shoulder.

"Move," he snapped at the girl. "Left side, now. Don't argue."

The girl stumbled, found her shoe with one foot, and bolted toward the families' medic tent clutching the sign to her chest.

Two officers grabbed Minjae from behind. One of them was young enough that his acne showed through the anti-fog smear on his visor. The other had an inhaler clipped to his vest and shouted through gasps as he twisted Minjae's arm higher than it needed to go.

"Assaulting an officer," the gasping one said. "Hands where I can see-"

"They're already on me," Minjae said, voice flat with pain. "You can stop pretending this is confusion."

He caught sight of Ina over the officer's shoulder. Rain ran from his hair into his eyes and he blinked hard, annoyed by it more than afraid.

"Ina," he shouted. "Get back in there. Don't waste this on me."

She stepped forward. A shield slammed between them, polycarbonate clouded with scratches. Somebody behind her shoved hard and she hit the shield edge with her hip. Pain flashed bright enough to make her vision white at the corners.

A fiberglass sign pole whipped out of the smoke and clipped the young officer's visor with a crack. He dropped to one knee, and the opening made three people lunge at once. A mounted horse cut across that gap so close Ina felt wet hide scrape her sleeve; its shoulder knocked a man into a barricade, and he screamed, clutching his wrist at a wrong angle.

From a command van, a riot captain barked through a loudspeaker, "Blue line, wedge left. Open a medical lane now." Shield teams obeyed, dragging two collapsed barricades into a diagonal funnel while mounted officers drove straight through the center.

The crowd's front ranks buckled under that move. Families with photo signs were pushed through the new lane toward the medic tent, and loyalists were peeled toward the curb. People retreated by meters, then by feet. The loudest agitators kept shouting, but the shape had changed from attack to stalemate. Sirens layered over chants. Drone cameras buzzed above the smoke.

Ina got to the side door with her coat torn at one pocket and red residue on her sleeve that might have been smoke dye or somebody else's blood. Inside the lobby, heat hit her face and made her eyes water harder than the pepper spray had. A clerk handed her a paper cup of water with shaking hands.

"You are Ms. Seo, right?" he asked.

He wore a mint-green tie with cartoon whales and had ink on his thumb from stamping witness badges too fast. His badge read KIM DAE-HO, Temporary Civil Service. He looked twenty-two and profoundly offended by how adulthood had introduced itself.

"Yes," Ina said.

"They want you in Chamber B. Ministry representatives are pushing a settlement discussion before the afternoon session."

"Where's Park Ji-eun?"

"Secure room with two marshals. She is furious."

"And Kang Minjae?"

Kim hesitated. "Detained at the south post. I heard they're booking him for obstruction and assault."

She closed her eyes once. Then she opened them. "Thank you."

Chamber B had no windows and reeked of reheated fish from somebody's lunchbox. Three ministry officials sat on one side of a long table with polished nameplates and tired eyes. The lead negotiator, Vice Minister Nam Gyu-tae, had a voice of soft sandpaper and a nervous habit of peeling labels off water bottles in one perfect spiral.

"Ms. Seo," Nam said, gesturing for her to sit. "Let us put a containment path in writing so this does not sprawl in front of cameras."

Ina stayed standing.

Nam slid a two-page form across the table. "Emergency closed-session compliance memo. You provide unredacted source drives to the prosecution desk before reconvening, consent to a temporary pause on further mirror uploads, and support in-camera authentication review. In exchange, I will recommend non-custodial handling while cooperation continues."

"Non-custodial means what, exactly?"

"It means you go home tonight without being booked, and your counsel retains access while we regularize the record."

"And the mirrors?"

"Temporarily stayed by court order while authenticity review runs."

"Who drafts that order?"

"The ministry legal bureau drafts it. The duty judge signs it."

She read the bold line above the signature block: REFUSAL WILL VOID DEFERMENT OFFER AND TRIGGER IMMEDIATE REFERRAL FOR CHARGING REVIEW.

A younger official beside Nam, tie askew, tapped his pen against the margin. "If you decline, referral for charging files before afternoon session. That is not a threat. It is procedural timing."

Ina set the form down. "Then run your timing."

Nam held her gaze, then initialed the bottom himself and stamped the page with the red seal beside his phone: DEFERMENT WITHDRAWN - REFERRED 13:42. He passed the packet to a marshal at the door.

The marshal tore off the carbon copy and handed it to Ina. "Service acknowledged," he said. "Please keep this for your counsel."

The bruise over her hip throbbed where the shield had caught her. She tucked the stamped copy into her evidence case.

"The panel reconvenes in six minutes," Nam said. "You have made your position clear."

When Ina entered the hearing room again, Ji-eun was already in the witness seat. Two microphones pointed straight at her. She sat with her ankles crossed exactly as camera training had taught her, but her left hand still trembled under the tablecloth. She pressed that hand flat against her thigh to hide it.

Ina wanted to interrupt before Im got momentum. If she stepped in now, he would tag every answer as coached.

She gripped the edge of her chair and stayed silent.

Im started gentle. "Ms. Park, you're under significant professional strain at the moment, yes?"

"You could say that."

"Your recent projects were canceled."

"Most of them."

"So you have motive to blame institutions for personal career decline."

Ji-eun smiled, bright and empty. "My career declined when your office leaked my private medical records to gossip channels. Are we doing motive or facts?"

A ripple went through the room. Im's jaw tightened.

"Let's do facts," he said. "Exhibit J-4, contract rider. Is that your signature?"

"Yes."

"Read clause twelve, line three."

Ji-eun read without stumbling. "'Artist shall comply with emergency performance directives, including script substitution, visual feed adjustment, and post-event wellness management as determined by Agency and associated public-interest partners.'"

"Public-interest partners meaning?"

"At the time, I was told it meant sponsors. Later I learned it meant people like Director Kwon's team and whoever they coordinated with in government."

"Speculation."

"No. I've got messages and call logs tied to that clause."

Im moved to another page. "Clause fifteen specifies debt recovery in breach. Amount?"

"Three hundred twenty million won."

"You were aware of this when you signed."

"I was nineteen and my mother had signed too because our landlord was threatening to lock us out. I was aware the number had a lot of zeros."

A few people laughed and then stopped when Ji-eun kept staring at Im without smiling.

"Ms. Park," Commissioner Yoon asked, "what do you mean by post-event wellness management?"

The bruise over Ina's hip throbbed under her skirt. She caught herself preparing to nod Ji-eun toward safe euphemisms and killed the impulse.

She lowered her eyes to her notes and kept both hands visible on the table.

Ji-eun's public tone flickered. She looked briefly at Ina, then back to the panel.

"It means if you panic on camera they can sedate you and call it care. After Jamsil, agency counsel told me I had thirty minutes to accept an injection and read an apology written for me." She held up her left hand. The tremor had gotten worse, visible to everyone now. "I did not take the injection because Ms. Seo got me out."

Im objected to narrative embellishment. Choi overruled him.

"Did Ms. Seo instruct you to make false statements today?" Im asked.

"No."

"Did she ask you to support her archive release?"

"She asked me to tell the truth and decide for myself whether I could survive it."

"And can you?"

Ji-eun let the practiced smile return for half a breath, then dropped it. "Apparently not. My agency terminated me this morning for reputational harm."

Phones lit up across the gallery at once. A reporter whispered, "Confirmation push just hit." Another cursed and started typing with both thumbs.

Ina's own phone vibrated once against her notebook. She shoved it deeper under the paper stack and kept looking at Ji-eun.

"Who notified you?" Commissioner Ryu asked.

Ji-eun reached into her coat and held up her phone. The case had peeling stickers of old girl-group logos she had not bothered to scrape off. "My manager sent a heart emoji and a PDF. Very modern execution."

Even Choi's mouth twitched before he caught it.

"Submit the notice as exhibit," Yoon said.

Kwon requested permission to address the panel again. Choi denied it this time.

Public comments opened for fifteen minutes under strict limits and instantly ignored those limits. A former MiraeWave editor described metadata tampering and started crying when she reached the names of two interns who had vanished after raising flags. A veterans' association representative shouted that dissolving the Council would gift propaganda space to hostile actors. A mother in a green parka read the date her son disappeared, then read it again because the first time nobody in the back could hear her over the hecklers.

The hearing had become exactly what officials claimed to fear: not clean procedure, but civic spillage. It was messy, contradictory, and impossible to repackage as one moral lesson. Ina watched Choi watching the room, his fingers resting near the lozenges but never taking one.

By six in the evening, winter light had gone from the high windows and the hall lights painted everybody sallow. The panel withdrew for deliberation. People stayed seated as if standing might break the thin thread holding the day together.

Ina found a corner near the vending machines. Her hip ached every time she shifted weight. Ji-eun sat beside her and opened a canned coffee with shaking fingers.

"I should've worn waterproof mascara," Ji-eun said. "If I'm going to get professionally erased, I could at least look expensive while it happens."

Ina snorted despite herself. "You looked terrifying."

"Good." Ji-eun took a drink and winced at the sweetness. "Did Minjae text?"

Ina checked. Nothing from him. One message from an unknown number: SUBJECT RELEASE PENDING CONDITIONS. REPORTING REQUIREMENTS TO FOLLOW.

Her stomach dropped. She showed Ji-eun, who swore softly.

"Pending conditions means tracker," Ji-eun said. "Or daily check-ins. Or both."

"I know."

"Are you going to fold if they indict you?"

Ina thought of Chamber B, of Nam's spiral of peeled plastic label, of how quickly compromise language turned into erasure.

"No," she said. "I already folded once in the flat. I'm done with that version of me."

At seven-twelve, the panel returned.

Choi read from a printed ruling with both hands on the page, voice steady and almost gentle. "This emergency civic panel finds credible evidence that the Restoration Council operated outside lawful mandate, used coercive contractual instruments against civilian participants, and coordinated manipulative broadcast interventions that contributed to public harm."

A sound moved through the room, dry and brittle as reeds rubbing in winter.

"Accordingly," Choi continued, "effective immediately: one, the Restoration Council is dissolved as a public-facing institution and its assets are frozen pending forensic audit. Two, an independent truth commission is authorized under emergency statute with subpoena power over participating agencies and contractors. Three, the panel refers associated criminal matters to the Prosecutor General's office, including allegations of coercion, unlawful surveillance, and operational negligence leading to loss of life."

Kwon did not flinch. He wrote something on his notepad and slid it to his lawyer. The lawyer read it and went pale.

Choi added, "This panel also recognizes that evidentiary releases in this matter may implicate secrecy and national-security statutes. Charging decisions remain with prosecutorial authorities and are not precluded by this ruling."

There it was, the hinge. The room felt it before the next speaker stood.

Senior prosecutor Im rose with a different folder, thinner and more dangerous.

"Pursuant to armistice-era secrecy law and unauthorized classified disclosure provisions," he said, "the office has filed an indictment against Seo Ina for unlawful dissemination of restricted state material and facilitation of prohibited cross-border communication channels."

There was no gasp this time, only a collective exhale, as if everyone had spent the last hour holding breath for exactly this line.

"Given her voluntary appearance and current public profile," Im continued, "the office does not seek immediate detention. Conditions include immediate surrender of passport, travel restricted to Seoul metropolitan jurisdiction without prior approval, and weekly reporting."

Before he sat down, a court marshal in a navy vest crossed to Ina with a yellow release packet and a portable scanner.

"Ms. Seo, travel document."

She pulled her passport from the evidence case. The marshal scanned its barcode, sealed it in a tamper envelope, then pressed her right thumb to an inkless pad and rolled the print onto a line that read REPORT EVERY MONDAY 09:00 / NO FOREIGN TRAVEL.

Her lawyer leaned close and whispered options, timelines, motions. Ina heard only half of it over the scratch of the marshal's pen as he signed receipt of custody.

Across the aisle, Ji-eun's phone started vibrating hard against the table. She answered and accidentally hit speaker.

"All active bookings are canceled effective now," her manager said, voice tinny through the microphone. "Finance is exercising setoff on your appearance escrow, and legal will proceed on debt recovery."

An alert stacked over the call: KRW 9,800,000 DEBIT - HANEUL RECOVERY HOLD.

"Blacklisted before dinner," Ji-eun said softly. "Efficient country."

"I'm sorry," Ina said.

Ji-eun shook her head. "Do not apologize for me telling the truth. Apologize if you disappear."

It was almost formal, that one uncontracted sentence, and it hit harder because of it.

Outside, the plaza had thinned to knots of stubborn people and press lights. Rain had stopped, leaving the pavement slick and black. Police buses idled at the curb with their heaters blasting, windows fogged by bodies in wet riot gear.

Minjae stood by the south gate under a floodlight with two plainclothes officers behind him pretending to check their phones. He had a fresh split at the corner of his mouth and a gray monitoring band strapped above his ankle where his pant leg rode up. He held two vending-machine coffees in one hand, a peace offering he did not believe in.

Ina crossed to him before the officers could object. He handed her one can. His knuckles were swollen.

"You're bleeding," she said.

"Mostly cosmetic. Makes me look dramatic." He glanced at the officers. "They gave me a choice: obstruction charge tonight or supervision release with tracker and daily report. I chose being useful."

"Useful to who?"

"Depends on the hour." He took a careful sip and grimaced. "You heard about the indictment?"

"Yes."

"You all right?"

She looked past him at the civic center facade, lit stone and reflective glass, all that institutional confidence built to survive scandal by converting it into paperwork.

"No," she said. "But the Council is gone."

"Gone on paper," Minjae said. "People are harder to dissolve."

"I know."

He shifted his weight and winced when his bruised shoulder caught. "Kid you saw out there? The one with the brother sign? She found me before they loaded me up. Said she had physics exam prep at six tomorrow and asked if she should still go."

Ina almost laughed, almost cried. "What did you tell her?"

"I told her yes. Bring an ice pack for your hand and go take your exam. Revolution can wait two hours."

One of the plainclothes officers stepped closer, impatient. Minjae held up a finger without looking at him.

"I can't stay long," he said to Ina. "They're logging every move. But I can still route messages through legal channels and community servers. Slower, cleaner."

"We might need slower," she said.

"You always hated slow."

"I learned."

He looked at her for a long second, searching for the version of her that had once trusted process more than people, maybe relieved not to find her.

"Weekly reporting starts tomorrow for me," he said. "For you too, sounds like."

"Lucky us."

"Call if they try to pull a surprise remand. I've got two lawyers who still answer because I fixed their cousin's heating system for free."

The ghost of a smile touched his mouth and vanished when he tasted blood from the split lip.

From the steps, Ji-eun shouted Ina's name. She had her coat collar up and her witness folder tucked under one arm, moving fast despite exhaustion. For the first time all day, her left hand was still.

"My manager just called again," she said when she reached them. "He offered to keep one ad contract alive if I post a statement saying I was emotionally unstable in testimony."

Minjae gave a humorless bark. "Classy."

"I told him he could sue me in public and practice saying the debt number in front of cameras until he got bored." Ji-eun looked at Ina. "If they freeze my account before rent, I might need your aunt's church floor again."

"You have it," Ina said. "No question."

A siren wailed down the avenue and faded. The two plainclothes officers finally approached for real.

"Mr. Kang," one said, "time."

He nodded, crushed the empty coffee can flat in his palm, and handed it to the officer instead of dropping it on the ground.

"See you at the next hearing," he said to Ina.

"Next hearing," she echoed.

He gave Ji-eun a short nod, then walked toward the waiting car with the slight uneven gait of someone pretending his shoulder did not hurt.

Ina and Ji-eun stood under the floodlight until the car pulled away. The plaza smelled of wet cardboard, extinguished smoke, and spilled instant coffee. Workers in orange vests were already gathering broken signs into trash bins, reducing the day to manageable pieces.

Ina opened her phone and read the new legal notice from the prosecution office line by line: case number, reporting desk, Monday at nine, no departure from Seoul metro without written permission. It looked absurdly clean, black text on white screen, no trace of red smoke or batons or the girl's muddy sock or Ji-eun's shaking hand pressed flat to hide itself.

When she tried to book a taxi to the hospital, the payment failed. A second notice explained why: PROSECUTORIAL PRESERVATION HOLD APPLIED. DAILY WITHDRAWAL CAP KRW 300,000. The app offered two buttons, ACCEPT or FILE OBJECTION.

She hit ACCEPT because her father still needed tomorrow's rehab deposit. Then she saved the legal notice to her archive folder, created a new subfolder labeled TRIAL, date-stamped it, and added a blank document titled CHAIN CONTINUES.
Before locking her phone, she set a predawn reminder: Seongdong print shop, scanner glass, donor calls.

The hearing had given her what she had asked for and what she had feared: institutional consequence and personal exposure in the same breath. The Council had been dissolved. The truth commission would begin. Kwon would not vanish into history cleanly.

Neither would she.


# Chapter 18

The predawn reminder buzzed against Ina's hip for the third time as she reached the Seongdong print shop. The sleet had turned the alley into a strip of gray glass and soaked through the canvas knees of her pants. The old signboard still said `Hanil Offset` in flaking blue paint, but someone had zip-tied a white plastic banner over the doorway: OPEN EXCHANGE BETA / PUBLIC ACCESS / SOURCE LOGS VISIBLE.

The banner had been printed with a borrowed church machine that left a faint vertical line through every vowel. She liked that defect. It made the thing look made by people with rent due.

Inside, the room held the same smells it had held when Kwon ran meetings there: wet wool, stale coffee, hot fan dust, ink. The blocked windows were gone now. They had dragged the plywood down two weeks ago and found a courtyard full of mossy bricks and a bent laundry pole. Morning light came in cold and honest and showed every scuff mark on the concrete floor.

Six folding tables stood in a U shape around extension cords and three consumer routers. A handwritten sign on the front table read NO CLOSED BRIEFINGS. NEXT TO EACH FILE: WHO SHOT IT, WHO CUT IT, WHO PAID FOR IT.

Ina set down two boxes of scanner glass and wiped her fingers on her coat. Her left wrist scar prickled in the damp. The donor spreadsheet sat open on the cheapest laptop they owned, and she already knew what had happened before she looked because nobody had bought fresh coffee filters.

Three red rows had appeared overnight.

`Miryang Civic Futures Foundation - KRW 90,000,000 pledged - WITHDRAWN`

`Haneul diaspora grant pool - KRW 40,000,000 pledged - ON HOLD`

`Anonymous legal-defense reserve - KRW 20,000,000 pledged - REVOKED`

A note from Miryang had arrived at 2:13 a.m.: after consultation with counsel, they could not be seen funding an entity under active national-security inquiry. The message had ended with sympathy and a request to reconnect after resolution, as if the country handed those out in envelopes.

Ina checked the account balance anyway. KRW 1,384,110. She could hear that number in machine sounds: one server fan and the old refrigerator in Hyejin's church office that clicked like bad teeth every ten minutes. One arrest would burn through legal fees before lunch.

She was rolling tape over a frayed Ethernet line when the back door kicked open and Seo Hyejin came in sideways, pushing a handcart stacked with catering pans.

"Don't touch the soup yet," Hyejin said before saying hello. "I put too much pepper because my hand slipped and I'm not apologizing for it. People lie less when they sweat."

She had her reading glasses on the silver chain around her neck and wore plastic rain covers over her loafers. In one coat pocket she kept peeled chestnuts in a folded paper cup, and every few minutes she popped one into her mouth and chewed while she read documents. Ina had seen her do that during church budget fights, funerals, and once while telling a landlord he could take his eviction threat and eat it in front of her.

"How bad?" Hyejin asked.

"Three pullouts. Big ones."

"Then we work with people who can't afford to pretend they're neutral."

She parked the handcart, patted Ina's cheek with a cold thumb, and moved straight to the whiteboard where the access rules were listed. She crossed out `trusted partners` and wrote `everyone on camera, everyone in the log` in neat block letters.

Twelve people had arrived by ten-thirty for a launch that was supposed to have thirty-two. The missing ones sent reasons that all sounded like weather and all smelled like fear.

Six empty chairs stared from the wall like a second attendance sheet. Ina felt the old detention-room chill climb her spine: turnout dropped first, raids came after.

People who had spent a year trying to bury one another still came.

Park Sunwoo arrived first, soaked through his union parka, carrying two hard drives in a zip bag with baby stickers on the corners. At the civic hearing last month he had stood on the courthouse steps and called Ina a broker in whistleblower clothing. Today he kept worrying the cracked skin on his knuckles with his thumbnail and asked if anyone had a clean USB cable because he did not trust the one in his pocket.

"My daughter stuck those stickers on," he said when Ina looked at the hard drives. "She said plain black looked like police equipment."

He set them down and added, almost defensive, "This is detention-family interview footage. Not mine. I only logged it."

Then Lee Minsu appeared, fifteen minutes late, carrying an aluminum camera case and a packet of nicotine gum. During Council days he had run metadata cleaning in Kwon's back room and had refused to make eye contact with anybody lower than director rank. He still wore cuff links out of habit even though his shirt cuffs were frayed.

"Before I hand this over," Lee said, tapping the case, "I need source-chain format fixed. If you let amateurs rename evidence, prosecutors will shred it in thirty seconds."

Sunwoo laughed once through his nose. "Prosecutors are going to shred it anyway."

"They can try," Lee said. "I'm not giving them avoidable help."

Ina watched them square up across the front table, old contempt waking by reflex in both men. One had spent months calling the other a state errand boy. The other had spent months pretending people like Sunwoo were noise.

Now they were the same kind of unemployed.

"No one gets sentimental points for being here," Ina said. "If you brought material, it goes in the public queue with provenance. If your file could expose a minor or someone still inside a detention zone, we redact in the open and we log who asked for what. If you need secrecy to feel important, leave now."

Lee looked at her for a long second, then opened the camera case.

Inside were labeled SSDs, each wrapped in antistatic sleeves with dates in precise handwriting.

"Council internal edits," he said. "Raw and final. You'll see exactly what got cut, by whom, with timestamps. I kept copies because I didn't trust our own archive unit."

Sunwoo pushed his hard drives forward. "Families wanted this released months ago. They said if I sat on it any longer, I could tell my kid I was preserving standards while people disappeared."

Router fans whined into a silence nobody wanted to break.

Park Ji-eun called in on speaker at eleven from a rental rehearsal room in Incheon. Someone had painted cartoon clouds on the studio wall behind her and a strip of kinesiology tape ran from the base of her left thumb to her wrist, bright pink against her skin.

"I can give you cleaned stems from the apology broadcast that the agency buried," she said. "Mic feed, in-ear feed, producer channel. You can hear exactly when they told me to cry."

She smiled because she always could, even after nights with no sleep. "Also, I'm teaching six middle-school kids who dance like caffeinated geese. If you need a chaos choir for the stream test, they're available and they'll demand snacks."

Hyejin leaned into the camera frame with a ladle in her hand. "Bring them. I made too much soup because your uncle pastor still thinks twelve people eat like pigeons."

Ji-eun's smile shifted into something less rehearsed. "I miss your soup, Auntie."

"Then don't miss your train."

The platform repository hit fifty-seven queued uploads by noon, with four arguments in progress.

The number made Ina's shoulders creep toward her ears. More files meant more truth and more ways to get somebody re-identified if one tired person clicked the wrong checkbox.

Argument one: whether all former Council material should carry warning labels for manipulation risk.

Argument two: whether warning labels could become another way of burying useful evidence.

Argument three: whether a truth commission subpoena counted as voluntary release.

Argument four: whether Ina herself should have admin privileges while indicted.

That last one got loud.

A graduate student with a shaved head and a notebook full of statutory citations said, "You're the reason this exists and also the reason people think this is controlled opposition. We can't pretend that conflict isn't there."

She had a chipped green tooth from a bike crash and she spoke in paragraphs, never glancing down, as if she had practiced honesty into a performance discipline.

Heat climbed Ina's neck. The student was right, and rightness never made the accusation lighter.

Ina nodded. "You're right. Put me on a two-key rule. No single-person deletions. I can upload only with co-sign."

"Who co-signs?"

"Not my family. Not Minjae. Pick someone who still thinks I'm dangerous."

Sunwoo gave a dry laugh. "That's my whole personality."

"Good," Ina said. "You get the second key until elections end."

The choice settled nothing but shifted the room from accusation to logistics, which was enough for the hour.

Another donor withdrew at one-fifteen. This one had already sent branded stickers. Hyejin peeled the sticker roll open and used them to seal soup containers.

"Their logo finally became useful," she said.

The brokerage turn came at two, when the room started to sag. People were hungry, wet, and tired of hearing themselves distrust each other with legal vocabulary.

Ina watched eyelids droop over keyboards and recognized the pre-collapse rhythm from strike tents and funeral kitchens: one more insult and half the room would walk.

Hyejin set down three steel trays of pepper soup and hit a spoon against a pot until everybody looked up.

"Eat first. Fight better after," she said. Then she pointed at Lee and Sunwoo with the same spoon. "You two. Bring your drives."

She marched them to a side table, opened her own cloth ledger bag, and took out a stack of photocopied pages bound with hair ties. Church account records. Relief reimbursements. Petty-cash vouchers. Names, dates, signatures.

Ina knew that bag. Hyejin had carried it to landlord meetings when Dohyun's rent went short. When the ledger came out, somebody was about to bleed in public.

"I kept these because everybody in this city lies about money with a straight face," she said. "You were both on opposite sides of my kitchen table for two years. You used to scream at each other while eating my radish kimchi and then ask for seconds. Don't tell me you can't sit and build a public index now."

Lee frowned. "Noona, this isn't the same scale."

"Scale isn't the point. Habit matters most. You both know how to track a number without flirting with it."

She turned to the room. "If you used to work for Kwon, you log what you touched. If you used to campaign against anybody who did, you log what you can't verify yet. We stop pretending purity is available. We settle for traceable."

Lee stared at the copied vouchers, jaw ticking. "Fine. First entry's mine. In March I stripped casualty tags from five Council exports after Director Kwon's office sent the order. My login signed every render."

He pulled a laptop toward him and typed into the open index field where everybody could see. "If I publish that, prosecutors can charge me with evidence tampering. They probably should."

Sunwoo cursed, then dragged one of his hard drives onto the table. "Put this with it. I sat on detention-family interviews for eighty-three days because I wanted a clean legal route and I was scared of burning my union local."

He looked around the room without blinking. "Release forms carry my legal name. If prosecutors call this cross-border coordination, my local loses subsidy contracts and my daughter gets my name on every hate board."

He signed the upload request anyway, pressing hard enough to rip the carbon copy underneath.

Nobody clapped. Nobody smiled. But people sat down with food and laptops and started to work side by side, shoulders still stiff.

Uploads came because Lee and Sunwoo had already paid in public. By late afternoon, once their downsides were named in the log, everyone else's purity speeches sounded expensive and fake.

A former agency paralegal sent scanned trainee debt riders and attached a voice memo in which she admitted she had hidden penalty clauses in plain language because she needed her brother's chemotherapy copays.

An ex-Council caption editor sent internal revision histories with comments that showed who requested euphemisms for casualties.

A neighborhood pastor from Uijeongbu sent three shoeboxes of letters from families split since the nineties, each envelope labeled in blue marker with permission status.

Nothing looked clean. That was the point.

Kang Minjae came in through the loading door at six carrying a weatherproof flight case and a paper cup of burned convenience-store coffee. He had a fresh scrape across one knuckle and the cuff of his black hoodie smelled like solder flux. Somebody had shaved the back of his head too close and missed a line, leaving a pale zigzag above his collar.

The sight of him hit Ina in two directions at once: relief first, then the betrayal ache that still lived in her molars.

He set the flight case on the floor with careful hands, as if setting down a child who had finally fallen asleep.

"Microwave link backup," he said to no one in particular. "If the mountain repeater freezes, this gives us twelve minutes before full drop. Maybe fifteen if nobody breathes on it wrong."

He looked at Ina, then at the room, then back to Ina. "You running this or getting outvoted?"

"Both are true," she said.

He gave a short, tired smile. "Sounds healthy."

Sunwoo did not look up from his laptop. "Tell your guy no hidden tunnels tonight. Full mirror logs."

Minjae bent, plugged in his case, and answered without heat. "I know what transparency means."

"Do you?"

Shoulders squared across the tables, and conversation died mid-breath.

Ina stepped between them before the old script could restart. "No personal baiting. We have a stream in four hours and a legal observer riding with us. If anybody wants a fistfight, schedule it after the kids are home."

Minjae rolled a cable between his fingers, gaze fixed on the floor. "I didn't come here to win arguments."

Ina heard the unsaid part. I came because my sister is still gone and work is what I have left.

They loaded gear into two vans at seven-thirty for the DMZ relay site. A civic-panel liaison rode in front with a clipboard and a jaw that looked permanently locked. Behind him, Seoul choir kids in puffy jackets argued about instant noodles and traded throat lozenges. One boy had written all the day’s camera IDs on his forearm with marker so he would not forget where to look when the feed switched.

Wind hit them as soon as they stepped out at the relay compound. The place was concrete and steel and warning signs: RESTRICTED BAND / NO UNAUTHORIZED TRANSMISSION / REPORT ANOMALIES IMMEDIATELY. The air smelled like wet rust and pine bark. Every exposed cable hummed.

Inside the control room, fluorescent lights buzzed over racks of patched hardware and a wall of monitors showing mirrored signals from Seoul, a church basement near Kaesong, and two proxy servers abroad. Because direct cross-border channels could still trigger prosecution under armistice law, everything had to route through legal gray corridors and latency buffers that added three to six seconds when the weather behaved. Tonight the weather did not behave.

Snow came in sideways around nine and rattled the metal siding like handfuls of dry rice.

Viewer count had climbed to one hundred and forty thousand by nine-forty across mirrored civic streams and fan channels. Comments scrolled too fast to read. Some sent heart emojis. Some sent death threats. Some demanded arrests in all caps.

A government notice hit the monitor at nine-fifty-two: transmission subject to immediate suspension if classified content appears or if cross-border coordination breaches emergency communications guidance.

The liaison from the civic panel cleared his throat. "You understand this warning is enforceable."

"I understand warnings," Ina said. "I grew up in them."

The Seoul children took their marks in a room that had once stored spare routers. They stood on taped Xs between coiled cables and borrowed acoustic panels. One girl had rabbit patches sewn onto both knees of her sweatpants, and she kept rubbing one ear lobe between thumb and forefinger when she got nervous. Another kid in oversized gloves whispered each lyric under his breath before singing it out loud, as if testing for trapdoors.

Across the feed, the Kaesong group appeared on a grainy camera in a church basement lit by two clip-on lamps. Their director wore a mustard cardigan with one elbow patched in denim and kept patting his shirt pocket to check if his tuning fork was still there.

Old enemies filled the back wall at separate stations: Sunwoo on moderation triage, Lee on source-chain overlays, a former military-band arranger checking pitch, two student volunteers tagging redactions live, Hyejin counting soup cups and extension outlets with equal seriousness.

No one pretended they trusted one another. They trusted the log window because it could not flatter.

The stream opened at ten-oh-three.

The first verse went clean. Children in Seoul and Kaesong traded lines about spring wind, market bells, and mothers waiting at doorways. Sound lag stretched call-and-response into a ragged braid, but viewers leaned in harder when they saw the lag because glitch felt like proof of non-scripted reality.

The second verse had a one-second drop and then recovered.

Five minutes later, at ten-oh-eight, the mountain repeater iced over.

Every screen stuttered. Video smeared into blocks. Audio clipped to a metallic buzz. The room lit red with packet-loss warnings.

"Link B is gone," Minjae said. "Switching to backup microwave. Hold Seoul on ambient mics only."

"Kaesong?" Ina asked.

"Delayed six seconds and climbing."

Outside, wind slammed the building hard enough to shake dust from the ceiling grates. Someone in the hallway cursed. The civic liaison stepped toward the master switch as if preparing to pull it.

"Don't kill it," Ina said without looking at him. "If you cut now, every rumor wins."

"If prohibited content hits," he said, "I've got to act."

"Then watch the same monitor I watch."

The banned lullaby verse was scheduled third.

It had been banned for decades in various forms because it named siblings separated by border and asked who profited from keeping the river unswum. Councils, agencies, broadcasters, and patriotic committees had all found different reasons to call it destabilizing. The words were simple enough for children. That was what frightened institutions.

Seoul got the cue at ten-ten. Kaesong froze on a frame of open mouths.

The kids in Ina's room looked at her instead of the camera. The girl with rabbit knees swallowed and whispered, "Do we stop?"

Ina heard her own voice come out steady. "You keep breathing, then you sing what you practiced."

Minjae's hands flew over the console, routing audio through a spare codec taped in place with blue painter's tape. "I can give them your click track," he said. "They will hear it late, but they will hear it."

"Do it."

A dry metronome pulse hit both channels: tick, tick, tick, stubborn as a cheap clock.

Seoul began the verse alone, thin voices first, then fuller as they found each other.

_When I was small, you tied my scarf twice_  
_and told me wind could not steal names._

Kaesong came in four beats late, the latency turning harmony into echo.

_When the river froze, we wrote on ice_  
_so spring would know where to return._

The lines collided, separated, collided again. It was messy, off by fractions, absolutely alive.

In the control room, nobody moved. Not even the liaison.

The third line landed, then the fourth. Every child held.

When the final word landed, comments exploded across the mirrored feeds in two scripts and three alphabets. Some were curses. Some were sobbing punctuation. Some were timestamps and requests for source files. One account posted, `my mother sang this while hiding the radio.`

Then the Kaesong feed went black.

A second later it returned with only audio, children laughing into the microphone because they had made it through.

Air rushed out of lungs all at once, like steam venting from a cracked pipe.

"Stream stays up for testimony segment," Ina said, because adrenaline needed a task or it would eat people alive.

They ran another twenty-three minutes. First up was a woman from Paju with her jaw wired after a custody beating, speaking through a hospital mask.

"They held my son six days on a three-day order," she said. "He came home missing two teeth, and they billed us 480,000 won for transport security."

The control room flinched. Hyejin snapped a plastic spoon in her fist. Sunwoo stopped typing and stared at the waveform as if it might split.

A delivery driver came next, holding a summons to his webcam. "I sang one verse on a union stream. They charged me under emergency-communications guidance, fined me three million won, and my company cut me before the hearing."

Under each clip, Lee pushed the supporting files into a scrolling source ledger with live checksum hashes while two student volunteers tagged chain-of-custody notes in real time.

They closed with no anthem at eleven.

Outside, snow had turned to cold rain. The parking lot reflected floodlights and barbed-wire shadows. Two police vans waited near the gate with engines idling.

Inside a narrow hallway that smelled like damp concrete, Ina and Minjae stood across from each other with the stream noise still ringing in their ears.

He held out his phone. On the screen was a message from an unknown number: `route lead seen near Sinuiju checkpoint. female possible. unconfirmed.`

It might have been his sister.

His jaw worked once. "I don't know if this is bait or real."

"Then we treat it as both," Ina said.

"I'm tired of both."

"So am I."

He put the phone down and looked straight at her, not sliding away this time. "If we keep working together, no more hidden channels. From either of us. If NIS calls you, I hear it that day. If a broker calls me, you hear it that day. No heroic silence."

Ina nodded once. "Right. And if your sister contacts you, we pause operations before either of us moves. Together. You don't disappear for forty-eight hours and call it protection."

His mouth twisted. "I hated hearing that then. It's still true."

"Two-key rule for all releases," she said. "No solo edits. No private cleanup at two in the morning."

"I can live with that."

"If one of us lies about mission-critical information, the work partnership ends. Not a fight. Not punishment. It ends."

He rubbed his scraped knuckle with his thumb. "One lie and we cut the line. Clean break."

Rain thudded harder on the corrugated roof.

She took a breath. "Now the other part."

He exhaled through his nose. "Yeah. Say it."

"No sex as a ceasefire after fights," she said. "I'm not doing that anymore."

His gaze stayed on hers. "Good. It was fucking up both of us."

"If either of us wants touch, we ask plainly first. No guessing game. No using old habits as consent."

"I can ask."

"If we're working and one of us says stop, we stop. No proving loyalty with pain."

Minjae's eyes flicked shut for one second. "Stop means stop. I can do that."

Ina's hands were shaking now that the stream was over, not from fear exactly, from the after-vibration when your body realizes it can collapse but you still need it upright.

He saw it and did not reach for her.

"Can I touch you?" he asked.

The question landed in her chest like a bolt sliding into place.

"Yes," she said.

He stepped in slow, palms warm through her damp coat, and wrapped her until her forehead rested against the hollow of his throat. He smelled like rain, solder, and the bitter coffee he kept drinking when sleep became a rumor.

She lifted his hoodie hem and ran her hand over the scarred ridge along his shoulder blade, the old line from hardware work and one bad border night he had never fully described. He flinched at first, then stayed still under her hand.

"Room three is empty," he said against her hair. "Generator noise is loud enough that nobody will hear anything except bad wiring."

"You just made that sound romantic."

"I've got range."

They went to room three, a maintenance cubicle with a narrow cot and a stack of folded emergency blankets. The fluorescent bulb flickered. Somebody had left a packet of barley candy on the windowsill and a screw sorted by size in a paper cup.

Ina locked the door, then unlocked it again and left it that way because locked doors still made her skin go cold after Hongje. Minjae watched her do it and did not comment.

He took off her wet coat and laid it over the back of a chair. She unzipped his hoodie, pushed it from his shoulders, and kissed the scrape on his knuckle before she kissed his mouth. He made a rough sound she felt in her own teeth.

"Tell me if you want me to stop," she said.

"I will. You tell me too."

"I will."

They moved carefully at first, then less carefully, all the control room static still in their blood. She sat on the edge of the cot and pulled him between her knees, hands under his shirt, palms memorizing callus and heat. He kissed the scar on her left wrist, then her throat, then the place beneath her ear where she always held tension like a secret.

When he reached for her belt, he looked up and waited. She nodded once. "Yes."

The cot squealed with every shift. She laughed into his shoulder and bit him when he said, "I told you the wiring would be the loud part."

It was not clean or choreographed. His knee knocked the metal frame hard enough to leave a bruise. Her hair stuck to her lip gloss and she cursed when it got in her mouth. He kissed her through it. When she got too close to panic at the sudden slam of wind against the wall, he held her face in both hands and said her name until the room came back into focus.

After, they lay tangled in an emergency blanket that smelled faintly of plastic and old smoke. Rain ticked across the sill. Somewhere down the hall Hyejin laughed at somebody for trying to pour soup one-handed while checking signal logs.

Minjae stared at the ceiling for a while before speaking.

"I still don't know if my sister is alive," he said.

"I know."

"I keep thinking if I find her, I've got to choose between pulling her out and staying in this."

Ina turned onto one elbow. "If we built this right, it's not only us. That's the whole point of open ownership. You should be able to leave for a month and it still runs."

"That sounds like hope." He gave a tired half-smile. "I'm suspicious of hope."

"Then call it load balancing."

He laughed, low and surprised. "That I trust."

Near midnight they went back to the control room. Most kids were asleep on folded coats while volunteers cleaned cables and labeled drives. Sunwoo sat cross-legged by a power strip, eating cold pepper soup with a plastic spoon and arguing online with a stranger about checksum standards. Lee had fallen asleep upright in a chair, nicotine gum still in one cheek, one hand resting on a stack of drives as if someone might steal them in his dreams.

Hyejin stood at the sink scrubbing pots with arthritic fingers gone red at the knuckles. She did not look up when Ina approached.

"How many uploads?" Ina asked.

"Two hundred and eleven complete, thirty-four pending, six rejected for no source chain. We keep the rejects visible with reason notes."

"Any shutdown notice?"

"One warning. No seizure yet."

Hyejin rinsed a pot and set it upside down to dry. "Also, your father called me three times because he saw your face on television and assumed jail vans were coming. I told him if jail vans were coming, I would hear them before he did."

Ina smiled despite herself. "Thank you."

"Don't thank me. Pay your share for the pepper."

Civic-stream analytics stabilized around one-thirty: viewership had dipped after the outage, then climbed higher once clips of the glitched lullaby spread. Not viral in the old engineered sense. Sticky in a different way. People kept posting timestamps and line references, asking for full source packs, making their own mirrors.

Former rivals stayed to write metadata together because none of them trusted any one person to summarize what had happened.

It was not resolution. It was labor.

Near dawn, Ina stepped outside with Minjae to the fence line where the relay compound met a strip of muddy grass. The rain had stopped. Fog hung low over the dark ridge beyond the wire. On the other side of the valley, a loudspeaker crackled to life with a military weather bulletin and a warning about unauthorized broadcasts. The voice was flat, practiced, almost bored.

Minjae handed Ina a paper cup of vending-machine tea that tasted like hot metal. She held it with both hands and watched steam ghost into the cold.

Her phone buzzed.

A court notice from her lawyer: prosecutors had filed an additional motion to restrict travel pending trial.

It was no surprise. Her gut still clenched hard enough to turn the hot tea metallic on her tongue.

She showed Minjae. He read it, jaw tightening, then gave the phone back.

"We expected this," he said.

"Expected doesn't mean easy."

"I didn't say easy."

They stood shoulder to shoulder in silence while the relay tower blinked red above them. Behind the control-room wall, somebody replayed the choir clip to check phase drift, and the children's voices floated out in broken intervals, still slightly out of sync, still carrying.

Ina thought of the old years when one office owned every copy and called that ownership stability. She thought of files hidden in rice bins, dead drops in hymnbooks, lovers turning on each other because the structure rewarded betrayal. She thought of how thin this new thing was: an overworked platform with unstable funding, public logs, and people who barely trusted each other enough to share a table.

Thin was not nothing.

Minjae lifted the edge of his coat and draped it around both of them against the morning cold. It was an awkward fit. Her shoulder did not quite fit under his arm with the tea cup in the way. They adjusted and stayed there anyway.

The first commuter train crossed the bridge south of the compound at six-oh-five, a low iron tremor through the fog. The day started whether or not they were ready.

Ina leaned into him, feeling the rough seam of his hoodie against her cheek, and listened as the children reached the banned line again on loop, voices colliding across delay and weather and law, refusing clean harmony, refusing silence.
